Nazir
נזיר
William Davidson Edition - English
https://korenpub.com/collections/the-noe-edition-koren-talmud-bavli-1

Nazir



Daf 1a



Daf 1b



Daf 2a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> One becomes a nazirite by taking a nazirite vow, in which he simply declares himself a nazirite, as detailed in the Torah (Numbers 6:1–21). Additionally, <b>all substitutes</b> for the language <b>of nazirite vows are like nazirite vows</b> and are binding. Furthermore, intimations of nazirite vows, i.e., incomplete statements that are understood from context to be meant as nazirite vows, are considered binding nazirite vows.
Consequently, <b>one who says: I will be,</b> without further clarification, <b>is a nazirite,</b> as this is his implied intention. <b>Or,</b> if he said: <b>I will be beautiful,</b> he is <b>a nazirite.</b> The substitutes for the language of nazirite vows are as follows: If one says: I will be <b>a <i>nazik</i>,</b> a <b><i>nazi’aḥ</i>,</b> or <b>a <i>pazi’aḥ</i>, he is a nazirite.</b> If one says: <b>I am hereby like this, I am hereby</b> a hair <b>curler, I am hereby growing</b> my <b>hair;</b> or: <b>It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to grow long hair, he is a nazirite.</b>
If one says: An obligation <b>is hereby</b> incumbent <b>upon me</b> with regard to <b>birds, Rabbi Meir says: He is a nazirite.</b> A nazirite brings two bird-offerings if he inadvertently becomes ritually impure from a corpse (Numbers 6:10), and it is understood that the individual used this indirect phrase to take a vow of naziriteship. <b>And the Sages say: He is not a nazirite.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara begins by clarifying why this tractate appears in the order of <i>Nashim</i> within the six orders of the Mishna. <b>Now, the <i>tanna</i> is engaged in</b> the study of <b>the order of <i>Nashim</i>,</b> which discusses laws concerning marriage and the resulting obligations as well as with forbidden sexual relations. <b>What</b> then <b>is the reason</b> that he <b>teaches</b> the laws of the <b>nazirite</b> here?
The Gemara answers: <b>The <i>tanna</i> is engaged in</b> the study of <b>the verse</b> pertaining to divorce: <b>“Then it comes to pass, if she finds no favor in his eyes, because he has found some unseemly matter about her”</b> (Deuteronomy 24:1). <b>And this is what he is saying: What caused</b> the woman <b>to</b> commit the <b>transgression</b> of adultery, alluded to in the verse by the phrase “unseemly matter”? It was <b>wine. And</b> the <i>tanna</i> is <b>saying: Anyone who sees a <i>sota</i> in her disgrace should abstain from wine.</b> Consequently, tractate <i>Nazir</i> is placed in the order of <i>Nashim</i>, immediately preceding tractate <i>Sota</i>, which is about a woman suspected by her husband of having been unfaithful, and tractate <i>Gittin</i>, which discusses divorce.
§ The Gemara asks a question with regard to the mishna’s presentation of the different topics it addresses: The <i>tanna</i> <b>began with</b> the statement that all <b>substitutes</b> for the language of nazirite vows are considered nazirite vows, <b>but</b> then it <b>explains</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>intimations</b> of nazirite vows by providing examples of intimations rather than examples of substitutes for nazirite vows. Why didn’t the mishna provide examples of substitutes immediately after stating the <i>halakha</i> concerning substitutes?
<b>Rava said, and some say</b> this statement <b>without attribution [<i>kedi</i>]:</b> The mishna <b>is incomplete and is teaching the following: All substitutes</b> for the language <b>of nazirite vows are like nazirite vows. And</b> similarly, all <b>intimations of nazirite vows are like nazirite vows. And these are</b> examples of <b>intimations: One who says: I will be, is a nazirite.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> nevertheless, <b>let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>explain</b> the cases of <b>substitutes first,</b> before providing examples of intimations, as the <i>halakha</i> of substitutes is mentioned before the <i>halakha</i> of intimations. The Gemara answers: The <b><i>tanna</i></b> employs the general style of the Mishna, which is that <b>the</b> subject <b>with which it concludes is the one that it explains first, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 20b): <b>With what may one light</b> the Shabbat lamp <b>and with what may one not light</b> it? <b>And</b> the mishna <b>explains</b> the details of what <b>one may not light first,</b> before providing examples of fuel that may be used to light the Shabbat lamp.
Similarly, another mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 47b) states: <b>In what may one insulate</b> a pot of cooked food on Shabbat eve, <b>and in what may one not insulate</b> it? <b>And</b> the mishna <b>explains</b> the cases of material in which <b>one may not insulate</b> it <b>first,</b> before providing examples of materials in which one may insulate a pot of cooked food.
A third example of this style is in the following mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 57a): <b>With what</b> items may <b>a woman go out</b> into the public domain on Shabbat <b>and with what</b> items may <b>she not go out? And</b> the mishna <b>explains</b> the items with which <b>a woman may not go out first,</b> before providing examples of items with which she may go out.
The Gemara challenges this explanation: <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 51b): <b>With what may an animal go out</b> into the public domain on Shabbat, <b>and with what may it not go out? And it explains</b> the items with which <b>a camel may go out first,</b> before providing examples of items with which it may not go out.
Similarly, it is taught in another mishna (<i>Bava Batra</i> 108a): <b>There are</b> some relatives who <b>inherit and bequeath,</b> e.g., a father and son, who are heirs to each other; some who <b>inherit but do not bequeath;</b> some <b>bequeath but do not inherit;</b> and some <b>do not inherit and do not bequeath. And</b> the mishna <b>explains</b> the cases of <b>those</b> who both <b>inherit and bequeath first,</b> before providing examples of the other categories that were mentioned later in the opening clause of the mishna.
<b>Rather,</b> the mishna <b>actually teaches</b> in <b>this</b> manner at times, <b>and</b> it <b>teaches</b> in <b>that</b> manner at other times. There are instances where the <i>tanna</i> begins by elaborating on the first principle mentioned in the mishna, while on other occasions he first elaborates upon the last principle mentioned.
<b>However,</b> there is a rationale as to when the <i>tanna</i> employs each style. <b>There,</b> in the passages concerning the fuels one may use to light the Shabbat lamp, the materials one may use to insulate a pot on Shabbat eve, and the items with which a woman may go out on Shabbat to the public domain, <b>where it is</b> the individual’s <b>own prohibition</b> that is being discussed, the <i>tanna</i> <b>explains</b> the cases pertaining to the individual’s <b>own prohibition first.</b> By contrast, <b>with regard to</b> the mishna that addresses <b>an animal</b> carrying into the public domain on Shabbat, <b>where the prohibition comes by means of</b> the <b>animal,</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>explains</b> what is <b>permitted first.</b>

Daf 2b

Similarly, <b>with regard to</b> the mishna that teaches that <b>there are</b> some relatives who <b>inherit</b> and bequeath, the <i>tanna</i> <b>also</b> had a reason for initially providing examples from the first category: He thereby <b>explains the principal</b> case of the Torah’s <i>halakhot</i> of <b>inheritance first.</b>
The Gemara now returns to its question: <b>But here, let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>explain</b> the cases of <b>substitutes first.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>Rather, this is</b> the <b>reason: Since intimations are derived from</b> the <b>exposition</b> of verses and are not explicitly mentioned in the Torah, they are <b>dear to</b> the <i>tanna</i> and he therefore mentions them first.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if that is so, then <b>let him begin with them first</b> in the opening clause of the mishna as well. The Gemara answers: <b>When</b> the <b><i>tanna</i> begins, he begins with the main offering</b> of the nazirite, i.e., with the <i>halakha</i> that has a basis in the Torah. <b>But with regard</b> to the <b>explanation</b> of these <i>halakhot</i>, <b>he explains</b> the cases of <b>intimations first,</b> as he favors that topic.
§ The mishna taught: <b>One who says: I will be, is a nazirite.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Perhaps he is saying: I will be fasting,</b> i.e., his intention is to take a vow that will obligate himself to fast rather than to be a nazirite. The Gemara answers that <b>Shmuel said:</b> The mishna is describing a case <b>where a nazirite was passing before him,</b> so that it is clear that he is taking a nazirite vow.
The Gemara asks: <b>Shall we say</b> that <b>Shmuel holds</b> as a principle that <b>ambiguous intimations are not</b> considered <b>intimations,</b> i.e., they are not considered vows? The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>Say</b> that <b>when a nazirite is passing before him, there is no</b> reason <b>to doubt</b> his intention. There is no possibility that he is referring <b>to another matter,</b> and therefore his statement is definitely an intimation of naziriteship. <b>However,</b> it is <b>certainly</b> the case that when <b>a nazirite is not passing before him,</b> and he states: I will be, <b>we say</b> that <b>perhaps he is saying: I will be fasting.</b> It is only in the latter case, where one’s intimation is so ambiguous that it offers no evidence whatsoever of his intentions, that Shmuel holds that one’s statement is not considered a vow.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> even if he made his statement when a nazirite was passing before him, <b>perhaps he was saying</b> that he intends to purchase the animals the nazirite will need for his offerings and thereby <b>exempt</b> the nazirite <b>from</b> paying for <b>his</b> own <b>offerings.</b> The Gemara answers: This is a case <b>where he said in his heart</b> that he accepts upon himself a nazirite vow.
The Gemara asks: <b>If that is so,</b> then <b>what</b> is the purpose <b>of stating</b> this <i>halakha</i>? Isn’t it obvious that he becomes a nazirite? The Gemara answers: It is necessary to state this <i>halakha</i>, <b>lest you say</b> that <b>we require his mouth and heart</b> to be <b>identical.</b> If that were the case, then if one did not clearly articulate his nazirite vow, he would not become a nazirite even if he intended to become one. Therefore, the mishna <b>teaches us</b> that since his words can be interpreted as referring to a nazirite vow, and that was his intention, he becomes a nazirite.
§ The mishna taught that if one says: <b>I will be beautiful [<i>na’e</i>],</b> he is <b>a nazirite.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But perhaps</b> when he said: I will be beautiful, he meant: <b>I will be beautiful before Him in mitzvot? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>“This is my God and I will glorify Him [<i>anvehu</i>]”</b> (Exodus 15:2). <i>Anvehu</i> has the same root as the word <i>na’e</i>; therefore, the verse means: <b>I will be beautiful before Him in mitzvot.</b> How is this done? <b>I will make before Him a beautiful <i>sukka</i>, a beautiful <i>lulav</i>, beautiful ritual fringes. I will write before Him a beautiful Torah scroll, and I will wrap it in beautiful silk cloths [<i>shira’in</i>].</b>
The Gemara answers that <b>Shmuel said:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where one is holding his hair and says: I will be beautiful.</b> This clearly indicates that he is referring to naziriteship.
The Gemara asks: Since <b>naziriteship</b> is <b>a matter of transgression,</b> can <b>we say about</b> a nazirite that he is <b>beautiful?</b>

Daf 3a

The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, as even according to Rabbi Elazar HaKappar, who said</b> that <b>a nazirite</b> is <b>a sinner, that applies</b> only <b>with regard to a ritually impure nazirite.</b> This is <b>because it is necessary</b> for him <b>to void</b> the days of his vow that have been observed and to begin his term anew, <b>as the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: <b>“But the first days will be void, because his consecration was defiled”</b> (Numbers 6:12). <b>It is there</b> that Rabbi Elazar HaKappar called the nazirite a sinner, because <b>perhaps</b> he will <b>come to transgress his naziriteship</b> now that he is a nazirite for a longer period than he originally intended. <b>However,</b> with regard to <b>a ritually pure nazirite,</b> Rabbi Elazar HaKappar <b>does not call him a sinner.</b>
§ The mishna taught that one who says: <b>I am hereby like this,</b> is a nazirite. The Gemara asks: <b>Though</b> this is <b>indeed</b> a case <b>where he is holding his hair</b> at the time, since <b>he did not say: I am hereby like this,</b> how can this statement constitute an acceptance of naziriteship? <b>Shmuel said:</b> It is a case <b>where a nazirite was passing before him</b> when he made his statement. His intention was to declare himself a nazirite like the individual passing before him, and his statement therefore constitutes a nazirite vow.
The mishna rules that one who says: <b>I am hereby a hair curler</b> is a nazirite. The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> is it known <b>that this</b> term is referring to the <b>curling [<i>silsul</i>]</b> of <b>hair</b> by allowing it to grow? The Gemara answers: <b>As that maidservant of the house of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said to a certain man</b> who grew his hair long: <b>Until when will you curl [<i>mesalsel</i>] your hair?</b> This shows that <i>mesalsel</i> means to grow hair.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Say</b> that the word <i>mesalsel</i> is referring to the study of <b>Torah, as it is written</b> with regard to Torah: <b>“Extol her [<i>salseleha</i>] and she will exalt you”</b> (Proverbs 4:8). The Gemara responds that <b>Shmuel said: Here too,</b> the case is <b>where he was holding his hair</b> at the time of his pronouncement.
The mishna rules that one who says: <b>I am hereby growing</b> my <b>hair</b> is a nazirite. The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> is it known <b>that this</b> term <b>is</b> a reference to <b>growing [<i>kilkul</i>] his hair?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 78b): With regard to the measure that determines liability for carrying out <b>lime</b> on Shabbat from a private to a public domain, <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> The measure is <b>equivalent</b> to that which is used <b>to spread on</b> one’s <b><i>kilkul</i>. And Rav said:</b> This is referring to the hair that grows on <b>the areas beneath the temple.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>Say</b> that he vowed to <b>sustain the poor, as it is written: “And Joseph sustained [<i>vaykhalkel</i>] his father and his brethren”</b> (Genesis 47:12). The Gemara responds that <b>Shmuel said: Here too,</b> in the mishna, the case is one <b>where the individual was holding his hair</b> when he made his pronouncement, so that it was clear that he was referring to growing his hair.
The mishna rules that with regard to one who says: <b>It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to grow [<i>leshale’aḥ</i>] long hair, he is a nazirite.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> is it known <b>that this</b> term <b><i>shilu’aḥ</i>,</b> which is a different form of the word <i>leshale’aḥ</i>, <b>is</b> an expression of <b>increase</b> and growth? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is written: “Your tresses [<i>shelaḥayikh</i>] are a park of pomegranates”</b> (Song of Songs 4:13).
The Gemara suggests: <b>Say</b> that it is referring to <b>a matter</b> involving <b>transfer, as it is written: “And sends [<i>shole’aḥ</i>] water upon the earth”</b> (Job 5:10), where the word <i>shole’aḥ</i> is referring to the transport of water from one place to another. Here too, the individual taking the vow might mean that he intends to transfer, i.e., to remove, his hair.
The Gemara answers: The <b><i>tanna</i> derives</b> the meaning of this term based upon a verbal analogy of the term <b>long locks [<i>pera</i>]</b> written with regard to a nazirite and the term <b>long locks [<i>pera</i>]</b> written with regard to a priest. <b>It is written here,</b> with regard to a nazirite: <b>“He shall be holy, he shall let the</b> hair of his head <b>grow [<i>gadel</i>] long locks [<i>pera</i>]”</b> (Numbers 6:5). <b>And it is written there, with regard to a common priest</b> serving in the Temple: <b>“And they will not let their locks grow long [<i>pera lo yeshaleḥu</i>]”</b> (Ezekiel 44:20). Just as the word <i>pera</i> with regard to a nazirite indicates growing long hair, so too, in the case of a priest it is referring to growing long hair. This proves that the term <i>shilu’aḥ</i> means letting one’s hair grow.
The Gemara offers an alternate answer: <b>And if you wish, say</b> that the meaning of the word <i>shole’aḥ</i> in <b>that</b> phrase: <b>Sends [<i>shole’aḥ</i>] water, is also increase</b> and growth, <b>as Rav Yosef translated</b> this verse into Aramaic in the following manner: <b>When one waters produce, it grows.</b> Consequently, the verse is referring to growth by means of water.
§ The mishna taught that if one says: An obligation <b>is hereby</b> incumbent <b>upon me</b> with regard to <b>birds, Rabbi Meir says: He is a nazirite,</b> and the Sages say: He is not a nazirite. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Meir? Reish Lakish said: He accepted upon himself</b> an obligation with regard to the <b>birds</b> that are <b>juxtaposed</b> in a verse <b>to hair, as it is written: “Until his hair was grown like eagles’ feathers, and his nails like birds’ claws”</b> (Daniel 4:30). Since the verse juxtaposes birds with growing hair, it is understood that when this individual accepted an obligation with regard to birds, he was referring to growing his hair as a nazirite.
The Gemara explains the basis of the dispute: <b>Rabbi Meir holds</b> that <b>a person associates</b> the object of his vow with <b>something that is juxtaposed</b> to it in a verse, so that when he says that it is incumbent upon him to bring birds, he means that it is incumbent upon him to grow his hair.

Daf 3b

<b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>a person does not associate</b> the object of his vow with <b>something that is juxtaposed to it</b> in a verse, and the individual did not mean to take a nazirite vow.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Everyone</b> agrees that one <b>does not associate</b> the object of his vow with something juxtaposed to it in a verse. <b>Rather, this is the reason of Rabbi Meir: We are concerned</b> that <b>perhaps he accepted upon himself the birds of an impure nazirite.</b> Since an impure nazirite must bring two birds as offerings if he inadvertently becomes impure from a corpse (Numbers 6:10), when the individual accepts upon himself an obligation pertaining to birds, he means that he is becoming a nazirite and will be responsible for bringing an offering of birds if he becomes impure.
The Gemara asks: <b>Since</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said</b> that, even according to Rabbi Meir, <b>we are concerned</b> that the individual intended to become a nazirite, but it is not clear that this was his intention, it can be argued that <b>perhaps he accepted upon himself a gift</b> offering of birds as a burnt-offering. Consequently, why is he considered a nazirite? The Gemara answers: <b>If</b> it was <b>so,</b> that his intention was to bring a gift offering, <b>he should have</b> said: <b>It is hereby</b> incumbent <b>upon</b> me to bring <b>a nest,</b> which is the common expression used to accept an obligation to bring a gift offering of birds.
The Gemara asks further: <b>But perhaps he</b> was <b>saying: It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring the <b>birds of a leper,</b> rendering himself liable to bring a leper’s offerings on a leper’s behalf (see Leviticus 14:4). The Gemara answers: This is a case <b>where a nazirite was passing before him</b> when he spoke, and he clearly had naziriteship in mind. The Gemara asks: <b>But perhaps</b> it was <b>an impure nazirite, and he</b> was <b>saying</b> that he accepts the obligation <b>to exempt</b> the nazirite <b>from his offerings</b> by sacrificing them on the nazirite’s behalf. The Gemara answers: This is a case <b>where a pure nazirite was passing before him.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the difference <b>between</b> the opinions of Reish Lakish and Rabbi Yoḥanan? After all, according to both explanations it is necessary to say that this is a case where a nazirite was passing before him.
The Gemara answers: <b>There is</b> a practical difference <b>between them</b> in a case <b>where he</b> explicitly <b>said:</b> An obligation is hereby incumbent <b>upon me</b> with regard to <b>the birds juxtaposed</b> in the verse <b>to hair. According to Rabbi Yoḥanan, although he said this, if a nazirite was passing before him, yes,</b> Rabbi Meir considers it a nazirite vow; <b>if not, no,</b> it is not considered a nazirite vow. Conversely, <b>according to Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish, although no nazirite was passing before him,</b> Rabbi Meir considers it a nazirite vow, as he holds that according to Rabbi Meir, one associates the object of his vow with something juxtaposed to it in a verse.
The Gemara asks: <b>Is there,</b> in fact, <b>anyone who says</b> that <b>a person does not associate</b> the object of his vow with <b>something juxtaposed to it</b> in a verse? <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that in the case of <b>one who says: Right [<i>yamin</i>], that is</b> an expression of <b>an oath? What is the reason</b> for this? Is it <b>not because it is written: “When he lifted up his right hand and his left hand unto heaven, and swore by Him that lives forever”</b> (Daniel 12:7)? It seems evident that the term “right” indicates an oath merely because in the verse it is juxtaposed to an oath.
The Gemara rejects this: <b>Say no,</b> it is <b>because</b> the <b>right</b> hand <b>itself is called an oath, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> in the case of <b>one who says: Right, that it is an oath?</b> It is derived from the verse where it <b>is stated: “The Lord has sworn by His right hand”</b> (Isaiah 62:8). <b>And from where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> in the case of <b>one who says: Left, that it is an oath?</b> It is derived from the continuation of that verse, where it <b>is stated: “And by the arm of His strength”</b> (Isaiah 62:8). Since this is the complementary phrase for the right hand, it must refer to the left hand. It is clear from here that both right and left are themselves expressions of an oath and do not indicate an oath merely because they are juxtaposed in a verse to an expression of an oath.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from</b> grape <b>seeds, or:</b> I am hereby a nazirite and therefore will refrain <b>from</b> grape <b>skins, or: From shaving, or: From impurity, he is a nazirite. And all details of naziriteship</b> are incumbent <b>upon him.</b> Not only does the prohibition he mentioned take effect, he is bound by all of the strictures of naziriteship.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> One <b>is not obligated</b> as a nazirite <b>until he vows</b> that <b>all</b> items and actions forbidden to a nazirite are forbidden <b>to</b> him. <b>And the Rabbis say: Even if he vowed</b> to abstain <b>from only one of them, he is a nazirite.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon? The verse states</b> with regard to a nazirite: “All the days of his naziriteship he shall not eat <b>from anything that is made of the grapevine, from pits to grape skin”</b> (Numbers 6:4), which indicates that his vow of naziriteship must include all the prohibitions of a nazirite. The Gemara continues to clarify: <b>And</b> according to <b>the Rabbis, what is the reason</b> that he becomes a nazirite even if he specified only one of the prohibitions of a nazirite? <b>The verse states: “He shall abstain from wine and strong drink”</b> (Numbers 6:3). This implies that even if one vows to abstain only from wine and strong drink, all of the <i>halakhot</i> of a nazirite take effect.
The Gemara asks: <b>And also</b> according to <b>Rabbi Shimon, isn’t it written “he shall abstain from wine and strong drink”?</b> The Gemara answers: Rabbi Shimon <b>requires that</b> verse <b>to prohibit</b> a nazirite from drinking <b>wine</b> that is consumed for <b>a mitzva</b> just <b>as</b> he is prohibited from drinking <b>wine</b> whose consumption is <b>optional.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> wine that is consumed for a mitzva? Is it the wine of <b><i>kiddush</i> and <i>havdala</i>?</b>

Daf 4a

<b>He is already sworn and obligated about it from Mount Sinai,</b> i.e., he is obligated by Torah law to keep the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship, and therefore it is obvious that he may not drink wine from <i>kiddush</i> or <i>havdala</i>, as drinking the wine is required by rabbinic law (Rambam).
<b>Rather,</b> it is <b>like that which Rava said:</b> If one said: I hereby take <b>an oath that I will drink</b> wine, <b>and he then said: I am hereby a nazirite,</b> the <b>naziriteship comes</b> and <b>applies to the</b> subject of his <b>oath.</b> Although drinking wine is a mitzva for him due to his oath, his naziriteship supersedes the previous oath and renders it prohibited for him to drink wine.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>the Rabbis also, isn’t</b> the verse <b>needed to prohibit</b> to a nazirite <b>wine</b> that is consumed as <b>a mitzva,</b> just <b>like wine</b> whose consumption is <b>optional?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>If</b> that is <b>so, let the verse say</b> only “he shall abstain <b>from wine”</b> (Numbers 6:3). <b>What</b> is the purpose of the additional phrase <b>“and strong drink”? Learn from it</b> that the verse teaches <b>two</b> <i>halakhot</i>, that one is a full-fledged nazirite even if he accepted only one of the prohibitions of naziriteship, and that a nazirite is prohibited from drinking wine even when its consumption is a mitzva.
The Gemara explains: <b>And Rabbi Shimon</b> could respond to this argument as follows: <b>This is the reason that</b> the verse <b>writes “strong drink”:</b> It is <b>to teach</b> a verbal analogy between <b>“strong drink”</b> written here and <b>“strong drink”</b> written <b>with regard to</b> entering and performing service in <b>the Temple, as it is written</b> that Aaron the priest was commanded: <b>“Do not drink wine or strong drink, you nor your sons with you,</b> when you go into the Tent of Meeting” (Leviticus 10:9). This teaches: <b>Just as with a nazirite, it is wine</b> alone <b>that is forbidden but other beverages</b> are <b>not</b> forbidden, <b>so too, with regard to</b> the <b>Temple, it is wine that is forbidden</b> to priests, <b>but other intoxicating beverages</b> are <b>not</b> forbidden to them.
<b>And</b> this is <b>to the exclusion of</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehuda, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> If one <b>ate a dried fig from Ke’ila, and</b> similarly if one <b>drank honey or</b> if one drank <b>milk,</b> which can dull the senses, <b>and entered the Temple, he is liable</b> for violating the prohibition against strong drink.
The Gemara suggests a different reason for the inclusion of the term “strong drink,” according to Rabbi Shimon. <b>If you wish, say</b> instead that it is necessary because <b>Rabbi Shimon does not</b> generally <b>accept</b> the principle that <b>a prohibition takes effect upon</b> a preexisting <b>prohibition.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon says: One who eats an animal carcass on Yom Kippur is exempt</b> from the punishment of <i>karet</i> for eating on Yom Kippur. It is prohibited to eat an animal carcass, and therefore the additional prohibition against eating on Yom Kippur does not take effect with regard to it. The inclusion of the term “strong drink” alludes to the fact that with regard to naziriteship, a second prohibition does take effect. Consequently, if one took an oath not to drink wine and afterward vowed to be a nazirite, both prohibitions apply.
The Gemara asks: <b>And also</b> according to <b>the Rabbis, isn’t it written: “Anything that is made of the grapevine”</b> (Numbers 6:4), which seems to indicate, as stated by Rabbi Shimon, that one becomes a nazirite only if he vows to accept all the prohibitions of a nazirite? The Gemara answers: <b>The Rabbis</b> could have <b>said to you: There</b> the verse <b>taught</b> that <b>the prohibitions of a nazirite combine with each other.</b> In other words, if a nazirite eats less than an olive-bulk of both grape skins and grape seeds, but together they amount to an olive-bulk, he receives lashes for transgressing a Torah prohibition.
The Gemara explains: <b>And Rabbi Shimon</b> does not interpret the verse in this manner because <b>he does not hold</b> that there is a need for the <b>combination</b> of quantities of different foods in order to render one liable to receive lashes, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> Even <b>the smallest quantity</b> of forbidden food is sufficient to render one liable <b>to</b> receive <b>lashes.</b> The Sages <b>stated</b> the measurement of <b>an olive-bulk only with regard to</b> the obligation to bring <b>an offering.</b> Consequently, in the case of a nazirite, who is not obligated to bring a sin-offering if he inadvertently eats grape products, there is no need for a special verse to teach that the different foods add up to the measurement of an olive-bulk. Therefore, the purpose of the verse must be to teach about the nature of a nazirite vow.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one said: <b>I am hereby like Samson, like the son of Manoah, like the husband of Delilah, like the one who tore off the doors of Gaza, like the one whose eyes were gouged out by the Philistines, he is a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson,</b> whose <i>halakhot</i> are explained in the next mishna (see Judges, chapters 13–16).
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need the <i>tanna</i> <b>to teach all these</b> cases? It should be enough to state only the <i>halakha</i> where one says: Like Samson. The Gemara answers: These specifications <b>are necessary because if one said</b> only: <b>I am hereby like Samson, I would say</b> he was referring to <b>another Samson,</b> and this is not a nazirite vow. The <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches us</b> that he adds: <b>Like the son of Manoah,</b> which shows he is referring to the biblical Samson.
<b>And if</b> the <i>tanna</i> had <b>taught</b> that he said he would be: <b>Like the son of Manoah, I would say there is</b> some person <b>who is called that</b> name, Samson, son of Manoah, and this is not a reference to the biblical Samson and is not an acceptance of naziriteship. The <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches us</b> that he adds: <b>Like the husband of Delilah,</b> or: Like the one who tore off the doors of Gaza, <b>or: Like the one whose eyes were gouged out by the Philistines.</b> It is therefore clear that he is referring to the biblical figure and that his statement is a vow of naziriteship.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>What is</b> the difference <b>between a permanent nazirite and a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson,</b> both of whom remain nazirites forever? In the case of <b>a permanent nazirite,</b> if <b>his hair grows</b> too <b>heavy</b> for him, <b>he lightens</b> it by cutting some hair <b>with a razor, and he</b> then <b>brings three animals</b> as a sin-offering, a burnt-offering, and a peace-offering, like one who completes his term of naziriteship. <b>And if he becomes ritually impure, he brings</b> the <b>offering</b> for <b>impurity</b> brought by a regular nazirite who became impure.
By contrast, in the case of <b>a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson,</b> if <b>his hair grows heavy he may not lighten</b> it, since he is entirely prohibited from cutting his hair. <b>And if he becomes impure, he does not bring an offering</b> for <b>impurity.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Who mentioned anything about a permanent nazirite?</b> Since the mishna has not yet mentioned this concept, how can it analyze the differences between it and a nazirite like Samson? The Gemara answers: The mishna <b>is incomplete and is teaching the following:</b> In the case of <b>one who says: I am hereby a permanent nazirite, he is</b> a permanent <b>nazirite. What is</b> the difference <b>between a permanent nazirite and a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson?</b> In the case of <b>a permanent nazirite,</b> if <b>his hair grows</b> too <b>heavy</b> for him, <b>he lightens</b> it <b>with a razor and he</b> then <b>brings three animals</b> for offerings. <b>And if he becomes ritually impure, he brings</b> the <b>offering</b> for <b>impurity.</b> In the case of <b>a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson,</b> if <b>his hair grows heavy he may not lighten</b> it <b>with a razor,</b>

Daf 4b

<b>but he does not bring</b> the <b>offering</b> for <b>impurity.</b>
The Gemara comments: From the words: But if he becomes impure he does not bring an offering for impurity, one can infer that <b>it is the offering that he does not bring. However,</b> all of the prohibitions of <b>naziriteship apply to him,</b> and it is prohibited for him to become impure from a corpse.
This leads to the following question: <b>Whose</b> opinion is expressed in <b>the mishna?</b> It is <b>not</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda and not</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: A nazirite</b> like <b>Samson is permitted to become impure from a corpse</b> <i>ab initio</i>, <b>as we find with Samson that he became impure. Rabbi Shimon says: One who says</b> he will be <b>a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson has not said anything, since we do not find with Samson that</b> an utterance of a vow of <b>naziriteship left his mouth.</b> Samson never took a vow to be a nazirite. He received his status from the angel’s instructions to his mother (see Judges 13:5). Consequently, Rabbi Shimon holds that one who vows to be a nazirite like Samson is not considered to have taken a nazirite vow.
The Gemara explains the question: <b>Whose</b> opinion is expressed in the mishna? <b>If</b> it is the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, didn’t he say</b> that it is permitted for a nazirite of this kind to become impure from a corpse <b>even <i>ab initio</i>, but the mishna teaches: If he becomes impure,</b> which indicates that he is prohibited from doing so <i>ab initio</i>? However, <b>if</b> you say that the mishna is according to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, didn’t he say</b> that <b>naziriteship does not apply to him at all?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually,</b> the mishna <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> and a nazirite like Samson may become impure from a corpse even <i>ab initio</i>. <b>And since it teaches with regard to a permanent nazirite: If he becomes impure,</b> as it is prohibited for a permanent nazirite to become impure from a corpse <i>ab initio</i>, the <i>tanna</i> <b>also taught</b> the same expression <b>with regard to a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson</b> and used the expression: <b>If he becomes impure.</b>
§ The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say that</b> Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Shimon <b>disagree with regard to</b> the issue that is the subject of <b>the dispute between these <i>tanna’im</i>, as we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one says: This object <b>is hereby</b> forbidden <b>to me like a firstborn, Rabbi Ya’akov prohibits</b> the individual from deriving benefit from the object, as he holds that a vow of this sort is valid. <b>And Rabbi Yosei permits</b> it, because the sanctity of a firstborn is not the result of a vow or sanctification. Rather, it is sacred of its own accord, and therefore its forbidden status cannot be extended by means of a vow to other items.
<b>What, is it not</b> the case that <b>Rabbi Yehuda holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ya’akov, who said</b> that in order for a vow to take effect, <b>we do not require</b> one to extend the forbidden status of <b>an item</b> rendered forbidden by means of <b>a vow?</b> Consequently, just as one can render an object forbidden by extending to it the sanctity of a firstborn animal, one can become a nazirite by accepting upon himself the status of Samson, whose prohibitions were not established by a vow. <b>And Rabbi Shimon holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, who said</b> that in order for a vow to take effect, <b>we do require</b> one to extend the forbidden status of <b>an item</b> rendered forbidden by means of <b>a vow.</b> Consequently, one cannot become a nazirite by accepting upon himself the status of Samson.
The Gemara responds: <b>No,</b> it can be explained <b>that everyone</b> agrees that <b>we require</b> one to extend the forbidden status of <b>an item</b> rendered forbidden by means of <b>a vow. And</b> according to Rabbi Ya’akov, the <i>halakha</i> <b>is different with regard to a firstborn, as it is written about</b> this in the verse pertaining to vows: “When a man vows a vow <b>to the Lord”</b> (Numbers 30:3). This comes <b>to include the firstborn</b> and teach that since the firstborn is consecrated, its status is comparable to animals designated as offerings by means of a vow, and one can extend its forbidden status to another item.
<b>And Rabbi Yosei</b> could have <b>said to you</b> in response that <b>he needs that</b> expression: <b>“To the Lord,” to include a sin-offering and a guilt-offering.</b> One may not obligate himself to bring these offerings by means of a vow. They are brought only when one becomes liable due to a transgression. Nevertheless, one can take a vow by extending to another item the forbidden status of a sin-offering or guilt-offering.
The Gemara questions Rabbi Yosei’s explanation: <b>And what did you see</b> that indicated to you <b>to include a sin-offering and a guilt-offering and to exclude a firstborn?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>I include a sin-offering and a guilt-offering, as one grants</b> consecrated status to the animals designated for these offerings <b>by</b> means of <b>a vow,</b> i.e., the act of designating specific animals for these offerings is comparable to taking a vow. <b>And I exclude a firstborn, as one does not grant it</b> consecrated status <b>by</b> means of <b>a vow.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Ya’akov</b> could have <b>said to you</b> in response: <b>Also</b> in the case of <b>a firstborn, one grants it</b> consecrated status <b>by</b> means of <b>a vow, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The Sages <b>of the house of our Rabbi,</b> i.e., Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, <b>said: From where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> when <b>a firstborn</b> male animal <b>is born in one’s herd,</b> there is <b>a mitzva for him to consecrate it,</b> although it is consecrated from the time it is born? <b>As it is stated:</b> “All firstborns <b>males</b> that are born to your herd and to your flock <b>you shall sanctify”</b> (Deuteronomy 15:19).
<b>And Rabbi Yosei</b> could have <b>said to you</b> in response: <b>Granted that</b> there is <b>a mitzva to consecrate it.</b> But <b>if he does not consecrate it, is it not consecrated</b> of its own accord? Since a firstborn is forbidden principally because of its inherent sanctity and not because of a vow, one cannot express a vow by extending a firstborn’s forbidden status to another item.
The Gemara asks: Both Rabbi Ya’akov and Rabbi Yosei agree that the phrase “to the Lord” indicates that one can take a vow by associating the object of his vow with an item whose prohibition does not stem from a vow. <b>With regard to a nazirite as well, isn’t it written:</b> “Shall clearly utter a vow, the vow of a nazirite, to consecrate himself <b>to the Lord”</b> (Numbers 6:2)? Why doesn’t Rabbi Shimon derive from this verse that one can become a nazirite by accepting the naziriteship of Samson, despite the fact that Samson did not accept his naziriteship by means of a vow?
The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> phrase <b>is required by him for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Shimon HaTzaddik said: In</b> all <b>my days</b> as a priest, <b>I never ate the guilt-offering of a ritually impure nazirite, apart from</b> the offering of <b>one man who came to me from the South,</b> who had <b>beautiful eyes and a fine countenance, and his locks were arranged in curls. I said to him: My son, what did you see</b> to become a nazirite, which would force you <b>to destroy this beautiful hair,</b> as a nazirite must cut off all his hair at the conclusion of his term?
<b>He said to me: I was a shepherd for my father in my town, and I went to draw water from the spring, and I looked at my reflection</b> in the water. <b>And my</b> evil <b>inclination quickly</b> rose <b>against me and sought to drive me from the world. I said to</b> my evil inclination: <b>Empty one! For what</b> reason <b>are you proud in a world that is not yours, as your end is to be maggots and worms</b> when you die. I swear by <b>the</b> Temple <b>service that I will</b> become a nazirite and <b>shave you for</b> the sake of <b>Heaven.</b>
Shimon HaTzaddik relates: When I heard his response, <b>I arose and kissed him on his head,</b> and <b>said to him: May there be more nazirites like you in Israel,</b> whose intentions are noble, and who would not regret their vow of naziriteship even if they became impure. <b>With regard to you the verse states: “When</b> either <b>a man</b> or a woman <b>shall clearly utter a vow, the vow of a nazirite, to consecrate himself to the Lord”</b> (Numbers 6:2). The verse speaks of a vow that is not undertaken out of anger or spite, but purely for the sake of God. The phrase “to the Lord” in this context means: For the sake of Heaven. It cannot be used to teach that if one declares his intention to become a nazirite like Samson, his statement constitutes a nazirite vow.
The Gemara challenges the assumption that Samson’s naziriteship was not accepted through a vow: <b>And was Samson not a nazirite</b> whose naziriteship was accepted by a vow? <b>Isn’t it written: “For the child shall be a nazirite of God from the womb”</b> (Judges 13:5)? The Gemara answers: <b>There it was the angel who spoke.</b> Samson’s nazirite status did not stem from a vow uttered by a human being.
The Gemara asks: <b>And from where do we</b> derive <b>that</b> Samson <b>became impure from corpses? If we say</b> it is <b>from</b> the fact <b>that it is written:</b> “And Samson said: <b>With the jawbone of an ass, I smote a thousand men”</b> (Judges 15:16), <b>perhaps he thrust</b> the jawbone <b>at them but did not touch them,</b> and he remained pure.
<b>Rather,</b> it is derived <b>from here: “And he smote thirty men of them, and took their garments”</b> (Judges 14:19). Since he stripped the clothes off the dead he must have come into contact with them. The Gemara counters: <b>Perhaps he stripped them first and afterward killed them.</b> The Gemara responds: <b>It is written: “And he smote…and took,”</b> in that order, indicating that first he killed them and then he took their clothing.
The Gemara asks: <b>But perhaps</b> he mortally wounded them and thereby <b>caused them</b> to be in the process of <b>dying,</b> and he then took their clothes before they died so that he would not touch their corpses. <b>Rather,</b> it must be concluded that <b>it is learned as a tradition</b> that Samson would become impure from corpses.
§ The Gemara clarifies a <i>halakha</i> taught in the mishna: <b>And where is</b> the concept of <b>a permanent nazirite written? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: Absalom was a permanent nazirite, as it is stated: “And it came to pass at the end of forty years, that Absalom said to the king: I pray to you, let me go and pay my vow, which I have vowed to the Lord, in Hebron”</b> (II Samuel 15:7). <b>And he cut</b> his hair <b>once</b> every <b>twelve months, as it is stated:</b> “And when he polled his head, <b>now it was at every year’s [<i>yamim</i>] end</b> that he polled it; because the hair was heavy on him” (II Samuel 14:26).

Daf 5a

<b>And</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>derives</b> the meaning of the term <b><i>yamim</i></b> stated with regard to Absalom based upon a verbal analogy from the word <b><i>yamim</i></b> that appears in the context <b>of houses of walled cities,</b> where it states: “For a full year [<i>yamim</i>] he shall have the right of redemption” (Leviticus 25:29). <b>Just as there,</b> in the case of walled cities, the term <i>yamim</i> means <b>twelve months,</b> as the verse states immediately afterward: “Within the space of a full year” (Leviticus 25:30), <b>so too here,</b> in the case of Absalom, the term <i>yamim</i> means <b>twelve months.</b>
<b>Rabbi Nehorai says:</b> Absalom <b>cut</b> his hair <b>once</b> every <b>thirty days. Rabbi Yosei says: He cut</b> his hair <b>from one Shabbat eve to</b> another <b>Shabbat eve, as we find that the sons of kings cut</b> their hair <b>from one Shabbat eve to</b> another <b>Shabbat eve.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reasoning of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi? He <b>derives</b> the meaning of the verse concerning Absalom <b>from</b> the verse concerning <b>houses of walled cities. But isn’t it Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi himself <b>who said</b> that one may not redeem his house until it has been in the buyer’s possession for at least two days, as the word <b><i>yamim</i></b> does <b>not</b> refer to <b>fewer than two</b> days? This demonstrates that Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi understands the word <i>yamim</i> in the case of walled city houses as referring to a period of two days rather than a year. How then can he employ a verbal analogy from houses of walled cities to derive that the word <i>yamim</i> in the case of Absalom indicates a period of twelve months?
The Gemara answers: <b>He learned this verbal analogy</b> because the verse states that Absalom would trim his hair <b>due to</b> its <b>weight</b> (II Samuel 14:26), <b>and in two days there is no</b> significant <b>weight</b> added to the hair. Consequently, the term <i>yamim</i> cannot be understood to mean two days in the case of Absalom.
The Gemara asks: <b>But say</b> that <i>yamim</i> means <b>two years, as it is written: “And it came to pass at the end of two years of days [<i>yamim</i>]”</b> (Genesis 41:1). The Gemara rejects this: <b>We derive</b> the meaning of the term <b><i>yamim</i></b> in a case <b>where it does not have</b> the term <b>years</b> mentioned <b>with it,</b> as in the case of Absalom, <b>from</b> another usage of the term <b><i>yamim</i> that does not have</b> the term <b>years</b> mentioned <b>with it,</b> i.e., the verse about houses of walled cities. <b>And this</b> usage of the term <i>yamim</i> <b>that does have</b> the term <b>years</b> mentioned <b>with it will not prove</b> otherwise.
The Gemara asks: <b>But say</b> that <i>yamim</i> means <b>thirty days, as it is written: “But a month of days [<i>yamim</i>]”</b> (Numbers 11:20). The Gemara rejects this: <b>We derive</b> the meaning of the term <b><i>yamim</i></b> in a case <b>where it does not have</b> the term <b>months</b> mentioned <b>with it from</b> another usage of the term <b><i>yamim</i> that does not have</b> the term <b>months</b> mentioned <b>with it. And this</b> usage of the term <i>yamim</i> <b>that does have</b> the term <b>months</b> mentioned <b>with it will not prove</b> otherwise.
The Gemara asks: <b>But say</b> that Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi should have derived the meaning of the term <i>yamim</i> <b>from here:</b> “The daughters of Israel went <b>from time to time [<i>yamim yamima</i>]</b> to lament the daughter of Jephthah the Gileadite four days in a year” (Judges 11:40). This teaches that the daughters of Israel would go four times a year, each time for one day, to lament her, in which case <i>yamim yamima</i> means once every three months. The Gemara rejects this: <b>We derive</b> the meaning of the term <b><i>yamim</i> from</b> another usage of the term <b><i>yamim</i>, and we do not derive</b> the meaning of the term <b><i>yamim</i> from</b> a case where the word <i>yamim</i> is used in conjunction with the term <b><i>yamima</i>.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>And what is the difference</b> between <i>yamim</i> and <i>yamima</i>? <b>Didn’t the school of Rabbi Yishmael teach</b> a verbal analogy with regard to leprosy of houses between the words <b>“and the priest shall return [<i>veshav</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 14:39) and the words <b>“and the priest shall come [<i>uva</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 14:44)? It is derived from this that just as <b>this is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>returning,</b> i.e., the priest returns after seven days, <b>this is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>coming,</b> which is also after seven days. Just as the Hebrew words <i>veshav</i> and <i>uva</i> can be used in a verbal analogy, certainly two words with a less pronounced variation, i.e., <i>yamim</i> and <i>yamima</i>, can be used to teach a verbal analogy.
The Gemara responds: <b>This applies</b> only <b>where there are no</b> terms <b>that are identical to it. However, where there are</b> terms <b>that are identical to it, we derive</b> the meaning of a term <b>from</b> the usage of terms <b>that are identical to it</b> rather than from the usage of terms that are merely similar.
<b>Some say</b> that the reason the <i>halakha</i> is not derived from <i>yamim yamima</i> is because the time period to which it refers is unclear: <b>From where do we know that</b> they would lament Jephthah’s daughter <b>once every three months,</b> at fixed intervals? <b>Perhaps</b> they would lament her <b>four times a year,</b> in irregular intervals. <b>Alternatively,</b> it might have taken place in regular but uneven intervals, e.g., after <b>four months</b> they would visit <b>one time,</b> and then after <b>two months</b> they would visit <b>one time,</b> and then they would visit again after a four-month interval and again after a two-month interval.
It was taught in the <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Nehorai says</b> that Absalom <b>cut</b> his hair <b>once every thirty days.</b> The Gemara explains the basis for this opinion: <b>What is the reason</b> that <b>with regard to priests,</b> the <i>halakha</i> is that they must cut their hair every thirty days? It is <b>because</b> after that interval <b>there is</b> noticeable <b>weight</b> to the hair. <b>Here, too,</b> in the case of Absalom, <b>there is</b> noticeable <b>weight</b> after this period of time has passed, and it is clear that Absalom cut his hair due to its weight, as it is stated: “Because the hair was heavy on him, therefore he polled it” (II Samuel 14:26).
It was taught in the <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> Absalom <b>cut</b> his hair <b>from one Shabbat eve to</b> another <b>Shabbat eve,</b> as the sons of kings would cut their hair every Shabbat eve. The Gemara asks: If so, <b>what</b> difference <b>is there between</b> Absalom, who was a nazirite, <b>and the rest of his brothers,</b> King David’s sons, who also cut their hair once a week?
The Gemara answers: There is a difference between them with regard to <b>a Festival that occurs in the middle of the week, as his brothers</b> would <b>cut</b> their hair in honor of the Festival, whereas <b>he</b> would <b>not cut</b> his hair. <b>Alternatively,</b> the difference between them concerns <b>cutting</b> hair <b>on the morning of Shabbat eve. His brothers would cut</b> their hair at that hour, whereas <b>he</b> would <b>not cut</b> his hair <b>until the evening,</b> shortly before Shabbat.
Having mentioned in 4b the verse “and it came to pass at the end of forty years, that Absalom said unto the king” (II Samuel 15:7), the Gemara asks: With regard to <b>these forty years, what is their purpose</b> in this discussion, i.e., to what time period do they refer? The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Nehorai says in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua:</b> It is referring <b>to the end of forty years,</b> since the Jewish people <b>requested for themselves a king</b> in the days of Samuel (see I Samuel, chapter 8). It was <b>taught: That year when they requested for themselves a king was the tenth year</b> of the leadership <b>of Samuel the Ramathite.</b>
<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In the case of <b>unspecified naziriteship,</b> where one does not state how long he wishes to be a nazirite, the term lasts for <b>thirty days.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived, that an unspecified naziriteship is thirty days? In answer to this question, <b>Rav Mattana said: The verse states</b> with regard to a nazirite: <b>“He shall be [<i>yihye</i>] holy”</b> (Numbers 6:5), and <b>the numerical value [<i>gimatriyya</i>] of</b> the letters of the word <b><i>yihye</i> is thirty.</b>
<b>Bar Padda said:</b> The number of days of an unspecified naziriteship <b>corresponds</b> to the number of appearances of the words <b>“nazirite,” “his naziriteship,”</b> and similar terms <b>that are stated in the Torah</b> in the chapter of naziriteship (Numbers, chapter 6): <b>Thirty less one</b> times. So too, an unspecified term of naziriteship is twenty-nine days.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rav Mattana should also derive from</b> the number of times that the words <b>“nazirite”</b> and <b>“his naziriteship”</b> appear, to conclude like bar Padda that an unspecified naziriteship lasts for twenty-nine days.
The Gemara answers: Rav Mattana could have <b>said to you: That</b> word is needed <b>for</b> a specific <b>exposition.</b> The words: <b>“He shall abstain [<i>yazir</i>] from wine and strong drink”</b> (Numbers 6:3), comes <b>to prohibit</b> a nazirite from drinking <b>wine</b> that is consumed for <b>a mitzva</b> just <b>as</b> he is prohibited from drinking <b>wine</b> whose consumption is <b>optional.</b> Likewise, the words: “When either man or woman <b>shall clearly utter a vow, the vow of a nazirite, to consecrate himself”</b> (Numbers 6:2), <b>teach that</b> one vow of <b>naziriteship takes effect where</b> another vow of <b>naziriteship</b> already exists. For example, if one takes a vow by stating: I will be a nazirite today, and then repeats the vow, he must observe two periods of naziriteship. Consequently, since sometimes the word nazirite, in its different forms, appears in order to serve as the source of a particular exposition, the number of times it occurs does not indicate the length of an unspecified term of naziriteship.

Daf 5b

<b>And bar Padda</b> could have <b>said to you</b> that although some of these verses are required for an exposition, is <b>there not</b> at least <b>one of them that is not</b> used <b>for an exposition? Rather, since that</b> usage of the term nazirite is stated <b>to</b> indicate <b>the number</b> of days in an unspecified term of naziriteship, <b>all of</b> the other usages of the term <b>are also</b> stated <b>to</b> indicate <b>the number</b> of days in an unspecified term of naziriteship in addition to the specific <i>halakhot</i> that are derived from them.
The Gemara analyzes the opinions of Rav Mattana and bar Padda in light of the mishna and relevant <i>baraitot</i>. <b>We learned</b> in the mishna: An <b>unspecified naziriteship</b> lasts for <b>thirty days. Granted,</b> according <b>to Rav Mattana</b> it <b>works out well. However, according to bar Padda</b> it is <b>difficult,</b> since in his opinion the mishna should have stated that an unspecified naziriteship lasts for twenty-nine days.
The Gemara answers: <b>Bar Padda</b> could have <b>said to you:</b> The principal days of naziriteship are twenty-nine days. However, <b>since there is the thirtieth day, when</b> the nazirite <b>shaves</b> his hair <b>and brings his offerings,</b> and the prohibitions of naziriteship remain in effect on that day until he has brought these offerings, <b>due to that</b> reason the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> that an unspecified naziriteship lasts for <b>thirty</b> days.
The Gemara asks: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna (16a): <b>One who said: I am hereby a nazirite, shaves</b> his hair on <b>the thirty-first day. Granted,</b> according <b>to Rav Mattana</b> this <b>works out well,</b> since one must finish his term of naziriteship, which lasts for thirty days, before he can shave his hair. <b>However,</b> according <b>to bar Padda,</b> it is <b>difficult.</b> Why doesn’t he shave his hair on the thirtieth day?
The Gemara answers: <b>Bar Padda</b> could have <b>said to you</b> in response: <b>Say the latter clause</b> of that mishna: <b>If he shaved</b> his hair <b>on the thirtieth day, he has fulfilled</b> his obligation. This indicates that the naziriteship itself lasts only twenty-nine days. <b>Rather, the latter clause</b> of that mishna certainly <b>supports his</b> opinion. As for <b>the first clause,</b> the reason a nazirite must wait until the thirty-first day in order to shave his hair <i>ab initio</i> is as follows: Since one who specifies that he is becoming a nazirite for thirty complete days may shave his hair only on the thirty-first day, the Sages ruled that even one who did not specify a time period <b>becomes like one who said</b> explicitly that he will be a nazirite for thirty <b>complete</b> days. Consequently, he may shave his hair <i>ab initio</i> only after thirty days have passed.
The Gemara asks: <b>And for Rav Mattana, the latter clause</b> of the mishna is <b>difficult,</b> since it states that one who shaves his hair on the thirtieth day has fulfilled his obligation, whereas Rav Mattana claims that his naziriteship is not yet complete at that time. The Gemara answers: He <b>holds</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that <b>of an entire</b> day. Although the term of naziriteship is thirty days, once the thirtieth day begins it is as though it has been completed, and a nazirite who shaves his hair on that day has fulfilled his obligation.
The Gemara asks further: <b>We learned</b> in that same mishna: In the case of one who says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty days, if he shaved</b> his hair on the <b>thirtieth day, he has not fulfilled</b> his obligation. This appears to contradict the opinion of Rav Mattana, who holds that part of the day is like an entire day. The Gemara answers: This is referring <b>to</b> one who <b>says</b> that he will be a nazirite for thirty <b>complete</b> days, in which case he certainly does not fulfill his obligation by shaving his hair on the thirtieth day itself.
The Gemara asks further: <b>We learned</b> in that mishna: <b>One who accepted two</b> terms of <b>naziriteship shaves</b> his hair for <b>the first</b> naziriteship on the <b>thirty-first day, and</b> he shaves his hair for <b>the second</b> term <b>on the sixty-first day. Granted,</b> according <b>to Rav Mattana</b> this <b>works out well,</b> since he shaves his head for the first naziriteship after a full thirty days have passed. The second term of naziriteship then commences on that day, which is the thirty-first day since he took his vows. He then shaves for the second time on the thirty-first day of his second naziriteship, which is the sixty-first day since he took his vows.

Daf 6a

<b>However, according to bar Padda</b> this is <b>difficult.</b> Why doesn’t he shave his hair on the thirtieth day of each term of naziriteship?
The Gemara answers: <b>Bar Padda</b> could have <b>said to you: Say the latter clause</b> of the mishna: <b>And if he shaved</b> his hair for <b>the first</b> term <b>on the thirtieth day, he shaves</b> his hair for <b>the second</b> term <b>on the sixtieth day.</b> This demonstrates that the periods of naziriteship really last only twenty-nine days. <b>Rather, the latter clause</b> of the mishna <b>supports his</b> opinion, whereas the <b>earlier clause</b> of the mishna, which teaches that he shaves his hair on the thirty-first and sixty-first days, is referring <b>to one who says</b> that his naziriteship will be for thirty <b>complete</b> days.
The Gemara now asks: <b>And according to Rav Mattana, the latter clause</b> of the mishna is <b>difficult.</b> The Gemara answers that <b>Rav Mattana</b> could have <b>said to you that</b> it is <b>as the latter clause teaches: The thirtieth day counts for</b> both <b>this and that,</b> i.e., it is considered both the last day of the first term of naziriteship, as well as the first day of the second term.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the novelty of the latter clause of the mishna? Is it that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that <b>of an entire</b> day? <b>But didn’t he</b> already <b>say this once</b> in an earlier clause in that mishna, as explained above? The Gemara answers: The latter clause was necessary; <b>lest you say</b> that <b>this</b> principle <b>applies</b> only <b>with regard to one</b> term of <b>naziriteship, but</b> with regard <b>to two</b> terms of <b>naziriteship, no,</b> this principle does not apply, the <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches us</b> that part of the day is like that of an entire day, even to allow counting the beginning of the day as the last day of one term of naziriteship and the end of the day as the first day of another term of naziriteship.
The Gemara raises another question. <b>We learned</b> in the mishna (16a): <b>If he shaved</b> his hair on <b>day sixty less one,</b> i.e., on the fifty-ninth day after he vowed to observe two consecutive terms of naziriteship, <b>he has fulfilled</b> his obligation, <b>as the thirtieth day counts</b> as part <b>of his tally. Granted,</b> according <b>to Rav Mattana</b> this <b>works out well,</b> since the mishna is stating that the thirtieth day can be counted for both terms of naziriteship due to the principle that part of a day is like an entire day. <b>However, according to bar Padda, why do I</b> need the mishna to state that the thirtieth day counts in his tally? <b>Didn’t he say</b> that an unspecified term of naziriteship is for <b>thirty</b> days <b>less one?</b> It is obvious that the thirtieth day counts for the second term of naziriteship.
The Gemara answers: Bar Padda could have <b>said to you: I too rely on this.</b> This is my proof for the principle that an unspecified naziriteship lasts for twenty-nine days.
The Gemara continues to ask: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna (16a): In the case of <b>one who said: I am hereby a nazirite,</b> if he <b>became ritually impure</b> on the <b>thirtieth day, it negates the entire</b> tally, and he must start his naziriteship afresh. <b>Granted,</b> according <b>to Rav Mattana</b> it <b>works out well,</b> since he became impure before the conclusion of his naziriteship. <b>However, according to bar Padda</b> it is <b>difficult,</b> as the term of naziriteship ended at the conclusion of the twenty-ninth day, and one who becomes impure after completing his term does not have to observe a new term of naziriteship.

Daf 6b

The Gemara answers: <b>Bar Padda</b> could have <b>said to you: Say the latter clause</b> of that mishna: <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It negates only seven</b> days. <b>If it enters your mind</b> that <b>we require thirty</b> days for a term of naziriteship, the nazirite <b>should negate all of them,</b> since he is still in the middle of his naziriteship. This proves that an unspecified naziriteship is only twenty-nine days long.
The Gemara responds that Rav Mattana can explain Rabbi Eliezer’s opinion as follows: This does not prove that an unspecified term of naziriteship is less than thirty days, as <b>Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that <b>of an entire</b> day, and it is viewed as though the nazirite has completed his term of naziriteship at the start of the thirtieth day.
The Gemara raises a difficulty with Rabbi Eliezer’s opinion. <b>We learned</b> in the mishna (16a): If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one hundred days,</b> if <b>he became impure</b> on the <b>one hundredth day, it negates the entire</b> tally. He must bring offerings for having become impure, and he then begins his term of naziriteship anew. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It negates only thirty days.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>And if it enters your mind</b> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that <b>of an entire</b> day, the nazirite <b>should negate seven</b> days because it is considered as though he became impure after completing his term of naziriteship. <b>And if he does not hold</b> that <b>part of the day is like an entire</b> day, <b>he should negate all of</b> the days, since he became impure during his naziriteship.
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually, we do not say</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that <b>of an entire</b> day. The Gemara asks: <b>If that is so,</b> then <b>let him negate all of</b> the days. <b>Reish Lakish said</b> that <b>this is the reason of Rabbi Eliezer: The verse states: “This is the law of the nazirite, on the day when the days of his consecration are complete”</b> (Numbers 6:13). <b>The Torah stated:</b> If <b>he became impure on the day of the completion</b> of his term of naziriteship, <b>give him the <i>halakha</i> of</b> an unspecified <b>nazirite,</b> and he must recount an unspecified term of naziriteship, which is thirty days.
§ Until this point the Gemara has been attempting to prove whether the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with the opinion of Rav Mattana or bar Padda. The Gemara now suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that this is <b>parallel to</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>,</b> as it is taught in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“Until the days are complete,</b> in which he consecrated himself to the Lord, he shall be holy, he shall let the locks of the hair of his head grow long” (Numbers 6:5).
<b>I hear</b> from this: The <b>least</b> amount of days that the plural <b>“days”</b> can indicate is <b>two,</b> implying that one can become a nazirite for a term of two days. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “He shall be holy, he shall let</b> the locks of the hair of his head <b>grow long,”</b> and <b>there is no</b> significant <b>growth of hair</b> in <b>less than thirty</b> days. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yoshiya.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yonatan says:</b> This proof is <b>not needed, as</b> the verse <b>says: “Until the days are complete.” What are the days that require completion? You must say</b> this is referring to the days of a month. Since a lunar cycle lasts for approximately twenty-nine and a half days, some months are twenty-nine days, whereas full months are <b>thirty</b> days. This demonstrates that a term of naziriteship must be at least one month.
The Gemara suggests: <b>What, is it not</b> the case that <b>Rav Mattana said in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoshiya,</b> who derives the minimum length of a term of naziriteship from the phrase “he shall be holy, he shall let his hair grow long,” and therefore a term of naziriteship lasts thirty days, <b>and bar Padda said in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yonatan,</b> who derives this <i>halakha</i> from the length of a month, and since a month can be twenty-nine days, the minimum length of a term of naziriteship is also twenty-nine days?
The Gemara responds: <b>Rav Mattana</b> could have <b>said to you: Everyone</b> agrees that <b>we require</b> a term of naziriteship to last at least <b>thirty</b> days, <b>and here they disagree with regard to</b> whether <b>“until”</b> means <b>until and including. Rabbi Yoshiya holds</b> that <b>“until</b> the days are complete” indicates that a term of naziriteship must last <b>until, but not</b> necessarily <b>including,</b> the thirtieth day of a full month. Consequently, he derives the minimum length of a term of naziriteship from the phrase “he shall let the locks of the hair of his head grow long.” <b>And Rabbi Yonatan holds</b> that the term <b>“until”</b> means <b>until and including</b> the thirtieth day of a full month, and he derives the minimum length of a term of naziriteship from the phrase “until the days are complete.”
The Gemara now analyzes the <i>baraita</i> that cites the opinions of Rabbi Yoshiya and Rabbi Yonatan. <b>The Master,</b> Rabbi Yonatan, <b>said: What are the days that require completion? You must say</b> this is referring to the <b>thirty</b> days of a full month. The Gemara asks: <b>But say</b> it is referring to the seven days of <b>a week.</b> The Gemara responds: In the case of <b>a week, is there</b> ever a week that is <b>lacking?</b> All weeks are seven days. Conversely, since some months are twenty-nine days and others are thirty days, it can be said that the thirtieth day completes that which was lacking.

Daf 7a

The Gemara asks further: <b>But say</b> it is referring to <b>a year,</b> which can also be full or lacking. The Gemara responds: <b>Do we count</b> years <b>by days? Didn’t the Sages of Caesarea say: From where</b> is it derived <b>that one does not count days toward years,</b> but that years are calculated according to months? <b>As it is stated: “Of the months of the year”</b> (Exodus 12:2). This teaches that <b>months are calculated to</b> comprise <b>years, but days are not</b> counted <b>toward years.</b> Therefore, only a month can be described as being completed by a particular day.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>one said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one long</b> term, or: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one short</b> term, or <b>even</b> if one said: I am hereby a nazirite <b>from now until the end of the world,</b> in all these cases he is <b>a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty days.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that even one who said: I am hereby a nazirite from now until the end of the world, becomes a nazirite for thirty days. The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> does he become a nazirite for only thirty days? <b>Didn’t he say: From now until the end of the world?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>This is what he is saying:</b> Because of the difficulties it entails, it is as though <b>this matter</b> of naziriteship <b>were as lengthy for me as</b> the time <b>from now until the end of the world.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna (8a): If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite from here until such and such a place, one estimates how many days</b> it takes to walk <b>from here until such and such a place.</b> If it is a distance of <b>less than thirty days,</b> he is <b>a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty days,</b> since this is the minimum term of naziriteship. <b>And if not,</b> i.e., if it takes more than thirty days to walk that distance, he is <b>a nazirite in accordance with the number of days</b> it takes to walk to that place. <b>But here too,</b> in that mishna, <b>say</b> that the individual intends to accept only a thirty-day term of naziriteship, and he means to say: <b>This matter</b> of naziriteship <b>is as lengthy for me as</b> the time it would take me to walk <b>from here until such and such a place.</b>
<b>Rava said:</b> That mishna is referring to one <b>who had</b> already <b>set out on the way,</b> so that it is apparent that his intention is to be a nazirite until he reaches his destination. The Gemara asks: Why is it assumed that he means to accept a single term of naziriteship that is the length of time it takes to travel to a certain destination? <b>And let each and every parasang [<i>parsa</i>]</b> be understood to refer to a separate term of naziriteship, such that the individual is understood to have accepted a separate term of naziriteship for each parasang that he must travel. <b>Rav Pappa said:</b> The mishna deals <b>with a place where</b> people <b>do not measure</b> distances in <b>parasangs.</b>
The Gemara asks further: <b>And let each and every way station [<i>avvana</i>],</b> where travelers lodge at night, be understood to refer to a separate term of naziriteship, such that the individual is understood to have accepted a separate term of naziriteship for each station that he passes along the way. <b>Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (8a): If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite like the dust of the earth, or: Like the hair of my head, or: Like the sand of the sea, he is a nazirite forever,</b> as it is understood that he accepted upon himself terms of naziriteship in accordance with the number of his hairs, or grains of dust, or sand, <b>and he shaves once</b> every <b>thirty days?</b>
The Gemara responds: <b>Everything that has a fixed amount is not taught</b> in this clause, which states that one is a nazirite forever. When one declares that he will be a nazirite for a length of time corresponding to an item that people consider infinite, e.g., the dust of the earth, his intention is not to define the length of a single term of naziriteship, as it is unknown how many grains of dust there are. However, when one mentions something that does have a fixed number, e.g., the number of days it takes to walk to a certain place, his intention is to define the length of a single term of naziriteship.
<b>And</b> similarly, <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite all the days of my life,</b> or: <b>I am hereby a permanent nazirite, he is a permanent nazirite.</b> However, if he said that he is a nazirite for a fixed period of time, <b>even</b> for <b>one hundred years</b> or <b>even</b> for <b>one thousand years, he is not a permanent nazirite. Rather,</b> he is a regular <b>nazirite forever,</b> since he will not live long enough to complete his term of naziriteship. This demonstrates that there is a difference between a naziriteship that lasts for a fixed time and a naziriteship that is unlimited.
<b>Rabba said</b> another reason for the distinction between one who accepts naziriteship based on the distance between places and one who accepts naziriteship based on the number of hairs on his head or the dust of the earth. <b>Hairs</b> are <b>different, since they are separated from each other.</b> Consequently, one who says: Like the hair of my head, is referring to distinct terms of naziriteship.
The Gemara asks: <b>Also with regard to days, isn’t it written: “And there was evening and there was morning, one day”</b> (Genesis 1:5), which indicates that each day is a separate unit? Consequently, one who vows to be a nazirite: From here until such and such a place, should be considered to have accepted upon himself distinct terms of naziriteship corresponding to the number of days it takes to travel to the designated destination.
The Gemara answers: The meaning of the verse <b>there is not that</b> days <b>are separated from each other,</b> because time is continuous. Rather, <b>what it is saying</b> is that the period of <b>day and night is one</b> calendar <b>day, but actually</b> days <b>are not separate from each other.</b>
The discussion above stemmed from the difficulty with the mishna’s case where one accepted naziriteship from now until the end of the world. <b>Rava said: Why</b> do <b>you raise all these difficulties? It is different there, as it teaches</b> that the individual said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one</b> long term. Since he specified that he is accepting one term of naziriteship, his statement is interpreted accordingly and is not interpreted as a reference to multiple terms of naziriteship.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite and one day,</b> or: <b>I am hereby a nazirite and one hour,</b> or: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one and a half, he</b> becomes <b>a nazirite</b> for <b>two</b> consecutive terms of naziriteship. When he says: I am hereby a nazirite, he accepts upon himself one thirty-day term of naziriteship. When he subsequently adds an additional amount of time, e.g., an extra day, he thereby accepts upon himself an additional term of naziriteship, and the minimal term of naziriteship is thirty days.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need the mishna <b>to teach all these</b> cases rather than giving just one example of the mishna’s principle? The Gemara explains: All the cases <b>are necessary, as, if</b> the mishna had <b>taught</b> only the case where one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite and one day,</b> one might have thought: <b>It is here that we say</b> that <b>there is no naziriteship for one day,</b> and <b>therefore he must count two</b> terms of naziriteship. <b>However,</b> if he said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite and one hour, let him count thirty-one days.</b> The mishna therefore <b>teaches us</b> that even in this latter case he must keep two terms of naziriteship.

Daf 7b

<b>And if</b> the mishna <b>taught</b> only the case where one said: I am hereby a nazirite and <b>one hour,</b> one might have said that he is obligated in two periods of naziriteship <b>because he did not go into specific detail [<i>davka</i>].</b> He merely stated that he accepts upon himself some part of a second term of naziriteship, and therefore he must observe a full second term of naziriteship. <b>However,</b> in the case of one who says he will be a nazirite for <b>one and a half, where</b> he <b>does go into specific detail,</b> one might <b>say he should not</b> have to <b>count two</b> full terms of naziriteship but rather forty-five days of naziriteship. The mishna therefore <b>teaches us</b> that in <b>all these</b> cases <b>he is a nazirite</b> for <b>two</b> full terms of naziriteship.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> One who says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty days and one hour,</b> becomes <b>a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty-one days, as there is no naziriteship for hours</b> but only for full days.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav said: They taught</b> that he is a nazirite for thirty-one days <b>only when he said: Thirty-one days. But</b> if <b>he said: Thirty days and one day,</b> he is <b>a nazirite</b> for <b>two</b> terms of naziriteship, since the thirty days are considered one term of naziriteship, and the additional day is the start of an additional term.
The Gemara comments: In this regard, <b>Rav holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, who derives</b> meaning from <b>superfluous language.</b> Rabbi Akiva believes that if one uses unnecessary words, it is inferred that he intended to add something. In this case, since the individual said: Thirty days and one day, without combining them into one amount, he intended to accept an additional term of naziriteship.
<b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Bava Batra</i> 64a): According to Rabbi Akiva, if one sold his house to another without specification, he has sold <b>neither the pit nor the cistern [<i>dut</i>]</b> with it, <b>although he wrote to him</b> in the document of sale: With <b>its depth and its height.</b> This is because anything that is not part of the house, e.g., pits and cisterns, must be explicitly mentioned in the contract. <b>And</b> the seller <b>must purchase a path</b> through the property he sold along with the house in order to reach the pit or cistern that he kept for himself. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva.</b>
