<b>And the Rabbis say: He does not need to purchase a path,</b> as it is assumed that just as he maintained his rights to the pit or cistern, he also maintained the right to walk through the rest of the property in order to access the pit or cistern. <b>And Rabbi Akiva concedes that when he says to him</b> in the document of sale: <b>Apart from these,</b> i.e., the pit and cistern, <b>that he does not need to purchase a path.</b> Rabbi Akiva’s reasoning is this: Since the seller unnecessarily stressed that the pit and cistern are not included in the sale, he must have intended to thereby reserve for himself the right of access. This proves that, according to Rabbi Akiva, if one adds a superfluous clause he must have had a specific meaning in mind, and something must be derived from his statement. Rav follows a similar line of reasoning with regard to the case of a nazirite.

Daf 8a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite like the hair of my head, or: Like the dust of the earth, or: Like the sand of the sea, he is a nazirite forever.</b> He has accepted a separate term of naziriteship for every hair or particle of dust or sand, which in practice means that he will be a nazirite forever. <b>And</b> he <b>shaves</b> his hair <b>once</b> every <b>thirty days.</b>
<b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: This</b> nazirite <b>does not shave</b> his hair <b>once</b> every <b>thirty days,</b> as he has accepted upon himself one long term of naziriteship lasting for as many days as there are hairs or particles of dust or sand. <b>And who is</b> the nazirite who <b>shaves</b> his hair <b>once</b> every <b>thirty</b> days? One <b>who says: It is hereby</b> incumbent <b>upon me</b> to observe <b>naziriteships like the hair of my head, or: Like the dust of the earth, or: Like the sand of the sea.</b> Since he used the plural term naziriteships, it is clear that he is accepting distinct terms of naziriteship.
If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> in accordance with <b>the capacity of the house, or: The capacity of the basket, one checks</b> with <b>him</b> what he had in mind. <b>If he said:</b> My intention was to <b>take a nazirite vow</b> for <b>one long</b> term of naziriteship, he is <b>a nazirite</b> for only <b>thirty days,</b> in accordance with the ruling of the mishna that the words long or short are of no account when used in a nazirite vow (7a). <b>And if he said: I took a nazirite vow without specification,</b> it is assumed that he meant to accept upon himself terms of naziriteship corresponding to the number of items that fit into the basket, and the smallest items normally placed in baskets are used for this evaluation. Consequently, one <b>views the basket as though</b> it were <b>full of mustard</b> seeds, which are extremely small, <b>and he is a nazirite</b> for <b>his entire life.</b>
If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite from here until such and such a place, one estimates how many days</b> it takes to walk <b>from here until such and such a place. If</b> it is <b>less than thirty days,</b> he is <b>a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty days,</b> since this is the minimum term of naziriteship. <b>And if not,</b> i.e., if it takes more than thirty days to walk that distance, he is <b>a nazirite in accordance with the number of days</b> it takes to walk to that place.
If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite in accordance with the number of days</b> in <b>a solar</b> year, he <b>counts</b> 365 consecutive <b>naziriteships, in accordance with the number of days</b> in <b>a solar</b> year. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: There was an incident</b> where someone took this vow and observed 365 consecutive terms of naziriteship. <b>Once he completed</b> all these terms of naziriteship, <b>he died.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that if one said: I am hereby a nazirite in accordance with the capacity of the basket, and he did not specifically intend to accept one term of naziriteship, one <b>views the basket as though</b> it were <b>full of mustard</b> seeds, <b>and he is a nazirite</b> for <b>his entire life.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But why</b> does one view the basket as though it were full of mustard seeds? <b>Let us view it as though</b> it were <b>full of cucumbers or gourds,</b> which are much larger. The basket would consequently hold fewer of them, <b>and there would be a remedy for him,</b> i.e., he would be able to complete his terms of naziriteship and resume living as a non-nazirite.
In response to this question, <b>Ḥizkiyya said:</b> This issue <b>is taught</b> as <b>a dispute</b> between <i>tanna’im</i>, <b>and</b> the mishna <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, who said: A person places himself in a state where</b> the resulting <b>uncertainty is more stringent than</b> if there were <b>certainty,</b> i.e., an individual willingly accepts conditions that are ambiguous although this may cause him to have to keep more stringent <i>halakhot</i> if the uncertainty is not clarified.
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to one who says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite on the condition that this pile</b> of grain <b>will be</b> found to contain at least <b>one hundred <i>kor</i>, and he went</b> to measure the pile <b>and found that it was stolen or lost,</b> making it impossible to determine whether it contained one hundred <i>kor</i>, <b>Rabbi Shimon prohibits</b> him to drink wine or cut his hair, <b>as</b> he holds that in a case of <b>uncertain naziriteship</b> one is required <b>to</b> act <b>stringently.</b> Similarly, in the case in the mishna, since it is not known whether one intended to accept naziriteship according to the number of mustard seeds in the basket or according to the number of gourds there, he must act stringently.
Conversely, <b>Rabbi Yehuda permits</b> him to drink wine or cut his hair, <b>as</b> he holds that in a case of <b>uncertain naziriteship</b> one is permitted <b>to</b> act <b>leniently.</b> The naziriteship does not take effect, since the pile might have contained less than one hundred <i>kor</i>.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: You</b> can <b>even say</b> that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> because the cases in the mishna and in the <i>baraita</i> are not comparable. <b>There,</b> in the case discussed in the <i>baraita</i>, <b>he does not</b> necessarily even <b>enter</b> a state of <b>naziriteship,</b> as it is unclear whether the pile contained a <i>kor</i> of grain. Consequently, he retains his previous status and is not considered a nazirite. Conversely, in the case discussed <b>here</b> in the mishna, <b>he</b> certainly <b>enters</b> a state of <b>naziriteship,</b> since he undoubtedly vowed to be a nazirite for some period of time. Consequently, <b>how</b> is it possible <b>to remove</b> the state of naziriteship <b>from him</b> when it is uncertain when his terms end? Therefore, even Rabbi Yehuda would agree that he remains a nazirite indefinitely.
The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is it <b>not</b> possible to remove from him the status of naziriteship? <b>Let us view the basket as though</b> it were <b>full of cucumbers or gourds,</b> as he has accepted at least as many terms of naziriteship as the number of cucumbers or gourds that can fit in the basket, <b>and</b> in this way <b>there will be a remedy for him. It entered our minds</b> to say that <b>it is</b> distinct <b>naziriteships that he accepted upon himself,</b> and so once he has completed the minimal number of naziriteships, he should no longer be considered a nazirite unless it can be determined that he accepted more than this number of terms of naziriteship.

Daf 8b

The Gemara answers: This is not possible, <b>as Rabbi Yehuda holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>as we learned</b> in the mishna (8a) with regard to one who vows: I am hereby a nazirite like the hair of my head, <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: This</b> nazirite <b>does not shave</b> his hair <b>once</b> every <b>thirty days,</b> as he is considered to have accepted one long term of naziriteship. <b>And who is</b> the nazirite <b>who</b> has accepted distinct terms of naziriteship and therefore <b>shaves</b> his hair <b>once</b> every <b>thirty days?</b> One <b>who says: It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me</b> to observe <b>naziriteships like the hair of my head, or: Like the dust of the earth, or: Like the sand of the sea.</b> Since Rabbi Yehuda agrees with Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, he holds that one who uses a formulation similar to that used in the first case in the mishna and says: I am hereby a nazirite according to the capacity of the basket, has accepted one long term of naziriteship.
The Gemara asks: <b>And does Rabbi Yehuda hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna that if one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite in accordance with the number of days</b> in <b>a solar</b> year, he counts 365 consecutive naziriteships, in accordance with the number of days in a solar year. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: There was an incident</b> where someone took this vow and observed 365 consecutive terms of naziriteship. <b>Once he completed</b> all these terms of naziriteship, <b>he died.</b>
The Gemara explains its question: <b>Granted, if you say</b> that <b>he accepts upon himself</b> many <b>naziriteships, that is the reason</b> for his statement <b>that once he completed</b> all these terms of naziriteship <b>he died. However, if you say</b> that Rabbi Yehuda does not agree with the unattributed opinion of the mishna and holds that <b>he accepted upon himself one</b> term of <b>naziriteship</b> lasting 365 days, <b>is there completion</b> here <b>at all?</b> Rabbi Yehuda would not have used this terminology in reference to the mishna’s previous statement if he himself holds that the individual accepted only one term of naziriteship.
<b>And furthermore, does</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> like <b>the number of piles [<i>helketei</i>] of figs [<i>kayitz</i>]</b> left to dry, <b>or:</b> Like <b>the number of sheaves of the Sabbatical Year,</b> he must <b>count</b> as many <b>naziriteships as the number of piles of figs or as the number of sheaves of the Sabbatical Year?</b> This proves that Rabbi Yehuda does not hold that the individual accepts only one long term of naziriteship.
The Gemara responds: This does not prove that Rabbi Yehuda does not agree with Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, since one who specifies: Like the <b>number, is different,</b> as even Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi would agree that he is referring to distinct terms of naziriteship rather than one long term of naziriteship.
The Gemara asks: <b>And is</b> the case of one who says: Like the <b>number, different</b> according <b>to Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that if one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite like the number of days</b> in <b>a solar</b> year, <b>he counts naziriteships</b> corresponding <b>to the number of days</b> in <b>a solar</b> year. If one says: <b>Like the days</b> in <b>a lunar</b> year, i.e., a year comprised of twelve lunar months, <b>he counts</b> terms of naziriteship corresponding <b>to the days</b> in <b>a lunar</b> year. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> He is assumed to have accepted one term of naziriteship <b>until he says:</b> It is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to observe <b>naziriteships like the number of days</b> in <b>a solar</b> year, <b>or: Like the number of days</b> in <b>a lunar</b> year. This demonstrates that if one did not explicitly use the plural term naziriteships, he is assumed to have accepted a single term of naziriteship even if he said: Like the number.
The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Yehuda holds in accordance with</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s opinion <b>in one</b> case <b>and disagrees with him in one</b> case. <b>He holds in accordance with his</b> opinion <b>in one</b> case, as follows: If one says: I am hereby a nazirite like the hairs of my head, he has <b>accepted upon himself</b> one term of <b>naziriteship</b> for as many days as the number of hairs on his head. <b>And he disagrees with him in one</b> case, <b>as</b> according to <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> the case of one who <b>counts</b> is <b>different</b> from the previous case, and so one who states: Like the number of hairs of my head, is considered to have accepted many distinct terms of naziriteship. However, according to <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, one who <b>counts</b> is <b>not different.</b> He is assumed to have accepted a single term of naziriteship unless he explicitly uses the plural term naziriteships.
§ <b>The Sages taught:</b> If <b>one says: I am hereby a nazirite all the days of my life,</b> or: <b>I am hereby a permanent nazirite, he is a permanent nazirite</b> and he trims his hair on a yearly basis like Absalom. However, in the case of one who accepts upon himself naziriteship for a fixed amount of time, <b>even</b> if he says: <b>One hundred years</b> or <b>even: One thousand years, he is not</b> the specific type of nazirite known as <b>a permanent nazirite. Rather,</b> he is a regular <b>nazirite forever,</b> and he may never cut his hair.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite and one, he counts two</b> terms of naziriteship, since he first took a nazirite vow and then accepted an additional term of naziriteship. If one said: I am hereby a nazirite and one <b>and more, he counts three</b> terms of naziriteship. If he added to that statement: <b>And again, he counts four.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> this last <i>halakha</i> <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: It is necessary to teach this <i>halakha</i>. <b>Lest you say</b> that the words <b>and again</b> are referring to terms of naziriteship <b>equal</b> in number to <b>all the</b> terms he has already accepted, <b>and he has</b> therefore accepted <b>six</b> terms of naziriteship altogether, the <i>baraita</i> therefore <b>teaches us that</b> this is <b>not</b> the correct interpretation of his statement.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite, Sumakhos says:</b> If he then added the word <b><i>hen</i>,</b> which means one in Greek, he has accepted <b>one</b> term of naziriteship. If one said: <b><i>Digon</i>,</b> which means two sides in Hebraicized Greek, he must observe <b>two</b> terms of naziriteship. If one said: <b><i>Trigon</i>,</b> which means triangle in Hebraicized Greek, then he has accepted three terms of naziriteship. If he said: <b><i>Tetragon</i>,</b> quadrilateral in Hebraicized Greek, then he has accepted <b>four</b> terms, and if he said: <b><i>Puntigon</i>,</b> pentagon in Hebraicized Greek, then he has accepted <b>five</b> terms of naziriteship.
The Gemara cites another <i>baraita</i> dealing with these same expressions: <b>The Sages taught: A round house, a two-sided</b> house, <b>a triangle-</b>shaped house, and <b>a pentagon-</b>shaped house <b>cannot become impure with leprosy,</b> whereas a house shaped like <b>a quadrilateral</b> can <b>become impure with leprosy. What is the reason</b> for this? In the verse <b>below it states,</b> when referring to <b>a wall,</b> the plural term <b>walls:</b> “And, behold, if the plague has spread in the walls of the house” (Leviticus 14:39). Additionally, in the verse <b>above it states,</b> when referring to <b>a wall,</b> the plural term <b>walls:</b> “And behold, if the plague be in the walls of the house” (Leviticus 14:37). These plural terms are unnecessary, and it is therefore understood that <b>there are four</b> walls mentioned <b>here</b> in order to indicate that a house can become impure through leprosy only if it has four sides.

Daf 9a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from dried figs and from cakes of dried figs, Beit Shammai say:</b> His statement renders him a full-fledged <b>nazirite,</b> and his addition: From dried figs, is insignificant, as this fruit is not included in the prohibitions of a nazirite, which include only products of the grapevine. <b>And Beit Hillel say: He is not a nazirite,</b> since he did not accept naziriteship upon himself. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: Even when Beit Shammai said</b> that this vow takes effect, <b>they said</b> that <b>only in</b> a case <b>where</b> one <b>said</b> that he meant: <b>They are hereby</b> forbidden <b>to me</b> as <b>an offering.</b> In that case it is as though he took a vow rendering the figs forbidden to him. However, Beit Shammai concede that although the vow takes effect, it is not a vow of naziriteship.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It is stated in the mishna that if one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from dried figs and from cakes of dried figs, Beit Shammai say:</b> He is a <b>nazirite.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But why? The Merciful One states</b> in the Torah in the passage dealing with naziriteship: <b>“From anything that is made of the grapevine</b>…he shall not eat” (Numbers 6:4). In naziriteship, only the fruit of the vine is prohibited. The Gemara answers: <b>Beit Shammai hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir, who says: A person does not utter a statement for naught.</b> In other words, if one utters a statement that cannot be fulfilled as stated, his statement is interpreted in a manner that renders it relevant. Here too, Beit Shammai say that he misspoke and actually intended to take a vow of naziriteship.
<b>And Beit Hillel hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, who says: A person is</b> also <b>held accountable for the conclusion of his statement.</b> If one states two irreconcilable clauses, e.g., this animal is a substitute for a burnt-offering, a substitute for a peace-offering; the second clause is not disregarded, and the animal assumes both statuses. <b>And this is a vow with its</b> inherent <b>opening,</b> i.e., reason for dissolution. Although he said: I am hereby a nazirite, by adding the words: From dried figs and from cakes of dried figs, he indicated that his intention was that the naziriteship would not take effect.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>Beit Shammai too, it is a vow with its</b> inherent <b>opening,</b> and since they certainly agree that a vow of this kind does not take effect, why do they hold that this vow takes effect?
The Gemara explains the dispute: <b>Rather, Beit Shammai hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir, who said: A person does not utter a statement for naught, and once he said: I am hereby a nazirite, he is</b> immediately rendered <b>a nazirite. When he</b> then <b>says:</b> And therefore I will refrain <b>from dried figs and from cakes of dried figs, he is coming to request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve his vow, as he regrets having taken the vow and is now seeking to dissolve it. <b>And Beit Shammai follow their</b> standard line of <b>reasoning, as they say:</b> One <b>cannot request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve a vow <b>of consecrated</b> property, which one can do for vows of prohibition. <b>And since</b> one <b>cannot request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve a vow <b>of consecrated</b> property, likewise one <b>cannot request</b> that a halakhic authority dissolve a vow <b>of naziriteship,</b> as the legal status of a vow of naziriteship is like that of a vow of consecrated property in this regard.
<b>And Beit Hillel hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Menaḥot</i> 103a): If one vows to bring a meal-offering from barley, since voluntary meal-offerings are brought only from wheat, the Rabbis say: He must bring a meal-offering from wheat, <b>and Rabbi Shimon exempts</b> him entirely, <b>as he did not donate in the manner</b> typical <b>of donors.</b> Since he donated an offering that cannot be sacrificed, his vow is meaningless. Here too, since he took a vow of naziriteship stating that he therefore will refrain from dried figs rather than from wine, his statement is meaningless.

Daf 9b

§ The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>this <i>tanna</i>. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Natan says</b> that if one says: I am hereby a nazirite and therefore will refrain from dried figs, <b>Beit Shammai say:</b> He has rendered dried figs <b>forbidden</b> to himself <b>by a vow, and</b> he is also <b>a nazirite; and Beit Hillel say:</b> He has rendered dried figs <b>forbidden</b> to himself <b>by a vow, but</b> he is <b>not a nazirite.</b> According to Rabbi Natan, <b>Beit Shammai hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir</b> that a person does not utter statements for naught and that he becomes a nazirite as soon as he says: I am hereby a nazirite; <b>and</b> he explains the opinion of Beit Shammai <b>as Rabbi Yehuda</b> does, i.e., that one in this situation has vowed that dried figs are forbidden to him, since the rest of his statement also has significance. <b>And Beit Hillel hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei</b> that one is held to the conclusion of his statement, and he has therefore vowed that dried figs are forbidden to him.
<b>Some say a different version</b> of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Natan says</b> that <b>Beit Shammai say:</b> He has rendered dried figs <b>forbidden</b> to himself <b>by a vow and he is not a nazirite; and Beit Hillel say:</b> He has <b>not</b> rendered dried figs <b>forbidden</b> to himself <b>by a vow, and</b> he is <b>not a nazirite.</b> According to this version, the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai is as Rabbi Yehuda</b> explained in the mishna, <b>and Beit Hillel hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon,</b> who maintains that a donation not performed in the manner typical of donors is not a donation.
§ The Gemara discusses a case mentioned earlier. <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Menaḥot</i> 103a): With regard to <b>one who says: It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to bring a meal-offering from barley,</b> since voluntary meal-offerings are made only with wheat <b>he must bring</b> a meal-offering <b>from wheat.</b> If one vows to bring a meal-offering from <b>flour,</b> since a standard meal-offering is made from fine flour <b>he must bring</b> one from <b>fine flour.</b> If one vows to bring a meal-offering <b>without oil and frankincense, he must bring it with oil and frankincense,</b> in accordance with the <i>halakha</i>. If one vows to bring a meal-offering with <b>half of a tenth</b> of an ephah of fine flour, <b>he must bring</b> a meal-offering with <b>a full tenth,</b> since a meal-offering cannot have less than one-tenth of an ephah of flour. If one vows to bring a meal-offering with <b>a tenth and a half, he brings two</b> units of a tenth of an ephah in his meal-offering. Since half of one-tenth of an ephah is the minimum, his vow is increased to two full tenths. <b>Rabbi Shimon exempts</b> him entirely in all these cases, <b>as he did not donate in the manner</b> typical <b>of donors.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught that when one said: It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring <b>a meal-offering from barley, he brings</b> a meal-offering <b>from wheat? Ḥizkiyya said</b> that this <i>halakha</i> <b>is taught as a dispute</b> between <i>tanna’im</i>, <b>and it is</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai.</b> He explains: <b>Didn’t Beit Shammai say that when one said:</b> I am hereby a nazirite and therefore will refrain <b>from dried figs and from cakes of dried figs, he is a nazirite,</b> indicating that he is held to only the first part of his statement, and the conflicting details are ignored? <b>So too, when he said</b> that he will bring a meal-offering <b>from barley, he brings</b> it <b>from wheat,</b> and the same analysis applies to the other cases listed in this mishna.
<b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said: You</b> can <b>even say</b> that <b>all,</b> including Beit Hillel, <b>agree</b> with the opinion of the Rabbis of the mishna, as the mishna may be stated <b>with regard to one who</b> later <b>says,</b> to clarify his earlier statement: <b>Had I known that one cannot vow in this manner,</b> that one cannot bring a voluntary meal-offering from barley, <b>I would not have vowed that way, only this way,</b> to bring a meal-offering from wheat. Mentioning barley was an error on his part rather than a condition, and he actually meant to bring a standard meal-offering. In this case even Beit Hillel, who maintain in the mishna here that there is no vow at all, deem him liable to bring a proper meal-offering.
With regard to this ruling <b>Ḥizkiyya says: They taught</b> that he must bring a meal-offering from wheat <b>only if he said</b> that he will bring a meal-offering <b>from barley. But</b> if <b>he said</b> he will bring a meal-offering <b>from lentils, he does not bring anything.</b> The difference is that the meal-offerings of the <i>omer</i> and of a <i>sota</i> are made from barley, so if he said: From barley, he may have mistakenly believed that a voluntary meal-offering can be prepared from barley as well. As everyone knows that no meal-offering is made from lentils, his statement demonstrates that he never intended to bring a meal-offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>Now</b> consider, <b>in accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>does Ḥizkiyya establish the mishna</b> in <i>Menaḥot</i>? It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai. But lentils relative to a meal-offering are comparable to dried figs relative to a nazirite,</b> as everyone is likewise aware that one cannot be a nazirite and therefore refrain from figs, <b>and</b> yet <b>Beit Shammai say he is a nazirite.</b> Why not say that one who vows to bring a meal-offering from lentils is obligated to bring a standard meal-offering? The Gemara answers: <b>Ḥizkiyya retracted from it</b> and no longer holds that the mishna in <i>Menaḥot</i> is in accordance with the opinion of Beit Shammai.
The Gemara asks: <b>And why did he</b> in fact <b>retract from it? Rava said:</b> The <b>mishna caused him difficulty</b> and demonstrated his error. If the mishna is in accordance with Beit Shammai’s opinion with regard to a nazirite, <b>why does</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>specifically teach</b> the case of one who says that he will bring a meal-offering <b>from barley? Let him teach</b> a greater novelty, i.e., that even one who vowed to bring a meal-offering <b>from lentils</b> is obligated to bring a meal-offering made from wheat. <b>Rather, Ḥizkiyya holds</b> that <b>when Beit Shammai stated</b> their opinion <b>there,</b> i.e., in the mishna with regard to a nazirite, their intention was <b>as</b> explained by <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> that one’s statement is interpreted to be meaningful and it can have the meaning of creating a vow that dried figs are forbidden to him. In the case of the meal-offering, even they agree that since one’s vow has no meaning, as there cannot be a meal-offering made of lentils, no vow takes effect.
<b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Even</b> one who vowed to bring a meal-offering <b>from lentils</b> must bring a meal-offering from wheat. The Gemara asks: <b>But Rabbi Yoḥanan is he who said</b> that the mishna is referring to a case of one <b>who says: Had I known that one cannot vow in this manner, I would not have vowed that way, only this way?</b> In the case of the lentils, it is not reasonable to say that he erred in thinking that one may vow to bring a meal-offering from lentils. The Gemara answers: <b>He stated</b> this ruling <b>in accordance with the statement of Ḥizkiyya.</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan was not stating his own opinion; he was challenging the ruling of Ḥizkiyya.
The Gemara explains his challenge: <b>What is the reason you retracted</b> your earlier opinion that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of Beit Shammai? You did so <b>because it does not teach: From lentils.</b> That is not conclusive, as <b>perhaps it is speaking</b> utilizing the style of: <b>It is not necessary,</b> as follows: <b>It is not necessary</b> to say that <b>when one says:</b> It is incumbent upon me to bring a meal-offering <b>from lentils, that he brings a proper meal-offering</b> of wheat. In that situation <b>it can be said</b> that by saying: From lentils, <b>he is retracting</b> his first statement and now wants to rescind his vow. <b>And</b> yet Beit Shammai hold that he is <b>held to the first</b> part of his <b>statement,</b> and they do not allow the rescinding of a vow of consecration. <b>But if he said:</b> It is incumbent upon me to bring a meal-offering <b>from barley,</b> since it is possible to explain that <b>certainly this is what he said: If</b> this meal-offering that I have vowed to bring from barley <b>is consecrated like the <i>omer</i> meal-offering,</b>

Daf 10a

<b>or like the meal-offering of a <i>sota</i>,</b> both of which are made of barley, <b>it should become consecrated,</b> and <b>if</b> it is <b>not</b> possible to volunteer a meal-offering from barley, it should <b>not</b> be a meal-offering at all. The mishna therefore <b>teaches us that one</b> nevertheless <b>brings</b> a meal-offering made <b>from wheat.</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan holds that the difficulty raised by Ḥizkiyya against his own explanation is inconclusive, and he need not have retracted it.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one <b>said: This cow said: I am hereby a nazirite if I stand up;</b> or if he said: <b>This door says: I am hereby a nazirite if I am opened, Beit Shammai say</b> he is <b>a nazirite, and Beit Hillel say</b> he is <b>not a nazirite. Rabbi Yehuda said: Even when Beit Shammai say</b> that the vow is effective, <b>they say</b> so <b>only</b> with regard to <b>one who said: This cow is hereby</b> forbidden <b>to me</b> as <b>an offering if it stands up.</b> In that case it is as if he took a vow that the cow is forbidden. However, Beit Shammai concede that although the vow takes effect, it is not a vow of naziriteship.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Does a cow speak?</b> What is the meaning of the statement: A cow said: I am hereby a nazirite? <b>Rami bar Ḥama said: With what are we dealing here?</b> We are dealing with a case <b>where there was a prone cow before him,</b> and he tried, without success, to cause it to stand, <b>and he said: This cow thinks it</b> will <b>not stand; I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from its flesh if it stands of its own accord, and</b> in fact <b>it stood of its own accord. Beit Shammai follow their</b> standard <b>approach and Beit Hillel follow their</b> standard <b>approach.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>Beit Shammai, who say</b> that one who vows to be a nazirite and therefore will refrain <b>from dried figs and from cakes of dried figs is a nazirite,</b> say that <b>here too, when he says: I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from its flesh, is a nazirite.</b> Since one does not utter a statement for naught, he is held to the first part of his statement: I am hereby a nazirite, and the words: And therefore will refrain from its flesh, are disregarded. <b>And Beit Hillel say: He is not a nazirite.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But</b> if Rami bar Ḥama is correct, <b>didn’t Beit Shammai</b> already <b>say this</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>one time?</b> According to Rami bar Ḥama’s explanation, this mishna and the previous one differ only with regard to the examples provided, but the principle is identical. <b>Rava said:</b> It is normal for the Sages to cite <b>two</b> or <b>three</b> examples from different cases that offer novel perspectives, although they essentially reflect the same principle. <b>And Rabbi Ḥiyya also taught two</b> or <b>three</b> examples with regard to this same issue. <b>And Rabbi Oshaya also said two</b> or <b>three</b> examples.
<b>And</b> all these cases <b>are necessary, as, if it were stated</b> only that he is a nazirite <b>in this</b> case of <b>dried figs and cakes of dried figs,</b> it could have been said that it is only <b>there that Beit Shammai say he is a nazirite, since</b> figs <b>are confused with grapes,</b> and it is reasonable to assume that he had grapes in mind. <b>But meat is</b> certainly <b>not confused with grapes,</b> and it could be that in the case of the mishna he is not a nazirite even according to Beit Shammai. <b>And if it were stated</b> only that he is a nazirite where he vowed that <b>meat</b> was forbidden to him, it could have been said that <b>it is here that Beit Shammai say he is a nazirite</b> because he was referring to the often-paired <b>meat and wine,</b> and it is reasonable to assume that he might have had wine in mind. <b>But dried figs and cakes of dried figs</b> are not paired with wine, so he should <b>not</b> be a nazirite. To counter that claim, the mishna <b>teaches us</b> that he is a nazirite in both cases.
<b>And if it were stated</b> only that he is a nazirite with regard to <b>these two</b> cases of figs and meat, it could be said: It is in <b>these</b> cases <b>that Beit Shammai say</b> he is a nazirite, <b>but</b> in the case of <b>a door, I will say they concede to Beit Hillel</b> that such a statement certainly does not constitute a vow of naziriteship. Therefore, this case had to be stated as well. <b>And</b> conversely, <b>if</b> it <b>taught</b> only the case of <b>a door,</b> the opposite could be said, i.e., that it is <b>in this</b> case that <b>Beit Hillel say</b> there is no naziriteship, <b>but in these two</b> earlier cases <b>I will say they concede to Beit Shammai</b> that the individual has taken a vow of naziriteship. The <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches us that</b> this is <b>not</b> the case; in fact, Beit Shammai hold that he is a nazirite in all three cases, and Beit Hillel hold that he is not.
After providing the answer to one difficulty raised against Rami bar Ḥama’s explanation, Rava presents another problem. <b>Rava said: Is</b> the mishna <b>teaching: If it stood of its own accord?</b> The mishna states: If it stands, and does not mention the condition of: On its own accord. <b>Rather, Rava said:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where there was a prone cow before him, and he said: It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to bring</b> it as a nazirite <b>offering,</b> and in this way the individual accepts naziriteship upon himself. The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>Granted, a cow</b> can <b>be an offering, but</b> can <b>a door be an offering?</b> How can his vow that a door should be an offering be considered an acceptance of naziriteship? <b>Rather, Rava said</b> it means the following: It is a case <b>where there was a prone cow before him</b> refusing to stand,

Daf 10b

<b>and he said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from wine if it does not stand,</b> since I will force it to do so, <b>and it stood of its own accord,</b> without him causing it to stand. <b>Beit Shammai hold: This man’s intention [<i>turpeih</i>] is based upon him having it stand by his</b> own <b>hand, and he did not have it stand.</b> Since he did not cause it to stand, his vow of naziriteship takes effect. <b>And Beit Hillel hold:</b> His intention <b>is based upon</b> the fact <b>that it was prone, and</b> now <b>it has stood.</b> Since the cow stood up it does not matter what caused it to stand, and his vow of naziriteship does not take effect.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, say the latter clause</b> of the mishna as follows: <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: Even when Beit Shammai said</b> the vow is effective <b>they said</b> so <b>only with regard to one who said: They are hereby</b> forbidden <b>to me</b> as <b>an offering.</b> But <b>does he</b> take a vow and <b>extend any</b> prohibition <b>to the cow?</b> Since according to this approach, he explicitly mentions naziriteship, the cow is not rendered forbidden but is merely the subject of a condition of the vow, so why does Rabbi Yehuda speak of a prohibition on the cow?
The Gemara offers another explanation: <b>Rather,</b> the mishna is referring to a case <b>where he said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from its flesh if it does not stand, and it stood of its own accord. Beit Shammai hold: That man’s intention is based upon</b> him <b>having it stand by his own hand, and he did not have it stand.</b> Since he did not cause it to stand, his vow of prohibition takes effect. <b>And Beit Hillel hold: This man’s intention is based upon</b> the fact <b>that it was prone, and</b> now <b>it has stood,</b> so his vow does not take effect.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> do <b>Beit Hillel hold</b> that <b>if</b> the cow <b>does not stand he will be a nazirite? But didn’t they say</b> that if one states: I am hereby a nazirite and therefore will refrain <b>from</b> the cow’s <b>flesh, he is not a nazirite,</b> just as they ruled in a case where one states that he is a nazirite from dried figs?
The Gemara answers: <b>They stated</b> their opinion <b>in accordance with the reasoning of Beit Shammai: According to our</b> opinion, <b>even</b> if the cow <b>does not stand he is also not a nazirite,</b> since naziriteship takes effect only if he vowed that products of the vine are forbidden to him. However, <b>according to your</b> reasoning <b>that you say he is a nazirite, in any event concede to us that this man’s intention is based upon</b> the fact <b>that</b> the cow <b>was prone, and it has stood,</b> so the naziriteship should not take effect. <b>And Beit Shammai</b> hold: <b>Isn’t this man’s intention based upon</b> him <b>having it stand by his own hand, and he did not have it stand?</b> Since his condition was not fulfilled, the naziriteship does not take effect.

Daf 11a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>they poured one a cup</b> of wine <b>and he said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from it, he is a</b> full-fledged <b>nazirite</b> who must observe all the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship. <b>An incident</b> occurred <b>with regard to a certain woman who was intoxicated</b> from wine, <b>and they poured a cup for her and she said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from it. The Sages said: This</b> woman <b>did not intend</b> to accept naziriteship <b>but</b> rather, meant <b>to say: It is hereby</b> forbidden <b>to me</b> as <b>an offering.</b> She vowed against deriving benefit from that cup alone, since she did not want to drink any more.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: Was <b>an incident</b> cited <b>to contradict</b> the previous ruling? <b>You said in the first clause</b> of the mishna that if one said that he is hereby a nazirite and therefore will refrain from a cup that was poured for him, then <b>he is a nazirite. And</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>then teaches: An incident</b> occurred <b>with regard to a certain woman</b> who said: I am hereby a nazirite and therefore will refrain from it, which the Sages interpreted as a vow rendering the cup forbidden to her like an offering. <b>Apparently it is</b> only <b>with regard to this</b> cup of wine <b>that is forbidden</b> to her; <b>consequently,</b> any <b>other wine is permitted,</b> and she is not a nazirite.
The Gemara answers: The mishna <b>is incomplete and is teaching the following:</b> If <b>they poured a cup</b> of wine <b>for one and he said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from it, he is a nazirite. And if he is intoxicated</b> and they pour a cup of wine for him <b>and he said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> and therefore will refrain <b>from it, he is not a nazirite. What is the reason</b> for this? <b>He is</b> considered to be <b>like one who said:</b> This cup <b>is</b> forbidden <b>to me</b> like <b>an offering.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And if you would say</b> that this was his meaning, <b>let him say so</b> explicitly; why would he say: I am hereby a nazirite and therefore will refrain from it? The reason is because <b>he maintains:</b> If I specify this cup, <b>they will bring me another</b> cup <b>and aggravate me</b> with it. It is better that <b>I say to them this statement, which is definitive to them,</b> and they will understand that I do not want to drink any more wine. The mishna continues: <b>And an incident also</b> occurred <b>with regard to a certain woman</b> who was intoxicated and took this vow, and the Sages explained her statement accordingly.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite on the condition that I will</b> be allowed to <b>drink wine and may become</b> ritually <b>impure from corpses,</b> i.e., he wishes to be a nazirite only with respect to the growth of his hair, <b>he is a</b> full-fledged <b>nazirite and is prohibited from</b> engaging in <b>all of the</b> behaviors forbidden to a nazirite, including consuming products of the vine and contracting impurity from a corpse.
If one stated a vow of naziriteship and then said: <b>I know that there is naziriteship, but I do not know that a nazirite is prohibited from wine, he is prohibited</b> in all the prohibitions of naziriteship. <b>But Rabbi Shimon permits</b> him, since he holds that naziriteship takes effect only if the person accepts all the relevant prohibitions. If one said: <b>I know that a nazirite is prohibited from wine, but I thought that the Sages</b> would <b>permit me</b> to drink wine <b>because I cannot live without wine, or:</b> I thought that the Sages would allow me to contract impurity from corpses <b>because I bury the dead, he is permitted</b> and the vow of naziriteship does not take effect, <b>but Rabbi Shimon prohibits</b> him.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> In the second case of the mishna, where one states that he did not know that the prohibitions of naziriteship include wine, Rabbi Shimon says that the naziriteship does not take effect because he did not include all of the prohibitions in his vow. The Gemara therefore asks: <b>Let Rabbi Shimon disagree</b> with the first <i>tanna</i> <b>in the first clause</b> in the mishna <b>as well,</b> where one said: I am hereby a nazirite on the condition that I may drink wine. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said:</b> In fact, <b>Rabbi Shimon disagrees even</b> with the first <i>tanna</i> <b>in the first clause</b> of the mishna, and the mishna’s statement: Rabbi Shimon permits him, is referring to both clauses.
<b>Ravina said: Rabbi Shimon does not disagree</b> with the first <i>tanna</i> <b>in the first clause</b> of the mishna. <b>What is the reason</b> for that? It is <b>because one was stipulating counter to that which is written in the Torah</b> by attempting to limit an explicit Torah law, <b>and</b> with regard to <b>anyone who stipulates counter to that which is written in the Torah, his stipulation is void.</b> The statement: I am hereby a nazirite, remains valid, and he is therefore a nazirite in all regards. Conversely, <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> could have <b>said to you: This</b> phrase: <b>On the condition,</b> does not mean he accepts upon himself all the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship and then adds a stipulation counter to that which is written in the Torah; rather, it is <b>considered like</b> he said: <b>Apart</b> from. It is as though he stated from the outset that he will be a nazirite apart from one particular aspect. According to Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi, Rabbi Shimon holds that since he is not trying to stipulate alternative <i>halakhot</i> for full naziriteship but is instead accepting a partial naziriteship, his vow takes effect.
The Gemara comments: It <b>is taught</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i> (<i>Nazir</i> 2:2) <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Ravina.</b> If one <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite on the condition that I will be allowed to drink wine and may become</b> ritually <b>impure from corpses, he is a nazirite and is prohibited from all of them because he stipulates counter to that which is written in the Torah, and</b> with regard to <b>anyone who stipulates counter to that which is written in the Torah, his stipulation is void.</b>
§ The final clause of the mishna teaches that if one says: <b>I know that a nazirite is prohibited from</b> drinking <b>wine</b> but I thought that the Sages would permit me to drink, the Rabbis say that the vow is void, but Rabbi Shimon disagrees. The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t you say in the earlier clause</b> of the mishna that with regard to one who says: But I did not know that a nazirite is prohibited from wine, the first <i>tanna</i> holds that he is <b>prohibited and Rabbi Shimon permits</b> him? What is the difference between the two cases? The Gemara answers: Emend the text and <b>say</b> in the final clause <b>as well: He is prohibited, and Rabbi Shimon permits</b> him. <b>And if you wish, say</b> instead: <b>Actually, do not reverse</b> the opinions, and leave the text as it is. Instead, distinguish between the two cases, as <b>there</b>

Daf 11b

in <b>the earlier clause,</b> where one said: I did not know that a nazirite is prohibited from drinking wine, that is a case <b>where he vowed naziriteship</b> with regard to only <b>one</b> of the prohibitions. The difference of opinion is as follows: <b>According to the Rabbis, who say that even if he vowed naziriteship</b> with regard to <b>only one of the</b> prohibitions of naziriteship <b>he is</b> nevertheless <b>a nazirite,</b> in that case <b>he is prohibited</b> by all of them, despite not knowing about the prohibition against consuming wine. <b>According to Rabbi Shimon</b> who <b>said</b> he is not a nazirite <b>unless he takes a vow of naziriteship</b> with regard to <b>all of them,</b> he is <b>permitted.</b>
Conversely, <b>the last clause</b> addresses one who <b>vowed</b> naziriteship with regard to <b>all of them,</b> thereby accepting upon himself all the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship, <b>and</b> now wishes <b>to request</b> dissolution <b>of one</b> of the prohibitions, thinking that a halakhic authority can dissolve one aspect of his naziriteship.
The Gemara explains: <b>According to the Rabbis, who say that even if he vowed naziriteship</b> with regard to <b>only one of the</b> prohibitions of naziriteship <b>he is</b> nevertheless <b>a nazirite,</b> in that case too, <b>when he requests</b> of a halakhic authority to dissolve <b>one of them, he is permitted</b> to engage in all of the behaviors forbidden to a nazirite. Just as the acceptance of one part of naziriteship causes one to be bound by all the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship, dissolution of one element of naziriteship nullifies the entire naziriteship. <b>According to Rabbi Shimon, who said</b> that he is not a nazirite <b>unless he takes a vow of naziriteship</b> with regard to <b>all of them, when he requests</b> dissolution <b>of that</b> prohibition, none of the prohibitions are permitted <b>until he requests</b> dissolution <b>of all of</b> the prohibitions of naziriteship. <b>Due to this</b> the mishna <b>teaches: And Rabbi Shimon prohibits</b> him.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead: The Rabbis and Rabbi Shimon <b>disagree with regard to vows</b> impeded by <b>circumstances beyond one’s control, and</b> their disagreement is <b>in the dispute between Shmuel and Rav Asi. As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Nedarim</i> 20b): <b>The Sages dissolved four</b> types of <b>vows</b> without the requirement of a request to a halakhic authority. The first category is <b>vows of exhortation,</b> where one employs a vow to urge another to perform some action. The second category is <b>vows of exaggeration [<i>havai</i>],</b> where one utters a vow that is dependent upon some outlandish claim, such as: I should be bound by a vow if I did not see a square snake. Since he knows that there are no square snakes, it is evident that he is not serious in taking his vow. The third category is <b>vows that are unintentional,</b> where one vows for a particular reason and later discovers he was mistaken with regard to the facts; and the fourth is <b>vows</b> whose fulfillment are impeded by <b>circumstances beyond one’s control.</b>
The Gemara relates the dispute between Shmuel and Rav Asi: <b>And Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav Asi said: These four vows require a request</b> made <b>to the halakhic authorities</b> to dissolve them, and the vows are not dissolved unless one does so. Rav Yehuda related: <b>When I stated this</b> <i>halakha</i> of Rav Asi <b>before Shmuel, he said to me: The <i>tanna</i> teaches</b> that <b>the Sages dissolved</b> them, <b>and you say they require a request</b> made <b>to the halakhic authorities?</b> Shmuel holds that they are dissolved automatically.
The Gemara continues its explanation: The dispute between the Rabbis and Rabbi Shimon in the last clause of the mishna is also with regard to this <i>halakha</i>. This case is referring to a kind of a vow impeded by circumstances beyond one’s control, as he claims that he has no choice but to drink wine or contract impurity from corpses. <b>The Rabbis hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Shmuel,</b> and therefore the vow is dissolved automatically, without the need to request dissolution from a halakhic authority; <b>and Rabbi Shimon holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Asi</b> that one must make a request to a halakhic authority to dissolve the vow, and he remains a nazirite until he does so.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite and it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave a nazirite,</b> meaning he will also pay for the offerings that a nazirite brings when he cuts his hair; <b>and another heard and said: And I</b> too am a nazirite <b>and it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave a nazirite,</b> the other is also a nazirite and is obligated to pay for the offerings of a nazirite. <b>If they were perspicacious</b> and wish to limit their expenses, <b>they shave each other.</b> They may each pay for the other’s offerings, so that their additional vows will not cost them anything. <b>And if not,</b> if this arrangement did not occur to them and each brought his own offerings, <b>they shave other nazirites,</b> i.e., they must pay for the offerings of other nazirites.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages. If one said: I am hereby a nazirite and it is incumbent upon me to shave a nazirite as well, and <b>another heard and said</b> only: <b>And I, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Do we say that: <b>And I, indicates</b> acceptance <b>of the entire statement</b> of the first one, which would mean he is both a nazirite and must shave a nazirite, <b>or perhaps it indicates</b> acceptance <b>of</b> only <b>half the statement</b> of the first speaker? <b>If you say it indicates</b> acceptance <b>of</b> only <b>half the statement,</b> does it indicate acceptance <b>of the first clause,</b> i.e., I am hereby a nazirite, <b>or the last clause,</b> i.e., it is incumbent upon me to shave a nazirite?
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from the mishna. If a second person said: <b>And I, and it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave a nazirite,</b> then <b>if they are perspicacious they shave each other. From</b> the fact <b>that it says: And I, and it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me,</b> you can <b>learn from this</b> that: <b>And I, indicates</b> acceptance <b>of</b> only <b>half the statement,</b> as otherwise he would not need to add the second part. The Sages <b>say</b> in response to this attempted proof: <b>Yes,</b> this proves that it <b>indicates</b> acceptance <b>of</b> only <b>half the statement. However,</b> the other question still has not been answered: Is he referring <b>to the first</b> part of the original statement <b>or to the last</b> part? The Gemara answers: This can be learned <b>from</b> the mishna <b>itself. From</b> the fact <b>that it says: And it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave</b> a nazirite, one can <b>learn from this</b> that: <b>And I, indicates</b> acceptance <b>of the beginning of the statement</b> to become a nazirite, which is why one has to add that he must also pay for the offerings of a nazirite.
<b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua, said to Rava: From where</b> do you know <b>that</b> it <b>is so</b> that the words: And I, are referring to the first part of the statement? <b>Actually, I will say to you</b> that: <b>And I,</b> is referring <b>to the entire statement, and if</b> you claim otherwise <b>because</b> he added: <b>And it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me,</b> the explanation of <b>what he is saying</b> is: <b>And it is</b> incumbent <b>on me</b> to do <b>this matter.</b> He was merely clarifying what his intention was when he said: And I. <b>As, if you do not say so,</b> then with regard to <b>that which is taught in the last clause,</b> in the following mishna, that if one said: <b>It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave half a nazirite, and another heard</b> that <b>and said: And I, it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave half a nazirite,</b> then <b>there, are there two statements?</b> That mishna is addressing only a single vow. <b>Rather, what is he saying</b> by using the words: <b>It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me?</b> He is referring <b>to that</b> whole <b>matter; here too,</b> in this mishna, <b>when he says: And it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me,</b> he is referring <b>to that</b> whole <b>matter.</b>
<b>Rava said to him: How can</b> these cases <b>be compared? Granted, if you say</b> that in <b>the first clause</b> it <b>is necessary</b> to teach both parts of the statement and in <b>the latter clause</b> it <b>is not necessary</b> to teach both parts of the statement, then it can be said that <b>he taught the unnecessary latter clause due to the necessary first clause,</b> as it is typical for a mishna to phrase both of its sections in the same style. <b>But if you say</b> that it <b>is not necessary</b> to teach both parts of the statement in <b>the first clause</b> and it is also <b>not necessary</b> to teach both parts of the statement in <b>the latter clause,</b> as one has accepted the other’s entire statement by saying: And I, would the <i>tanna</i> <b>teach an unnecessary first clause and teach an unnecessary latter clause?</b> Since the addition of: And it is incumbent upon me, is not required in the latter mishna, it must be necessary in this mishna, so the inference of Rava is correct.
§ After analyzing the mishna itself, the Gemara turns to a related issue. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Yosef said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: One who says to his agent:</b>

Daf 12a

<b>Go out and betroth a woman for me, without specifying</b> a particular woman; from that moment onward the one who appointed the agent is <b>forbidden to all the women in the world</b> until he finds out which woman the agent betrothed. There is a <b>presumption</b> that <b>an agent performs his</b> assigned <b>agency</b> and that he has betrothed a woman for him, <b>and since</b> the agent <b>did not clarify to him</b> which woman he chose, <b>he</b> therefore <b>does not know which</b> woman <b>is the one betrothed to him.</b> If he now betroths another woman, it is possible that she is the daughter, sister, or mother of the one his agent betrothed on his behalf and is therefore forbidden to him.
<b>Reish Lakish raised an objection to Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> from a mishna (<i>Kinnim</i> 2:1): With regard to an impure person who comes to undergo his purification process, and for this purpose set aside <b>an unspecified nest,</b> meaning a pair of turtledoves or pigeons, to use for his offerings. One is to be a burnt-offering and one is to be a sin-offering, and he had not yet specified which bird will be used for which offering. If <b>one fledgling of the</b> pair <b>flew away</b> and escaped <b>to the</b> open <b>air of the world, or</b> if it <b>flew among</b> birds invalidated for <b>sin-offerings that</b> have been left to <b>die, or if one of them died,</b> in each of these cases the owner of the nest <b>purchases a partner for the second,</b> i.e., remaining, bird. At that point he may decide which is for a sin-offering and which is for a burnt-offering.
<b>But</b> if a fledgling flew away or died from <b>a specified nest,</b> after the owner had designated which bird would be used for which offering, and it is not known which bird escaped, <b>it has no</b> means of <b>remedy.</b> This is because he does not know whether the remaining fledgling is for a burnt-offering or a sin-offering.
Reish Lakish infers: <b>But generally, other nests</b> belonging to other people are <b>fit;</b> there is no concern with regard to them. Reish Lakish states his objection to the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan: According to your opinion that there is a concern that each woman might be a relative of the woman the agent betrothed, <b>then why</b> should the <i>halakha</i> with regard to nests be so? <b>Let us</b> likewise <b>say</b> with regard to <b>each and every</b> fledgling bird in the world that <b>perhaps this is the one</b> that was consecrated and flew away. How can anyone ever use a bird for their own offering, as it may be the bird that flew away from someone else’s nest?
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to</b> Reish Lakish: <b>I state</b> my opinion that there is a concern for other women only with regard to <b>a woman, who does not move</b> but is fixed in her home. An unidentified item is presumed to have the same legal status as the majority of items like it, and there is no concern that it may be forbidden, even if there are some forbidden items like it. However, if this unidentified item was fixed in its place, there is an equal presumption that it may belong to the class of similar permitted items or to the class of similar forbidden items. Consequently, all women in the world are forbidden to him, as there is an equal presumption that she may or may not be a relative of the woman that the agent betrothed to him. <b>And you speak to me</b> of <b>a prohibition that moves</b> and is not fixed. Since the fledgling bird is not fixed in one spot, the majority is followed and the minority is ignored.
<b>And if you would say: Here too,</b> she <b>moves,</b> since it is possible to <b>say that</b> the agent <b>found</b> the woman <b>in the marketplace and betrothed her</b> there to the one who appointed the agent, so the prohibited woman was not in a fixed location? Nevertheless, <b>there</b> she eventually <b>returns to her</b> place of <b>rest,</b> i.e., her home, and is therefore considered to be fixed. Conversely, <b>with regard to a nest, does</b> the fledgling <b>return</b> to a fixed place? Since it does not, the assumption is that any bird has the status of the majority of birds in the world, which have not been consecrated as offerings.
<b>Rava said: And Rabbi Yoḥanan concedes</b> that with regard to <b>a woman who does not have a daughter, nor a daughter of a daughter, nor a daughter of a son, nor a mother, nor a mother of a mother, nor a sister</b> who was single when he appointed the agent, that the one who appointed the agent may betroth her. There is no concern that the agent might have betrothed a relative that would now render such a woman forbidden. <b>And even though she had,</b> when he appointed the agent, a married <b>sister,</b> or daughter, or other female relative, <b>and she was later divorced,</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan concedes <b>that this</b> woman whom he wishes to now marry is <b>permitted.</b>
<b>What is the reason</b> for this? It is <b>that at that moment, when he told</b> the agent to go betroth a woman on his behalf, these women <b>were married to men.</b> There is a principle that <b>when one appoints an agent,</b> he does so only <b>with regard to a matter present before him.</b> In other words, he instructs his agent to betroth a woman who is available at that time, but <b>he does not appoint an agent with regard to a matter that is not present before him.</b> Consequently, even if the agent betrothed one of the relatives of the woman after they were divorced, the betrothal would not take effect because he was not authorized to betroth them, and therefore the one who appointed the agent is permitted to the woman.
The Gemara now proceeds to raise a difficulty against this last argument from the mishna: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna that if one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite and it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave a nazirite, and another heard</b> him <b>and said: And I</b> am hereby a nazirite, <b>and it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave a nazirite, if they are perspicacious they shave each other; and if not, they shave other nazirites.</b> According to the reasoning that one takes into account only that which is possible at the time, the following difficulty arises: <b>Granted, the last</b> person <b>has the first one before him</b> and may have had in mind to volunteer to pay for the other’s offerings; <b>but</b> with regard to <b>the first</b> person, <b>is the last one before him?</b> When the first one stated his vow, the other one was not yet a nazirite, and he could not have been taking him into account when he vowed to pay for the offerings of another. How can paying for the offerings of the second nazirite be considered a fulfillment of his vow?

Daf 12b

<b>Rather,</b> it can be explained that <b>this is what he is saying: If I find</b> someone <b>who becomes a nazirite, I will shave him. Here too,</b> in the case of betrothal, <b>this is what</b> the one who appointed the agent <b>is saying to</b> the agent: Even <b>if</b> the woman <b>you find</b> is married at this moment but when you come to her you discover that she <b>has</b> been <b>divorced</b> in the meantime, <b>betroth</b> her <b>to me.</b> If so, he would be prohibited from marrying her sister, contrary to the ruling of Rava.
The Gemara rejects this comparison: The Sages <b>say</b> that there is a distinction between the two cases: <b>A person appoints an agent only for a matter that he</b> himself <b>can perform now,</b> at the time of the appointment, <b>but for a matter that he cannot perform now, he does not appoint</b> an agent. Consequently, the agent cannot betroth a woman who was married at the time of his appointment. The one who appointed the agent may therefore marry the sister of the recently divorced woman, as stated by Rava.
The Gemara asks another question on the ruling of Rava: <b>And</b> can one <b>not</b> appoint an agent to betroth a woman in this manner? <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i> that indicates the contrary: <b>One who says to the steward [<i>apotropos</i>] of his</b> affairs: <b>All vows that my wife will vow from now until I come from such and such a place, nullify for her, and</b> the steward <b>nullified</b> them <b>for her,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought <b>they are nullified.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Her husband sustains</b> the vow <b>and her husband nullifies</b> the vow” (Numbers 30:14); this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yoshiya.</b> The repetition of “husband” teaches that it is the husband alone who may nullify his wife’s vows. <b>And Rabbi Yonatan says: We have found in all places that</b> the legal status of <b>a person’s agent is like</b> that of <b>himself.</b> Therefore, a steward, who serves as the husband’s agent, may nullify the wife’s vows.
Even according to Rabbi Yoshiya, who maintains that the steward cannot nullify her vows, <b>the reason is that the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: <b>“Her husband sustains</b> the vow <b>and her husband nullifies</b> the vow,” <b>but if</b> it were <b>not so,</b> the <b>steward could nullify</b> even the future vows of the wife. <b>However, with regard to</b> the husband <b>himself</b> it <b>is taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Nedarim</i> 75a): <b>One who says to his wife: All vows that you will vow from now until I come from such and such a place shall be ratified, he has not said anything.</b> However, if a husband says: All vows that you will vow from now until I come from such and such a place, <b>they are hereby nullified, Rabbi Eliezer says: It is nullified, and the Rabbis say: It is not nullified.</b>
The Gemara finishes the question: <b>It enters our mind</b> to say that <b>when Rabbi Yoshiya said</b> that the steward cannot nullify the vows, he spoke <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, who say</b> that the husband <b>is not able to nullify</b> her vows ahead of time, <b>and</b> yet, even according to their approach, <b>if the Merciful One had not stated</b> in the Torah: <b>“Her husband sustains</b> the vow <b>and her husband nullifies</b> the vow” the <b>steward would</b> be able to <b>nullify</b> such vows. This proves that one can appoint an agent for something he himself cannot do at the time, which contradicts the statement of Rava.
The Gemara rejects this: <b>And perhaps</b> Rabbi Yoshiya spoke <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, who says</b> the husband <b>can nullify</b> the vows ahead of time, and it is for this reason that he can appoint the steward to do the same. The Gemara rejects this: <b>If so, why do I</b> need <b>to make him into an agent; let him nullify</b> the future vows <b>for her</b> before he sets out on his journey. The Gemara answers: <b>He thinks, perhaps I will forget, or become angry, or be occupied</b> with other matters when I am about to set out on my journey. This is why he appoints an agent to nullify the vows on his behalf, and no proof can be derived from this <i>baraita</i>.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one says: <b>It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave half a nazirite,</b> i.e., he is vowing to pay half the costs of a nazirite’s offerings, <b>and another heard and said: And I, it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me to shave half a nazirite, this</b> one <b>shaves a whole nazirite and that</b> one <b>shaves a whole nazirite,</b> i.e., each pays the full cost of a nazirite’s offerings; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir,</b> since there is no such entity as half a nazirite. <b>And the Rabbis say: This</b> one <b>shaves half a nazirite and that</b> one <b>shaves half a nazirite;</b> they may join together to pay for the offerings of one nazirite.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to this dispute, <b>Rava said: All concede</b> that <b>whenever one said: Half of the offerings of a nazirite are</b> incumbent <b>upon me, he brings half of the offerings,</b> since he vowed to pay only that amount. Also, everyone agrees that if he said: <b>The offerings of half a nazirite are</b> incumbent <b>upon me, he needs to bring all of the offerings</b> of a nazirite. <b>What is the reason</b> that he must bring all of the offerings of a nazirite? It is <b>that we have not found</b> such an entity as <b>half a naziriteship.</b> If one vowed to be half a nazirite, he is a full nazirite.
<b>And when they disagree,</b> it is only <b>in</b> a case of one who used <b>the</b> precise <b>wording of the mishna. Rabbi Meir holds</b> that <b>once he said: It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me, he is obligated in all of the naziriteship offerings, and when he</b> later <b>says: Half a naziriteship,</b> it <b>is not in his power</b> to uproot his first obligation. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>it is a vow with</b> its inherent <b>opening.</b> By saying he had only half the offerings of a nazirite in mind from the outset, he has nullified his own vow.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite when I will have a son, and a son was born to him, he is a nazirite.</b> If <b>a daughter, a <i>tumtum</i>, or a hermaphrodite [<i>androginos</i>] is born to him, he is not a nazirite,</b> since a son was not born to him. However, <b>if he says:</b> I am hereby a nazirite <b>when I will have a child,</b> then <b>even if a daughter, a <i>tumtum</i>, or a hermaphrodite is born to him, he is a nazirite.</b>

Daf 13a

However, if <b>his wife miscarried he is not a nazirite,</b> since his wife did not give birth to a live child. <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> Since it is possible that the fetus was viable, in which case his vow of naziriteship takes effect, <b>he should say</b> the following: <b>If</b> this fetus <b>was viable</b> in terms of its development but died due to other causes, <b>I am hereby an obligatory nazirite</b> in fulfillment of my vow; <b>and if</b> it was <b>not</b> viable, <b>I am hereby a voluntary nazirite.</b> He then proceeds to observe naziriteship.
If, subsequent to this, his wife <b>gave birth again, he is a nazirite,</b> since the unattributed opinion in the mishna holds that the condition of his vow has now been fulfilled. <b>Rabbi Shimon says,</b> following his earlier ruling: He must now accept upon himself an additional naziriteship and <b>he should say: If the first</b> fetus was <b>viable</b> then my naziriteship for <b>the first</b> child was <b>obligatory, and this</b> naziriteship is <b>voluntary; and if</b> the first child was <b>not</b> viable, then the naziriteship for <b>the first</b> one was <b>voluntary and this</b> naziriteship is <b>obligatory.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the statement of the mishna that one who vowed to be a nazirite when a son is born to him is a nazirite when his son is born, the Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the purpose <b>of stating this</b> ruling? Of course he is a nazirite. The Gemara answers: This <i>halakha</i> is stated <b>due to the latter clause</b> of that mishna, which states that if <b>a daughter, a <i>tumtum</i>, or a hermaphrodite</b> are born to him, <b>he is not a nazirite.</b> The Gemara questions this, too: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious,</b> since he specified a son? The Gemara answers: It is necessary <b>lest you say</b> he did not literally mean a son, but rather <b>he meant to say: When I will be built up</b> by means of any child, including the types listed. The mishna therefore <b>teaches us that</b> this is <b>not</b> the case.
The mishna also taught: <b>And if he said: When I have a child,</b> then even if he has a daughter, a <i>tumtum</i>, or a hermaphrodite, his vow takes effect. The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> it <b>obvious</b> that this is the case? The Gemara answers: It is necessary to state this <b>lest you say</b> that <b>we require a child</b> of the kind <b>that is considered</b> significant <b>by people,</b> and he meant to exclude these other types of children when he vowed. The mishna therefore <b>teaches us</b> that this is not so.
§ The mishna taught that if <b>his wife miscarried he is not a nazirite,</b> even though it may have been a viable child. The Gemara clarifies: According to <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this taught?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda with regard to a heap</b> of wheat. Rabbi Yehuda holds that if one vows to be a nazirite if a heap contains a certain amount of wheat and it is unclear whether or not his condition was fulfilled, the <i>halakha</i> is ruled leniently, and he is not a nazirite.
The mishna further taught that <b>Rabbi Shimon says</b> that the individual <b>should say: If</b> this fetus <b>was viable</b> in terms of its development but died due to other causes, <b>I am hereby an obligatory nazirite</b> in fulfillment of my vow; <b>and if</b> it was <b>not</b> viable, <b>I am hereby a voluntary nazirite.</b> The Gemara relates that <b>Rabbi Abba inquired of Rav Huna:</b> If one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite when I will have a son, and his wife miscarried, and he separated an offering</b> for his naziriteship but did not sacrifice it, <b>and</b> his wife <b>gave birth again</b> to a son, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the offering he separated?
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion did Rabbi Abba pose his question? <b>If</b> he asked it <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon, what dilemma is he raising? Didn’t Rabbi Shimon say:</b> In a case of <b>uncertainty</b> with regard to <b>naziriteship,</b> the ruling is <b>to be stringent?</b> Here too, since the fetus might have been viable, he was required to separate the offerings after she miscarried, and he may not use those offerings for the naziriteship brought about by the later birth. <b>Rather,</b> one should say that the question was <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehuda, who said</b> that in a case of <b>uncertainty</b> with regard to <b>naziriteship,</b> the ruling is <b>to be lenient.</b> The question is as follows: <b>What</b> is the <i>halakha</i> in such a situation? Are the offerings already considered <b>consecrated</b> and need not be consecrated again, <b>or</b> are they <b>not consecrated</b> and therefore he must consecrate them a second time?
The Gemara asks: <b>What difference is there?</b> In any case, he is certainly obligated to observe naziriteship now, and he must separate the offerings. The Gemara answers: The question is referring <b>to</b> the issue of <b>its shearing and its labor.</b> If they are considered consecrated from the initial consecration, it is prohibited to shear their wool and use them for labor, like any other consecrated animal. But if they are not yet consecrated, it is permitted to use them. No answer was found for this question, and the Gemara concludes that the dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
§ With regard to one who accepted naziriteship upon himself that would begin upon the birth of his son, the Sage <b>ben Reḥumi inquired of Abaye:</b> If one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite when I will have a son, and another heard</b> him <b>and said: And it is</b> incumbent <b>upon me, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the second person? Is <b>the implication</b> of his statement a concurrence <b>to the statement</b> of the first one, which would mean that he too accepts naziriteship upon himself when the first has a son, <b>or</b> is <b>the implication</b> of his statement meant to be understood <b>about himself,</b> i.e., that he has vowed to be a nazirite when he has a son of his own?
The Gemara develops the question further: Even <b>if you say</b> that the phrase: And it is incumbent upon me, has <b>the implication</b> of meaning that it is to be understood <b>about himself, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> if one <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite when I will have a son, and another heard</b> him <b>and said: And I?</b> What is the meaning of the second person’s statement? Is it to be understood that here too, <b>he is speaking of himself,</b> meaning: I shall be a nazirite when I will have a son of my own, <b>or perhaps this is what he is saying: I love you as you</b> love <b>yourself;</b> I would be as happy as you at the birth of your son, and I too will be a nazirite when you have a son. Ben Reḥumi continues: <b>If you say</b> that <b>anything</b> he says to another <b>in front of him</b>

Daf 13b

should be understood in light of the fact that <b>the matter is embarrassing for him,</b> the second person is likely to mean that he will become a nazirite upon the birth of a child to the first person, as he will be embarrassed to seem indifferent about the birth of the child to the person standing before him, then the following question arises: If one <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite when so-and-so will have a son, and another heard and said: And I, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Do we say</b> that since the second person <b>did not</b> vow <b>in front of</b> the subject of the first person’s vow, he therefore <b>speaks of himself</b> when he says: And I, meaning that he will be a nazirite when he has a son of his own? <b>Or perhaps this is what he is saying to him: I love him as you do,</b> and I too will be a nazirite when he has a son. As in the previous cases, no answer was found for this question, and <b>the dilemma remains</b> unresolved.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In a case where one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> now, <b>and</b> I will be <b>a nazirite when I will have a son,</b> and <b>he began counting his</b> own term of naziriteship, i.e., his first vow, <b>and afterward</b> in the middle of this naziriteship period <b>a son was born to him,</b> he first <b>completes his</b> own initial term of naziriteship <b>and afterward he counts</b> the term of naziriteship he vowed on the condition <b>of</b> the birth of <b>his son.</b> However, if he reversed the order and said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite when I will have a son, and</b> I am hereby <b>a nazirite,</b> and he <b>began counting his</b> own term of naziriteship <b>and afterward,</b> during this period, <b>a son was born to him,</b> he <b>sets aside his</b> own term of naziriteship <b>and counts that</b> which he vowed on condition of the birth <b>of his son, and afterward</b> he <b>completes his</b> own term of naziriteship.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> In light of the ruling of the mishna, <b>Rava asks:</b> If one <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> for a standard term of thirty days and will begin observing it <b>after twenty days, and</b> I am also a nazirite <b>from now</b> for <b>one hundred days, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Should one say that <b>since these one hundred</b> days of naziriteship are <b>not completed within</b> those first <b>twenty</b> days, it could be said that the one hundred days of naziriteship <b>do not take effect</b> at all until after he has completed the thirty-day naziriteship? <b>Or perhaps, since he</b> still <b>has</b> at least thirty days of <b>hair growth at the end,</b> as after the thirty-day term he could observe an additional eighty days, therefore the one hundred days of naziriteship <b>take effect</b> from now, and he counts twenty days, pauses to observe the other term of naziriteship for thirty days, shaves, and then completes the final eighty days of the long term of naziriteship.
The Gemara asks: <b>And let him raise</b> this <b>dilemma</b> with regard to <b>a short</b> term of <b>naziriteship,</b> when fewer than thirty days would remain if he suspended the first term of naziriteship in order to observe the other. The Gemara answers: <b>He raises one dilemma as a result of the other.</b> In other words, Rava’s question was an outgrowth of a different inquiry, which in turn led to his question. The full discussion is as follows:

Daf 14a

<b>If you say</b> that when he accepts naziriteship upon himself after twenty days, and then vows <b>a short</b> term of <b>naziriteship</b> of thirty days starting now, then <b>since,</b> if he pauses from his first term of naziriteship after twenty days, <b>it is</b> only <b>ten days that are left over</b> to complete after the second term of naziriteship, <b>those ten should not be reckoned for him</b> to complete a full term of naziriteship. Ten days are insufficient for hair growth. Therefore, he must observe a full thirty-day term of naziriteship after the second term of naziriteship, which means he is not a nazirite for the first twenty days. With that assumption in mind, the question arose: <b>Certainly</b> if he vows to be <b>a nazirite</b> for <b>one hundred days, since he has</b> a further <b>eighty days at the end,</b> which is sufficient time for his hair to grow, do the first twenty days <b>count for him</b> as part of the one hundred days <b>or not?</b>
