This inquiry leads to yet another question: <b>And if you say</b> that the first days of naziriteship <b>take effect,</b> and his term of naziriteship will be completed in the eighty days after the second term of naziriteship, then if he <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite after twenty days, and from now</b> I am <b>a permanent nazirite, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Does</b> the permanent naziriteship <b>take effect for him</b> during those twenty days <b>or not?</b> Since permanent naziriteship is unlimited, he could not have intended to complete its days after the second term of naziriteship, and it is reasonable to assume that he intended for the permanent naziriteship to begin after the second term of naziriteship has been completed.
<b>If you say: Here,</b> with regard to cases of a standard permanent naziriteship after twenty days, <b>since</b> it <b>is possible to request</b> of a halakhic authority to dissolve the vow, which would result in the first term of naziriteship taking full effect, it therefore <b>takes effect</b> during those twenty days, then the following question arises: If one <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson after twenty days, and from now</b> I am hereby <b>a nazirite without specification, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Since <b>here it is not possible to request</b> of a halakhic authority to dissolve the vow of naziriteship like Samson, and there is no way of completing the first term of naziriteship, <b>does</b> the first naziriteship <b>take effect or not?</b> The Gemara adds another question: If one <b>said:</b> I am hereby <b>like Moses on the seventh</b> day <b>of Adar, what</b> is the <i>halakha</i>? Is this considered an expression of naziriteship in that just as Moses passed away on that date and drank no more wine, so too, the speaker vows to be a nazirite? Alternatively, perhaps this phrase does not indicate the acceptance of naziriteship.
The Gemara comments: At least <b>resolve the first of these</b> questions, the one raised at the beginning of the discussion, as it is stated explicitly in the <i>Tosefta</i> (2:5): If one says: <b>I am hereby a nazirite after twenty days, and</b> I am hereby a nazirite <b>from now</b> for <b>one hundred days, he counts twenty</b> days of his term of naziriteship immediately, <b>after which he counts thirty</b> days for the subsequent term of naziriteship, <b>and afterward he counts eighty</b> days <b>in order to complete</b> his <b>first</b> term of <b>naziriteship.</b>
§ The Gemara returns to its discussion of the statement of the mishna with regard to one who vowed to be a nazirite upon the birth of a son in addition to a standard term of naziriteship. The mishna ruled that he begins his term of naziriteship, and if he has a son during that term, he pauses from that term of naziriteship and starts the term for his son, after which he completes the first term. The Torah states that a nazirite who becomes impure while observing his term of naziriteship must start counting afresh, which leads to the following question: What happens if <b>he became impure during the days of</b> his term of naziriteship for <b>his son? Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> This <b>negates</b> all of it, even the days he has counted for his own term of naziriteship, and he must observe two full terms of naziriteship. <b>Reish Lakish says: This does not negate</b> the days he observed for his own term of naziriteship, only those of the term of naziriteship for his son.
The Gemara clarifies the two approaches: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says</b> it <b>negates</b> all of it, since he holds that <b>it is one long</b> term of <b>naziriteship. Reish Lakish says it does not negate</b> the days he observed for his own term of naziriteship because <b>his</b> term of <b>naziriteship is</b> a <b>discrete</b> naziriteship, <b>and that for his son is</b> a <b>discrete</b> naziriteship.

Daf 14b

These two <i>amora’im</i> have a similar dispute in the case of one who contracted leprosy during his naziriteship and <b>became impure</b> by coming into contact with corpse <b>during the days of his leprosy.</b> Although the days of his leprosy do not negate the previous days of his naziriteship, they are not counted as part of his naziriteship either. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> Contraction of impurity from a corpse <b>negates</b> the days of naziriteship observed before his leprosy, which means he must start counting afresh once he is purified of leprosy. <b>Reish Lakish says: It does not negate</b> the days he has observed, and he need observe only the remaining days of his naziriteship after his purification.
The Gemara clarifies their respective approaches. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says</b> the impurity from a corpse <b>negates</b> the days he already observed, <b>since he remains in</b> a period of <b>naziriteship</b> even when he is a leper, as his leprosy does not negate his naziriteship. <b>Reish Lakish says it does not negate</b> the days he observed, since he maintains that <b>leprosy is discrete and naziriteship is discrete.</b> One’s time as a leper is not considered part of his naziriteship.
The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> it is <b>necessary</b> to teach this dispute in both cases. <b>As, if it were stated in this first one,</b> with regard to one who became impure during his term of naziriteship for the birth of his son, one might say that it is specifically <b>in that</b> case that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said it</b> also <b>negates</b> the days of his standard naziriteship because <b>there is one label of naziriteship</b> that applies to both vows, <b>but in this</b> case, where the leper became impure from a corpse, one might <b>say he concedes to Reish Lakish</b> that it does not negate the days of his naziriteship that he has counted, <b>since a nazirite is discrete and leprosy is discrete.</b>
<b>And</b> conversely, <b>if it were stated</b> only <b>in this</b> case, that of the leper who contracted impurity from a corpse, one might say that it is only <b>in this</b> case that <b>Reish Lakish said</b> it does not negate his days of naziriteship, <b>but in that</b> case, where one became impure while observing the term of naziriteship for his son, one might <b>say he concedes to Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> that it negates the entire period, since they are all days of naziriteship. Therefore, it is <b>necessary</b> for the dispute to be taught in both cases.
In connection with the dispute between Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish, the Gemara discusses the case of a nazirite <b>who became impure on the day of</b> his <b>hair growth.</b> The case is that of one who contracted leprosy during his naziriteship and then was purified from his condition and shaved, as required of him. At this point he must wait another thirty days so that his hair can grow enough for him to shave it for the end of his naziriteship. If he became impure during this additional period, <b>Rav said: It does not negate</b> the days he previously counted for his naziriteship. The Gemara explains: <b>Even according to Rabbi Yoḥanan, who says</b> that if one became impure during the days of the term of naziriteship for his son it <b>negates</b> the entire period, <b>this statement</b> applies only <b>where he</b> still <b>remains in</b> a period of <b>naziriteship,</b> but the time of <b>hair growth is an addition to</b> his period of <b>naziriteship</b> and is not considered part of the term itself.
<b>And Shmuel said:</b> It <b>negates</b> the earlier days. The Gemara explains: <b>Even according to Reish Lakish, who says</b> that becoming impure during the naziriteship for his son <b>does not negate</b> the days of his naziriteship, <b>there</b> it is referring to a case of <b>two</b> distinct terms of <b>naziriteship,</b> a standard one for himself and another for his son. But <b>here</b> it is <b>one naziriteship,</b> since the days he observes for the growth of his hair complete his naziriteship.
§ <b>Rav Ḥisda said: All concede that if the hair was consecrated</b> during the sprinkling <b>of the blood, he has no</b> means of <b>remedy.</b> That is, if the nazirite had completed his term and brought his offerings, and the blood of the offerings was sprinkled, and he then became impure before shaving, he has no way to shave or to render wine permitted to himself. It has not been permitted through the offerings he brought because he has become impure, and he cannot bring other offerings for this purpose since one may not bring two sets of offerings for the same term of naziriteship.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion did Rav Ḥisda state this <i>halakha</i>? <b>If</b> one says that it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Eliezer,</b> this cannot be, <b>since</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>said</b> that <b>shaving is indispensable</b> to the completion of naziriteship. The impurity <b>is</b> therefore contracted <b>within the full term</b> of his naziriteship, in which case it would not make sense that he has no remedy; <b>but let</b> it instead <b>negate</b> his days of naziriteship, and he will observe them again, after which he will shave. <b>Rather,</b> perhaps Rav Ḥisda spoke <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis; didn’t they say</b> that <b>shaving is not indispensable</b> to the completion of naziriteship, which means his impurity occurred when he was no longer a nazirite? The Gemara answers: <b>Actually,</b> Rav Ḥisda ruled <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis, and what</b> is the meaning of: <b>He has no</b> means of <b>remedy?</b> It means: <b>He has no</b> means of <b>remedy for the mitzva of shaving,</b> i.e., he cannot fulfill the mitzva of shaving in purity as required.
§ <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, said: A nazirite whose days</b> of naziriteship <b>are completed</b> but who has yet to bring his offerings <b>is flogged for the</b> contracting of <b>impurity</b> if he becomes impure from a corpse, like any nazirite who becomes impure, <b>but he is not flogged for the</b> act of <b>shaving, nor for the</b> drinking of <b>wine.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is different</b> with regard to <b>impurity that he is flogged</b> for it? It is <b>as the verse states: “All the days that he has consecrated himself unto the Lord</b> he shall not come near to a dead body” (Numbers 6:6). The additional word “all” comes <b>to include</b> the <b>days after the completion</b> of his naziriteship and teaches that they are <b>like the days before</b> its <b>completion</b> with regard to the prohibition against contracting impurity from the dead. Therefore, he is liable to receive lashes.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, let him also be liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for shaving, since the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: <b>“All the days of his vow of naziriteship there shall no razor come upon his head”</b> (Numbers 6:5). Here too, the word “all” should <b>render</b> the <b>days after</b> the <b>completion</b> of his naziriteship <b>like the days before</b> its <b>completion. And furthermore,</b> with regard to wine as well, the verse states: <b>“All the days of his naziriteship, from anything that is made of the grapevine,</b> from the pressed grapes even to the grapestone, he shall not eat” (Numbers 6:4). Once again, the word “all” should <b>render</b> the <b>days after</b> the <b>completion</b> of his naziriteship <b>like the days before</b> its <b>completion</b> with regard to drinking wine. What is the reason for the difference in <i>halakha</i> between contracting impurity on the one hand, and shaving and drinking wine on the other?

Daf 15a

The Gemara answers: <b>Here,</b> with regard to impurity, <b>it is different, as the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: <b>“And he defile his consecrated head”</b> (Numbers 6:9), which indicates that even <b>one whose naziriteship is</b> dependent only <b>upon his head,</b> i.e., one who has completed his naziriteship other than shaving, is liable to receive lashes if he contracts impurity. The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to the statement of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A nazirite whose days are completed is forbidden to shave, and</b> is forbidden <b>to drink wine, and</b> is forbidden <b>to contract impurity from corpses. And if he did shave, or drink wine, or contract impurity from corpses, he incurs the forty</b> lashes administered to one who violates a Torah prohibition. This <i>baraita</i> clearly states that he is flogged for any of the three prohibitions, which is a <b>conclusive refutation</b> of the statement of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of one who said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite when I will have a son, and</b> he added: I am hereby <b>a nazirite</b> from now for <b>one hundred days,</b> and he then began observing the one hundred days of his naziriteship, if <b>a son is born to him up to seventy</b> days from the start of his naziriteship <b>he has not lost anything.</b> He pauses from the observance of the naziriteship of one hundred days and observes the thirty-day term for his son. He then completes the thirty or more days left of his initial naziriteship. However, if his son is born <b>after seventy</b> days, this <b>negates</b> the first <b>seventy</b> days, and he must observe a full hundred days after he completes the naziriteship for his son. The reason is that here, he is unable to merely complete the remaining days of his initial naziriteship after shaving at the completion of the naziriteship for his son, <b>since shaving cannot</b> be performed after a period of <b>less than thirty days.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav said: The seventieth day</b> itself <b>counts for here and for there,</b> as the last of the seventy days of his term of naziriteship as well as the first day of the naziriteship for his son. The Gemara questions Rav’s statement. <b>We learned</b> in the mishna: If a son is <b>born to him up to seventy, he has not lost anything.</b> This seems to include the seventieth day as well. <b>And if it enters your mind</b> that it <b>counts for here and for there,</b> not only has he not lost anything, but he even <b>gains</b> a day, so why would the mishna state: He has not lost anything? The Gemara answers: <b>Rather, by right</b> the mishna should <b>not</b> have <b>taught: Up to seventy</b> he has not lost anything, since if the son is born on the seventieth day he actually gains, as stated above, <b>but due to</b> the fact <b>that it is taught</b> in <b>the last clause</b> of the mishna: <b>After seventy it negates seventy,</b> in which case he does lose, the <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>teaches the first clause</b> with the contrasting expression: Up to <b>seventy</b> he has not lost anything.
The Gemara asks further: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a statement that contradicts Rav’s opinion <b>from the last clause</b> of the mishna: If the son <b>is born after seventy</b> days, <b>it negates</b> the first <b>seventy</b> days. If, as Rav stated, one day can count for both terms, then the final day of the thirty-day term for his son also counts toward his hundred-day term, meaning that there will be thirty days remaining for a full hair growth; in that case, why should he forfeit the first days? The Gemara answers this: <b>What is</b> the meaning of: <b>After?</b> It means <b>after, after.</b> The mishna is referring to the second day after the seventieth, the seventy-second day, so that there do not remain thirty days for his hair to grow.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> according to this explanation, <b>what</b> would be the <i>halakha</i> if a son is born on the day that <b>actually</b> comes <b>after</b> the seventieth, the seventy-first day; <b>so too,</b> would Rav say <b>that it does not negate</b> the previous days, because he has thirty days remaining to grow his hair? <b>If so, why</b> does the <i>tanna</i> <b>specifically teach:</b> If he is <b>born up to seventy he has not lost anything?</b> The same would hold true <b>even</b> for a case where he was born on the day <b>after seventy as well,</b> as <b>didn’t you say</b> that <b>it does not negate? Rather,</b> one must <b>learn from this</b> that: <b>After,</b> means the <b>actual</b> day after, the seventy-first day, <b>and likewise</b> conclude that <b>the mishna</b> is difficult <b>for Rav.</b> The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>learn from this</b> that the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna disagrees with Rav.
§ The Gemara asks: <b>And Rav, in accordance with whose</b> opinion did <b>he say his <i>halakha</i>?</b> Since the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna disagrees with him, which <i>tanna</i> does he follow in ruling that one day may be counted for two different observances? <b>If we say</b> that he stated the ruling <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul, as we learned</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i> (<i>Mo’ed Katan</i> 2:9): With regard to <b>one who buries his dead three days before a pilgrimage Festival,</b> the rabbinic <b>decree of seven</b> days of mourning <b>is voided for him,</b> i.e., once the Festival begins, he no longer observes the prohibitions and customs of the first seven days of mourning. Since he has mourned for three days, the Festival voids the remainder of the seven days.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If he buried his dead <b>eight days before a pilgrimage Festival,</b> the rabbinic <b>decree of</b> the restrictions of <b>thirty</b> days of mourning <b>is voided for him</b> as well. Since he already observed one day of this type of mourning he need not complete the entire period, <b>and it is</b> therefore <b>permitted</b> for him <b>to cut his hair on the eve of the pilgrimage Festival</b> in honor of the Festival. <b>But if he did not cut his hair on the eve of the pilgrimage Festival, it is prohibited</b> for him <b>to cut his hair after the pilgrimage Festival,</b> until thirty days of mourning have passed.

Daf 15b

<b>Abba Shaul says: Even</b> if he did <b>not cut his hair before the pilgrimage Festival, it is permitted</b> for him <b>to cut his hair after the pilgrimage Festival.</b> His reasoning is <b>that just as</b> the <b>mitzva of three voids</b> the rabbinic <b>decree of seven,</b> as was taught before; <b>so</b> the <b>mitzva of seven voids</b> the rabbinic <b>decree of thirty.</b> Since he completed the observance of the seven days of mourning before the Festival, he need not observe the thirty days of mourning.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the rationale of Abba Shaul</b> for his opinion that if he observed seven days of mourning before the Festival commenced the thirty-day mourning period is voided? <b>Isn’t it because he holds</b> that the <b>seventh</b> day <b>counts for here and there,</b> i.e., the seventh day is considered both the end of the seven days and the start of the thirty days of mourning, so he had already begun observing his thirty days of mourning before the start of the Festival? This ruling may be the basis for the ruling of Rav. The Gemara rejects this: This does not support Rav, since <b>perhaps Abba Shaul was saying</b> his ruling that the same day counts for both here and there <b>only with regard to the mourning</b> of <b>seven</b> days, which is <b>by rabbinic law, but with regard to a nazirite,</b> whose obligation is <b>by Torah law,</b> he would <b>not</b> say so.
<b>Rather, Rav spoke in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei says: A woman who observes</b> a clean <b>day for</b> each <b>day</b> she experiences a discharge is a woman who discharges blood for one or two days at a time when she does not expect her menstrual period. The case under discussion is one where she experienced a discharge for one day and <b>they slaughtered</b> a Paschal offering <b>and sprinkled</b> the blood <b>for her on her second</b> day, after she immersed in a ritual bath. At that point, it is unclear whether she will remain clean of discharges for the remainder of the day, in which case she is retroactively pure from the time she immersed and may eat the Paschal offering at night, or whether she will experience a discharge of blood during the day, in which case her immersion is retroactively invalid and she was impure the entire time.
<b>And after that, she saw</b> blood, thereby retroactively clarifying that at the time the Paschal offering was slaughtered she was unfit to partake of it. The <i>halakha</i> is that <b>she may not eat</b> from the Paschal lamb due to her ritual impurity, <b>but she is exempt from observing</b> the <b>second <i>Pesaḥ</i>,</b> which is observed by those who did not sacrifice the Paschal offering on the first Pesaḥ. The reason is that since she was pure when they slaughtered the Paschal offering on her behalf, she has fulfilled the obligation of the offering, despite the fact that she became impure and was unable to eat the offering.
The Gemara clarifies this: <b>What is the rationale of Rabbi Yosei</b> for his opinion that she is exempt from observing the second <i>Pesaḥ</i>? <b>Isn’t it because he holds</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that <b>of an entire</b> day? Since she was pure for part of the day, it is considered as though she was pure the entire day. The Gemara rejects this: <b>From where</b> do you know that this is the rationale? <b>Perhaps</b> it is <b>because</b> Rabbi Yosei <b>holds</b> that <b>one becomes impure from now and onward.</b> He holds that the impurity of a <i>zava</i> starts only from the moment she had a discharge of blood, but she is ritually pure up to that point, so she was ritually pure when they slaughtered the offering for her.
The Gemara questions this explanation: <b>And does Rabbi Yosei hold</b> in accordance with <b>this</b> ruling that she is impure only from that moment onward?
<b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> With regard to <b>a man who experiences a gonorrhea-like discharge [<i>zav</i>] who has had two sightings</b> of discharge, <b>for whom they slaughtered</b> a Paschal offering <b>and sprinkled</b> its blood <b>on the seventh,</b> and final, day of impurity, <b>and similarly,</b> with regard to <b>a woman who observes</b> a clean <b>day for a day, for whom they slaughtered</b> a Paschal offering <b>and sprinkled</b> its blood, and <b>afterward they saw</b> their respective discharges, <b>although they render objects designed for lying and sitting impure retroactively, they are exempt from observing</b> the <b>second <i>Pesaḥ</i>.</b> This is proof that Rabbi Yosei holds that their ritual impurity applies retroactively, rather than from the moment of discharge onward. It must be that the reason why they are nevertheless exempt from observing the second <i>Pesaḥ</i> is that part of the day is like the entire day, and the part of the day before they became impure, during which the blood of the Paschal offering was sprinkled for them, is considered a whole clean day.
The Gemara responds to this attempted proof: <b>What</b> is the meaning of Rabbi Yosei’s statement that the ritual impurity applies <b>retroactively?</b> It means that the ritual impurity applies retroactively <b>by rabbinic law.</b> However, by Torah law, the <i>zav</i> or <i>zava</i> is impure only from the time of the new sighting and onward. The Gemara adds: <b>So too, it is reasonable</b> that this is Rabbi Yosei’s opinion, <b>as, if it enters your mind</b> that they become ritually impure retroactively <b>by Torah law, why are they exempt from observing</b> the <b>second <i>Pesaḥ</i></b> if by Torah law they were impure at the time the offering was slaughtered?
The Gemara responds: This is no proof, since <b>actually, I</b> could <b>say to you</b> that this retroactive <b>impurity</b> is <b>by Torah law,</b> and the reason they are exempt from observing the second <i>Pesaḥ</i> is that the Sages <b>permitted</b> impurity of <b>the deep of <i>ziva</i>.</b> The <i>halakha</i> is that in the case of a Paschal offering, an unknown impurity of the dead, called the impurity of the deep, is permitted. In a case where one brought his offering and later became aware that he had contracted impurity from a corpse, if this source of impurity was unknown to everyone, he is not required to observe the second <i>Pesaḥ</i>. The Gemara suggests that Rabbi Yosei may hold that the same <i>halakha</i> applies to the impurity of a <i>zava</i> as well: Since the owner of the offering was pure when it was brought and could not have known that she would become impure due to discharge later that day, it is similarly considered an impurity of the deep, and she has therefore fulfilled her obligation.
§ The Gemara points out: <b>And even Rabbi Oshaya holds</b> that, according to Rabbi Yosei, the fact that a <i>zav</i> renders objects designed for sitting or lying impure <b>retroactively</b> is only <b>by rabbinic law</b> in this circumstance. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Oshaya says:</b> In a case of <b>one who sees gonorrhea-like discharge on his seventh</b> clean day, this <b>negates</b> the clean days <b>that preceded it</b> and starts his seven days anew. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said to him that it should negate only its own day,</b> i.e., he should require only one additional clean day.
The Gemara expresses surprise: <b>Whichever way you</b> look at Rabbi Yoḥanan’s statement, it is difficult. <b>If</b> this discharge <b>negates</b> clean days just as the case of a <i>zav</i> who had a discharge during his seven days, <b>it should negate all</b> his clean days, not only the last one. <b>If it does not negate</b> clean days, since it is not considered a discharge within his seven days but rather, as the first sighting of a new <i>ziva</i>, it should <b>not negate</b> any of it, <b>and not</b> even <b>its own day.</b> What, then, is the logic of Rabbi Yoḥanan’s ruling that it negates a single day? <b>Rather, say</b> instead that Rabbi Yoḥanan said as follows: It does <b>not negate</b> at all, <b>and not</b> even <b>its own day,</b> since this discharge is considered to be the first of a new series.

Daf 16a

<b>And</b> Rabbi Oshaya <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan: <b>Rabbi Yosei holds in accordance with your</b> opinion, <b>as he</b> too <b>says</b> that in this case one <b>becomes impure from now and onward,</b> and any impurity after the start of the seventh day does not cause him to forfeit the previous clean days. The Gemara analyzes Rabbi Oshaya’s statement: <b>But didn’t Rabbi Yosei say</b> that the impurity is <b>retroactive,</b> as stated above? Rather, Rabbi Oshaya must certainly hold as follows: <b>What is</b> the meaning of Rabbi Yosei’s ruling that he is impure <b>retroactively?</b> It means that he is rendered impure only <b>by rabbinic law,</b> but not by Torah law.
The Gemara poses a question with regard to Rabbi Yosei’s statement: <b>And</b> as to <b>Rabbi Yosei, now, he holds</b> that the status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that <b>of an entire</b> day. <b>How can you find a full-fledged <i>zava</i> who brings an offering? Since she sees</b> a discharge <b>at the midpoint of the day, the other half of the day counts for her</b> as a full day of <b>observing,</b> as part of the day is considered to be like all of a day. The Gemara answers: <b>If you wish, say that</b> she <b>continuously discharges</b> blood for <b>three days consecutively. And if you wish, say that she saw</b> a discharge on <b>three</b> consecutive <b>days close to sunset.</b> In that case she is impure on each occasion at the end of one day and the beginning of the next, so that <b>there was not</b> any <b>time</b> to become pure on the following day so as <b>to be included for her counting</b> of a clean day corresponding to an impure one.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who said: I am hereby a nazirite,</b> without specifying how long his term of naziriteship would last, <b>shaves</b> his hair on the <b>thirty-first day</b> after the start of his naziriteship, as an unspecified term of naziriteship lasts thirty days. <b>And if he shaved on the thirtieth day, he has fulfilled</b> his obligation. If he explicitly said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty days,</b> then, <b>if he shaved on the thirtieth day, he has not fulfilled</b> his obligation. Since the naziriteship would have been for thirty days even without him stating: For thirty days, this addition is understood to indicate that he will observe naziriteship for a full thirty days.
<b>One who accepted two</b> terms of <b>naziriteship shaves</b> at the close of <b>the first</b> naziriteship on the <b>thirty-first day, and</b> at the close of <b>the second</b> term on the <b>sixty-first day.</b> Since his second term of naziriteship begins after shaving on the thirty-first day, the sixty-first day of the first term is the thirty-first day of his second term. <b>And if he shaved</b> for <b>the first</b> term on the <b>thirtieth day, he shaves</b> for <b>the second</b> term on the <b>sixtieth day,</b> which is the thirty-first day after the start of his second term of naziriteship. <b>And if he shaved</b> for the second term on <b>day sixty less one, he has fulfilled</b> his obligation, as this is the thirtieth day of his second term.
<b>And this testimony was attested to</b> by <b>Rabbi Pappeyas,</b> who heard from his teachers <b>with regard to one who vowed</b> to observe <b>two</b> terms of <b>naziriteship, that if he shaved</b> for <b>the first</b> term on the <b>thirtieth day, he shaves</b> for <b>the second</b> term <b>on the sixtieth day. And if he shaved</b> for the second term <b>on</b> the <b>day sixty less one, he has fulfilled</b> his obligation, <b>because the thirtieth day</b> of the first term of naziriteship <b>counts</b> as part <b>of his tally</b> of the second term.
<b>One who said: I am hereby a nazirite,</b> without further specification, if <b>he became ritually impure</b> through contact with a corpse on the <b>thirtieth day</b> of his term of naziriteship, <b>it negates the entire</b> tally, and he must start his naziriteship afresh. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It negates only seven</b> days, which he must observe until his purification, after which he brings his offerings. If he said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty days,</b> and <b>he became impure</b> on the <b>thirtieth day,</b> everyone agrees that <b>it negates the entire</b> tally. If he said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one hundred days,</b> if <b>he became impure</b> on the <b>one hundredth day, it negates the entire</b> tally. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It negates only thirty</b> days, and he observes the final thirty days again. If <b>he became impure</b> on the <b>one hundred and first day</b> before bringing his offerings, <b>it negates</b> only <b>thirty days,</b> but does not negate all of the observed days. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It negates only seven</b> days.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught: <b>One who said: I am hereby a nazirite,</b> and <b>became ritually impure</b> on the <b>thirtieth day, it negates the entire</b> tally; <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It negates only seven.</b>

Daf 16b

The Gemara explains: <b>Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that when <b>anyone</b> becomes ritually impure <b>after the completion</b> of his term of naziriteship, but before he brought his offerings, only <b>seven</b> days are <b>negated,</b> and the thirtieth day is after the completion of his term. The mishna then teaches that one who said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>thirty days, and became impure</b> on the <b>thirtieth day, it negates the entire</b> tally. <b>Rabbi Eliezer does not disagree</b> in this case, since it is an instance <b>where he</b> explicitly <b>said that</b> he accepts naziriteship on himself for <b>a full</b> thirty days, and his ritual impurity therefore occurs during his naziriteship period.
The mishna also taught that if one said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one hundred days, and he became impure</b> on the <b>one hundredth day, it negates the entire</b> tally. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says it negates only thirty. And this entire</b> mishna should be explained <b>as we discussed it, in accordance with</b> the opinions <b>of bar Padda and Rav Mattana</b> earlier (5a–6b).
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery, even if he was there</b> for a full <b>thirty days</b> without leaving, those days he spent in the cemetery <b>do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally,</b> since his naziriteship has not yet gone into effect. <b>And he</b> therefore <b>does not bring</b> the three <b>offerings of impurity,</b> brought by a nazirite when rendered ritually impure by contact with a corpse, despite having been in a cemetery. If <b>he left</b> the cemetery <b>and entered</b> it again, those days <b>do count</b> as part <b>of his tally,</b> meaning the naziriteship takes effect, <b>and he does bring the offerings of impurity</b> for reentering the cemetery.
<b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> This <i>halakha</i> does <b>not</b> apply to one who entered the cemetery <b>on the very day</b> that he left it, <b>as it is stated</b> with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of a ritually impure nazirite: <b>“But the first days shall be void”</b> (Numbers 6:12), which indicates that he does not bring the offerings <b>until he will have “first days”</b> of purity, during which he observed his naziriteship.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It was stated</b> that the <i>amora’im</i> disagree with regard to <b>one who vowed to be a nazirite</b> while <b>in a cemetery. Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Naziriteship takes effect for him, and Reish Lakish said: Naziriteship does not take effect for him.</b> The Gemara clarifies their respective opinions: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> who <b>said naziriteship takes effect for him, holds</b> that from the moment he accepts naziriteship upon himself it is <b>pending,</b> so that <b>when</b> it <b>is found</b> that he is in a state of <b>ritual purity</b> it <b>takes</b> immediate <b>effect.</b> The vow registers from when he states it, but it cannot take effect in practice as long as he stands in a ritually impure location. <b>And Reish Lakish said</b> that <b>naziriteship does not take effect for him</b> at all. Consequently, <b>if</b> he <b>again said</b> after leaving the cemetery that he accepts a vow of naziriteship, <b>it takes effect for him; but if</b> he does <b>not</b> repeat his vow, he is <b>not</b> a nazirite.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan raised an objection to</b> the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish</b> from the mishna: <b>One who took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery, even if he was there</b> for a full <b>thirty days</b> without leaving, those days he spent in the cemetery <b>do not count</b> as part <b>of the tally,</b> since his naziriteship has not yet gone into effect. <b>And he</b> therefore <b>does not bring the offerings of impurity</b> despite having been in a cemetery. Rabbi Yoḥanan infers from this: <b>It is the offerings of impurity that he does not bring, but</b> as far as the issue of the naziriteship <b>taking effect, it does take effect for him.</b> Reish Lakish <b>said to him:</b> It means <b>he is not</b> included <b>in the law of</b> prohibited <b>ritual impurity, and</b> therefore <b>he is not</b> included <b>in the law of the offerings.</b> He is not a nazirite at all.
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>raised an objection to</b> the opinion of Reish Lakish from what was taught in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 2:14): <b>One who was impure and took a vow of naziriteship</b> must still observe the <i>halakhot</i> of a nazirite: He is <b>prohibited from shaving, and from drinking wine, and from becoming impure from a corpse. And if he shaved, or</b> if he <b>drank wine, or</b> if <b>he became impure from a corpse, he incurs [<i>sofeg</i>] the forty</b> lashes administered to one who actively transgresses a negative Torah prohibition. Rabbi Yoḥanan asks: <b>Granted, if you say</b> that naziriteship <b>takes effect</b> despite his ritual impurity, <b>that is the reason that he incurs the forty</b> lashes, similar to any nazirite who transgresses the prohibitions of naziriteship. <b>But if you say</b> the naziriteship <b>does not take effect</b> while he is ritually impure, <b>why does he incur the forty</b> lashes?

Daf 17a

Reish Lakish responded: <b>With what are we dealing here</b> in this <i>baraita</i>? <b>With one who left and entered,</b> meaning that after having left the cemetery and purifying himself, he then vowed again to be a nazirite and subsequently reentered the cemetery.
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>raised</b> another <b>objection to</b> Reish Lakish’s opinion from a different source: <b>The difference between a ritually impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship and a ritually pure nazirite who became impure is only</b> the following <i>halakha</i>: If <b>a ritually impure</b> person <b>took a vow of naziriteship, his seventh</b> day of purification <b>counts</b> as part <b>of his tally</b> of his term of naziriteship, since he starts counting his term of naziriteship as soon as he becomes pure. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>a ritually pure nazirite who became impure, his seventh</b> day of purification <b>does not count</b> as part <b>of his tally.</b> Rather, he starts counting his days of naziriteship from the following day, which is the day on which he brings his offerings. <b>And if it enters your mind</b> that a vow of naziriteship stated while ritually impure <b>does not take effect, why</b> does the <i>baraita</i> state that the seventh day <b>counts</b> as part <b>of his tally,</b> indicating that the naziriteship takes effect without a need for him to restate his vow?
As a result of this question, the Gemara offers a different interpretation of their dispute. <b>Mar bar Rav Ashi said:</b> The dispute between Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish is not as stated above. With regard to the question of the vow of naziriteship <b>taking effect, everyone agrees that</b> it <b>takes effect</b> from the moment of his acceptance of naziriteship, even if he was in the cemetery. <b>Rather, when they disagree</b> it is with regard <b>to being flogged,</b> as follows: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan holds</b> that <b>since</b> the vow <b>takes effect, he is</b> therefore <b>flogged</b> for becoming impure, <b>and Reish Lakish holds that he is not flogged</b> for becoming impure, <b>but</b> the vow <b>does take effect</b> with regard to the prohibitions of naziriteship.
According to this version of the dispute as well, <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan raised an objection to Reish Lakish</b> from the mishna, which states: <b>One who took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery, even if he was there</b> for a full <b>thirty days</b> without leaving, those days he spent in the cemetery <b>do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally,</b> since his naziriteship has not yet gone into effect. <b>And he</b> therefore <b>does not bring the offerings of impurity,</b> despite having been in a cemetery. Rabbi Yoḥanan infers from this: <b>It is the offerings of impurity</b> that <b>he does not bring, but</b> he is <b>flogged for</b> contracting impurity, which is not in accordance with the opinion of Reish Lakish.
The Gemara answers: This inference is not correct, as <b>by right</b> the mishna <b>should teach: He is not flogged, but due to</b> the fact that the <i>tanna</i> <b>wants to teach the latter clause</b> of the mishna, which states: If <b>he left</b> the cemetery <b>and entered</b> it again, those days <b>do count</b> as part <b>of his tally,</b> meaning the naziriteship takes effect, <b>and he does bring the offerings of impurity</b> for reentering the cemetery, the <i>tanna</i> therefore <b>taught</b> a similar phrasing in <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna: <b>He does not bring the offerings of impurity,</b> so this should not be seen as an indication that he is not flogged.
The Gemara suggests another proof in support of Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion. <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from another <i>baraita</i>: <b>The difference between an impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship and a pure nazirite who became impure is only</b> the following <i>halakha</i>: <b>That</b> in the case of <b>an impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship, his seventh</b> day of purification <b>counts</b> as part <b>of his tally</b> of the term of naziriteship. <b>But</b> in the case of <b>a pure nazirite who became impure, his seventh</b> day of purification <b>does not count</b> as part <b>of his tally.</b> The Gemara infers: <b>But with regard to flogging, this</b> nazirite <b>and that</b> nazirite <b>are equal.</b> Reish Lakish <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan: This is <b>not</b> a correct inference; rather, it is with regard <b>to shaving</b> on the seventh day of purification that <b>this</b> nazirite <b>and that</b> nazirite <b>are equal,</b> since even a ritually impure person who took a vow of naziriteship must shave on that day.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> in that case, according to Reish Lakish, <b>what</b> is the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to flogging? This</b> nazirite <b>is flogged, and that</b> nazirite, who was ritually impure when he vowed, <b>is not flogged?</b> If so, <b>let him teach this</b> difference as well; why does the <i>baraita</i> state that there is only one difference between them? The Gemara answers: This <i>baraita</i> <b>is speaking of his remedy; it is not speaking of</b> a matter that is <b>detrimental to him.</b> Consequently the <i>baraita</i> does not discuss a nazirite’s punishments and discusses only the means by which he can resume his observance of naziriteship.
The Gemara further suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from that which is taught in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 2:14): <b>One who was ritually impure and took a vow of naziriteship</b> must still observe the <i>halakhot</i> of a nazirite. He is <b>prohibited from shaving and from drinking wine. And if he shaved, or</b> if <b>he drank wine, or</b> if <b>he became ritually impure from a corpse, he incurs the forty</b> lashes administered to one who actively transgresses a negative Torah prohibition. The Gemara concludes: This is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion of Reish Lakish, as explained by Mar bar Rav Ashi.
§ After concluding that one who takes a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery is liable to receive lashes, <b>Rava asks: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> if one took a vow to be <b>a nazirite while</b> standing <b>in a cemetery:</b> Is it <b>necessary</b> that his exit from the cemetery be <b>delayed</b> for a specific period of time for him to become liable <b>to</b> receive <b>lashes,</b> just as one who becomes ritually impure while in the Temple receives lashes only if he remains there for a specific period of time, <b>or</b> is it <b>not</b> necessary?
The Gemara clarifies: <b>What are the circumstances</b> in which this question is relevant? <b>If we say that they said to him</b> in warning: <b>Do not take a vow of naziriteship</b> in the cemetery, and he ignored their warning and took a vow of naziriteship, <b>why do I</b> need him to <b>delay</b> his exit? <b>What is the reason</b> that one who was already <b>a nazirite</b> when he entered a cemetery <b>does not need to delay</b> his exit in order to be liable to receive lashes? <b>Because they warn him</b> not to enter, and if he enters he has transgressed a prohibition and is flogged. <b>Here too, they warn him</b> not to take the vow, and he should therefore be liable to receive lashes if he does take the vow.

Daf 17b

<b>Rather,</b> Rava’s question should be understood to refer to a case <b>where</b> one <b>entered</b> the cemetery <b>in a chest, box, or cabinet,</b> i.e., in large wooden containers that do not contract ritual impurity. One can enter a cemetery in such a container without becoming impure. If one was carried into a cemetery inside one of these containers, then took a vow of naziriteship, <b>and another came and removed the top [<i>ma’aziva</i>] from above him,</b> he would become ritually impure by virtue of being in the cemetery.
If he was then warned to leave the cemetery, the question is as follows: <b>When we learned</b> by tradition that one is liable to receive lashes for <b>delaying</b> his exit, does that apply only <b>to the Temple, but outside</b> the Temple, such as when a nazirite is in a cemetery, does the <i>halakha</i> <b>not</b> apply, so that he is liable to receive lashes even if he did not delay his exit? <b>Or perhaps it is not different,</b> and a nazirite in a cemetery is flogged only if he remains there for a specific period of time. The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
§ <b>Rav Ashi raises a dilemma:</b> If <b>one took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery,</b> is he <b>required to shave</b> upon becoming ritually pure, before starting his term of naziriteship, <b>or not?</b> The Gemara explains the two sides of the question: <b>When</b> a ritually impure nazirite <b>is required to shave</b> before starting his tally, does that refer only to <b>a ritually pure</b> nazirite <b>who became impure, who has defiled his naziriteship? But a ritually impure</b> person <b>who</b> only <b>took a vow of naziriteship,</b> and whose naziriteship has not yet begun, is he <b>not</b> obligated to shave? <b>Or perhaps it is not different.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from the mishna: <b>One who took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery, even if he was there</b> for a full <b>thirty days</b> without leaving, those days he spent in the cemetery <b>do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally,</b> since his naziriteship has not yet gone into effect, <b>and he</b> therefore <b>does not bring the offerings of impurity.</b> The Gemara infers from this: <b>It is the offerings of impurity</b> that <b>he does not bring, but he is required to shave.</b> The Gemara rejects this proof: It is possible that the mishna <b>is saying: What is the reason,</b> as follows: <b>What is the reason</b> that <b>he does not bring the offerings of impurity? Because he does not require shaving.</b> This indicates that the naziriteship has not yet begun, therefore, he does not bring the offerings either.
The Gemara suggests another proof. <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from another <i>baraita</i>: <b>The difference between a ritually impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship and a ritually pure nazirite who became impure is only</b> the following <i>halakha</i>: With regard to <b>an impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship, his seventh</b> day of purification <b>counts</b> as part <b>of his tally</b> of the term of naziriteship, <b>but</b> with regard to <b>a pure nazirite who became impure, his seventh</b> day of purification <b>does not count</b> as part <b>of his tally.</b> The Gemara infers: <b>What, is it not</b> so that <b>with regard to shaving, this and that are equal,</b> and he must shave in either case? The Gemara rejects this: <b>No,</b> there is a different inference: <b>It is with regard to flogging</b> that <b>this and that are equal,</b> but one who took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery does not have to shave.
The Gemara questions this answer: <b>But what</b> is the <i>halakha</i> concerning <b>shaving?</b> Does <b>this</b> one, who became ritually impure, <b>shave, and that</b> one, who vowed while in a cemetery, <b>not shave?</b> If so, <b>let him teach this</b> difference as well. The Gemara answers: The <i>tanna</i> <b>taught: His seventh day, and all matters</b> relevant to it, including the <i>halakha</i> of shaving. Once it says that the seventh day is part of the tally of the ritually impure nazirite who became pure, it can be inferred that he does not bring the offerings of impurity on the eighth day, and therefore he does not shave on the seventh day.
The Gemara further suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>I have</b> derived <b>only that</b> a nazirite’s <b>days of ritual impurity do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally; from where</b> do I derive that <b>his days of confirmed</b> leprosy, in a case where a nazirite became a leper during his term, do not count toward his tally of naziriteship? <b>And</b> it would seem that <b>by right</b> it should be so: <b>Just as</b> at the end of <b>his days of ritual impurity</b> the nazirite <b>shaves and brings offerings, so too</b> at the end of <b>his days of confirmed</b> leprosy the <i>halakha</i> is that he <b>shaves and brings offerings</b> for his leprosy. <b>And</b> this comparison can be extended: <b>Just as the days of his ritual impurity do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally, so too his days of confirmed</b> leprosy <b>do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>No, if you say</b> so <b>with regard to his days of ritual impurity,</b> in that case it is <b>because with them, the previous</b> days of his naziriteship observed in ritual purity <b>are negated. Therefore,</b> those days <b>do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally.</b> But will <b>you say</b> the same <b>with regard to his days of confirmed</b> leprosy, <b>which do not negate the previous</b> days? If thirty days, enough time for his hair to regrow, remain in his term of naziriteship after becoming purified from his leprosy, the days before he contracted leprosy are not negated. It is <b>therefore</b> possible to argue that <b>they do count</b> as part <b>of his tally.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: But <b>you can say</b> that <b>just as</b> with regard to <b>a nazirite</b> who was <b>in the grave,</b> i.e., a cemetery, <b>whose hair is fit for shaving,</b> those days spent in the cemetery <b>do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally, his days of confirmed</b> leprosy, <b>which are not fit for</b> counting as part of the tally required for the <b>shaving,</b> since he must first shave as part of the purification process for his leprosy, is it <b>not all the more so that they do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally?</b> This concludes the <i>baraita</i>.
The Gemara now attempts to prove from the <i>baraita</i> that one who took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery must shave at the close of his process of purification: <b>What, is it not</b> so that when the <i>baraita</i> mentions shaving, it is referring to <b>the shaving of impurity,</b> and it is teaching that one who took a vow of naziriteship in a cemetery must shave at the end of his purification process? The Gemara rejects this: <b>No,</b> it is referring to <b>the shaving of purity,</b> meaning the shaving done after one completes his term of naziriteship. He then will shave all of his hair, including that which grew during the time he was ritually impure due to his presence in the cemetery. The Gemara adds: <b>So, too, it is reasonable</b> to say that this is the correct interpretation,

Daf 18a

<b>as if it enters your mind</b> to say that the <i>baraita</i> is discussing the <b>shaving</b> done as a result of the nazirite’s <b>impurity,</b> and contrasts a nazirite with a leper, do the end of a leper’s <b>days of confirmed</b> leprosy <b>not require shaving?</b> A leper must shave when he is purified from his condition, so how can he be described as unfit for shaving? Rather, the <i>baraita</i> must be referring to the nazirite’s shaving of purity, as suggested above (17b). The Gemara rejects this: <b>No,</b> it could be that the <i>baraita</i> is discussing the shaving done as a result of the nazirite’s impurity, and when it states that a leper is unfit for shaving, the <i>baraita</i> <b>is teaching</b> about the <b>shaving of naziriteship,</b> and the <i>baraita</i> is stating that a leper is unfit for any shaving of a nazirite, since he must first shave for his leprosy.
The Gemara suggests another proof to answer the question of whether one who vows naziriteship while in a cemetery must shave upon undergoing purification. <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“And he defiles his consecrated head,</b> he shall shave his head on the day of his purification, on the seventh day shall he shave it” (Numbers 6:9). The <i>baraita</i> explains: <b>The verse is speaking of a ritually pure</b> nazirite <b>who became impure, who requires hair removal and the bringing of birds</b> as offerings. And this verse comes <b>to exempt a nazirite</b> who vowed while <b>in the grave,</b> i.e., a cemetery, <b>that he does not require hair removal and the bringing of birds.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues its analysis of this <i>halakha</i>. For one might have explained differently: <b>And are</b> these <b>matters,</b> the ruling that such a nazirite is exempt, <b>not</b> inferred with an <b><i>a fortiori</i> inference</b> to reach the opposite conclusion: <b>Just as a</b> nazirite who was <b>pure</b> from the outset and <b>who</b> subsequently <b>became impure requires hair removal and the bringing of birds,</b> if <b>one was impure from the outset,</b> as he took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery, <b>is it not logical that he should require hair removal and the bringing of birds?</b>
Therefore <b>the verse states: “And he defiles his consecrated head,”</b> indicating that <b>the verse speaks</b> only <b>of one who was a pure</b> nazirite <b>and</b> later <b>became impure,</b> and <b>that</b> only <b>he requires hair removal and the bringing of birds. And</b> the verse serves <b>to exempt the nazirite</b> who vowed while <b>in</b> a place of <b>a grave,</b> who was ritually impure from the outset. The Gemara concludes: <b>Learn from this</b> that a nazirite who was ritually impure from the outset does not have to shave; this answers the question of Rav Ashi.
§ After resolving the question, the Gemara discusses additional <i>halakhot</i> involving a ritually impure person who took a vow of naziriteship. <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught this</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>that the Sages taught</b> in the previous discussion: <b>The difference between an impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship and a pure nazirite who became ritually impure is only</b> the following <i>halakha</i>: <b>That</b> with regard to <b>an impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship, his seventh</b> day of purification <b>counts</b> as part <b>of his tally</b> of the term of naziriteship. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>a pure nazirite who became impure, his seventh</b> day of purification <b>does not count</b> as part <b>of his tally.</b>
<b>Rav Ḥisda said: It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>as Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said: Naziriteship of purity takes effect only from the eighth day.</b> When a nazirite becomes impure during his term, he begins to count his term of observing naziriteship in purity only on the day following his purification. <b>For if you say it</b> follows the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, didn’t he say</b> that the <b>naziriteship of purity takes effect from the seventh</b> day of his purification process?
The Gemara clarifies: <b>What is</b> the statement of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>and what is</b> the statement of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> that Rav Ḥisda referred to? <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to a ritually impure nazirite who underwent the purification rite: <b>“And he shall sanctify his head on that day”</b> (Numbers 6:11). This means that he renews the sanctity of his hair growth, i.e., begins observing his naziriteship in purity, <b>on the day of the bringing of his offerings,</b> the eighth day of his purification. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says:</b> This means that he renews the sanctity of his hair growth <b>on the day of his shaving,</b> the seventh day of his purification.
§ The Gemara cites a related <i>halakha</i>: <b>And that which we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Karetot</i> 9a): If <b>a nazirite became impure</b> by contracting <b>many</b> consecutive <b>impurities, he brings only one</b> set of <b>offerings. Who taught</b> that? <b>Rav Ḥisda said: It is Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, who said</b> the <b>naziriteship of purity takes effect from the seventh</b> day of his purification process. <b>And you find this,</b> that he contracts ritual impurity several times consecutively, in a case <b>where he became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day <b>and,</b> after undergoing the purification process, <b>again became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day. <b>And whose</b> opinion is it? <b>It is</b> that of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda. Since</b> the <b>appropriate time to bring an offering had not</b> yet <b>arrived,</b> as all agree that his offerings are only brought on the eighth day, if he became ritually impure a second time <b>he is obligated</b> to <b>bring only one</b> set of <b>offerings.</b>
<b>For if you say it</b> follows the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, how can this mishna be explained? <b>If</b> it deals with a nazirite <b>who became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day, <b>and,</b> after undergoing the purification process, <b>again became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day, <b>and,</b> after undergoing the purification process, <b>again became ritually impure on the seventh</b> day, Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi would hold that <b>all these</b> impurities <b>are</b> in fact one <b>long ritual impurity,</b> because he never reached the eighth day, which would renew his observance of naziriteship in ritual purity, so the mishna would not refer to this as a case of multiple impurities. <b>And if</b> the mishna is referring to a nazirite <b>who became ritually impure on the eighth</b> day <b>and,</b> after undergoing the purification process, <b>again became ritually impure on the eighth</b> day, the <b>appropriate time to bring an offering had</b> already <b>arrived,</b> and if he becomes ritually impure at that stage, it is not viewed as a continuation of the previous ritual impurity, and he is obligated to bring another set of offerings.
The Gemara clarifies the dispute between Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi and Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda: <b>What is the reason for Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi’s ruling that he begins counting only from the eighth day? The Gemara answers: <b>The verse states:</b> “And the priest shall prepare one for a sin-offering, and the other for a burnt-offering, <b>and make atonement for him, for he sinned by the soul”</b> (Numbers 6:11), <b>and</b> the same verse <b>again</b> states immediately afterward: <b>“And he shall sanctify his head.”</b> This shows that the naziriteship of purity commences only after he has brought his offerings. <b>And Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> says in response: <b>If so,</b> if the verse intended to teach us that his naziriteship starts only on the eighth day, <b>let the verse</b> merely <b>state: “And he shall sanctify his head,”</b> since that phrase addresses the bringing of his offerings, which is on the eighth.

Daf 18b

<b>Why do I</b> need the extra phrase <b>“on that day”</b> (Numbers 6:11)? This teaches that <b>if it does not</b> apply <b>to the matter</b> of the <b>eighth</b> day, since the verse is speaking of that day anyway, <b>apply it to the matter of</b> the <b>seventh</b> day, so that “on that day” means the day when he becomes ritually purified, even before he brings his offerings. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>also, isn’t it written “on that day”?</b> What <i>halakha</i> does he derive from these additional words? The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi could have <b>said to you: That</b> verse <b>comes for this</b> purpose, <b>to tell you</b> that <b>even though he did not</b> yet <b>bring his offerings,</b> he starts counting his naziriteship of purity from the eighth day. This is derived from the verse’s emphasis of: “And he shall sanctify his head on that day,” indicating that it depends on the day itself, rather than the bringing of the offerings.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what forced Rav Ḥisda to establish</b> this mishna <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda? Let him establish it</b> as referring to a case <b>where</b> one <b>became impure by seeing,</b> i.e., contracting, impurity <b>on the night</b> preceding <b>the eighth</b> day each time. Even according to Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, the naziriteship of purity begins at the start of, presumably the night preceding, the eighth day. Therefore, this case will be one of a nazirite contracting ritual impurity many times. Nevertheless, the nazirite will be obligated to bring only one set of offerings for all of them, because each case of ritual impurity occurred at night, when he is unable to bring the offerings for his previous ritual impurity, as offerings can be brought only during the day. <b>And</b> therefore <b>it is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Since he did not establish it in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi in this way, <b>shall we say that</b> Rav Ḥisda <b>holds</b> that <b>night is not</b> considered <b>premature?</b> In other words, although he cannot in practice bring his offerings until the morning, as the Temple service was performed only in the daytime, the night is not considered a premature time, but rather it is viewed as part of the day in which it is appropriate to bring his offerings. Consequently, the <i>halakha</i> of one who becomes ritually impure on the night preceding the eighth day is the same as that of one who contracted ritual impurity on the eighth day itself, and they are both required to bring an additional set of offerings.
<b>Rav Adda bar Ahava said:</b> This conclusion is incorrect, as even if Rav Ḥisda holds that the night is considered to be premature, he could not establish the mishna in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, as the offerings and his new term of naziriteship <b>depend on each other: If you say night is</b> considered <b>premature, when is</b> he <b>fit to</b> bring his <b>offerings?</b> Only <b>in the morning.</b> Accordingly, <b>naziriteship also does not take effect until the morning. And if you say night is not</b> considered <b>premature,</b> and he is already obligated in his offerings at night, in that case <b>naziriteship of purity takes effect from the evening,</b> which means that the ritual impurity he suffered at night renders him obligated to bring an additional set of offerings. Consequently, the mishna does not follow the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, whether night is considered premature or not, and nothing can be inferred from here with regard to Rav Ḥisda’s opinion in that matter.
§ The Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 4:8) concerning <b>the</b> matter of a nazirite who contracted ritual impurity many times <b>itself:</b> If a nazirite became impure, and then <b>became impure on the seventh</b> day of his purification process, <b>and again became impure on the</b> following <b>seventh</b> day of his purification process, <b>he brings only one</b> set of <b>offerings.</b> If <b>he became impure on the eighth</b> day, <b>and again became impure on the</b> following <b>eighth</b> day, <b>he brings</b> a set of <b>offerings for each and every</b> time he became impure. Nevertheless, he <b>begins counting</b> his ritually pure naziriteship <b>immediately</b> on the eighth day, even if he has not yet brought his offerings. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer.</b>
<b>And the Rabbis say:</b> If he had not yet brought his offerings on the eighth day when he became impure, it is considered one long period of ritual impurity, and he brings <b>one</b> set of <b>offerings for all</b> the times he became impure, <b>until he has brought his sin-offering</b> at the end of his period of ritual impurity. It is only then that he can begin counting his next term of naziriteship. Therefore, if he <b>brought his sin-offering and</b> then <b>became impure, and</b> again <b>brought his sin-offering and</b> then again <b>became impure, he brings</b> a set of <b>offerings for each and every one. If he brought his sin-offering and did not</b> yet <b>bring his guilt-offering,</b> he begins to <b>count</b> his term of naziriteship in ritual purity.
<b>Rabbi Yishmael, son of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Beroka, says: Just as</b> not bringing <b>his sin-offering precludes him</b> from starting his naziriteship in ritual purity, <b>so</b> too, not bringing <b>his guilt-offering precludes him</b> from starting his naziriteship in ritual purity, and if he became impure again before he brought his guilt-offering, he only brings one set of offerings for all his impurities.
After having quoted the <i>baraita</i>, the Gemara proceeds to analyze the three opinions: <b>Granted, according to Rabbi Eliezer,</b> his reason is that <b>the verse states: “And he shall sanctify his head on that day”</b> (Numbers 6:11), which indicates: <b>Even though he has not brought his offerings,</b> the eighth day determines the start of his ritually pure naziriteship. <b>And the Rabbis</b> agree that the phrase “on <b>that</b> day” is extra, and it teaches that he begins counting from that day <b>even though he has not brought his guilt-offering,</b> but he does not begin counting until after bringing his sin-offering.
<b>But</b> according to <b>Rabbi Yishmael, why do I</b> need the phrase “on <b>that</b> day”? According to his opinion the issue depends on the offerings, not the day. The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yishmael could have <b>said to you:</b> The phrase “on <b>that</b> day” teaches that he can begin counting <b>even though he has not brought his burnt-offering,</b> as he agrees that not bringing the burnt-offering does not preclude him from counting his naziriteship. <b>And the Rabbis</b> respond to this: <b>A burnt-offering does not require a limitation</b> in the text to teach that its omission does not preclude the start of his naziriteship in ritual purity, since <b>it is merely a gift</b> and not part of his atonement process.
The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason of the Rabbis</b> for ruling that only refraining from bringing his sin-offering precludes the start of his new count of naziriteship? <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to a nazirite who became ritually impure: <b>“And he shall consecrate to the Lord the days of his naziriteship, and he shall bring a lamb in its first year for a guilt-offering”</b> (Numbers 6:12). <b>What does this teach?</b> Doesn’t he wait to begin the days of his naziriteship until after he has brought all of his offerings? Rather, <b>since we have found that all guilt-offerings that are</b> mentioned <b>in the Torah are indispensable</b> for atonement, and before he has brought his guilt-offering he is forbidden from partaking of any sacred offerings, one <b>might</b> have thought that not having brought <b>this</b> guilt-offering <b>also precludes him</b> from counting his ritually pure naziriteship,

Daf 19a

<b>the verse</b> therefore <b>states: “And he shall consecrate</b> to the Lord the days of his naziriteship, <b>and he shall bring</b> a lamb in its first year for a guilt-offering,” indicating: <b>Even though he has not brought</b> his guilt-offering <b>he has</b> nevertheless <b>consecrated</b> his days for the start of a new term of <b>naziriteship.</b> The opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, son of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Beroka,</b> is as follows: <b>The verse states: “And he shall consecrate</b> to the Lord the days of his naziriteship, <b>and he shall bring,”</b> which means: <b>When has he consecrated</b> his days of <b>naziriteship,</b> i.e., when does his new term of naziriteship begin? It begins <b>when he has</b> already <b>brought</b> his guilt-offering.
The Gemara poses a question: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught this that the Sages taught:</b> With regard to <b>a woman who vowed to be a nazirite and became ritually impure,</b> leading her to designate a bird for a sin-offering, a bird for a burnt-offering, and a sheep for a guilt-offering, <b>and afterward her husband nullified</b> her vow of naziriteship <b>for her, she brings the bird sin-offering and she does not bring the bird burnt-offering?</b>
<b>Rav Ḥisda said: It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael,</b> son of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Beroka. According to the opinion of the Rabbis, the burnt-offering is a gift, and she would bring it despite the fact that her naziriteship was nullified. According to Rabbi Yishmael, the burnt-offering is part of the atonement process, and since her naziriteship was nullified, there is no longer a need for atonement.
The Gemara asks: <b>What does he hold? If he holds</b> that the <b>husband uproots</b> a vow entirely when he nullifies it, and she is considered not to have vowed at all, <b>she should not bring the bird sin-offering,</b> as she was never a nazirite, and she does not need atonement. Conversely, <b>if he holds</b> that the <b>husband severs</b> the vow from that point onward, but it did take effect beforehand, <b>she should also bring the bird burnt-offering,</b> as she requires atonement for becoming impure while she was a nazirite. The Gemara answers: <b>Actually, he holds</b> that the <b>husband uproots</b> the vow, <b>and</b> why is she obliged to bring a sin-offering? <b>Rabbi Yishmael holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar HaKappar.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Elazar HaKappar, the esteemed one, says: What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> with regard to a nazirite: <b>“And make atonement for him, for he sinned by the soul”</b> (Numbers 6:11)? <b>And with which soul did this</b> person <b>sin</b> by becoming a nazirite? <b>Rather,</b> in <b>afflicting himself</b> by abstaining <b>from wine,</b> he is considered to have sinned with his own soul, and he must bring a sin-offering for the naziriteship itself, for causing his body to suffer. <b>And an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference can be learned from this: <b>Just as this</b> person, in <b>afflicting himself</b> by abstaining <b>only from wine, is</b> nevertheless <b>called a sinner,</b> in the case of <b>one who afflicts himself</b> by abstaining <b>from everything,</b> through fasting or other acts of mortification, <b>all the more so</b> is he described as a sinner. According to this opinion, Rabbi Yishmael holds that since the woman afflicted herself by abstaining from wine she must bring a sin-offering, even though, due to her husband’s nullification, she did not actually become a nazirite.
The Gemara raises a difficulty with Rabbi Elazar HaKappar’s dictum: <b>But this</b> verse, labeling the nazirite a sinner, <b>is written with regard to an impure nazirite, and we are saying</b> that <b>even a pure nazirite</b> is a sinner. The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Elazar HaKappar holds</b> that <b>a pure nazirite is also a sinner. And this is the reason that</b> the statement that a nazirite is a sinner <b>is written</b> in reference <b>to an impure nazirite</b> rather than a pure one: <b>Since he repeated his sin,</b> as his impurity causes him to start his naziriteship again, he thereby deprives himself for a longer period. He should have taken extra care to prevent this from happening.
§ The mishna taught that if one took a vow of naziriteship while in a cemetery, <b>left</b> the cemetery, <b>and</b> then <b>entered</b> it again, the days he spent outside <b>do count</b> as part <b>of his tally</b> of his term of naziriteship, and he is obligated to bring the offerings of ritual impurity upon reentering the cemetery. The mishna <b>teaches: They do count</b> as part <b>of his tally.</b> The Gemara questions the meaning of this linkage: <b>Does naziriteship take effect for him because he</b> merely <b>left</b> the ritually impure place? He is still ritually impure, and he cannot begin counting his term of naziriteship until after he has undergone the purification process. <b>Shmuel said:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where he left and</b> received the <b>sprinkling</b> of the ashes of the red heifer on the third day, <b>and</b> he <b>again</b> received the sprinkling on the seventh day <b>and immersed,</b> after which he entered the cemetery a second time. Since he is now ritually pure, his naziriteship takes effect.
The Gemara poses another question: According to the precise reading of the mishna, his term of naziriteship starts only if he reentered the cemetery; <b>however,</b> is it only if he returned and <b>entered</b> the cemetery that those days <b>count</b> as part <b>of his tally,</b> but if he did <b>not enter,</b> and remained outside the cemetery, those days <b>do not count</b> as part <b>of his tally?</b> Why should the start of the naziriteship be dependent upon his reentering the cemetery? The Gemara answers: The <i>tanna</i> <b>is speaking</b> utilizing the style of: <b>It is not necessary,</b> as follows: <b>It is not necessary</b> to state this <i>halakha</i>, that those days count as part of his tally, in the case of one who <b>left</b> the cemetery and began his naziriteship, <b>but even if he entered</b> the cemetery again immediately after his purification, those days <b>count</b> as part <b>of his tally,</b> and he will be obligated to bring the offerings of ritual impurity upon his reentry.
<b>Rav Kahana and Rav Asi said to Rav: What is the reason you did not explain</b> it <b>to us with these words</b> of Shmuel, as explained above? <b>He said to them: I said</b> to myself that <b>perhaps you do not require that</b> explanation, as I thought it was apparent that this is the proper explanation of the mishna.
§ The mishna also taught an additional <i>halakha</i>: <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> This <i>halakha</i> does <b>not</b> apply to one who entered the cemetery <b>on that very day</b> that he left it, <b>as it is stated</b> with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of an impure nazirite: <b>“But the first days shall be void”</b> (Numbers 6:12), which indicates that he does not bring the offerings <b>unless he had</b> his <b>“first days”</b> of ritual purity, during which he observed his naziriteship. <b>Ulla said: Rabbi Eliezer said</b> this <i>halakha</i>, that one day of naziriteship in purity is not sufficient to obligate him to bring offerings if he becomes impure, <b>only with regard to an impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship, but a pure nazirite who became impure, even</b> if he was only pure for <b>one day</b> of naziriteship, <b>it negates</b> that day of his tally and he must bring the offerings of an impure nazirite.

Daf 19b

<b>Rava said: What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Eliezer,</b> according to Ulla’s explanation? <b>The verse states:</b> “But the first days shall be void <b>because his consecration was ritually impure”</b> (Numbers 6:12), which he explains as follows: Why are his first days rendered void? They are void <b>because he took a vow of naziriteship,</b> consecrating himself, <b>when</b> he was <b>in</b> a state of <b>ritual impurity.</b>
<b>Abaye raised an objection to</b> Rava from a <i>baraita</i> that is not in accordance with the opinion of Ulla: One who said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one hundred days, and he became ritually impure</b> immediately, <b>at the beginning</b> of the <b>one hundred</b> days, one <b>might</b> have thought <b>it should negate</b> the time he spent as a nazirite. <b>The verse</b> therefore <b>states: “But the first days shall be void”</b> (Numbers 6:12), which indicates that this <i>halakha</i> does not apply <b>until he will have “first days”</b> as a nazirite, <b>and</b> in <b>this</b> case the nazirite <b>does not have</b> his <b>first</b> days completed, as he became ritually impure right away.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If one <b>became ritually impure at the end of one hundred</b> days, that is, on the hundredth day, one <b>might</b> have thought <b>it should negate</b> the days he had counted. <b>The verse</b> therefore <b>states: “But the first days shall be void,”</b> indicating <b>by inference that there are</b> other days that can be called the <b>last</b> ones, <b>while this</b> nazirite <b>does not have last</b> days, as he has already completed the tally of his naziriteship. If <b>he became impure on the one hundredth day less one,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought <b>it should not negate</b> the days he had counted. <b>Therefore, the verse states: “But the first days shall be void,”</b> indicating <b>by inference that there are last</b> ones, <b>and this</b> nazirite <b>has first</b> ones <b>and last</b> ones.
Abaye now concludes his objection to Rava: <b>But</b> with regard to <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i> of the <i>baraita</i>, <b>you cannot say</b> it is referring <b>to an impure</b> person <b>who took a vow of naziriteship from</b> the fact <b>that it teaches: I am hereby a nazirite</b> for <b>one hundred</b> days, <b>and he became impure</b> immediately <b>at the beginning</b> of the <b>one hundred,</b> indicating that it is discussing one who became impure after his term had already started. <b>And it</b> further <b>teaches: Until he will have “first days,”</b> which proves that Rabbi Eliezer states his <i>halakha</i> even with regard to a pure nazirite who later became impure. This is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of Ulla, and his opinion is rejected.
<b>Rav Pappa said to Abaye: Those</b> first <b>days that we said</b> he must observe in ritual purity according to Rabbi Eliezer, does it mean <b>that one</b> day <b>has finished and a second has started,</b> so that if he became impure on the second day it negates his tally, <b>or perhaps</b> it means <b>that two</b> days <b>have finished, and a third has started,</b> which would mean it negates his tally only if he became impure after the beginning of the third day? An answer <b>was not available to him,</b> so Rav Pappa <b>went</b> to <b>ask Rava,</b> who <b>said to him:</b> It <b>is written:</b> “But the former days <b>shall be void [<i>yippelu</i>]”</b> (Numbers 6:12) in the plural, which means at least two days need to have passed.
The Gemara comments: <b>And it was necessary</b> for the verse <b>to write “days” and it was</b> also <b>necessary</b> for it <b>to write “shall be void”</b> in the plural. <b>For if the Merciful One wrote</b> in the Torah only <b>“days” and had not</b> also <b>written “shall be void”</b> in the plural, <b>I would say that</b> the <i>halakha</i> applies only <b>if two</b> days <b>have finished and a third has started. The Merciful One</b> therefore <b>wrote</b> in the Torah the plural form of <b>“shall be void.” And if</b> the Merciful One <b>wrote</b> in the Torah <b>“shall be void” and had not</b> also <b>written “days,” I would say even one</b> day, that is, the <i>halakha</i> applies even if he became ritually impure on the first day. <b>The Merciful One</b> therefore <b>wrote</b> in the Torah <b>“days,”</b> indicating that he must have observed at least part of the second day.
