The Gemara asks: This formulation indicates that this <i>halakha</i> pertains only to a nazirite, <b>but is there no other</b> case where the surplus goes toward the purchase of gift offerings? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>And the rest of those liable to</b> bring <b>nests by Torah law,</b> i.e., those paupers who are obligated to bring only a bird-offering, e.g., a poor leper, who must bring two turtledoves, one for a burnt-offering and one for a sin-offering, instead of a sheep or a ewe,

Daf 26a

<b>who separated money for their nests</b> and then became wealthy, if the owner <b>wishes</b> to change their designation and <b>to bring an animal sin-offering with them, he may bring</b> a sin-offering with them. If he wishes to use them to buy <b>an animal burnt-offering he may bring</b> it, supplementing the required amount with other money. If the owner <b>died and he had unallocated funds,</b> they all <b>will be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings, including the value of the sin-offering. This shows that the <i>halakha</i> that unallocated funds are used for gift offerings applies in cases other than that of a nazirite.
The Gemara answers: <b>He taught</b> the case of <b>a nazirite and</b> also the case of <b>those obligated to</b> bring <b>nests, which is similar to</b> that of a nazirite and is therefore treated identically with regard to its <i>halakha</i>. This serves <b>to exclude that</b> case <b>which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>. The situation discussed in the <i>baraita</i> involves <b>one who was obligated to</b> bring <b>a sin-offering</b> for a transgression he committed, <b>and he</b> also <b>said: It is</b> incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring a gift <b>burnt-offering, and he separated money and said: These are hereby for my obligatory</b> offering. Since he might have meant either his obligation of the sin-offering or his burnt-offering for the new vow, the question arises as to what should be done with the money.
The <i>baraita</i> explains that if he <b>wishes to bring an animal sin-offering with it, he may not bring</b> one; if he wishes to use it to purchase <b>an animal burnt-offering, he may not bring</b> it either. If he <b>died and had unallocated funds,</b> one must <b>take</b> them and cast them into <b>the Dead Sea.</b> Since the two offerings are not part of the same obligation, the unallocated funds may not be used for gift offerings.
§ <b>Rav Ashi said: That</b> which <b>you said</b> with regard to a nazirite who had <b>allocated</b> money, that he may <b>not</b> use it all for gift offerings because the value of the sin-offering must be taken and cast into the Dead Sea, <b>do not say that</b> this is referring only to a case where he explicitly <b>said: These are for my sin-offering, and these are for my burnt-offering, and these are for my peace-offering,</b> each one separately. <b>Rather, even</b> if <b>he said: These are for my sin-offering and for my burnt-offering and for my peace-offering, they are</b> considered <b>allocated</b> for the purposes of this <i>halakha</i>, despite the fact that he did not designate the money for particular offerings.
<b>And some say</b> a different version of this statement. <b>Rav Ashi said: Do not say</b> they are deemed allocated <b>only if he says: These are for my sin-offering and for my burnt-offering and for my peace-offering; rather, even if he said</b> it in broader terms: <b>These are for my obligation, they are</b> considered <b>as allocated.</b>
§ <b>Rava said: That</b> which <b>we said,</b> that if one had <b>unallocated funds</b> they <b>will be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings, applies only if the money for all of the offerings was mixed together. However, <b>if the money for the sin-offering fell</b> and was separated <b>from</b> the others, all the remaining money <b>is</b> now considered <b>as allocated.</b> This means that instead of the entire sum being used for a gift burnt-offering, part of it is used for a peace-offering, which is eaten for one day and does not require bread.

Daf 26b

The Gemara notes that it <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rava:</b> If one said: <b>This</b> money <b>is for my sin-offering and the rest is for the rest of my</b> obligations of <b>naziriteship,</b> and he died, <b>the money for the sin-offering</b> is <b>taken</b> and cast into the <b>Dead Sea, and</b> as for <b>the rest, he brings a burnt-offering with half of it, and half of it</b> goes <b>for a peace-offering. And</b> one who benefits <b>from all of it</b> is liable for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property, due to the value of a burnt-offering that is included in the money. <b>But one is not</b> liable for <b>misuse of</b> consecrated property if he benefits from <b>some</b> of the money, as the money he took is possibly that of the peace-offering, to which the prohibition against misuse does not apply.
If one said: <b>This</b> money <b>is for my burnt-offering and the rest is for the rest of my</b> obligations of <b>naziriteship, the money for the burnt-offering goes for a burnt-offering, and</b> one who benefits <b>from it</b> is liable for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property. <b>And the rest is allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings, as the sum includes the value of a sin-offering. <b>And</b> one who benefits <b>from all of it</b> is liable for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property, due to the value of a sin-offering included in it, <b>but one is not</b> liable for <b>misuse of</b> consecrated property if he benefits from <b>some</b> of the money, as he might have taken the money for a peace-offering, as stated above.
§ <b>Rav Huna said</b> that <b>Rav said: They taught only</b> that there is a difference between unallocated and allocated money of a nazirite with regard to <b>money</b> designated for the purchase of offerings. <b>However,</b> if one designated <b>an animal it is</b> treated <b>as allocated.</b> A nazirite is obligated to bring three types of animals, a female sheep for a sin-offering, a male sheep for a burnt-offering, and a ram in its second year for a peace-offering. It is therefore evident which offering he had in mind when designating a particular animal. Consequently, if the owner died each offering is treated in the appropriate manner: The sin-offering must be left to die, like all sin-offerings whose owners have died; the burnt-offering is sacrificed as a burnt-offering; and the peace-offering is brought as a peace-offering, although it must be eaten in one day and does not require bread.
<b>Rav Naḥman said: When they say</b> that if one designates <b>an animal it is</b> considered <b>as allocated, they taught</b> this <b>only</b> if it is <b>unblemished</b> and is fit to be sacrificed itself. <b>However,</b> if one separated <b>a blemished</b> animal, even if he set aside the three required types, a female sheep, a male sheep, and a ram in its second year, each one <b>is</b> considered <b>as unallocated.</b> This is because one will not sacrifice the animals themselves but will sell them and use the money. <b>However,</b> this is <b>not</b> the case with regard to <b>a bar of silver [<i>naskha</i>].</b> If one separated three silver bars they are considered allocated, as each is a distinct item, designated for a particular offering.
<b>And Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said: Even a silver bar</b> is considered unallocated; <b>however, a pile [<i>sevar</i>] of beams</b> is <b>not.</b> If he set aside three piles of construction beams for his offerings, they are treated as allocated money. <b>Rav Shimi bar Ashi said to Rav Pappa: What is the reasoning of the Rabbis,</b> i.e., Rav, Rav Naḥman, and Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak, <b>who say: Money and not an animal, and not a silver bar;</b> and similarly, <b>money and not a pile?</b> Do they maintain that the <i>halakha</i> of unallocated funds applies only to money and not to other items? <b>However, if that is so,</b> one should likewise say that it applies to <b>money and not birds.</b>
<b>And if you would say: So too,</b> this is in fact the case, and birds cannot be considered allocated, <b>but</b> what about <b>this</b> statement <b>that Rav Ḥisda said: Nests,</b> i.e., a pair of turtle doves or pigeons, one for a burnt-offering and the other for a sin-offering, <b>are</b> considered <b>allocated only by either the acquisition of the owner,</b> if the owner designates each bird for a particular offering upon their purchase, <b>or by the actions of the priest</b> who decides which bird is for which offering when he sacrifices them. This clearly indicates that the birds are considered unallocated beforehand.
Therefore, the question arises: <b>Why</b> is this so? Didn’t <b>we learn this</b> <i>halakha</i> only with regard to <b>money,</b> whereas Rav Ḥisda’s statement indicates that birds are also considered unallocated? If Rav Ḥisda’s opinion is accepted, the same <i>halakhot</i> should also apply to animals, bars, and piles of beams.

Daf 27a

Rav Pappa <b>said to him: And according to your reasoning,</b> which leads to the conclusion that all items can have the status of unallocated funds, consider <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna (45a): <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says:</b> If one <b>brought three</b> different <b>animals</b> for his naziriteship <b>and did not specify</b> which was for which offering, <b>that which is fit for a sin-offering,</b> a ewe, <b>is sacrificed as a sin-offering;</b> the animal suitable <b>for a burnt-offering,</b> a male sheep or bull, <b>is sacrificed as a burnt-offering;</b> and the animal fit <b>for a peace-offering,</b> any other male or female, <b>is sacrificed as a peace-offering.</b> But according to your opinion, <b>why</b> is this so? <b>Didn’t you say</b> that <b>an animal is not considered as allocated,</b> but is instead like unallocated funds; how can one decide which animal to use for each offering?
Rav Shimi bar Ashi <b>said to him:</b> As for the case of birds, this is an exception, as there is an explicit verse to this effect <b>there:</b> It states with regard to the owner of a bird offering: <b>“And he shall take</b> one he-lamb for a guilt-offering to be waved, to make atonement for him, and one tenth part of an ephah of fine flour mingled with oil for a meal-offering, and a <i>log</i> of oil; and two turtledoves or two young pigeons, such as his means suffice for, and the one shall be a sin-offering and the other a burnt-offering” (Leviticus 14:21–22), and it later states with regard to the priest: <b>“And he shall sacrifice</b> one of the turtledoves, or of the young pigeons, such as his means suffice for; even such as his means suffice for, the one for a sin-offering, and the other for a burnt-offering” (Leviticus 14:30–31).
<b>The Merciful One</b> thereby <b>states</b> that the birds are allocated and designated for their offering <b>either by the acquisition of the owner,</b> by designating each bird for a particular offering upon their purchase, <b>or by the action of the priest.</b> With regard to the ruling of Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, it presents no difficulty either, as it is a special case as well. <b>Here too,</b> when one separates these particular animals for his naziriteship offerings,

Daf 27b

<b>how can you say</b> that <b>this</b> one <b>that</b> is <b>for the sin-offering shall be sacrificed</b> as <b>a burnt-offering? Here,</b> only <b>a female</b> may be sacrificed as a sin-offering, whereas <b>here,</b> in the case of a burnt-offering, it must be <b>male.</b> Therefore, it is as though he allocated each animal for a specific offering. Consequently, you cannot compare this case to that of items that are not inherently designated for a particular purpose, e.g., a blemished animal or a silver bar, as they have the status of unallocated funds.
§ <b>Rav Hamnuna raised an objection</b> against Rav Naḥman’s statement: <b>And do we say</b> that <b>a blemished animal is considered like unallocated</b> funds? <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof to the contrary from the following <i>baraita</i>, which begins: <b>How,</b> in what case, did the Sages <b>say</b> that <b>a man shaves,</b> i.e., bring the offerings sacrificed at the end of a term of naziriteship, <b>for his father’s naziriteship?</b> The Gemara cites a part of that <i>baraita</i> which records a case where the son does not bring the offerings for his father’s naziriteship: <b>When he and his father were</b> both <b>nazirites, and his father separated money for</b> the offerings of <b>his naziriteship and died, and</b> the son <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite on the condition that I will shave,</b> i.e., bring my offerings, <b>through the money</b> my <b>father</b> set aside, in that case he cannot shave for his father’s naziriteship.
Consequently, if the father <b>had unallocated funds, they are allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings. If <b>he had allocated animals,</b> that which is for <b>a sin-offering must</b> be left to <b>die,</b> like any sin-offering whose owner has died; the animal for <b>a burnt-offering is sacrificed</b> as <b>a burnt-offering, and</b> the animal for <b>a peace-offering is sacrificed</b> as <b>a peace-offering. What, is it not</b> referring <b>even</b> to the case of <b>a blemished</b> animal, as the <i>baraita</i> differentiated only between money and animals? This indicates that a blemished animal is also considered allocated, which contradicts Rav Naḥman’s ruling.
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>No;</b> it is referring only to <b>an unblemished</b> animal, which is considered allocated because it is suitable for an offering itself. The Gemara asks: <b>However,</b> if that is so, is <b>a blemished</b> animal <b>considered like unallocated</b> funds? In that case, <b>why</b> does the <i>baraita</i> <b>specifically</b> distinguish between animals and unallocated <b>funds? Let</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>say</b> as follows: If he <b>had a blemished animal</b> its money <b>is allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings. The <i>baraita</i> would thereby distinguish between two similar cases that involve animals, from which one could infer the <i>halakha</i> of unallocated funds.
The Gemara answers: <b>So too,</b> this is the meaning of the <i>baraita</i>, and there is no difference between a blemished animal and money. The Gemara clarifies this claim: <b>For what</b> purpose is <b>a blemished</b> animal <b>consecrated?</b> It is <b>for the value</b> of its sale, and this <b>value is</b> provided in <b>money.</b> Consequently, this <i>baraita</i> does not refute Rav Naḥman’s opinion that the <i>halakha</i> of a blemished animal is like that of unallocated funds.
§ <b>Rava</b> also <b>raises an objection</b> against Rav Naḥman’s opinion, from a <i>baraita</i> that deals with sin-offerings. The Torah states: “And he shall bring for <b>his offering”</b> (Leviticus 4:23), which indicates: <b>He fulfills</b> his obligation <b>with his</b> own <b>offering, but he does not fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with his father’s offering,</b> if his father was obligated to bring a sin-offering and died after separating an animal for this purpose. One <b>might</b> have thought that one <b>does not fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with an offering that his father separated</b> only in a case where the son’s transgression is not equal in severity to that of his father’s sin, e.g., sacrificing his offering <b>from</b> animals that his father designated to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression to atone <b>for</b> his own <b>major</b> transgression, <b>or from</b> animals that his father designated to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression, to atone <b>for</b> his own <b>minor</b> transgression.
<b>However,</b> one might have thought that <b>he does fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with an offering that his father separated</b> if it is <b>from</b> animals his father set aside to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression and the son atones <b>for</b> his own <b>minor</b> one, <b>or from</b> animals the father set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression and the son atones <b>for</b> his own <b>major</b> one. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “His offering”</b> (Leviticus 4:23), and it repeats: <b>“His offering”</b> (Leviticus 4:28), to emphasize that <b>he fulfills</b> his obligation <b>with his</b> own <b>offering, and he does not fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with his father’s offering</b> even for similar transgressions.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: One <b>might</b> have thought that a son <b>does not fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with his father’s offering,</b> i.e., <b>with an animal that</b> his father <b>separated, even from</b> animals that his father designated to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression, to atone <b>for</b> the son’s <b>minor</b> one, <b>or from</b> animals the father set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression, to atone <b>for</b> the son’s <b>major</b> one, as stated above, <b>as a person cannot shave,</b> i.e., bring an offering, <b>for naziriteship with his father’s animal.</b> Consequently, the <i>halakhot</i> of inheritance do not apply to this animal. <b>However,</b> it is nevertheless possible that a son can <b>fulfill</b> his obligation by purchasing a sin-offering <b>with money that his father separated</b> for his own sin-offering, <b>even from</b> money the father set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression in a case where the son is atoning <b>for a minor</b> transgression, <b>or from</b> money the father set aside to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression in a case where the son is atoning <b>for a major</b> one, <b>as a person can shave,</b> i.e., purchase an offering, <b>with the money his father</b> set aside <b>for naziriteship,</b>

Daf 28a

<b>when they are unallocated and not when they are allocated.</b> Consequently, the same should apply to one whose father separated money for a regular sin-offering, i.e., he should be able to use them for his own sin-offering. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “His offering”</b> (Leviticus 4:32), which serves to emphasize: <b>He fulfills</b> his obligation <b>with his</b> own <b>offering, but he does not fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with</b> the money separated for <b>his father’s offering.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues this line of argument: One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>he does not fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with the money that</b> his father <b>separated</b> if it is <b>from</b> money the father set aside to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression and the son atones <b>for a minor</b> one, <b>or from</b> money the father set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression and the son atones <b>for a major</b> one. <b>However,</b> the son may <b>fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with the offering he separated for himself. From</b> animals he set aside to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression, he may atone <b>for a major</b> one, <b>or from</b> animals he set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression he may atone <b>for a minor</b> one. Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “Then he shall bring for <b>his offering</b> a goat, a female without blemish, <b>for his sin”</b> (Leviticus 4:28), which indicates that he does not fulfill his obligation <b>unless his offering is for the sake of his</b> particular <b>sin,</b> not for some other transgression.
One <b>might</b> have thought that he <b>does not fulfill</b> his obligation <b>with the animal he separated for himself, from</b> animals he set aside to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression to atone <b>for a minor</b> one; <b>or from</b> animals he set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression, to atone <b>for a major</b> one; or <b>even</b> when <b>from</b> animals he set aside to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression to atone <b>for a major</b> one; <b>or from</b> animals he set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression to atone <b>for a minor</b> one.
The reason is <b>that if he separated an animal</b> to bring as an offering <b>for</b> unintentionally eating forbidden <b>fat and he</b> instead <b>brought</b> that sin-offering <b>for</b> unintentionally consuming <b>blood, or</b> if he separated an animal to bring as an offering to atone <b>for blood and</b> instead <b>brought</b> it <b>for</b> eating forbidden <b>fat,</b> in that case he <b>has not misused</b> consecrated property, as the animal cannot lose its consecrated status. <b>And</b> just as it cannot lose its consecrated status, so too it cannot be redesignated to atone for a different sin, and therefore this animal also <b>does not atone</b> for him.
<b>However,</b> one might think that he <b>fulfills</b> his obligation <b>with the money he separated for himself, from</b> money he set aside to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression <b>to</b> atone for a different <b>minor</b> transgression; <b>or from</b> money he set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression <b>to</b> atone for a different <b>major</b> transgression; or <b>from</b> money he set aside to atone for <b>a major</b> transgression <b>to</b> atone for <b>a minor one; or from</b> money he set aside to atone for <b>a minor</b> transgression <b>to</b> atone for <b>a major</b> one.
The reason is <b>that if he separated money for himself</b> to purchase a sin-offering to atone <b>for</b> unintentionally eating forbidden <b>fat and he</b> instead <b>brought</b> a sin-offering with that money <b>for</b> unintentionally consuming <b>blood, or</b> if he separated money to purchase a sin-offering to atone <b>for</b> consuming <b>blood and</b> with that money he instead <b>brought</b> a sin-offering <b>for</b> unintentionally eating forbidden <b>fat,</b> in that case he <b>has misused</b> consecrated property if he uses that money for a non-sacred purpose, as money can lose its sacred status when misappropriated. <b>And</b> just as it can lose its consecrated status, it can be redesignated to atone for a different sin; therefore this money <b>atones</b> for him if it was used to purchase an offering for a different transgression.
Therefore, <b>the verse states: “For his sin”</b> (Leviticus 4:35), which indicates that he does not fulfill his obligation <b>unless his offering is for the sake of his</b> particular <b>sin,</b> and he is not permitted to use money he consecrated for one type of sin to atone for a different sin. This concludes the <i>baraita</i>.
Rava now asks his question: <b>In any event,</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> that a son may not use <b>an animal</b> separated by his father for his naziriteship offering. <b>What, is it not</b> referring <b>even</b> to <b>a blemished</b> animal? The fact that the <i>baraita</i> does not differentiate between types of animals indicates that a blemished animal has the status of allocated funds, as opposed to Rav Naḥman’s ruling that this animal is like his father’s unallocated funds, which the son himself may use. The Gemara rejects this: <b>No;</b> the <i>baraita</i> refers solely to <b>an unblemished</b> animal that is fit to be sacrificed; only an animal of this kind is considered allocated.
The Gemara asks: <b>However,</b> according to this explanation, <b>what</b> is the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a blemished</b> animal? Is it <b>considered like an unallocated</b> animal? If so, <b>why</b> does the <i>baraita</i> <b>specifically teach</b> that one may purchase an offering with the unallocated <b>funds that his father separated? Let</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teach</b> this <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>a blemished</b> animal, and one would infer that the same applies to money. The Gemara answers as above: <b>So too,</b> this is the case; there is no difference between the two. The reason is <b>that what is</b> a blemished animal <b>fit for?</b> It is fit <b>for</b> its <b>value,</b> and this <b>value is</b> essentially <b>money.</b> Consequently, this <i>baraita</i> does not contradict Rav Naḥman’s opinion that a blemished animal has the status of unallocated funds.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The previous mishna discussed the case of a husband who nullified his wife’s vow after she separated her offerings of naziriteship. This mishna deals with a husband who nullified his wife’s naziriteship after she had completed her term and brought her offerings to the Temple. If <b>the blood</b> from <b>one of</b> her naziriteship offerings <b>was sprinkled</b> on the altar <b>on her behalf,</b> the husband <b>cannot nullify</b> her vow at this point. <b>Rabbi Akiva says: Even</b> before the sprinkling of the blood, <b>he cannot nullify</b> the vow as soon as <b>any one of the animals</b> for her offerings <b>has been slaughtered on her behalf.</b>
The mishna continues: <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement,</b> that he can no longer nullify the vow, <b>said?</b> It is when she is bringing the offerings <b>for</b> her <b>shaving of ritual purity,</b> when she has completed her term of naziriteship without becoming ritually impure (see Numbers 6:18). <b>However,</b> if she is sacrificing the offerings <b>for</b> her <b>shaving of impurity,</b> when she became ritually impure during her term of naziriteship, after which she restarts her naziriteship (see Numbers 6:9), her husband <b>can nullify</b> her vow. The reason is <b>that he can say: I do not want a downcast [<i>menuvvelet</i>] wife,</b> who does not drink wine. She would have to refrain from wine for a lengthy period if she were to begin her naziriteship anew. <b>Rabbi Meir says: He can nullify</b> her vow <b>even</b> at the stage <b>of</b> her <b>shaving of purity,</b> after she has begun sacrificing her offerings, <b>as he can say: I do not want a shaven wife,</b> and a nazirite is obligated to shave after bringing his or her offerings.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna,</b> which rules that a husband cannot nullify his wife’s naziriteship after the blood of her offering has been sprinkled at the end of the naziriteship term, <b>is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer. As, if</b> it followed the ruling of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, didn’t he say</b> that <b>shaving is indispensable</b> for the end of a nazirite’s term, i.e., a nazirite at the end of his naziriteship is prohibited from drinking wine and becoming impure from the dead until he actually shaves? <b>And</b> in this case, <b>since she has not</b> yet <b>shaved, she</b> remains <b>prohibited from</b> drinking <b>wine. And since she becomes downcast</b> through her abstinence from wine, evidently the husband <b>can nullify</b> her vow even after the sprinkling of the blood of her offerings of purity.

Daf 28b

<b>The <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna <b>holds: Once</b> the <b>blood has been sprinkled on her behalf</b> she is <b>immediately permitted</b> to drink <b>wine, and</b> therefore <b>she is not downcast.</b> Consequently, the husband has no right to nullify her naziriteship vow at that point, as her vow does not affect him. <b>And Rabbi Akiva holds: Even</b> if the blood has yet to be sprinkled and wine remains forbidden to her, as soon as <b>an animal is slaughtered</b> for one of her offerings the husband <b>can no</b> longer <b>nullify</b> the vow, <b>due to the loss of consecrated</b> property. If he were to nullify her vow she would have no further need of the offerings, and it is prohibited to waste Sanctuary property.
<b>Rabbi Zeira objects to this: And why</b> should the result be a loss to the Sanctuary? He can avoid this by <b>sprinkling their blood not for the sake</b> of the offerings of a nazirite, <b>and he will</b> thereby <b>permit the meat</b> of the offering to <b>be eaten,</b> and the consecrated animal will not go to waste. <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to the communal peace-offering of two <b>sheep</b> that accompanies the two loaves on <b><i>Shavuot</i>, if one slaughtered them not for the sake</b> of that offering, <b>or slaughtered them before their time,</b> on the eve of the Festival, <b>or after their time,</b> after the Festival, <b>the blood shall be sprinkled,</b> although not for the sake of that offering, as it is no longer fit for that purpose, <b>and the meat is eaten.</b>
<b>And if it was a Shabbat,</b> the blood <b>may not be sprinkled.</b> Since the meat cannot be eaten on that day, sprinkling the blood is considered a form of unnecessary labor on Shabbat. <b>And if he sprinkled</b> the blood on Shabbat anyway, the offering <b>is accepted,</b> and he must wait <b>to burn</b> its sacrificial <b>parts</b> on the altar <b>in the evening,</b> after the conclusion of Shabbat. In any case, this shows that it is permitted to sprinkle the blood of an offering not for its own sake <i>ab initio</i> so that its flesh can be eaten.
The Sages <b>say</b> in response: <b>If he had slaughtered</b> only the woman’s naziriteship offerings of the <b>burnt-offering or</b> the <b>peace-offering, so too,</b> he may certainly proceed to sprinkle the blood not for the sake of that offering, to avoid the loss of a consecrated animal. In that case Rabbi Akiva would agree that the husband can still nullify her vow. <b>However, with what are we dealing here?</b> It is with a case <b>where he slaughtered the sin-offering first.</b> Since a sin-offering whose blood was sprinkled not for its sake is invalid, if the husband were to nullify her vow this would cause a loss of consecrated property.
The Gemara cites the source that the order of a nazirite’s offerings may be changed and the sin-offering may be sacrificed first. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (45a): <b>If he shaved</b> after the sacrifice <b>of one of the three</b> nazirite offerings, either the burnt-offering, the peace-offering, or the sin-offering, <b>he has fulfilled</b> his obligation.
§ The mishna taught: <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement,</b> that a husband cannot nullify his wife’s vow, <b>said?</b> It is <b>with regard to a shaving of ritual purity; however, with regard to a shaving of impurity</b> the husband can <b>nullify</b> it if he wishes. <b>And Rabbi Meir says: He may even nullify</b> the vow <b>at</b> her <b>shaving of purity because he can say: I do not want a shaven wife.</b>
The Gemara analyzes these opinions: <b>And the first <i>tanna</i></b> could have <b>said to you</b> in response to Rabbi Meir’s argument: It is <b>possible</b> for her to compensate <b>by</b> wearing <b>a wig,</b> and therefore she would not appear shaven, and her husband would have no cause for complaint. <b>And Rabbi Meir holds:</b> As for compensating <b>by</b> wearing <b>a wig, since</b> it is <b>dirty he is not amenable</b> to this solution, and he may therefore nullify her vow.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A man can vow</b> that <b>his</b> minor <b>son should be a nazirite,</b> i.e., a father can declare his son a nazirite, <b>but a woman cannot vow</b> that <b>her son should be a nazirite. How so;</b> what are the details of this naziriteship? If the son <b>shaved</b> his hair, thereby demonstrating his rejection of the vow imposed by his father; <b>or</b> if <b>his relatives shaved him;</b> or if the son <b>objected</b> by saying that he has no desire for this naziriteship; <b>or if his relatives objected</b> on his behalf, the naziriteship is canceled.
If this son who canceled the naziriteship <b>had animals separated</b> for his offerings, the one set aside for <b>the sin-offering must die, and the burnt-offering is sacrificed as a burnt-offering, and</b> the <b>peace-offering is sacrificed as a peace-offering. And</b> the peace-offering <b>is eaten for one day,</b> like the peace-offering of a nazirite, rather than the two days of a regular peace-offering, <b>and it does not require bread,</b> i.e., the loaves that accompany a nazirite’s peace-offering.
If <b>he had unallocated funds,</b> they <b>will be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings. If he had <b>allocated funds</b> for his offerings, <b>the money for the sin-offering is taken</b> and cast <b>into the Dead Sea,</b> as one may <b>not benefit</b> from it <i>ab initio</i>, <b>but</b> if he benefits <b>from it,</b> he is <b>not</b> liable to bring an offering for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property. With <b>the money for</b> the <b>burnt-offering they bring a burnt-offering;</b> it is prohibited to derive benefit from those coins <b>and</b> if he benefits <b>from it,</b> he is liable to bring an offering for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property. With <b>the money for</b> the <b>peace-offering they bring a peace-offering, and</b> it <b>is eaten for one day and does not require bread.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that <b>a man</b> can vow that his son should be a nazirite, <b>but a woman cannot</b> do so. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for this? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: It is a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>with regard to a nazirite. And Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b>

Daf 29a

said that <b>Reish Lakish said:</b> The purpose is <b>in order to educate him in mitzvot,</b> to teach him how to observe the mitzva of naziriteship. The Gemara asks: <b>If so, even a woman as well</b> should be able to impose naziriteship on her son for educational purposes. The Gemara answers: Reish Lakish <b>holds</b> that <b>a man is obligated to educate his son in mitzvot, but a woman is not obligated to educate her son</b> in mitzvot.
The Gemara asks: <b>Granted, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> who <b>said it is a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>with regard to a nazirite, for that reason</b> one can understand the ruling of the mishna, which indicates that for <b>his son, yes,</b> a father can vow that he should be a nazirite, but with regard to <b>his daughter, no,</b> he cannot do so, as a <i>halakha</i> learned by tradition cannot be questioned. <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish,</b> a father should <b>even</b> be able to impose naziriteship upon <b>his daughter</b> for the sake of her education. Why does the mishna specify a son? The Gemara answers that Reish Lakish <b>holds:</b> A father <b>is obligated to educate his son,</b> whereas <b>he is not obligated to educate his daughter,</b> and for this reason he cannot vow that she should be a nazirite.
The Gemara asks another question: <b>Granted, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> who <b>said</b> that <b>it is a <i>halakha</i> with regard to a nazirite, this is why</b> the mishna indicates that <b>with regard to naziriteship, yes,</b> a father can impose a vow upon his son, but <b>with regard to</b> other <b>vows, no,</b> he cannot do so. <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish,</b> who says it is for the son’s education, a father should <b>even</b> be able to impose regular <b>vows</b> upon him <b>as well.</b> Why does the mishna specify naziriteship?
The Gemara answers: According to Reish Lakish the <i>tanna</i> of this mishna <b>is speaking</b> utilizing the style of: <b>It is not necessary,</b> as follows: <b>It is not necessary</b> to ask whether a father can impose regular <b>vows</b> of a mitzva <b>where there is no deprivation</b> of the son when he fulfills his father’s vow, <b>but even</b> with regard to <b>naziriteship, where there is deprivation</b> of his son, as the son must refrain from wine and shaving, <b>even so</b> the father <b>is obligated to educate him,</b> and therefore he can vow in this manner too.
The Gemara asks another question: <b>Granted, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said it is a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>with regard to a nazirite, this</b> explanation <b>is</b> consistent with <b>that which</b> the mishna <b>teaches:</b> If <b>he objected, or his relatives objected for him,</b> the naziriteship is canceled, as the transmitted <i>halakha</i> may be that the acquiescence of the relatives is necessary.
The Gemara continues its question: <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> who said that <b>Reish Lakish said</b> that this vow serves educational purposes, <b>is this</b> really <b>in the power of the relatives to say to</b> the father: <b>Do not teach him mitzvot?</b> The Gemara answers: Reish Lakish <b>holds</b> that with regard to <b>any education</b> that is <b>not important,</b> e.g., the optional mitzva of naziriteship, the son <b>is not amenable</b> to the suffering he must endure for this purpose, and therefore he or his relatives can object.
The Gemara raises a further difficulty: <b>Granted, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said it is a <i>halakha</i> with regard to a nazirite,</b> it is <b>due to that</b> reason that the son must <b>shave</b> all of his hair at the conclusion of his naziriteship, despite the fact that <b>he</b> thereby <b>performs the rounding</b> of the corners of his head, in violation of the prohibition: “You shall not round the corners of your heads” (Leviticus 19:27). Since a <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai has the status of Torah law, a minor nazirite shaves despite this prohibition.
The Gemara continues its question: <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> who said that <b>Reish Lakish said</b> that the reason the vow of naziriteship takes effect is <b>in order to educate him in mitzvot,</b> and it applies only by rabbinic law, how does he explain the fact that this nazirite <b>performs the rounding</b> of the head? How does a naziriteship, which is by rabbinic law, override a Torah prohibition?
The Gemara answers: Reish Lakish <b>holds</b> that <b>the rounding of the entire head</b> is prohibited only <b>by rabbinic law,</b> as the Torah itself prohibited shaving only the corners of the head, <b>and the mitzva of education</b> also applies <b>by rabbinic law. And</b> therefore the mitzva of <b>education,</b> which is <b>by rabbinic law, comes and overrides</b> the prohibition against <b>rounding</b> the head, which likewise applies <b>by rabbinic law.</b>
The Gemara continues to ask along the same lines: <b>Granted, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said it is a <i>halakha</i> with regard to a nazirite, this is why</b> when he <b>shaves</b> at the conclusion of his naziriteship <b>he brings an offering,</b> as the <i>halakha</i> is that the minor is a nazirite in all regards, which means his offering is obligatory, like that of an adult nazirite.
The Gemara continues its question: <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> who said that <b>Reish Lakish said</b> that the reason is <b>in order to educate him in mitzvot,</b> the minor’s offering is not a Torah obligation, which means <b>that he brings non-sacred</b> animals <b>into the Temple courtyard</b> for slaughter. The Gemara answers: Reish Lakish <b>holds</b> that the prohibition against slaughtering <b>non-sacred</b> animals <b>in the Temple courtyard</b> does <b>not</b> apply <b>by Torah law</b> but by rabbinic law, and therefore this prohibition is disregarded due to the importance of the son’s education.
The Gemara asks yet another question: <b>Granted, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said it is a <i>halakha</i> with regard to a nazirite, this is why when</b> the son <b>becomes ritually impure he brings an offering of birds, and</b> the <b>priest</b> may <b>eat</b> the bird sin-offering that was killed by means of <b>pinching,</b> nipping the neck of the bird, rather than by regular slaughter. This permits a bird to be eaten only in the case of a proper bird offering.
The Gemara continues its question: <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> who said that <b>Reish Lakish said</b> that the reason the vow takes effect is to educate the son in mitzvot, in this case the priest <b>eats</b> an unslaughtered <b>animal carcass.</b> If the son is not obligated to bring the offering, the priest will be eating a bird that was killed in a manner that does not render it fit for consumption, which means it has the status of an unslaughtered carcass.
The Gemara answers: Reish Lakish <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who holds <b>that slaughter of a bird is not</b> obligatory <b>by Torah law.</b> Rather, by Torah law birds are fit to be eaten no matter how they are killed, and it was the Sages who decreed that they must be slaughtered. <b>And</b> he also maintains that the prohibition against bringing <b>non-sacred</b> animals for slaughter <b>in the Temple courtyard</b> does <b>not</b> apply <b>by Torah law.</b> Consequently the rabbinic mitzva of education overrides these prohibitions, as they too are rabbinic.
The Gemara asks: <b>And does Rabbi Yosei hold this</b> opinion? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says: From where</b> is it derived <b>that the bird sin-offering that comes due to uncertainty is not eaten? The verse states: “And they who have an issue, whether a male or a female”</b> (Leviticus 15:33), which <b>juxtaposes a female to a male: Just as a male brings an offering for a definite</b> sin, i.e., for a transgression he is sure he committed inadvertently, <b>so too, a female brings an offering for a definite</b> sin. <b>And just as a male brings</b> a provisional guilt-offering <b>for</b> his <b>uncertain</b> transgression, <b>so too, a female brings</b> an offering <b>for</b> her case of <b>uncertainty.</b>
<b>And</b> furthermore: <b>Just as a male brings</b> an animal <b>for</b> his <b>uncertain</b> transgression, a ram as a guilt-offering, <b>from</b> the same <b>type from</b> which <b>he brings a definite</b> sin-offering, an animal sin-offering, <b>so too, a female brings</b> the same type <b>for her</b> case of <b>uncertainty from</b> the same <b>type from</b> which <b>she brings for a definite</b> offering, i.e., a bird if she is a <i>zava</i> or gave birth. This leads to the following question: <b>If</b> so, one can continue this line of thought: <b>Just as a male brings an offering and</b> it <b>is eaten, so too, a female brings an offering and</b> it should be <b>eaten</b> when she sacrifices an offering for uncertain childbirth. With regard to this case <b>you say:</b>

Daf 29b

<b>No,</b> you cannot make this claim, because <b>if you say</b> that this is true <b>with regard to a male, when</b> bringing his offering involves only <b>one</b> uncertain <b>prohibition,</b> i.e., that he might be bringing non-sacred animals into the Temple courtyard, <b>shall you</b> also <b>say</b> that this is the case <b>with regard to a female, whose</b> case includes <b>two prohibitions?</b> This concludes the <i>baraita</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> are these <b>two prohibitions?</b> Are they <b>not the prohibition</b> against consuming an unslaughtered <b>animal carcass</b> by eating a bird killed by pinching, <b>and</b> the prohibition against bringing <b>non-sacred</b> animals <b>into the</b> Temple <b>courtyard?</b> This shows that the prohibition against bringing non-sacred animals into the Temple courtyard applies by Torah law according to the opinion of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda. <b>Rav Aḥa, son</b> of <b>Rav Ika, objects to this: And perhaps</b> one is not liable for these prohibitions by Torah law, rather, the <i>baraita</i> means that one <b>is liable for this because it appears like two</b> Torah <b>prohibitions,</b> although they actually apply only <b>by rabbinic law.</b>
§ The Gemara returns to the question of whether the ruling that a man can impose naziriteship on his son is a <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai or whether it is part of the boy’s education. <b>Let us say</b> that this is <b>parallel to</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>.</b> As it is taught: <b>Until when can</b> a father <b>vow</b> that <b>his son</b> should <b>be a nazirite?</b> It is <b>until</b> the son <b>develops two</b> pubic <b>hairs;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says:</b> It is <b>until he reaches the age of vows,</b> usually approximately a year earlier, when he demonstrates an understanding of the nature of vows. If he vows at that age and comprehends the significance of his utterance, his vow is valid.
<b>What, is it not</b> the case that this <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>: As Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds</b> that <b>it is a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>with regard to a nazirite, and</b> therefore <b>even though</b> the son has already <b>reached the age of vows</b> and is capable of making his own decision with regard to the vow, this <i>halakha</i> states that the father <b>continues to vow for him until he develops two</b> pubic <b>hairs,</b> the sign of maturity. <b>And Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, who says</b> that the father can vow for the son only <b>until he reaches the age of vows, holds</b> that he imposes a vow on his son <b>in order to educate him in mitzvot, and since</b> the son <b>has left</b> the father’s <b>supervision</b> to the degree that he can utter his own vows, the father <b>is no longer obligated</b> to educate him in this area of <i>halakha</i>.
The Gemara rejects this argument. The Sages <b>say</b> in response: <b>No;</b> this is not the correct explanation of that dispute, as one can say <b>that everyone,</b> both Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi and Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, agrees with Rabbi Yoḥanan that <b>it is a <i>halakha</i> with regard to a nazirite, and here they disagree with regard to</b> the issue of <b>a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority.</b> In other words, they dispute the case of a child who utters a vow when he is almost mature, on the verge of puberty. It is an accepted <i>halakha</i> that the vow of this child is valid if he understands its meaning. The dispute concerns the source of this <i>halakha</i>.
<b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds</b> that the status of <b>a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority</b> applies <b>by rabbinic law, and</b> therefore the <i>halakha</i> <b>by Torah law</b> that a father can vow on behalf of his son <b>comes and overrides</b> the decree that one who is near the age of maturity is considered an independent adult with regard to vows, which is <b>by rabbinic law. And Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, holds</b> that the status of <b>a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority</b> applies <b>by Torah law.</b> Consequently, the <i>halakha</i> that a father can vow on behalf of his son does not apply to this case.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead <b>that everyone</b> agrees that a father vows for his son <b>in order to educate him in mitzvot,</b> which means it is by rabbinic law, <b>and</b> likewise the status of <b>a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority is</b> also <b>by rabbinic law.</b> In that case, the <i>tanna’im</i> dispute the following: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds</b> that the mitzva of <b>education,</b> which applies <b>by rabbinic law, comes and overrides</b> the status of <b>a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority,</b> which is also <b>by rabbinic law.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, who said</b> that a father can vow for his son only <b>until he reaches the age of vows, holds</b> that the mitzva of <b>education by rabbinic law does not come and override</b> the status of <b>a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority.</b> Consequently, there is no necessary connection between this dispute of <i>tanna’im</i> and the argument between Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish as to whether the ruling that a father can impose naziriteship on his son is a <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai or an educational tool.
With regard to the dispute concerning the time period for a father’s vow on behalf of his son, the Gemara suggests: <b>Shall we say</b> that the dispute between <b>these <i>tanna’im</i> is like</b> the dispute between <b>those</b> other <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Nidda</i> 5:9): <b>An incident</b> occurred <b>involving Rabbi Ḥanina</b> in <b>which his father vowed</b> that he should <b>be a nazirite</b> when he was a minor, <b>and they brought him before Rabban Gamliel, and Rabban Gamliel examined him to discern if he had</b> already <b>developed two</b> pubic <b>hairs,</b> in which case the vow would not take effect, or <b>if he had not developed</b> them, which would mean that the vow was valid.
<b>Rabbi Yosei says</b> that Rabban Gamliel examined him <b>to discern whether he had reached the age of vows or not,</b> which he maintains is the decisive factor. The child <b>said to</b> Rabban Gamliel: <b>My teacher, do not go to the trouble of examining me,</b> since <b>if I am a minor I shall be</b> a nazirite <b>due to</b> my <b>father’s</b> vow, and <b>if I am an adult, I shall be</b> a nazirite <b>due to my own</b> vow. <b>Rabban Gamliel arose and kissed</b> the child <b>on his head. He said: I am certain of this</b> child <b>that</b> he will eventually become <b>an authority of <i>halakha</i> for the Jewish people. They said:</b> In fact, <b>it was only a few years</b> later <b>that</b> the child <b>issued rulings for the Jewish people.</b>
The Gemara explains its suggestion: <b>Granted, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, who said</b> that a father can vow on behalf of his son only <b>until he reaches the age of vows, this is</b> why the child <b>said: If I am a minor I shall be</b> a nazirite <b>due to</b> my <b>father,</b> as he means: If I have not yet reached the age of vows. <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>who said</b> that a father can vow naziriteship for his son <b>until he develops two</b> pubic <b>hairs,</b> despite the fact that he is already mature with regard to vows, what is the meaning of: <b>And if I am an adult, I shall be</b> a nazirite <b>due to my own vow?</b>

Daf 30a

After all, he still <b>remains under his father’s authority</b> with regard to naziriteship, as he has yet to develop two pubic hairs. <b>Rather,</b> one must explain <b>that he said</b> the following: <b>I shall be</b> a nazirite <b>due to</b> my <b>father; I shall be</b> a nazirite <b>due to my own</b> vow. In other words, he did not link his statement to the question of whether he was old enough to vow but to the issue of his father’s authority with regard to naziriteship, i.e., whether he had developed two hairs.
The Gemara asks: According to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, <b>if he had developed two</b> pubic <b>hairs from the outset,</b> i.e., before his father vowed, he would <b>stand</b> bound <b>by his own naziriteship, and</b> if he reached physical maturity <b>at the end,</b> i.e., after the vow ended, he would <b>stand</b> bound <b>by his father’s</b> vow of <b>naziriteship. But if he developed</b> two hairs <b>in the middle</b> of his naziriteship term, of <b>what</b> use would the son’s vow be?
The Gemara clarifies: <b>This works out well according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, who said</b> that a father can vow on behalf of his son <b>until he reaches the age of vows.</b> The reason is that this stage is by rabbinic law, and therefore the fact that the son has reached this stage does not automatically cancel the father’s vow. The son would complete his term and bring his offerings.
<b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>who said</b> that a father can vow for his son only <b>until he develops two</b> pubic <b>hairs, what can be said?</b> If he grew two hairs during his father’s naziriteship it is no longer in effect, as by Torah law he is no longer under his father’s authority, so what is the <i>halakha</i> in that case? The Sages <b>say</b> in response: According <b>to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, this child <b>has no rectification until he sits for his</b> term of naziriteship <b>and</b> also <b>sits for</b> the naziriteship <b>of his father,</b> i.e., he must be a nazirite for sixty days, to ensure that he completes a full term of thirty days, either for his own naziriteship or for that of his father.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A man can shave,</b> i.e., bring the offerings at the close of his term of naziriteship, <b>by</b> using offerings originally designated for <b>his father’s naziriteship, but a woman cannot shave by</b> means of the offerings for <b>her father’s naziriteship. How so;</b> how is this <i>halakha</i> applied? It applies to <b>one whose father was a nazirite and separated unallocated money for his naziriteship,</b> i.e., he did not state which coins were for which of his offerings, <b>and he died</b> before buying the animals, <b>and</b> the son <b>said</b> after his father’s death: <b>I am hereby a nazirite on the condition that I will shave by</b> means of <b>the money</b> that my <b>father</b> set aside.
<b>Rabbi Yosei said:</b> In that case <b>these</b> coins are <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings, and the son may not use them, as <b>this is not</b> the case of the <i>halakha</i> that a son <b>can shave by</b> using <b>his father’s naziriteship.</b> Rather, <b>who is</b> the son <b>who can shave by</b> using <b>his father’s naziriteship?</b> This is referring to a son <b>and his father who were</b> both <b>nazirites</b> during his father’s lifetime, <b>and his father separated unallocated money for his naziriteship and died; this is the one who may shave by</b> using <b>his father’s naziriteship.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for this difference between a man and a woman? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: It is a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>with regard to a nazirite.</b> The Gemara asks: It is <b>obvious</b> that this is so, even without this <i>halakha</i>. <b>What</b> is the purpose <b>of stating</b> this? Is Rabbi Yoḥanan coming to say <b>that a son inherits</b> from <b>his father</b> whereas <b>a daughter</b> does <b>not,</b> and therefore only a son who inherits from his father can use his animals, but not a daughter? This is obvious, as it is stated in the Torah that a daughter does not inherit from her father if he has a son (see Numbers 27:8).
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> this <i>halakha</i> is <b>necessary</b> in a case <b>when he has only a daughter,</b> who does inherit from him. <b>Lest you say</b> that <b>we learned</b> this <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>heirs,</b> i.e., that the <i>halakha</i> is that all heirs, including a daughter, can shave by means of their father’s offerings,

Daf 30b

the mishna therefore <b>teaches us</b> that it is <b>a <i>halakha</i></b> that is unrelated to inheritance; there is simply a tradition that a son can use the offerings for his father’s naziriteship while a daughter cannot.
§ <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Do <b>the Rabbis disagree with Rabbi Yosei, or do</b> they <b>not disagree? If you say</b> they <b>disagree,</b> do they take issue <b>with the first clause or with the latter clause</b> of his <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara suggests an answer: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>How did</b> the Sages <b>say</b> that <b>a man can shave by</b> using <b>his father’s naziriteship?</b>
With regard to <b>one whose father was a nazirite and his father separated money for his naziriteship and died, and</b> the son <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite on the condition that I can shave by</b> using my <b>father’s money, this is the one who can shave by</b> means of <b>his father’s money. However,</b> if a son <b>and his father were</b> both <b>nazirites, and his father separated money for his</b> own <b>naziriteship and died, this</b> money is <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yosei.</b>
<b>Rabbi Eliezer, and Rabbi Meir, and Rabbi Yehuda said: This is the one who can shave by</b> using <b>his father’s money.</b> Their emphasis of: This is the one, indicates that they disagree with Rabbi Yosei entirely. In other words, where Rabbi Yosei rules that one can use his father’s money, they maintain that he cannot do so; and conversely, in a case where Rabbi Yosei says that one cannot spend his father’s money for his nazirite offerings, the Rabbis rule that he can do so.
§ <b>Rabba raised a dilemma:</b> If a father <b>has two nazirite sons, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara clarifies the sides of the dilemma: Does one say that <b>we learned it</b> as <b>a <i>halakha</i></b> that a son can shave using money left for his father’s naziriteship, and therefore <b>any</b> son <b>who precedes</b> his brother <b>and shaves</b> using his father’s money has <b>shaved</b> and gained the entire sum? <b>Or perhaps we learned</b> this <i>halakha</i> from the case of <b>inheritance, and</b> consequently, each receives <b>a half?</b>
Similarly, <b>Rava raised a dilemma:</b> If one has two nazirite sons, <b>a firstborn and a regular</b> son, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Does one say that <b>we learned it</b> as <b>a <i>halakha</i></b> that a son may shave using money left for his father’s naziriteship, and this ruling is unconnected to inheritance, <b>and therefore he does not need to shave</b> only <b>in accordance with what he receives</b> as an inheritance? Instead, either each son receives half the money, or whoever precedes the other receives the entire sum. <b>Or perhaps it is an inheritance, and just as</b> the firstborn <b>takes a double portion</b> of the estate, <b>so too he shaves</b> with a double portion of this money?
<b>And if you say it is an inheritance and he shaves in accordance with</b> the portion <b>he receives, it is</b> still possible that it is only with regard <b>to non-sacred</b> matters <b>that</b> a firstborn <b>has a double portion, but with regard to consecrated</b> property this <i>halakha</i> does <b>not</b> apply. <b>Or perhaps, since he acquires that</b> money, although he uses it <b>for</b> the purpose of <b>shaving</b> it <b>is no different,</b> i.e., it does not matter that the money is for a sacred matter.
The Gemara raises another dilemma: If <b>his father</b> was <b>a permanent nazirite and he was an unspecified nazirite,</b> or if <b>his father was an unspecified nazirite and he was a permanent nazirite, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Can this son shave using money left for his father’s naziriteship? Does one say that <b>when we learned the <i>halakha</i></b> that a son can shave using money left for his father’s naziriteship, this applies only <b>in</b> a case where they are both <b>unspecified nazirites, or perhaps</b> it <b>is no different,</b> and the same <i>halakha</i> applies even if their naziriteships are of two different kinds?
<b>And if you say: Here, both</b> cases are at least similar in that they involve <b>ritually pure naziriteship,</b> and therefore the son can make use of his father’s money, then <b>Rav Ashi raises</b> a different <b>dilemma:</b> If <b>his father</b> was <b>an impure nazirite and he was a pure nazirite,</b> or if <b>his father was a pure nazirite and he was an impure nazirite, what</b> is the <i>halakha</i>? Can the son shave using money left for his father’s naziriteship or not? No answer was found for any of these dilemmas, and the Gemara says that they <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Beit Shammai say: Consecration</b> that one performs in <b>error</b> nevertheless renders property <b>consecrated,</b>

Daf 31a

<b>and Beit Hillel say</b> it is <b>not consecrated. How so;</b> what is considered an act of erroneous consecration? If one <b>said: A black bull that will emerge from my house first is consecrated, and a white</b> bull <b>emerged</b> first, <b>Beit Shammai say</b> it is <b>consecrated and Beit Hillel say</b> it is <b>not consecrated.</b> Similarly, if one said: <b>A gold dinar that will come up first in my hand is consecrated, and</b> when he reached into his pocket a dinar <b>of silver came up, Beit Shammai say</b> it is <b>consecrated and Beit Hillel say</b> it is <b>not consecrated.</b> Likewise, if one said: <b>A barrel of wine that will come up first in my hand</b> when I enter the cellar <b>is consecrated, and</b> a barrel <b>of oil came up</b> in his hand instead, <b>Beit Shammai say</b> it is <b>consecrated and Beit Hillel say</b> it is <b>not consecrated.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that <b>Beit Shammai say</b> that <b>consecration</b> performed in error renders property consecrated, and Beit Hillel say it is not consecrated. The Gemara analyzes their dispute: <b>What is the reason of Beit Shammai?</b> They maintain <b>that we derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>the initial</b> stage <b>of consecration from the final</b> stage <b>of consecration.</b> The final stage of consecration is referring to substitution, when one attempts to substitute a non-consecrated animal for a consecrated one. <b>Just as</b> an act of <b>substitution</b> takes effect <b>even in error,</b> i.e., if one meant to say that his black bull should be a substitute for his consecrated animal and he actually said: This white bull, the white bull is rendered consecrated, <b>so too,</b> the initial stage of <b>consecration</b> takes effect <b>even</b> when done <b>in error.</b>
<b>And Beit Hillel say: This matter,</b> i.e., the <i>halakha</i> that consecration takes effect even when done in error, applies only to <b>substitution,</b> where there is an animal that is already fully consecrated. <b>However, we do not have</b> the initial status of <b>consecration descend</b> upon an item <b>in error.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai,</b> who derive the <i>halakha</i> of the initial stage of consecration from substitution, <b>just as if one said,</b> at the start of the day: <b>This</b> animal <b>is</b> a substitute <b>in exchange for this</b> animal <b>in the middle of the day, would it become a substitute from that time</b> when he issued the statement, in opposition to his explicit statement? It would not. <b>Rather,</b> Beit Shammai certainly concede that the animal does not become consecrated as a substitute <b>until the middle of the day arrives,</b> at which point <b>it becomes a substitution. So too,</b> in the case of the mishna, the consecration should take effect <b>when the situation is revealed</b> to be in accordance with his statement, i.e., only if a black bull emerges first. Only then should the animal be rendered consecrated, but not if a white bull emerges first. Why do Beit Shammai hold that in the case of the mishna the consecration takes effect in opposition to his explicit statement?
<b>Rav Pappa said:</b> Beit Shammai concede that consecration does not take effect in opposition to one’s explicit statement. Rather, they maintain that it is <b>for this</b> reason that the man <b>states:</b> The black bull that will emerge from my house <b>first,</b> as he means the following: <b>When</b> the <b>first</b> black bull of all the black bulls I possess <b>will emerge</b> from my house, it will be consecrated. When Beit Shammai ruled that the bull is consecrated, they were referring to the first black bull that emerged, even if it was not the first bull that emerged, as a white bull preceded it.
The Gemara asks: <b>But he said: Black bull,</b> and <b>are we not dealing</b> even with a case <b>where he only has this</b> one black bull? If so, the Gemara’s initial interpretation of his statement is correct: The black bull is consecrated only if it is the first to emerge, but not if a white bull precedes it. The Gemara answers: <b>No;</b> it is <b>necessary</b> to state this <i>halakha</i> with regard to a case <b>where he has two</b> or <b>three</b> black bulls. <b>And Beit Hillel say: If so,</b> i.e., if he intended to consecrate the first of his black bulls to emerge from the house, <b>he should have</b> said: The <b>first</b> black bull <b>that will emerge</b> from my house. Since he did not say this, he must have meant that the black bull should be consecrated only if it is the first bull of any kind to emerge.
<b>Rava from Barnish said to Rav Ashi,</b> with regard to the explanation of Rav Pappa: Is <b>this</b> case he mentioned one of <b>erroneous consecration? It is intentional consecration.</b> According to the interpretation of Rav Pappa, there is no error. He intended to consecrate the first black bull that emerged, and that is what was consecrated. The Gemara answers: Nevertheless, it is called an erroneous consecration <b>because he erred in his first statement.</b> His statement of consecration gave the mistaken impression that he desired to consecrate the first bull that emerges, even if it is white. In any case, Rav Pappa indicates that even Beit Shammai hold that an erroneous act of consecration does not take effect. 
The Gemara questions this assumption: <b>And</b> do <b>Beit Shammai hold</b> that an indisputably <b>erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration is not</b> considered an act of <b>consecration? But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna (31b): With regard to <b>one who took a vow of naziriteship and</b> later <b>made a request to the halakhic authorities</b> to dissolve his vow, <b>and they dissolved</b> his vow, <b>and he had</b> already <b>separated an animal</b> for one of his nazirite offerings beforehand, <b>it shall go out and graze among the flock,</b> like any other non-sacred animal.
The mishna continues: <b>Beit Hillel said to Beit Shammai: Don’t you concede that</b> the reason for this ruling is because <b>it is an erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration, and</b> that a consecration of this kind does not take effect, and that is the reason <b>it shall go out and graze among</b> the <b>flock?</b> The same <i>halakha</i> should apply to all erroneous acts of consecration. One can learn from here <b>by inference that Beit Shammai hold</b> that an entirely <b>erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration is</b> considered <b>consecration,</b> as is evident from Beit Hillel’s question.
The Gemara answers: This is not the case; <b>rather, it is Beit Hillel who erred</b> in their understanding. <b>They thought</b> that <b>Beit Shammai’s reasoning</b> was <b>because an erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration is</b> considered <b>consecration,</b> and therefore they raised a difficulty with regard to the case of a nazirite. <b>And Beit Shammai said to them:</b> Our reasoning in the case of the black bull is <b>not because</b> it is <b>an erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration. Rather,</b> it is merely called an erroneous consecration <b>because he erred in his first statement,</b> as he actually meant to consecrate the first of his black bulls to emerge from his house.
The Gemara continues to ask: <b>And</b> do <b>Beit Shammai hold</b> that an indisputably <b>erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration is not</b> considered <b>consecration? Come</b> and <b>hear</b> proof from the mishna (32b) that they maintain that an erroneously consecrated item is considered consecrated: If there <b>were</b> several people <b>walking along the way,</b>

Daf 31b

<b>and one</b> other person was <b>approaching them, and one</b> of those walking <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite if this</b> person coming toward us <b>is so-and-so. And</b> another <b>one</b> of them <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite if this is not so-and-so,</b> while a third member of the group said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if one of you</b> two <b>is a nazirite,</b> and a fourth said: I am hereby a nazirite <b>if neither of you is a nazirite,</b> and another added: I am hereby a nazirite <b>if both of you are nazirites.</b> Finally, the last person said: I am hereby a nazirite <b>if all you</b> who spoke before me <b>are nazirites. Beit Shammai say</b> that <b>they are all nazirites,</b> as by saying: I am hereby a nazirite, they have accepted naziriteship upon themselves even if their statement turns out to be incorrect.
The Gemara analyzes this mishna: <b>But here, it is</b> clearly a case of <b>an erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration,</b> as the statements of some of these individuals must have been incorrect, <b>and</b> yet the mishna <b>teaches</b> that Beit Shammai maintain that <b>they are all nazirites.</b> The Sages <b>say</b> in response: In fact, in general <b>Beit Shammai hold</b> that <b>an erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration is</b> considered <b>consecration,</b> as is evident from this <i>halakha</i> involving nazirites. However, the particular mishna <b>here,</b> concerning black and white bulls, is <b>not</b> based on that <i>halakha</i>. Rather, Rav Pappa’s explanation is the correct one.
<b>Abaye said</b> a different explanation of the mishna: It should <b>not enter your mind</b> that the mishna is dealing with one <b>who was standing in the morning</b> and referred to a future event, i.e., that an animal will emerge from the house. <b>Rather, with what are we dealing here?</b> With one <b>who is standing at noon,</b> after the bulls had already left the house, <b>and said:</b> The <b>black bull that emerged first from my house first shall be consecrated. And</b> people <b>said to him: A white</b> bull <b>emerged</b> first. <b>And he said to them: Had I known</b> that <b>a white</b> bull <b>emerged, I would not</b> have <b>said black.</b> Therefore, the consecration was erroneous.
The Gemara asks: <b>How can you say</b> that the mishna <b>deals</b> with one <b>who is standing at noon</b> and is speaking of a past event? <b>But</b> in a subsequent example the mishna <b>teaches: A gold dinar that will come up</b> in my hand first shall be consecrated, which is clearly referring to a future event. The Gemara answers: You should emend the mishna and <b>teach:</b> A gold dinar <b>that came up,</b> in the past tense. The Gemara continues to ask: Didn’t the mishna state: <b>A barrel of wine that will come up</b> in my hand first shall be consecrated, which is also referring to the future tense. The Gemara similarly answers that one should <b>teach</b> in the mishna: A barrel <b>that</b> already <b>came up.</b>
§ The Gemara quotes a statement related to the case in the mishna with regard to black and white bulls. <b>Rav Ḥisda said: A black</b> bull <b>among white ones</b> is <b>deficient,</b> as white bulls are superior in quality, and <b>a white</b> patch <b>on a black</b> bull is <b>a deficiency.</b> Having stated these assessments, the Gemara returns to discuss the mishna. <b>We learned</b> in the mishna that if one said: The <b>black</b> bull <b>that will emerge from my house first is consecrated,</b> and a white one emerged. <b>It entered our minds</b> to assume that <b>when</b> one <b>consecrates</b> property to the Temple treasury <b>he consecrates sparingly,</b> i.e., he does not give his property that is superior in quality or value, unless he expressly says so. <b>And</b> yet <b>Beit Shammai say</b> that the white bull in this case <b>is consecrated,</b> which indicates that the white one is inferior in quality, which contradicts the statement of Rav Ḥisda.
The Gemara examines this assumption: <b>Rather, what then?</b> Will you say that according to the opinion of Beit Shammai one typically <b>consecrates generously</b> and donates his property that is superior? However, the continuation of the mishna states that if one said: The <b>gold dinar that will come up in my hand first, and a silver one came up, Beit Shammai say it is consecrated.</b> If Beit Shammai hold that one would have in mind to consecrate only the superior property, why would the inferior silver coin be consecrated?
The Gemara counters: <b>Rather, what then?</b> Does a person <b>consecrate sparingly?</b> Yet the subsequent example of the mishna states that if one said: <b>A barrel of wine that will come up in my hand first, and one of oil came up, Beit Shammai say</b> it is <b>consecrated. But oil is preferable to wine,</b> so why is the oil consecrated? The Gemara answers: <b>If</b> the problem is <b>due to that, this</b> is <b>not difficult,</b> as this mishna <b>was taught in the Galilee, where wine is preferable to oil.</b> Olive trees are plentiful in the Galilee, and therefore oil is cheaper than wine. Therefore, the entire mishna can be explained in accordance with the opinion that people consecrate sparingly.
The Gemara comments: In any case, <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna poses <b>a difficulty to</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Ḥisda,</b> as it indicates that a white bull is less valuable than a black one. The Gemara answers that <b>Rav Ḥisda</b> could have <b>said to you: When I said</b> that a white one is superior, I was referring only <b>to a Karmanian bull,</b> a type of bull in which the white animals are superior in quality to the black ones. In all other cases black bulls are considered superior, and the mishna was referring to standard bulls.
The Gemara quotes another statement with regard to bulls: <b>And Rav Ḥisda said</b> with regard to bulls: <b>A black</b> bull is good <b>for its hide; a red one</b> is good <b>for its meat;</b> while <b>a white</b> bull is good <b>for plowing.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t Rav Ḥisda say: A black</b> bull <b>among white ones</b> is <b>deficient,</b> which indicates that a black one is inferior in all regards? The Gemara again answers that Rav Ḥisda could reply: <b>When I said</b> that, I was referring only <b>to a Karmanian bull,</b> but not to other bulls.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>one who took a vow of naziriteship,</b> who then regretted his vow and stopped observing the prohibition against drinking wine, <b>and</b> later <b>requested of a halakhic authority</b> to dissolve his vow, <b>and</b> the authority <b>ruled</b> that <b>he is bound</b> by his vow, finding no reason to dissolve it, he <b>counts</b> the term of naziriteship <b>from the time that he vowed,</b> including the days when he acted as though the vow were dissolved. In a case where <b>he requested of a halakhic authority</b> to dissolve his vow <b>and</b> the authority <b>dissolved it,</b> if <b>he had an animal separated</b> as a nazirite offering <b>it shall go out and graze among</b> the <b>flock.</b>
On the basis of this <i>halakha</i>, and continuing their discussion in the previous mishna, <b>Beit Hillel said to Beit Shammai: Don’t you concede with regard to this</b> case <b>that it is an erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration, and</b> yet the <i>halakha</i> is <b>that it shall go out and graze among</b> the <b>flock?</b> This shows that you too accept the principle that an erroneous act of consecration does not take effect. <b>Beit Shammai said to</b> Beit Hillel: <b>Don’t you concede with regard to one</b> who was separating the animal tithe from his herd, i.e., passing his animals before him single file and consecrating every tenth one as a tithe, <b>that</b> if he <b>erred and called the ninth</b> animal: <b>Tenth; and the tenth: Ninth; and the eleventh: Tenth, that</b> each of them <b>is consecrated?</b> This proves that an erroneous act of consecration does take effect.
<b>Beit Hillel said to them:</b> It is <b>not the rod</b> that <b>consecrates it.</b> The touch of the rod does not consecrate the animal, nor does the fact that he said: Tenth, by mistake. Not all errors cause the tithe to be consecrated, and the proof is as follows: <b>And what</b> would be the <i>halakha</i> <b>if he had erred and placed the rod on the eighth or on the twelfth,</b> and labeled them: Tenth? Can it be suggested that <b>perhaps he performed anything</b> of consequence? The <i>halakha</i> is that the eighth or twelfth animal cannot be consecrated as tithe. <b>Rather,</b> why is the ninth or eleventh animal consecrated? There is a specific reason for this <i>halakha</i>, as the same <b>verse that consecrated the tenth</b> also <b>consecrated the ninth</b>

Daf 32a

<b>and the eleventh.</b> It is a Torah edict that the consecration takes effect with regard to those two animals. Therefore, one cannot infer from this case that an erroneous act of consecration takes effect.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Whose</b> opinion is expressed in <b>the mishna,</b> when it rules that one whose request to dissolve his vow was rejected by a halakhic authority counts the duration of his naziriteship from the moment he took the vow, including the days on which he did not observe the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship in practice? The Gemara responds: It is <b>neither</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, nor</b> that of <b>the Rabbis.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta Nedarim</i> 1:6): With regard to <b>one who took a vow</b> of naziriteship <b>and</b> later <b>transgressed his</b> vow of <b>naziriteship</b> by drinking wine, the halakhic authorities <b>do not attend to his</b> request to dissolve his vow, nor do they let him complete his term as a nazirite by sacrificing offerings, <b>unless he counted,</b> i.e., observed, days of the <b>prohibitions</b> of naziriteship <b>for the same</b> number of <b>days in which he behaved with permissiveness</b> concerning the restrictions of a nazirite. Only after he observes the prohibitions of naziriteship for the number of days that his observance lapsed will a halakhic authority hear his request for dissolution, or allow him to bring his offerings. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: Thirty days is enough for him.</b> He is required to observe additional days of naziriteship only if he transgressed his vow of naziriteship for thirty days or more.
The Gemara elaborates: Which of these opinions might correspond to that of the mishna? <b>If</b> it is that of <b>the Rabbis,</b> it is <b>difficult</b> with respect to <b>a short naziriteship,</b> i.e., a standard term of naziriteship, which lasts for thirty days. The Rabbis maintain that he cannot include all the days from the time he took the vow even if his naziriteship was short. They hold that he must add days corresponding to the days that he failed to observe the <i>halakhot</i> required of a nazirite. <b>If</b> it is that of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> although he agrees that one who transgresses his vow of naziriteship for a short period of less than thirty days need not add to his term, it is nevertheless <b>difficult</b> with regard to <b>a lengthy naziriteship,</b> as even Rabbi Yosei rules that in such a case the individual must observe naziriteship for additional days.
The Gemara answers: <b>If you wish,</b> you can <b>say</b> that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, and if you wish,</b> you can <b>say</b> it is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>If you wish,</b> you can <b>say</b> that the mishna is in accordance with <b>Rabbi Yosei: Here,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is speaking <b>of a lengthy naziriteship; there,</b> the mishna is referring <b>to a short naziriteship.</b> In other words, the mishna refers only to one who transgresses his vow of naziriteship for less than thirty days.
<b>And if you wish,</b> you can <b>say</b> that the mishna follows the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> by emending its wording: <b>Do not say</b> that he must observe naziriteship <b>from the time that he vowed; rather, say: Like from the time that he vowed.</b> That is, he must count his naziriteship corresponding to the time that has elapsed since he took his vow, exactly as stated by the Rabbis.
