§ The mishna taught that with regard to one who <b>requested of the halakhic authorities and they dissolved</b> the vow <b>for him,</b> and he had already separated an animal for a nazirite offering, it shall go out and graze among the flock. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said: From</b> the ruling <b>of Beit Shammai one can learn</b> a <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to</b> the opinion <b>of Beit Hillel.</b> Is it <b>not</b> the case that <b>Beit Shammai say</b> that <b>an erroneous</b> act of <b>consecration is</b> considered <b>consecration,</b> and yet <b>once the matter is revealed that his vow of naziriteship</b> was <b>not right,</b> i.e., it has been dissolved, the animal is considered non-sacred and <b>shall go out and graze among</b> the <b>flock.</b>
Rabbi Yirmeya continues: <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Hillel as well, even though</b> they <b>say</b> that <b>a substitution</b> of a consecrated animal for another performed <b>in error is</b> a valid <b>substitute, this applies</b> only <b>when the initial consecration,</b> i.e., the consecration of the first animal, <b>is</b> in effect, in which case a substitution can take place. <b>However,</b> in a situation <b>where the initial consecration has been uprooted,</b> i.e., a halakhic authority dissolved the vow pertaining to the first consecration, the first animal is no longer consecrated, and therefore the <b>substitute is also uprooted,</b> i.e., the animal remains non-sacred.
§ <b>The Master said</b> in the mishna: <b>Don’t you concede with regard to one who called the ninth</b> animal: <b>Tenth,</b> that it is consecrated? It <b>was stated</b> that <i>amora’im</i> engaged in a dispute concerning this point. With regard to the animal <b>tithe, Rav Naḥman said:</b> It is consecrated in the above manner only if it resulted from <b>his error, but not</b> from <b>his intentional</b> declaration. If the owner was aware that it was the ninth animal and called it: Tenth, on purpose, his consecration is ineffective. <b>Rav Ḥisda and Rabba bar Rav Huna say: His error</b> consecrates the animal, <b>and all the more so his intentional</b> declaration, i.e., if he called the ninth or eleventh animals: Tenth, in full knowledge that they were not the tenth.
<b>Rava said to Rav Naḥman: According to your</b> opinion, <b>that you say</b> it is only <b>his error</b> that consecrates the ninth animal <b>and not his intentional</b> declaration, consider <b>that Beit Shammai said</b> the following <b>to Beit Hillel</b> in the mishna as proof that erroneous consecration is valid: <b>Don’t you concede that if he called the ninth: Tenth; the tenth: Ninth; and the eleventh: Tenth, that all three are consecrated? And Beit Hillel were silent</b> in face of this question.
However, according to your opinion, <b>let</b> Beit Hillel <b>say to</b> Beit Shammai: While it is correct that an erroneous act of consecration takes effect with regard to animal tithe, one cannot learn the <i>halakha</i> of other types of consecration from there, as <b>what</b> is unique <b>about tithe is that it is not consecrated</b> if his declaration concerning the wrong animal was <b>intentional,</b> whereas other types of consecration are typically the result of a purposeful act. Since other types of consecration take effect with intent, an erroneous act of consecration is not considered consecration.
<b>Rav Shimi bar Ashi said: This is the reason that</b> Beit Hillel <b>did not say</b> that answer <b>to</b> Beit Shammai, as one can argue in the opposite manner, by saying <b>that it is an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>If tithe, which is not consecrated</b> when he acts <b>intentionally,</b> is nevertheless <b>consecrated</b> if he acted <b>erroneously;</b> with regard to other types of <b>consecration, which are consecrated intentionally,</b> is it <b>not all the more so</b> that an act of erroneous consecration should render an item consecrated?
The Gemara rejects this argument: <b>And this is not so,</b> as this <i>a fortiori</i> inference is flawed, <b>as consecration depends on the intention of the owner,</b> and therefore it cannot apply when done in error. By contrast, the animal tithe is not consecrated through the intention of its owner but merely by counting, as every tenth animal is consecrated.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>one who took a vow of naziriteship and went to bring his animal</b> which he set aside for his nazirite offering <b>and discovered that it was stolen,</b> and due to the need to separate an additional animal now regrets having taken his vow, <b>if he took a vow of naziriteship before his animal was stolen, he is a nazirite,</b> as a vow cannot be dissolved as the result of a later event.

Daf 32b

<b>But if he took a vow of naziriteship after his animal was stolen, he is not a nazirite,</b> as it is retroactively established that his vow was taken in error from the outset, as he relied on an animal he did not possess. <b>And this</b> was the <b>error</b> that <b>Naḥum the Mede erred</b> when he failed to distinguish between an event that occurred before the vow was taken and an event that occurred afterward. The incident in question was as follows: <b>When nazirites were ascending from the exile</b> to sacrifice their offerings, <b>and they found the Temple destroyed, Naḥum the Mede said to them: If you had known that the Temple</b> would be <b>destroyed, would you have taken a vow of naziriteship? They said to him:</b> Certainly <b>not,</b> as there is no remedy for a naziriteship in this case. <b>And Naḥum the Mede dissolved</b> the vow <b>for them.</b>
<b>And when the matter came before the Rabbis, they said:</b> His ruling is incorrect. Rather, <b>whoever took a vow of naziriteship before the Temple was destroyed,</b> like these nazirites from the exile, he is <b>a nazirite,</b> as he committed no error at the time of his vow, and one cannot dissolve vows based a new situation. However, one who stated his vow <b>after the Temple was destroyed is not a nazirite,</b> as he vowed based on an erroneous assumption.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> In relation to the mishna’s statement with regard to the dissolution of a vow of naziriteship due to a new situation, the Gemara cites a statement that <b>Rabba said: The Rabbis overwhelmed Rabbi Eliezer</b> until he retracted his ruling <b>and established</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with their opinion.</b> To what does this refer? <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna in <i>Nedarim</i> (64a): They <b>may broach</b> dissolution <b>by</b> asking about <b>a new situation,</b> i.e., a halakhic authority can dissolve a vow due to a new situation that the one who took the vow did not anticipate at the time he took his vow. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer; but the Rabbis prohibit</b> this. Since Rabbi Eliezer does not disagree in the case of naziriteship in this mishna, he must have accepted the opinion of the Rabbis.
<b>And Rava said,</b> with regard to the same issue: <b>Even though the Rabbis said</b> that they <b>may not broach</b> dissolution <b>by</b> asking about <b>a new situation, however,</b> they <b>may broach</b> dissolution <b>by</b> asking about <b>the conditions of a new situation,</b> i.e., with situations similar to a new situation. <b>What are the circumstances</b> of this type of broaching dissolution? The halakhic authorities <b>say to</b> the nazirites who took their vows before the destruction of the Temple: <b>If a person had come and said to you</b> before you took your vow <b>that the Temple</b> will be <b>destroyed, would you have vowed?</b> Although the destruction of the Temple itself is a new situation, its potential occurrence existed when they vowed, and therefore if they answered that they would not have vowed had they known this, their vows are dissolved.
<b>Rav Yosef said: If I had been there,</b> when those nazirites arrived, <b>I would have said</b> the following <b>to them,</b> in order to dissolve their vows: <b>Isn’t it written: “The Sanctuary of the Lord, the Sanctuary of the Lord, the Sanctuary of the Lord, are these”</b> (Jeremiah 7:4). <b>This</b> thrice repetition of “Sanctuary of the Lord” is referring to the <b>First Temple and</b> the <b>Second Temple</b> which are destined to be destroyed, leading to a Third Temple. These nazirites should have considered the possibility of the Temple’s destruction, and this can serve as a means of broaching the dissolution of their vows.
The Gemara responds: <b>Although they</b> might have <b>known</b> that the Second Temple <b>would be destroyed,</b> as the verse speaks of three Temples, <b>did they know when</b> it would be destroyed? Would they have considered that it might occur in their lifetimes, preventing them from sacrificing their offerings? <b>Abaye said: And</b> did <b>they not know when? But isn’t it written: “Seventy sevens are decreed upon your people and upon your sacred city”</b> (Daniel 9:24), which indicates that the Second Temple would be destroyed seventy Sabbatical cycles of seven years after the destruction of the First Temple, which is 490 years. The Gemara answers: <b>And still, did we know on which day</b> it would be destroyed? It was therefore impossible to use this factor as a means to broach the dissolution of their vows.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If there <b>were</b> people <b>walking along the way, and one</b> other person was <b>approaching them,</b> and <b>one</b> of those walking <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite if this</b> person approaching us <b>is so-and-so. And</b> another <b>one</b> of them <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite if this is not so-and-so,</b> while a third member of the group said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if one of you</b> two <b>is a nazirite,</b> and a fourth said: I am hereby a nazirite <b>if neither of you is a nazirite,</b> and another added: I am hereby a nazirite <b>if both of you are nazirites.</b> Finally, the last person said: I am hereby a nazirite <b>if all you</b> who spoke before me <b>are nazirites.</b>
<b>Beit Shammai say</b> that <b>they are all nazirites,</b> as by saying: I am hereby a nazirite, they have accepted naziriteship upon themselves even if their statements turn out to be incorrect. Beit Shammai maintain that a vow of naziriteship taken in error is considered a valid vow of naziriteship. <b>And Beit Hillel say: Only he whose statement was not fulfilled is a nazirite. And Rabbi Tarfon says: Not</b> a single <b>one of them is a nazirite,</b> including those whose statements were correct. Rabbi Tarfon maintains that a vow of naziriteship must be pronounced in an explicit manner, without any hint of uncertainty. In this case, none of them knew for sure the identity of the person coming toward them, and therefore they could not be certain they were nazirites at the time of their vows.
If the person approaching them <b>turned back</b> so that his identity was never discovered, <b>not</b> one of them is <b>a nazirite.</b> The matter was never clarified, and the <i>halakha</i> is lenient in cases of uncertain naziriteship. <b>Rabbi Shimon says</b> that the <i>halakha</i> is stringent with regard to an uncertainty of this kind, and therefore they should proceed as follows in order to avoid any uncertainty: Each of those who took a vow <b>should say: If it was in accordance with my statement, I am hereby an obligatory nazirite,</b> as my condition was fulfilled, <b>and if not, I am hereby a voluntary nazirite,</b> and in this manner they are all nazirites either way.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara questions the opinion of Beit Hillel: <b>Why is he whose statement was not fulfilled a nazirite? Rav Yehuda said:</b> One must emend the wording of the mishna so that it <b>says:</b> Only <b>he whose statement was fulfilled</b> becomes a nazirite.

Daf 33a

<b>Abaye said:</b> One must correct his understanding of the mishna, so that it deals with a situation <b>where</b> in addition to his first statement <b>he said: Even if it is not so-and-so, I shall be a nazirite. And what is</b> the meaning of the phrase: Whose <b>statement was not fulfilled?</b> It means: <b>His first statement was not fulfilled,</b> as it was not that person; <b>rather,</b> he is a nazirite because <b>his last statement</b> was fulfilled.
§ The mishna taught that if the individual walking toward them <b>turned back,</b> so that his identity remained unknown, <b>not</b> one of them is <b>a nazirite.</b> The Gemara clarifies: <b>The reason is</b> only <b>because he turned back,</b> from which it may be inferred that if that person <b>comes before us</b> and is identified, the one who said he would be a nazirite if it is so-and-so <b>is</b> considered to be <b>a nazirite.</b> The Gemara inquires: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion is this <i>halakha</i>? <br>This <i>amud</i> has no Gemara text, as in the classic Vilna layout it is entirely filled with the commentary of <i>Tosafot</i>.

Daf 33b



Daf 34a

<b>If we say</b> it is the opinion of <b>Rabbi Tarfon,</b> even in a case where the individual in question came before us and identified himself, <b>is</b> the one who claimed to know his identity <b>a nazirite? Since at the time when he vowed he did not know whether</b> the subject of their debate <b>is so-and-so or not, does naziriteship take effect with regard to him? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 3:19) that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Tarfon:</b> In the case of the mishna <b>not one of</b> the six people <b>is a nazirite, since naziriteship is imposed</b> upon someone <b>only if</b> the vow is stated with <b>explicitness [<i>hafla’a</i>]</b>. The Torah says that a vow must be “clearly” pronounced, as the verse states: “When either man or woman shall clearly utter [<i>yafli</i>] a vow, the vow of a nazirite” (Numbers 6:2). This verse indicates that his vow is valid only if it is explicit.
<b>Rather,</b> the mishna represents the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, as</b> stated with regard to <b>a heap</b> of grain. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 2:9): If someone said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite on the condition that this heap</b> of grain <b>contains one hundred <i>kor</i>,</b> a measurement of volume, <b>and</b> when <b>he went</b> to measure it <b>he discovered that</b> the heap <b>was stolen or lost,</b> so that the amount it held cannot be determined, <b>Rabbi Shimon deems</b> him <b>prohibited</b> in all the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship, as the heap might have contained one hundred <i>kor</i>. <b>And Rabbi Yehuda deems him permitted,</b> as he maintains that the <i>halakha</i> is lenient in cases of uncertain naziriteship. Rabbi Yehuda’s reasoning applies equally to the case of the mishna.
As for <b>Rabbi Shimon,</b> who disputes the ruling of the mishna, he <b>holds</b> with regard to the heap of grain: <b>Since if</b> the heap had <b>not been stolen perhaps it might have contained one hundred <i>kor</i> and he</b> would have been <b>a nazirite, now too, he is a nazirite</b> due to the uncertainty. <b>And here too,</b> in the case of the mishna, <b>since if</b> that person would have <b>come before us and we</b> would have <b>known that he was so-and-so,</b> the one who took the vow <b>would have been a nazirite,</b> consequently, <b>now too, he is a nazirite</b> as a result of the uncertainty cast by the unknown identity of the individual.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> Someone <b>saw a kosher animal with characteristics of both a domesticated animal and a non-domesticated animal [<i>koy</i>], and said: I am hereby a nazirite if this is a non-domesticated animal;</b> and another individual said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if this is not a non-domesticated animal;</b> and a third person said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if this is a domesticated animal;</b> and a fourth said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if this not a domesticated animal.</b>
The mishna continues: A fifth person added: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if this is a non-domesticated animal and a domesticated animal,</b> and a sixth person said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if this is neither a non-domesticated animal nor a domesticated animal.</b> Someone who heard all the above statements said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if one of you is a nazirite,</b> and another one stated: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if not one of you is a nazirite,</b> and a final person said: <b>I am hereby a nazirite if all of you are nazirites.</b> In this case, <b>they are all nazirites.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the ruling of the mishna that they are all nazirites, it <b>is taught</b> in <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i> that this case involves a total of <b>nine nazirites, and</b> it <b>is taught</b> in <b>the other</b> <i>baraita</i> that there are <b>nine</b> sets of <b>naziriteship</b> here. The Gemara clarifies: <b>Granted,</b> one can understand the <i>baraita</i> that says that there are <b>nine nazirites, for example, if there were many people who associated</b> their naziriteship with the status of <b>this</b> <i>koy</i>, each with one of the statements listed above.
<b>However, how can you find these</b> circumstances of <b>nine</b> sets of <b>naziriteship in one person? Granted, you find six, as we learned</b> in the mishna that there are six ways in which the nature of a <i>koy</i> can be expressed: It is a non-domesticated animal; it is not a non-domesticated animal; it is a domesticated animal; it is not a domesticated animal; is a non-domesticated animal and a domesticated animal; it is neither a non-domesticated animal nor a domesticated animal. One person could associate his acceptance of naziriteship with all of the above possibilities.
<b>However, how can you find these</b> circumstances of the other <b>three</b> sets of naziriteship? The statements: If one of you is a nazirite, and: If one of you is not a nazirite, are necessarily expressed by individuals who did not make the initial six statements, as these last three statements address the group of people who made the first six statements. <b>Rav Sheshet said:</b> It is possible in a case <b>where</b> nine people issued the statements mentioned in the mishna, and someone <b>said: I am hereby a nazirite and the naziriteship of all of them are</b> incumbent <b>upon me.</b> Since each of the nine people is obligated to observe a term of naziriteship, this last one is obligated to observe nine terms.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Three types</b> of actions <b>are prohibited for a nazirite: The</b> contraction of <b>ritual impurity</b> from a corpse, <b>and the shaving</b> of one’s hair, <b>and</b> eating or drinking any substances <b>that emerge from the vine.</b> The mishna adds: <b>And all</b> products <b>that emerge from the vine combine with one another</b> to the amount that renders a nazirite liable to receive lashes. <b>And he is liable only if he eats an olive-bulk of the grapes.</b>

Daf 34b

<b>An initial</b> version of the <b>mishna says</b> that a nazirite is liable to receive lashes <b>only if he drinks a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> of <b>wine. Rabbi Akiva says: Even</b> if he <b>soaked his bread in wine and</b> the two together <b>contain enough to combine</b> to the amount of <b>an olive-bulk,</b> he is <b>liable. And</b> furthermore, a nazirite is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for</b> consuming <b>wine by itself, and for grapes by themselves, and for <i>ḥartzannim</i> by themselves, and for <i>zaggim</i> by themselves,</b> as each of these is forbidden separately by the Torah. <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says: He is liable only if he eats</b> an olive-bulk that includes at least <b>two <i>ḥartzannim</i> and</b> one <b><i>zag</i>,</b> in accordance with the verse “From <i>ḥartzannim</i> to <i>zag</i>” (Numbers 6:4), where the first term is in the plural and the second in the singular.
The mishna discusses the meaning of these terms: <b>Which</b> parts <b>are <i>ḥartzannim</i> and which are <i>zaggim</i>? The <i>ḥartzannim</i> are the outside</b> parts, the skin of the grape, while <b>the <i>zaggim</i> are the inner</b> parts, the seeds. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Yosei says:</b> The opposite is the case and this is the mnemonic so <b>that you should not err:</b> It is <b>like a bell [<i>zog</i>]</b> worn <b>by an animal,</b> in which <b>the outer</b> part, which corresponds to the skin of a grape, is called <b><i>zog</i>, and the inner</b> portion of the bell, the clapper, which corresponds to the seeds in a grape, is called <b><i>inbal</i>.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that <b>three types</b> of actions <b>are prohibited for a nazirite:</b> Contracting <b>ritual impurity,</b> shaving, and consuming products of the vine. The Gemara infers: <b>That which emerges from the vine, yes,</b> it is included in the prohibition; however, any part of <b>the vine itself,</b> other than its fruit, <b>no,</b> this is not forbidden. The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar.</b> This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Elazar says: Even</b> the <b>leaves and tendrils</b> of the vine <b>are included</b> in the prohibitions of naziriteship.
<b>Some say this</b> statement with regard <b>to the latter clause</b> of the mishna: <b>He is liable</b> to receive lashes <b>only if he eats an olive-bulk of the grapes.</b> The Gemara infers: If he eats <b>from the grapes, yes,</b> he is liable, but if he eats <b>from the vine itself, no,</b> he is not liable. If so, <b>the mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar.</b> This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Elazar says: Even</b> the <b>leaves and tendrils are included</b> in the prohibition.
The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what do</b> Rabbi Elazar and the Rabbis <b>disagree;</b> what is the basis of their dispute? The Gemara explains: <b>Rabbi Elazar interprets</b> the verses employing the hermeneutical principle of <b>amplifications and restrictions,</b> while <b>the Rabbis interpret</b> them employing the hermeneutical principle of <b>generalizations and details.</b> The Gemara clarifies: <b>Rabbi Elazar interprets</b> the verses employing the principle of <b>amplifications and restrictions,</b> as the phrase: <b>“He shall abstain from wine and strong drink”</b> (Numbers 6:3), <b>restricts</b> the prohibition to wine and strong drink, and the subsequent phrase: <b>“Anything that is made of the grapevine”</b> (Numbers 6:4), <b>amplifies</b> the prohibition.
The Gemara elaborates: In this manner, the Torah <b>restricts and amplifies,</b> which, according to the principles of exegesis, <b>amplifies</b> and includes virtually <b>all</b> substances. <b>What</b> does it <b>amplify</b> and include? It <b>amplifies</b> and includes <b>all matters</b> and substances that come from the vine. <b>What</b> does it <b>restrict?</b> After all, the phrase “He shall abstain from wine and strong drink” must be excluding something. According to this interpretation, the verse <b>restricts</b> only one part of a vine, <b>the branches.</b> A nazirite who eats the branches of the vine has not committed a transgression.
<b>And</b> conversely, <b>the Rabbis interpret</b> the verses employing the principle of <b>generalizations and details,</b> as the phrase <b>“He shall abstain from wine and strong drink”</b> (Numbers 6:3) is an example of <b>a detail,</b> the phrase <b>“anything that is made of the grapevine”</b> (Numbers 6:4) is <b>a generalization,</b> and when the verse continues: <b>“From pits to grape skin,”</b> it has given an example of <b>a detail again.</b> This is <b>a detail, and a generalization, and a detail.</b> According to this exegetical method, <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail: Just as</b> the items mentioned in <b>the detail</b> are clearly <b>defined as a fruit or fruit waste,</b> i.e., grape seeds or skins, <b>so too, everything</b> forbidden by the generalization is <b>a fruit or fruit waste,</b> but not leaves or tendrils, as maintained by Rabbi Elazar.
The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> so, one can suggest an alternative derivation: <b>Just as</b> the items mentioned in <b>the detail</b> are clearly <b>defined as a complete,</b> i.e., ripe, <b>fruit, so too, everything</b> forbidden must be <b>a complete fruit,</b> excluding unripe fruit. Why, according to the Rabbis, are unripe grapes included in the prohibition? <b>You</b> will <b>say</b> the following counterargument: <b>If so, what has the verse left you</b> to derive <b>from its meaning that it has not stated?</b> If you say <b>grapes and raisins,</b> these are explicitly <b>written;</b> if you say <b>wine and vinegar,</b> these are also <b>written.</b> What, then, does the generalization come to add? Evidently, <b>you cannot learn in accordance with the last version,</b> which excludes unripe fruit; <b>rather,</b> you must learn <b>in accordance with the first version,</b> which includes this fruit and excludes only leaves and tendrils.
The Gemara asks: <b>And as we eventually include everything</b> that is similar to the detail of fruit and the waste of fruit, <b>what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “From pits to grape skin”?</b> This serves <b>to tell you</b> a general principle of exegesis that applies throughout the Torah: <b>Wherever you find</b> only <b>a detail and a generalization, you cannot broaden it and learn</b> that the generalization is limited to be <b>like the detail,</b> by saying that the generalization merely clarifies the previous detail.
<b>Rather, the generalization becomes added to the detail</b> so that it includes all matters, even those dissimilar to the detail, <b>until the verse specifies</b> and adds another detail after the generalization, <b>in the manner that it specified with regard to a nazirite,</b> by inserting the phrase “from pits to grape skin” after “anything that is made of the grapevine.” The exegetical method of: A detail, a generalization, and a detail, means that the generalization includes only something that is similar to the detail. In this particular example, leaves and tendrils are excluded.
The Gemara analyzes the details of this <i>baraita</i>. <b>The Master said</b> above: <b>Just as</b> the items mentioned in <b>the detail</b> are clearly <b>defined as a fruit or fruit waste, so too everything</b> forbidden to the nazirite by the Torah is <b>a fruit or fruit waste.</b> The Gemara clarifies: <b>The fruit</b> mentioned by the Torah is <b>grapes. What is fruit waste? Vinegar,</b> as in the verse: “He shall drink no vinegar of wine or vinegar of strong drink” (Numbers 6:3).
<b>What is</b> the statement: <b>So too everything</b> forbidden by the generalization is <b>a fruit,</b> serving to include? It serves to include <b>unripe</b> grapes. And <b>what is</b> the statement: <b>So too everything</b> forbidden by the generalization is a fruit or fruit waste, stated with regard <b>to fruit waste,</b> serving to include? Which fruit waste unspecified by the verse is added by means of this derivation? <b>Rav Kahana said:</b> This serves <b>to include grapes that have become wormy.</b> With regard to the phrase “from pits <b>to grape skin,” Ravina said:</b> This serves <b>to include</b> the part of the fruit <b>that</b> is <b>in between</b> the seeds and the skin.
The Gemara continues to analyze the <i>baraita</i>, which it cites at length. <b>The Master said</b> above: <b>If</b> so, <b>just as</b> the items mentioned in <b>the detail</b> are clearly <b>defined as a complete fruit, so too everything</b> is <b>a complete fruit. You say: If so, what has the verse left you</b> to derive <b>from its meaning that it has not stated? Grapes and raisins,</b> these are <b>written; wine and vinegar,</b> these are <b>written. You cannot learn in accordance with the last version; rather,</b> you must learn <b>in accordance with the first version. And as we eventually include everything, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “From pit to grape skin”?</b> This serves <b>to tell you</b> that <b>wherever you find a detail and a generalization, you cannot broaden it and learn</b> that the generalization is limited to be <b>like the detail; rather, the generalization becomes added to the detail, until the verse specifies for you</b>

Daf 35a

<b>in the manner that it specified with regard to a nazirite: “From pits to grape skin”</b> (Numbers 6:4). The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya, who establishes this</b> verse: <b>“From pits to grape skin”</b> (Numbers 6:4), as serving <b>to say that</b> a nazirite <b>is liable only if he eats two grape seeds and a grape skin,</b> if so <b>from where does he</b> derive <b>the detail?</b> It is unclear how he applies this method of a detail, a generalization, and a detail, as according to his interpretation, the phrase “from pits to grape skin” does not serve to limit the previous generalization but to state a different <i>halakha</i>. The Gemara answers: Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar, who interprets</b> this by saying that a verse <b>restricts and amplifies.</b>
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead that Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya holds <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis</b> that this is a case of a detail, a generalization, and a detail. <b>As, if it should enter your mind</b> that the verse is merely teaching that <b>which was stated</b> by <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya, let the Merciful One write this</b> phrase: <b>“From pits to grape skin,” alongside</b> the other <b>details</b> of wine and vinegar. <b>For what <i>halakha</i> did</b> the Torah <b>write:</b> “From pits to grape skin,” <b>after the generalization? Conclude from it that</b> you should <b>derive</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>by</b> means of the method of <b>a generalization and a detail.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if so, you can <b>say</b> that the <b>entire</b> phrase <b>comes</b> only <b>for this</b> purpose, for a generalization and a detail, and Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya should not derive his <i>halakha</i>, that a nazirite is liable only if he eats two grape seeds and a grape skin, from this verse at all. The Gemara answers that Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya would say: <b>If so, let</b> the Torah <b>write either two grape seeds and two grape skins,</b> with both terms in the plural, <b>or a grape seed and a grape skin,</b> with both terms in the singular. <b>For what <i>halakha</i> did the Merciful One write: “From pits to grape skin”? Learn from it</b> that one should <b>interpret it</b> in the manner of <b>a generalization and a detail, and</b> one <b>can also interpret</b> from <b>it</b> that a nazirite is liable <b>only if he eats two grape seeds and a grape skin.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Elazar, who interprets</b> by the method of <b>restriction and amplification</b> that even tendrils and the leaves of a grapevine are included in the prohibition, <b>from where does he</b> derive the method of <b>a detail, a generalization, and a detail?</b>
<b>Rabbi Abbahu says: He derives it from this verse,</b> which deals with a bailee: <b>“And if a man deliver to his neighbor a donkey, or an ox, or a sheep,</b> or any animal to guard, and it dies…the oath of the Lord shall be between them both” (Exodus 22:9). The phrase “a donkey, or an ox, or a sheep” is <b>a detail; “or any animal”</b> is <b>a generalization</b> that includes all animals; and in the phrase <b>“to guard”</b> the Torah <b>detailed again.</b> This is <b>a detail, and a generalization, and a detail.</b> In this case, <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail;</b> i.e., items that can be guarded.
<b>Rava said:</b> Rabbi Elazar <b>derives</b> the method of a detail, a generalization, and a detail <b>from this verse:</b> “And if his offering is from the flock, whether of the sheep or of the goats, for a burnt-offering, he shall offer it a male without blemish” (Leviticus 1:10). The phrase <b>“and if</b> his offering is <b>from”</b> is <b>a detail,</b> as it indicates part but not all of something, <b>“the flock”</b> is <b>a generalization</b> that includes animals that have been used sinfully, and when it stated: <b>“Sheep,” and: “Goats,”</b> the Torah has <b>detailed again.</b>
This is <b>a detail, and a generalization, and a detail,</b> and therefore <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail.</b> The details teach that only animals that copulated with a person may not be brought as offerings.
<b>Rav Yehuda of Diskarta said to Rava: And let</b> Rabbi Elazar <b>derive</b> the method of a detail, a generalization, and a detail <b>from this</b> earlier <b>verse,</b> which appears in the same chapter: “From animals, from the herd or from the flock, you shall bring your offering” (Leviticus 1:2), in the following manner: <b>“From”</b> is <b>a detail</b> that excludes an undomesticated animal; <b>“animals”</b> is <b>a generalization</b> which includes undomesticated animals (see Deuteronomy, chapter 14); and when it states: <b>“Herd,” and: “Flock,”</b> the Torah <b>has detailed again.</b> This is <b>a detail, and a generalization, and a detail,</b> and therefore <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail,</b> i.e., domesticated animals.
Rava <b>said to</b> Rav Yehuda of Diskarta: One <b>cannot derive</b> the method of a detail, a generalization, and a detail <b>from this</b> verse, <b>as if</b> the source were <b>from there, I would say</b> the phrase <b>“animals”</b>

Daf 35b

is referring even to undomesticated animals, as <b>an undomesticated animal is included</b> in the general category of <b>animal.</b>
Rav Yehuda of Diskarta <b>said to</b> Rava: How can you suggest that in this verse <b>an undomesticated animal is included</b> in the general category of <b>animal?</b> It is <b>written: “Herd” and: “Flock,” and this</b> entire phrase <b>is a detail, and a generalization,</b> and a detail, from which <b>you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail,</b> which are herd and flock, not undomesticated animals.
§ The Gemara asks: <b>And from where do we</b> derive <b>that it is so,</b> that in the methodology of generalizations and details, the generalizations are similar to the details? It is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to the mitzva to bring money for the second tithe to Jerusalem: “And you shall bestow the money on all that your heart desires, on cattle, on sheep, on wine, and on strong drink, and on whatever your soul requests” (Deuteronomy 14:26).
The <i>baraita</i> elaborates: The phrase <b>“And you shall bestow the money on all that your heart desires”</b> is <b>a generalization,</b> as no particular type of food is specified. The phrase <b>“on cattle, on sheep, on wine, and on strong drink”</b> is <b>a detail,</b> as specific foods are mentioned. And when the verse concludes: <b>“On whatever your soul requests,”</b> it <b>then generalized</b> again, as no specific type of food is stated.
Since the verse is formulated as <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization, you may deduce</b> that it is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail. Just as</b> the items mentioned in <b>the detail</b> are clearly <b>defined as the produce of produce,</b> i.e., not only the produce itself but also items that come from it, such as grapes from a seed, <b>and</b> they are also items <b>grown from the ground,</b> as all of these items grow from the ground or receive their main sustenance from it, <b>so too,</b> the generalization includes <b>all</b> items that are <b>the produce of produce and are grown from the ground.</b> This includes birds, but it does not include fish, water, or salt.
§ The Gemara discusses a series of problems with regard to these and other methods of halakhic exegesis: <b>Now,</b> in the case of <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization, one derives</b> that all items which are <b>like the detail</b> are included. However, if that is so, <b>what purpose does the last generalization</b> stated in the verse serve? The same conclusion would be reached if the verse had stated merely a generalization and a detail. The Gemara answers: The <b>purpose</b> of the last generalization is <b>to add all that is similar to it,</b> i.e., even those articles or cases not explicitly listed among the details.
<b>And furthermore,</b> in the case of <b>a detail, and a generalization, and a detail, one</b> again <b>derives</b> that all items which are <b>like the detail</b> are included. If so, <b>what purpose does the last detail</b> stated in the verse serve? The same conclusion would apply if there was simply a detail and a generalization. The Gemara answers: <b>If</b> it were <b>not</b> for <b>the last detail, I would say</b> that <b>the generalization becomes added to the detail,</b> which is broadened in all possible ways. Therefore, the last detail limits the generalization to items or cases that are similar to the detail.
The Gemara continues this line of questioning. <b>And now</b> that it has been established that both with regard to <b>two generalizations and a detail,</b> i.e., a generalization, a detail, and a generalization, <b>and two details and a generalization,</b> i.e., a detail, a generalization, and a detail, <b>one derives</b> that all items that are <b>like the detail</b> are included, <b>what</b> difference <b>is there between this</b> method <b>and that</b> one? The two methods are apparently identical.
The Gemara answers: <b>There is</b> a difference between them, <b>as whereas</b> in a case of <b>two generalizations and a detail, if there is</b> another <b>detail that is similar to</b> the detail specified in the verse <b>even in one aspect, one includes</b> it, due to the two generalizations. By contrast, in the case of <b>two details and a generalization, if there is</b> another <b>detail that is similar to</b> the one mentioned in the verse <b>in two aspects, one includes it.</b> However, if it is similar <b>in</b> only <b>one aspect one does not include</b> it, as the <i>halakha</i> is limited by two details.
The Gemara asks another question: <b>Now,</b> in the method of <b>a detail and a generalization, the generalization becomes added to the detail, and all matters are included</b> by the generalization. <b>And</b> the method of <b>restriction and amplification also amplifies</b> and includes <b>everything, and</b> therefore <b>all matters are included</b> in both cases. If so, <b>what</b> difference <b>is there between</b> the method of <b>restriction and amplification and</b> that of <b>a detail and a generalization?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>There is</b> the following difference, <b>as whereas</b> in the method of <b>a detail and a generalization one includes</b> and renders forbidden to a nazirite <b>even leaves and tendrils</b> of the vine, with the method of <b>restriction and amplification</b> one includes less, as <b>tendrils, yes,</b> they are included in the prohibition, whereas <b>leaves, no,</b> they are not included.
§ <b>Rabbi Abbahu says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> With regard to <b>all prohibitions that are</b> written <b>in the Torah, a permitted</b> substance <b>does not combine with a forbidden</b> substance. If one eats a permitted food with a forbidden food and together they constitute the minimum prohibited measure, he is exempt from punishment for this act of consumption. This principle applies to all <i>halakhot</i> <b>except for the prohibitions of a nazirite,</b> who is liable for eating a mixture of that kind, <b>as the Torah said</b> with regard to a nazirite: “Neither shall he drink anything <b>soaked</b> in grapes” (Numbers 6:3). This verse indicates that a nazirite is prohibited from consuming not only wine and vinegar, but also any food that was soaked in these liquids.

Daf 36a

<b>Ze’eiri says:</b> Permitted and forbidden substances combine with regard to <b>the prohibition against offering leaven</b> on the altar, <b>as well,</b> as it states: “For any [<i>kol</i>] leaven and any [<i>kol</i>] honey shall not be offered as a burnt-offering before the Lord” (Leviticus 2:11). This indicates that one is also liable for sacrificing leaven in a mixture in addition to the liability for sacrificing pure leaven. The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion did Ze’eiri issue his ruling? It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, who derives</b> from the term <b><i>kol</i></b> in the verse “You shall eat nothing [<i>kol</i>] leavened” (Exodus 12:20) that a mixture with part leaven is forbidden on Passover.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>If so,</b> then with regard <b>to the matter of leavened bread</b> on Passover, one should <b>also</b> be liable for eating leaven combined with a permitted substance, e.g., less than an olive-bulk of bread soaked in wine, so that the volume is now an olive-bulk. The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, indeed it is so. Rather,</b> when Ze’eiri specifies that the prohibition applies with regard to sacrificing leaven in offerings, he meant <b>to exclude</b> the statement <b>of Abaye, who says: There is</b> significance to <b>sacrificing less than an olive-bulk</b> of leaven on the altar, i.e., one is flogged for sacrificing an offering of that kind. By noting that one is liable due to the fact that permitted substances combine with forbidden substances, Ze’eiri <b>teaches us</b> that <b>there is no</b> significance to <b>sacrificing less than an olive-bulk,</b> and therefore this is not punishable by lashes.
§ <b>Rav Dimi sat and stated this <i>halakha</i></b> of Rabbi Yoḥanan that a permitted substance does not combine with a forbidden substance except in the case of a nazirite. <b>Abaye raised an objection to his</b> opinion from a mishna (<i>Tevul Yom</i> 2:3): In a case where <b>the thick soup</b> was made with produce that had the status <b>of <i>teruma</i> but the garlic and oil</b> therein were <b>of non-sacred</b> produce, <b>and one</b> who was ritually impure <b>who immersed that day</b> and is waiting for nightfall for the purification process to be completed <b>touched some of</b> the contents, <b>he</b> has <b>disqualified all</b> the contents of the pot from being eaten, as it all is considered <i>teruma</i> soup. However, if <b>the thick soup</b> was made with <b>produce of non-sacred</b> status <b>but the garlic and the oil</b> had the status <b>of <i>teruma</i></b> produce, <b>and one who immersed himself that day touched some of them, he</b> has <b>disqualified only</b> the contents in the <b>place that he touched.</b>
Abaye continues: <b>And we discussed</b> this issue: <b>Why</b> are the contents in the <b>place that he touched disqualified?</b> Since the primary ingredients of the dish are of non-sacred produce, it should not be disqualified by contact with one who immersed himself that day. <b>And Rabba bar bar Ḥana says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: What is the reason</b> that the contents become disqualified? It is <b>due to</b> the fact that <b>a non-priest is flogged for</b> eating <b>an olive-bulk</b> of the soup, as anything into which <i>teruma</i> is mixed is considered <i>teruma</i> by Torah law.
Abaye concludes his question: <b>What is</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan’s <b>reason</b> that one is flogged for eating this mixture?

Daf 36b

Is it <b>not due to</b> the fact <b>that the permitted</b> substance <b>combines with the forbidden</b> substance? This would mean that this principle applies in other areas of Torah law besides naziriteship. Rav Dimi <b>said to him: No; what</b> is the meaning of <b>an olive-bulk</b> in this mishna? It means <b>that there is</b> enough <i>teruma</i> in the mixture so that when one eats from the mixture he will consume <b>an olive-bulk</b> of <i>teruma</i> in the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread. In that case one is liable to receive a punishment for eating this olive-bulk, as though he ate the <i>teruma</i> alone.
Abaye asked him: <b>But is</b> eating an olive-bulk in the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread prohibited <b>by Torah law,</b> and is one flogged for it? Rav Dimi <b>said to him: Yes.</b> Abaye asked in response: <b>If so, why do the Rabbis disagree with Rabbi Eliezer with regard to</b> eating <b>Babylonian <i>kutaḥ</i>,</b> a dip that contains bread, on Passover? The Rabbis maintain that one is not punished by Torah law for eating a mixture that contains leaven. Although the Rabbis do not derive from the term “nothing [<i>kol</i>],” that leaven in a mixture is forbidden, they should nevertheless hold one liable for eating an olive-bulk of a forbidden substance in the time it takes to eat a half-loaf of bread.
Rav Dimi <b>said to</b> Abaye: <b>Leave</b> aside the case of <b>Babylonian <i>kutaḥ</i>, as there is no</b> possibility that one will consume an <b>olive-bulk</b> of the leaven in the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread. <b>If</b> he eats <i>kutaḥ</i> in its pure, unadulterated form, <b>by swallowing [<i>shareif</i>] it</b> as food, not as a dip, <b>his intention is</b> rendered <b>irrelevant by</b> the opinions of <b>all</b> other <b>people.</b> It is unusual for a person to eat a pungent dip by itself, and especially so quickly. One receives no punishment for conduct that anomalous. And <b>if he dips [<i>shatar</i>]</b> other food into the <i>kutaḥ</i> and eats it, he will <b>not</b> be <b>found</b> to have consumed <b>an olive-bulk in</b> the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread. Due to the pungency of the dip, one typically adds only a small portion of it to his food.
Abaye <b>raised an objection to</b> Rav Dimi from a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>two</b> spice <b>mortars, one</b> used <b>for <i>teruma</i></b> spices <b>and one</b> used <b>for non-sacred</b> spices, <b>before which</b> were <b>two pots, one of <i>teruma</i></b> produce <b>and</b> the other <b>one of non-sacred</b> produce, <b>and</b> the contents of <b>these</b> mortars <b>fell into these</b> pots, but it is unknown which produce fell into which pot, the contents of <b>both</b> pots <b>are permitted;</b> the pot containing the <i>teruma</i> produce is permitted for a priest and the pot containing non-sacred produce is permitted for all. This is <b>because I say,</b> with no definitive proof to the contrary, that the <b>non-sacred</b> spices <b>fell into the non-sacred</b> produce <b>and</b> the <b><i>teruma</i></b> spices <b>fell into the <i>teruma</i>.</b>
Abaye explains his objection: <b>And if it would enter your mind</b> to say that eating <b>an olive-bulk</b> of a forbidden substance in the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread is prohibited <b>by Torah law, why do we say</b> this principle: <b>Because I say</b> that the non-sacred spices fell into the non-sacred produce? If the <i>teruma</i> spices fell into the pot containing non-sacred produce, one who eats from the mixture will consume an olive-bulk of <i>teruma</i> within the time it takes to eat a half-loaf of bread, and he will thereby violate a Torah prohibition. One is not lenient in a case of this kind.
Rav Dimi said to him: <b>Rather, what</b> will you say? That the <b>permitted</b> substance <b>combines with the forbidden</b> substance? But if so, one can still ask <b>why we say</b> this principle: <b>Because I say</b> that the non-sacred spices fell into the non-sacred produce. After all, it is possible that the second mortar contained slightly less than an olive-bulk of <i>teruma</i>, and the permitted substance combined with it to form the amount of an olive-bulk. <b>Rather, leave</b> aside the case of <b><i>teruma</i></b> separated <b>from spices,</b> which <b>is</b> <i>teruma</i> <b>by rabbinic law.</b> By Torah law one is required to separate <i>teruma</i> only from grain, wine, and oil. The Sages are lenient with regard to <i>teruma</i> by rabbinic law.
Abaye <b>raised an objection to</b> Rav Dimi from a similar <i>baraita</i>: There were <b>two baskets, one</b> filled <b>with <i>teruma</i> and one</b> filled <b>with non-sacred</b> produce, <b>and before them</b> were <b>two</b> containers each containing <b>a <i>se’a</i></b> of produce, <b>one of non-sacred</b> produce <b>and</b> the <b>one of <i>teruma</i>, and these,</b> the contents of each of the baskets, <b>fell into those,</b> each of the containers before them. Although it is prohibited for non-priests to eat a mixture of <i>teruma</i> and non-sacred produce, and it is possible that the <i>teruma</i> fell into the non-sacred produce, the contents of <b>both</b> of the vessels are nevertheless <b>permitted;</b> the container of <i>teruma</i> produce is permitted for a priest and the container of non-sacred produce is permitted for all. This is <b>because I say</b> that <b>the non-sacred</b> produce <b>fell into the non-sacred</b> produce <b>and the <i>teruma</i> fell into the <i>teruma</i>.</b>
Abaye explains: <b>And if it would enter your mind</b> to say that eating <b>an olive-bulk</b> of forbidden food in the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread is <b>prohibited</b> by Torah law, <b>why do we say</b> the principle: <b>Because I say</b> the non-sacred produce fell into the non-sacred produce? Why are the Sages not concerned that one might eat an olive-bulk of <i>teruma</i> in the time it takes to eat a half-loaf of bread, which is prohibited by Torah law?

Daf 37a

<b>Granted, according to my</b> opinion, <b>as I say</b> that this is <b>because permitted</b> food <b>combines with forbidden</b> food, I can explain that this is referring to a case <b>where</b> there <b>is more non-sacred</b> produce than <i>teruma</i>, and the combination assumes the status of the forbidden item or <i>teruma</i> only when those are the majority. <b>However, according to your</b> opinion, <b>that you say</b> this is <b>because there is an olive-bulk</b> consumed in the time it takes <b>to eat a half-loaf</b> of bread, then even <b>if</b> there <b>is more non-sacred</b> produce, <b>what of it?</b> In any case there is an olive-bulk of <i>teruma</i> eaten within the time it takes to eat a half-loaf of bread.
Rav Dimi <b>said to</b> Abaye: <b>Leave</b> aside <b><i>teruma</i> in the present,</b> as it applies <b>by rabbinic law.</b> Since the exile of the Jewish people from Eretz Yisrael, the <i>halakhot</i> of <i>teruma</i> and tithes apply by rabbinic law, not Torah law. This is the basis for the lenient ruling with regard to this mixture.
<b>Abaye said to him: From where</b> do you derive <b>that this</b> verse: “Nor shall he drink anything <b>soaked</b> in grapes” (Numbers 6:3), <b>comes</b> to teach the principle that <b>permitted</b> food <b>combines with forbidden</b> food, as stated by Rabbi Yoḥanan (35b)? <b>Perhaps</b> instead <b>it comes to establish</b> the principle that the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> of a forbidden food is <b>like</b> that of its <b>substance.</b> This principle states that any food that absorbs the taste of a forbidden item assumes the status of this forbidden item itself.
The Gemara expresses surprise at Abaye’s question. <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye, initially that which Rav Dimi said was difficult for him.</b> Rav Dimi had cited Rabbi Yoḥanan as saying that permitted food combines with forbidden foods only in the case of naziriteship (36a), due to the term “soaked,” <b>and</b> Abaye <b>had objected</b> with <b>all these</b> aforementioned <b>refutations</b> to prove that this principle applies in all areas of Torah law. And yet <b>he then said to him</b> that one should derive a very different principle from that same verse, that the verse <b>establishes</b> the principle that the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> of a forbidden food is <b>like</b> that of its <b>substance.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>After</b> Rav Dimi <b>resolved</b> Abaye’s difficulties, and Abaye had accepted his answer that the principle that permitted food combines with forbidden food does not apply to the rest of Torah law, <b>he said</b> to Rav Dimi that <b>perhaps</b> the verse <b>comes to establish</b> that the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> of a forbidden food is <b>like</b> that of its <b>substance,</b> with the following application.
This is relevant <b>for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The term <b>“soaked”</b> serves <b>to establish</b> the principle that the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> of a forbidden food is <b>like</b> that of its <b>substance. As, if</b> a nazirite soaked <b>grapes in water and</b> the water <b>has the taste of wine,</b> he <b>is liable</b> to receive punishment for drinking this liquid, as it assumes the status of wine. <b>And from here you derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to all prohibitions of the Torah;</b> in all cases, the legal status of the taste of a forbidden food is like that of its substance. The fact that with regard to all other prohibitions, the legal status of the flavor of a forbidden food is like that of its substance, is derived from the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship.
The <i>baraita</i> explains the derivation: <b>And just as</b> with regard to <b>a nazirite, whose prohibition</b> against eating grapes <b>is not a permanent prohibition,</b> as he will be permitted to eat grapes once his term of naziriteship is over, <b>and</b> furthermore <b>his prohibition is not a prohibition</b> against deriving <b>benefit</b> from wine, <b>and there is</b> a way <b>to permit his prohibition</b> against eating grape products by requesting from a halakhic authority to dissolve his vow, and nevertheless, in <b>his</b> case the Torah <b>rendered</b> the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> of food <b>like</b> that of its <b>substance.</b> With regard to a forbidden mixture of <b>diverse kinds in a vineyard,</b> i.e., grain seeds sown with grape seeds, <b>whose prohibition is a permanent prohibition and whose prohibition is a prohibition</b> against deriving <b>benefit, and there is no</b> way <b>to permit their prohibition, is it not right that</b> the Torah <b>should render</b> the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> of its forbidden food <b>like</b> that of its <b>substance?</b>
The <i>baraita</i> adds: <b>And the same is true for the prohibition against eating the fruit of a tree during the first three years after its planting [<i>orla</i>], on two</b> of three counts: Although the prohibition of <i>orla</i> is not a permanent prohibition, as one may eat the fruit of this tree after three years have passed, it is prohibited to derive benefit from <i>orla</i>, and this prohibition cannot be permitted, as the fruits that grow during the first three years remain forbidden. Similarly, all other prohibitions in the Torah are more severe than the case of a nazirite in one of these aspects, and therefore this principle is universal. Abaye is asking Rav Dimi: In any case doesn’t this entire derivation present a difficulty for Rabbi Yoḥanan, who derives a different <i>halakha</i> from the term “soaked”?
<b>One of the Sages said to</b> Abaye: <b>When Rabbi Abbahu said</b> that Rabbi Yoḥanan said that the principle of permitted food combining with forbidden food is derived from the term “soaked,” he spoke in accordance with the opinion <b>of Rabbi Akiva.</b> The Gemara asks: To <b>which</b> statement of <b>Rabbi Akiva</b> is the Gemara referring? <b>If we say</b> it is referring to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva of this</b> mishna, <b>as we learned</b> (<i>Shevuot</i> 21b) that <b>Rabbi Akiva says: Even</b> if <b>a nazirite soaked his bread in wine, and</b> the bread and the wine <b>contain</b> enough <b>to combine to</b> constitute <b>an olive-bulk, he is liable; but from where</b> do we know that Rabbi Akiva means an olive-bulk taken from the bread and the wine together? <b>Perhaps that</b> ruling applies only <b>when there is an olive-bulk</b> of wine <b>as is,</b> without the bread?
<b>And if you would say:</b> In that case, <b>what</b> is the purpose <b>of stating</b> this ruling? What is the novelty of Rabbi Akiva’s statement if the mixture contains an olive-bulk of wine? One can say that it serves <b>to exclude</b> the opinion of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>, who said</b> that he is liable <b>only if he drinks a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of wine.</b> Rabbi Akiva emphasizes that one is liable even if he drinks the amount of an olive-bulk.
<b>Rather,</b> the reference is to the following statement of <b>Rabbi Akiva in a <i>baraita</i>. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Akiva says: A nazirite who soaked his bread in wine and ate an olive-bulk of</b> the mixture of <b>bread and wine is liable.</b> This <i>baraita</i> indicates that according to the opinion of Rabbi Akiva a permitted substance combines with a forbidden substance.
<b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Avya, said to Rav Ashi: According to Rabbi Akiva, who establishes</b> the verse <b>“neither</b> shall he drink <b>anything soaked”</b> (Numbers 6:3) as referring <b>to</b> the principle that the <b>permitted combines with the forbidden, from where does he</b> derive the principle that the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> is <b>like</b> that of the <b>substance</b> itself? The Gemara answers: <b>He derives</b> this principle <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>meat</b> cooked <b>in milk.</b> Is it <b>not</b> the case that there is no actual milk present, and it <b>is merely</b> the <b>taste</b> of the milk absorbed in the meat, <b>and</b> yet the mixture <b>is prohibited? Here too,</b> in the case of other prohibitions, it <b>is no different,</b> and the legal status of the flavor is like that of the substance itself.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Akiva and derive the <i>halakha</i> that the legal status of the flavor is like that of the substance itself from the phrase “neither shall he drink anything soaked,” why do they not derive this principle from the case of meat cooked in milk? The Gemara answers: The Rabbis claim that <b>we do not derive</b> other prohibitions <b>from meat</b> cooked <b>in milk, as</b> that prohibition <b>is a novelty,</b> and one does not learn general <i>halakhot</i> from unusual cases.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the <b>novelty</b> of that prohibition? <b>If we say that</b> it is unique in <b>that this</b> meat <b>alone and that</b> milk <b>alone</b> are each <b>permitted, and</b> yet <b>together they are forbidden,</b> that characteristic is not unique to meat cooked in milk. In the case of forbidden mixtures of <b>diverse kinds too, this</b> element <b>alone and that</b> element <b>alone</b> are each <b>permitted, and</b> yet <b>together they are forbidden.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Rather,</b> the novelty is <b>that if one soaks</b> meat <b>in milk all day, it is permitted</b> by Torah law, despite the fact that the meat certainly absorbed some taste of the milk, whereas if <b>one cooked</b> meat in milk even for a short time, the mixture <b>is forbidden</b> by Torah law. The novelty is that it is not the fact that they are mixed together that renders meat and milk forbidden, but the act of cooking.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Akiva too,</b> he certainly agrees that the <i>halakha</i> of <b>meat</b> cooked <b>in milk is a novelty.</b> How can he derive a general principle from this case? <b>Rather,</b>

Daf 37b

Rabbi Akiva <b>derives</b> the principle: The legal status of the flavor of forbidden food is like that of the substance itself, <b>from</b> the vessels <b>of gentiles</b> that require <b>purging [<i>gi’ulei</i>],</b> where the Jews were commanded to purge the non-kosher flavor from the vessels they seized from the Midianites. <b>As the Merciful One states</b> in the section of the Torah that deals with the spoils of Midian: <b>“Every thing that passes through the fire,</b> you shall make it pass through the fire” (Numbers 31:23). <b>That is to say that</b> the vessels <b>of gentiles</b> that require <b>purging are forbidden</b> to be used until they have been purged through fire and purified. <b>Isn’t</b> there <b>mere taste</b> absorbed in the vessels through the process of cooking? <b>And</b> even so, these vessels are <b>forbidden</b> if this purging was not performed. <b>Here, too,</b> with regard to other matters of Torah law, it <b>is no different;</b> and the legal status of the flavor is like that of the substance itself.
Rav Aḥa further suggested to Rav Ashi: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who derive this principle from the verse: “Neither shall he drink anything soaked” (Numbers 6:3), <b>let them also derive</b> it <b>from</b> the vessels of <b>gentiles</b> that require <b>purging,</b> as does Rabbi Akiva. Rav Ashi <b>said to him:</b> The Rabbis maintain that <b>there</b> the <i>halakha</i> of purging vessels of gentiles <b>is</b> also <b>a novelty.</b> What is the novelty of this <i>halakha</i>? <b>As</b> with regard <b>to all</b> dietary laws in the <b>Torah, anything that gives flavor,</b> i.e., contributes taste, that renders the food <b>tainted,</b> is <b>permitted.</b> If the taste added by the forbidden food does not enhance the permitted food, it does not render that food forbidden.
<b>But</b> here, <b>with regard to</b> the <i>halakha</i> of vessels of <b>gentiles</b> that require <b>purging,</b> the Torah states that even if they contribute taste that renders the food tainted, nevertheless they are <b>forbidden.</b> If twenty-four hours have passed since food was cooked in a pot, the assumption is that the flavor released from the pot will contribute a deleterious taste to any foods subsequently cooked in the pot. Even so, vessels taken from gentiles remain forbidden until they have been purged, despite the fact that the taste they contribute taints the food.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva too,</b> the case of vessels of gentiles that require purging <b>is</b> apparently <b>a novelty.</b> Rabbi Akiva derives this principle from the required purging of the vessels of gentiles. How then does he respond to the previous claim? <b>Rav Huna, son of Ḥiyya, said: The Torah prohibited</b> the use of unpurged vessels of gentiles <b>only in</b> the case of <b>a pot</b> that was used <b>on that day, which is not</b> a case where the pot <b>gives flavor</b> that renders the food <b>tainted.</b> Accordingly, it is not a novelty that the vessels were forbidden.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> why don’t <b>the Rabbis</b> derive the <i>halakha</i> from there, as it is no longer a novelty? The Gemara answers: They hold that <b>even</b> in the case of <b>a pot</b> used <b>on that day, it is impossible that</b> the vessel <b>does not slightly taint</b> the food absorbed in the vessel. Consequently, the <i>halakha</i> of purging vessels of gentiles is a novelty, from which general principles cannot be derived.
§ <b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Avya, said to Rav Ashi: From</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, we infer</b> the proper understanding of the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva. Don’t the Rabbis say</b> that the term <b>“soaked” teaches</b> that the principle that the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> of forbidden food is <b>like</b> that of its <b>substance</b> applies not only to a nazirite, but that <b>from here you derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to all prohibitions of the Torah? According to Rabbi Akiva as well, who establishes this</b> term: <b>“Soaked,” as</b> teaching that <b>the permitted</b> substance <b>combines with the forbidden</b> substance with regard to a nazirite, <b>let us say</b> that <b>from here you derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to all prohibitions of the Torah.</b> This explanation runs counter to the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan, who applies this principle only to a nazirite.
Rav Ashi <b>said to him:</b> This cannot serve as a proof, <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>a nazirite and</b> those of <b>a sin-offering</b> are from <b>two verses that come as one,</b> i.e., to teach the same matter, <b>and any two verses that come as one do not teach</b> their common aspect to apply to other cases. The Gemara comments: The derivation that a permitted substance joins together with a forbidden substance in the case of <b>a nazirite</b> is <b>that which we stated,</b> from the term “soaked.” With regard to <b>a sin-offering, what is</b> the derivation that a permitted substance combines with a forbidden substance?
It is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse discussing a sin-offering <b>“Anything that touches in its flesh shall become consecrated”</b> (Leviticus 6:20). One <b>might</b> have thought that non-sacred meat that touched any part of a sin-offering is consecrated <b>even if it did not absorb</b> the taste of the sin-offering it touched. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “In its flesh,”</b> to teach that this meat is not consecrated <b>until</b> the taste of the sin-offering <b>is absorbed within its flesh.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>“Shall become consecrated,”</b> means that its legal status <b>becomes like</b> that of the sin-offering itself; <b>that</b> is, <b>if</b> the sin-offering <b>is disqualified,</b> the non-sacred meat that touched it <b>will</b> also <b>be disqualified. And if</b> the sin-offering <b>is valid,</b> the non-sacred meat that touched it <b>may be eaten in accordance with the more stringent</b> standards <b>of</b> a sin-offering, with regard to when and where it may be eaten. The principle that a permitted substance combines with a forbidden substance is thereby stated in the case of a sin-offering as well. Consequently, the principle cannot be extended to the entire Torah, as a <i>halakha</i> stated in two cases is not applied elsewhere.
<b>And the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Akiva, would maintain that both these sources <b>are necessary,</b> as neither the case of a nazirite nor the case of a sin-offering could be derived from the other. Therefore, this is not a case of two verses that come as one. The Gemara elaborates: <b>For had the Merciful One written</b> this <i>halakha</i> only <b>with regard to a sin-offering, I would say</b> that the case of <b>a nazirite cannot be derived from it, due to</b> the fact that <b>we do not derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a nazirite from</b> that of <b>consecrated</b> items. This is because the prohibitions of naziriteship are unrelated to the Temple and offerings, and therefore it is possible that the <i>halakha</i> in question is unique to consecrated items.
<b>And</b> conversely, <b>had the Merciful One written</b> this <i>halakha</i> only <b>with regard to a nazirite, I would say</b> that <b>we do not derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a sin-offering <b>from</b> that of <b>a nazirite due to</b> the fact <b>that its prohibition is stringent.</b> In what way? It is stringent <b>as even a grape seed is forbidden to</b> a nazirite, although the seeds are not usually eaten. <b>Therefore,</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a sin-offering <b>cannot be derived from</b> the case of a nazirite, which means that this is not a case of two verses that come as one. Consequently, one can derive a general <i>halakha</i> from them.
<b>And Rabbi Akiva</b> could have <b>said to you,</b> in rebuttal of this claim: <b>Why are</b> these cases both <b>necessary? Granted, if the Merciful One had written</b> this principle only with regard <b>to a sin-offering,</b> one would <b>not derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a nazirite from it,</b> as <b>we do not derive</b> <i>halakhot</i> of <b>non-sacred</b> items <b>from</b> those of <b>consecrated</b> items. Certain stringencies and strictures apply only to consecrated property. <b>However, let the Merciful One write</b> this principle <b>with regard to a nazirite, and you</b> could <b>derive</b> the case of the <b>sin-offering from</b> that of a nazirite, just <b>as</b> the application of this principle to <b>all prohibitions in the Torah is derived from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a nazirite.</b> Since it is not necessary to state this principle in both cases, one cannot derive a general principle from them.
The Gemara asks: <b>And the Rabbis,</b> who maintain that the principle that the legal status of the flavor of a forbidden substance is like that of the substance itself is not limited to these two cases, <b>would say to you</b> that both sources are required. The case of <b>a sin-offering</b> is necessary <b>to</b> derive the principle <b>that a permitted</b> substance <b>combines with a forbidden</b> substance, <b>and</b> this stringency is not extrapolated to other prohibitions, in accordance with the principle that <b>one cannot derive</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>non-sacred</b> items <b>from consecrated</b> items.
<b>And</b> the term <b>“soaked,”</b> which appears in the context of a nazirite, <b>teaches</b> the principle that the legal status of the <b>flavor</b> of forbidden food is <b>like</b> that of its <b>substance.</b> Since both examples are necessary, they are not considered two verses that come as one, and it is therefore possible to derive a general principle from them. <b>And</b> consequently, <b>from here you derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to all of the prohibitions of the Torah.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> how would <b>Rabbi Akiva</b> respond to this claim? The Gemara answers: Rabbi Akiva would say that <b>both</b> cases teach the principle <b>that a permitted</b> substance <b>combines with a forbidden</b> substance, <b>and they are two verses that come as one,</b> to teach about the same issue. <b>And</b> the rule is that <b>any two verses that come as one do not teach</b> their common aspect to apply to other cases.
§ <b>Rav Ashi said to Rav Kahana: But</b> consider <b>that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>. The verse “He shall not eat <b>anything that is made of the grapevine”</b> (Numbers 6:4) <b>taught with regard to prohibitions of a nazirite that</b> these substances <b>combine with each other.</b> If a nazirite ate only a small amount of each substance, which together amount to the measure that determines liability, he is liable. <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, now</b> that <b>one says</b> that <b>permitted</b> substances <b>combine with forbidden</b> substances, <b>is it necessary</b> to teach that one <b>forbidden</b> substance <b>combines with</b> another <b>forbidden</b> substance? According to Rabbi Akiva’s opinion, this derivation is apparently unnecessary.
Rav Kahana <b>said to</b> Rav Ashi that this derivation is necessary because the two cases are not identical: <b>Permitted</b> substances combine <b>with forbidden</b> substances only when they are eaten <b>simultaneously,</b> whereas <b>forbidden</b> substances <b>combine with</b> other <b>forbidden</b> substances <b>even</b> when eaten <b>one after the other.</b> Therefore, according to the opinion of Rabbi Akiva, if a nazirite eats half an olive-bulk of grape skins and then eats half an olive-bulk of grape seeds, he is liable.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Shimon,</b>

Daf 38a

<b>who is not of</b> the opinion that the principle of <b>combination</b> is operative, as he renders one liable for consumption of forbidden substances of any amount (see <i>Makkot</i> 13a), <b>what does he derive</b> from the verse <b>“anything that is made</b> of the grapevine” (Numbers 6:4)? The Gemara answers: Rabbi Shimon could have <b>said to you: That</b> verse is <b>necessary</b> to teach that <b>one is never</b> considered <b>a nazirite until he vows naziriteship from all of them.</b> Rabbi Shimon maintains that if one vows to be a nazirite only with regard to one or two of the prohibitions of naziriteship, the vow does not take effect at all.
<b>Rabbi Abbahu said</b> that <b>Rabbi Elazar said:</b> With regard to <b>every</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>that is in the Torah</b> that involves <b>a quarter-</b><i>log</i>, <b>a permitted</b> substance <b>does not combine with a forbidden</b> substance to complete this measure, <b>apart from the quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of a nazirite.</b> In the case of a nazirite, permitted liquid combines with wine to render him liable, <b>as the Torah stated: “Soaked”</b> (Numbers 6:3). The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> difference <b>is there between</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> who stated (35b) that a permitted substance does not combine with a forbidden one with regard to any prohibition of the Torah apart from that of a nazirite, <b>and</b> that of <b>Rabbi Elazar,</b> who apparently says the same thing in different terms?
The Gemara answers: <b>There is</b> a difference <b>between them, as Rabbi Yoḥanan amplifies</b> the <i>halakha</i> to include <b>even foods,</b> i.e., he renders a nazirite liable for eating an olive-bulk of bread and grapes combined, <b>and Rabbi Elazar</b> maintains that with regard to <b>liquids, yes,</b> the permitted combines with the forbidden, but for <b>other matters,</b> i.e., solids, <b>no,</b> the different foods do not combine.
§ Apropos a quarter-<i>log</i>, the Gemara cites a statement that <b>Rabbi Elazar says: There are ten</b> applications of the <b>quarter</b>-<i>log</i> measurement in various areas of <i>halakha</i>, <b>and Rav Kahana held in his hand</b> the following mnemonic for them: <b>Five</b> are <b>red and five</b> are <b>white.</b> The Gemara elaborates: The <b>five red</b> ones of wine and blood are listed in the following mnemonic: <b>A nazirite; and one who performs</b> the ritual of <b>the Paschal offering; who instructed; in the Temple; and they died.</b>
The Gemara explains this mnemonic: <b>A nazirite,</b> this is referring to the <b>quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of wine for</b> which <b>a nazirite</b> is liable for drinking. <b>Who performs</b> the ritual of the <b>Paschal offering,</b> this is <b>as Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Shmuel says:</b> It is a mitzva to drink <b>these four cups</b> at the Passover seder and <b>they must</b> each <b>contain</b> the amount of <b>a quarter</b>-<i>log</i>. <b>Who instructed,</b> this is referring to the following <i>halakha</i>: One who <b>drank a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of wine may not issue rulings</b> to others in matters of <i>halakha</i>, lest he err. <b>In the Temple,</b> this is referring to the <i>halakha</i> that one who <b>drank a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of wine and entered the Temple is liable</b> to receive <b>the death penalty</b> (see Leviticus 10:9).
<b>And they died,</b> this is <b>as it is taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Oholot</i> 2:10): <b>From where</b> is it derived with regard <b>to a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of blood that emerges from two corpses, that it renders</b> people and objects <b>ritually impure in a tent,</b> i.e., a house, meaning that one who enters that house contracts ritual impurity imparted by a corpse? <b>As it is stated,</b> with regard to the prohibition against ritual impurity for priests: <b>“Neither shall he go in to any dead bodies”</b> (Leviticus 21:11). The plural form indicates that the blood of two people joins together to form the minimum amount of a quarter-<i>log</i> for ritual impurity in a tent.
<b>And</b> the <b>five white</b> cases of oil and water are listed in this mnemonic: <b>The loaf of, a nazirite, and a leper, which were disqualified, on Shabbat.</b> The Gemara elaborates: <b>The loaf of,</b> this is referring to the <b>quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of oil</b> added <b>to the loaves</b> of a thanks-offering. <b>A nazirite,</b> this is referring to the <b>quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of oil for</b> the wafers of <b>a nazirite,</b> which were brought with his offering. <b>A leper,</b> this is referring to the <b>quarter</b>-<i>log</i> of spring <b>water</b> into which a bird is slaughtered <b>for</b> the ritual purification of <b>a leper</b> (see Leviticus 14:5).<b>Which were disqualified,</b> this is <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Me’ila</i> 17b): <b>And all other ritually impure liquids disqualify the body</b> to the extent that one who drinks them may not eat <i>teruma</i>, if one drank the amount <b>of</b> one <b>quarter</b>-<i>log</i>.
<b>On Shabbat,</b> this is <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna which lists the minimum amounts of various liquids for which one is liable for violating the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat for carrying them from one domain to another. At the conclusion of this list, the mishna states (<i>Shabbat</i> 76b): <b>And</b> the measure that creates liability for carrying <b>all other liquids,</b> those not specified in the list, is <b>a quarter</b>-<i>log</i>, <b>and</b> the measure for carrying <b>all waste water</b> is likewise <b>a quarter</b>-<i>log</i>. This completes the list of ten <i>halakhot</i> that feature the quarter-<i>log</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>And are there no more</b> than ten? <b>But there is</b> also the following mishna (<i>Yadayim</i> 1:1): <b>With a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> of water <b>one can wash the hands of one</b> person before eating bread, <b>and</b> this amount can be used <b>even for two</b> people, if they do so in the correct manner. The Gemara answers: Rabbi Elazar, who listed ten cases, <b>does not deal</b> with <i>halakhot</i> that are subject <b>to a dispute,</b> and some Sages disagree with the ruling that two people can wash their hands with a single quarter-<i>log</i>.
The Gemara further asks: <b>But there is</b> this <i>halakha</i> concerning a <i>sota</i> (<i>Sota</i> 15b): The priest who dealt with a <i>sota</i> <b>would bring an earthenware vessel [<i>pailei</i>] and place in it a half</b>-<b><i>log</i> of water from the basin</b> in the Temple, <b>and Rabbi Yehuda says</b> it was <b>a quarter</b>-<i>log</i>. This is another case involving a quarter-<i>log</i>. The Gemara answers as before, that Rabbi Elazar <b>does not deal</b> with <i>halakhot</i> that are subject <b>to a dispute,</b> and this quarter-<i>log</i> applies only according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda.
The Gemara continues to ask. <b>But there is</b> the following <i>halakha</i>: <b>How much water must one place in</b> a vessel that contains urine, before he can pray nearby? <b>Any quantity</b> is sufficient. <b>Rabbi Zakkai said: A quarter</b>-<i>log</i>. The Gemara again answers that Rabbi Elazar <b>does not deal</b> with <i>halakhot</i> that are subject <b>to a dispute.</b> The Gemara suggests another example: <b>But there is</b> the case of <b>a ritual bath,</b> as it is taught that one may immerse extremely small vessels in a quarter-<i>log</i> of rainwater in the ground. The Gemara answers: His list <b>excludes that</b> case, <b>as the Sages annulled</b> that <i>halakha</i> by ruling that one must use a standard ritual bath of forty <i>se’a</i> even for extremely small vessels.

Daf 38b

§ The mishna taught: <b>And he is liable</b> to receive lashes <b>only if he eats an olive-bulk of the grapes.</b> The mishna continues by noting that with regard to drinking, the first <i>tanna</i> holds that a nazirite is liable for a quarter-<i>log</i>, whereas Rabbi Akiva maintains that the amount of an olive-bulk applies to liquids as well.
