The Gemara explains this dispute: <b>The first <i>tanna</i> does not liken all the</b> other <b>prohibitions of a nazirite to</b> the prohibition of <b>drinking</b> wine, and therefore the measurements for eating and drinking are the same as for prohibitions in other contexts: An olive-bulk of food and a quarter-<i>log</i> of drink. <b>And Rabbi Akiva</b> maintains that <b>since it is written: “Nor eat fresh grapes or dried”</b> (Numbers 6:3), this verse teaches that <b>just as eating</b> is measured by the amount of <b>an olive-bulk, so too, all the prohibitions</b> of a nazirite are measured by <b>an olive-bulk.</b>
§ The mishna further taught: <b>And he is liable for</b> consuming <b>wine by itself,</b> and for grapes by themselves, and for grape seeds by themselves, and for grape skins by themselves. <b>The Sages taught:</b> The verse: <b>“Nor eat fresh grapes or dried”</b> (Numbers 6:3), serves <b>to render</b> him <b>liable for this by itself and for that by itself,</b> i.e., that he need not consume all of the grape products listed in the verses. <b>From here you derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to all prohibitions of the Torah: Just as here there is one type</b> of food, grapes, <b>which are</b> called by <b>two names,</b> fresh or dried, <b>and he is liable for this by itself and for that by itself, so too,</b> in <b>all</b> cases where there <b>is one type and they are</b> called by <b>two names, one is liable for this by itself and for that by itself.</b>
The Gemara adds that in the case of a nazirite this principle serves <b>to include new wine,</b> i.e., grape juice, <b>and grapes.</b> Although these are apparently a single type, as the juice, before fermenting, is merely grapes in liquid form, nevertheless one is liable for grape juice and grapes separately, as they have different names.
§ <b>Abaye says:</b> A nazirite who <b>ate a grape seed is flogged twice,</b> i.e., two sets of thirty-nine lashes, one set for the specific prohibition stated with regard to grape seeds, and the other for the general prohibition “anything that is made of the grapevine” (Numbers 6:4). Likewise, if he <b>ate a grape skin he is flogged twice.</b> If he <b>ate a grape seed and a grape skin he is flogged three</b> times, for the seed, for the skin, and for the prohibition “anything that is made of the grapevine.” <b>Rava says: He is flogged only once</b> if he ate a seed or a skin. He is not flogged for “anything that is made of the grapevine,” <b>because one is not flogged for</b> violating <b>a general prohibition.</b> One is not liable to receive lashes for violation of a single prohibition that includes many items or cases.
<b>Rav Pappa raised an objection</b> to Abaye’s statement. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: A nazirite who was drinking wine all day is liable to</b> receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. If two witnesses <b>said to him</b> in warning: <b>Do not drink, do not drink, and he</b> continued <b>drinking, he is liable for each and every one</b> of the warnings that was followed by an act of drinking. If he <b>ate grapes and raisins, grape seeds and grape skins, and squeezed a cluster of grapes and drank</b> the juice, <b>he is flogged five</b> times. The difficulty for Abaye is as follows: <b>If so,</b> that Abaye’s opinion is accepted, <b>let him be flogged six</b> times, to include <b>one</b> more <b>for</b> transgressing the verse <b>“anything that is made</b> of the grapevine” (Numbers 6:4).
Abaye responded: Rabbi Eliezer <b>taught</b> certain prohibitions <b>and omitted</b> others. He did not include all the prohibitions for which one is liable to be flogged. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> else <b>did he omit that he omitted this?</b> He would not have listed all relevant prohibitions less one. The Gemara answers: <b>He</b> also <b>omitted</b> the additional prohibition of <b>“He shall not profane his word”</b> (Numbers 30:3), as the nazirite profaned his word by violating his vow of naziriteship.
The Gemara retorts: <b>If</b> it is <b>due to that</b> verse, this <b>is not an omission, as when</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>teaches</b> and lists the lashes which a nazirite is liable to receive, he includes only <b>matters that are not</b> found <b>elsewhere,</b> i.e., he mentions only prohibitions that apply exclusively to a nazirite. The prohibition <b>“He shall not profane his word” is</b> relevant <b>to vows</b> in general, and therefore he omitted it. Consequently, Rabbi Eliezer should have mentioned the prohibition “anything that is made of the grapevine,” and the fact that he did not do so presents a difficulty for the opinion of Abaye.
<b>Ravina from Parzakya said to Rav Ashi: But he omitted</b> the case <b>of in between.</b> Rabbi Eliezer could have added that a nazirite is liable not only for the seed and skin of a grape but also for the substances in between them, as the verse: “From pits to grape skin” (Numbers 6:4), comes to include all that is in between the two (see 34b). <b>Rather, Rav Pappa said:</b> Actually, Rabbi Eliezer <b>did not teach anything</b> about <b>five</b> sets of lashes, but merely stated that he is flogged for all those actions, without enumerating the precise number. Therefore, this source does not present a difficulty for Abaye. The Gemara asks: <b>But</b>

Daf 39a

Rav Pappa himself <b>raised</b> his <b>objection to</b> Abaye’s opinion based on the premise that the <i>tanna</i> taught <b>five</b> sets of lashes, <b>and since</b> in the <i>baraita</i> it <b>is not taught five</b> sets of lashes, <b>what is the reason</b> that Rav Pappa <b>raised</b> his <b>objection to</b> Abaye’s opinion? <b>Rav Pappa said: I held</b> that <b>it is not a tradition in</b> Abaye’s <b>hand</b> that a nazirite is also flogged for “anything that is made of the grapevine,” but merely his own opinion, <b>and</b> therefore I assumed that were I to cite a <i>baraita</i> that explicitly contradicts his opinion <b>he</b> would <b>retract</b> his opinion. Consequently, I myself changed the wording of the <i>baraita</i> to see how Abaye would respond. <b>But I did not know that</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>is a tradition in</b> Abaye’s <b>hand and</b> that therefore <b>he did not</b> retract his opinion.
§ The mishna taught: <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says:</b> He is liable only if he eats at least two <i>ḥartzannim</i> and one <i>zag</i> that together form the volume of an olive-bulk. The mishna then cites two opinions as to whether <i>ḥartzan</i> is a grape seed and <i>zag</i> is the skin, or the reverse. <b>Rav Yosef said: In accordance with whose</b> opinion do <b>we translate</b> the verse “from <i>ḥartzannim</i> to <i>zag</i>” (Numbers 6:4) as: <b>From the seeds [<i>purtzanin</i>] to the skins [<i>itzurin</i>]?</b> He explains: This translation is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A naziriteship</b> of <b>unspecified</b> length lasts for <b>thirty days.</b> If a nazirite <b>shaved</b> his hair during that period, <b>or if he was shaved by bandits [<i>listim</i>]</b> against his will, this <b>negates thirty days</b> of his naziriteship, which he must count afresh. With regard to <b>a nazirite who shaved</b> his hair, <b>whether</b> he did so <b>with scissors or with a razor, or</b> if he <b>pulled out [<i>sifsef</i> ] any amount,</b> he <b>is liable.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Does <b>this hair</b> that grows on the body <b>grow from the bottom or from the top?</b> Which part of the hair is new? The Gemara explains: <b>What</b> is the <b>difference</b> between these possibilities? The Gemara answers: It is relevant for the case <b>of a nazirite who was shaved by bandits</b> or shaved himself, <b>but</b> his head was not entirely shaved. Rather, <b>they left</b> part <b>of it,</b> so that the hair is long <b>enough to bend its end to its root.</b>
The Gemara explains the two sides of the dilemma: <b>If you say</b> that hair <b>grows from the bottom, he has removed</b> the hair of <b>the naziriteship,</b> which is the hair he vowed not to shave, and therefore he must add extra days to his term of naziriteship to allow for its proper growth. <b>But if you say that</b> it <b>grows from the top, that</b> hair <b>which he sanctified still exists</b> in part. Consequently, he may conclude his naziriteship and perform the mitzva of shaving.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a solution to this dilemma <b>from</b> an examination of <b>this live louse [<i>inba</i>], which is</b> always <b>found in the root of the hair. And if it should enter your mind</b> that hair <b>grows from the bottom,</b> the louse <b>should be situated on the end of the hair,</b> as it would be pushed up with the hair’s growth. The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>Actually,</b> one could say that hair <b>grows from the bottom, and because it is alive the louse continuously descends,</b> as it is always crawling toward the scalp from where it draws its nourishment.
The Gemara suggests another proof: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> from the fact that <b>a dead louse is</b> invariably <b>found on the end of the hair. And if it should enter your mind</b> that hair <b>grows from on top,</b> it <b>should be situated on the root of the hair.</b> The Gemara rejects this: <b>There too,</b> one could say that <b>because it has no strength it is caught</b> where it was when it died <b>and goes</b> up with the growing hair.
The Gemara proposes a different proof: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear from</b> an examination of <b>the long hair [<i>belorit</i>] of gentiles,</b> which is grown for idolatrous purposes, by braiding the ends of the hair. <b>As, after they braid it, it becomes loose underneath,</b> which suggests that hair grows from below. The Gemara rejects this: <b>There too, since it crumples from</b> the effect of the person who <b>lies</b> upon it, <b>it becomes loose</b> underneath.
The Gemara offers an additional suggestion: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> from <b>the dye</b> used on sheep, <b>as the</b> dyed <b>wool becomes loose underneath.</b> This shows that hair grows from the bottom. The Gemara adds: <b>And</b> this case of dyeing sheep is not merely anecdotal, as it <b>was taught</b> with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of animal tithe. <b>And furthermore, when old men dye their beards,</b> we see that the beards <b>turn white</b>

Daf 39b

at <b>the roots of their hair.</b> You can <b>learn from it that</b> hair <b>grows from the bottom,</b> as the new hair is not dyed. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>learn from it</b> that it is so.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> what about <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>that is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a nazirite who was shaved by bandits, and they left him</b> with <b>enough</b> hair <b>to bend its end to its root,</b> this <b>does not negate</b> the days of his naziriteship. <b>And if it should enter your mind</b> that hair <b>grows from the bottom, let</b> it <b>negate</b> the days of his naziriteship in that case as well, as the remaining hair grew only after his acceptance of naziriteship. The Gemara answers: This is referring to a case <b>where they shaved him after the completion</b> of his naziriteship but before he sacrificed his offerings, <b>and whose</b> opinion is expressed in this <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, who says:</b> With regard to <b>any</b> nazirite who became ritually impure <b>after the completion</b> of his term, this <b>negates</b> only <b>seven</b> days.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason of Rabbi Eliezer?</b> The Gemara answers: He <b>derives</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>the shaving of ritual purity</b> at the end of his term of naziriteship (see Numbers 6:18) <b>from the shaving of impurity</b> (see Numbers 6:9): <b>Just as</b> with the <b>shaving of impurity,</b> if he became impure on the day of the completion of his term he must wait <b>seven</b> days, at which point he is purified from the impurity imparted by a corpse and shaves his hair, <b>so too</b> with the <b>shaving of purity;</b> if he was shaved before he brought his offerings, he negates only <b>seven</b> days.
<b>And the Sages,</b> including Rabbi Eliezer, <b>have an</b> accepted <b>tradition</b> that <b>every seven days hair grows enough to bend its end to its root.</b> Consequently, if this amount of hair remained after he was shaved on the day of the completion of his naziriteship, he does not forfeit any days and need not wait any longer.
§ The mishna taught: With regard to <b>a nazirite who shaved</b> his hair, <b>whether</b> he did so <b>with scissors or with a razor, or</b> if he <b>pulled out any amount,</b> he is <b>liable. The Sages taught:</b> The Torah states with regard to a nazirite: <b>“A razor</b> shall not come upon his head” (Numbers 6:5). <b>I have</b> derived <b>only a razor; from where</b> do I derive that he is liable if he <b>tore out, uprooted,</b> or <b>pulled out any amount of</b> his hair? <b>The verse states: “He shall be holy, he shall let the locks of the hair of his head grow long”</b> (Numbers 6:5). This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yoshiya.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yonatan says</b> a different interpretation: From <b>“razor” I have</b> derived <b>only a razor,</b> whereas if he <b>tore out, uprooted,</b> or <b>pulled out any amount</b> of his hair, <b>he is exempt.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it written: “He shall be holy”?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>That is to say that if he shaved</b> his hair <b>with a razor he stands</b> liable <b>for</b> violating both <b>a positive mitzva and a prohibition.</b> By shaving with a razor he also transgresses the positive mitzva of: “He shall be holy, he shall let the locks of the hair of his head grow long” (Numbers 6:5).
<b>It is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: From <b>“razor” I have</b> derived <b>only a razor; from where</b> do I derive that he is also liable if he <b>tore out, uprooted,</b> or <b>pulled out any amount of</b> it? <b>The verse states:</b> “A razor <b>shall not come upon his head”</b> (Numbers 6:5), indicating that he may not remove his hair in any manner. The <i>baraita</i> asks: <b>And since we eventually include everything</b> that removes hair, <b>what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “A razor shall not come upon his head”?</b> Why does the verse mention a razor, when other implements are equally forbidden?
The <i>baraita</i> answers: It is <b>because we have not learned that the last shaving,</b> i.e., the nazirite’s shaving of ritual purity, <b>must be</b> performed specifically <b>with a razor,</b> as the verse merely states: “He shall shave his head” (Numbers 6:18), without specifying an implement. <b>It is impossible to learn</b> this requirement <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> that <b>a leper</b> must use a razor (see Leviticus 14:9),

Daf 40a

<b>as one does not derive</b> a <i>halakha</i> in <b>a lenient</b> case <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> in a more <b>stringent</b> one in a manner that would cause one <b>to be stringent</b> in the more lenient case. Since the case of a leper is more stringent than that of a nazirite, as a leper must shave his entire body, one cannot derive from the <i>halakhot</i> of a leper that a stringent <i>halakha</i> applies to a nazirite. It is therefore necessary for the verse to specify that a nazirite’s final shaving must be performed with a razor.
<b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> that the term “razor” itself <b>is not necessary</b> to teach that a nazirite’s final shaving must be performed with a razor, <b>as</b> the verse <b>states: “A razor shall not come upon his head until the completion</b> of the days that he vowed naziriteship to God” (Numbers 6:5). This full sentence indicates that <b>the Torah said: After the completion</b> of his term <b>the shaving must be</b> performed <b>only with a razor.</b>
The Gemara comments: Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi claims that the term “razor” indicates that shaving, in this case that of a nazirite after the completion of his term, may be performed only with a razor. If so, the verse should be understood in the same manner with regard to the prohibition of shaving. <b>But</b> if one examines the first part of the verse by itself, <b>isn’t it written: “A razor shall not come upon his head”</b> (Numbers 6:5), and the first <i>tanna</i>, whose opinion Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi does not dispute, derives from this verse that he is prohibited from shaving by other means as well, notwithstanding the explicit mention of a razor.
The Gemara explains that the phrase “shall not come upon his head” teaches that a nazirite may not remove his hair in any manner. As for the mention of razor in this context, it serves to teach <b>that he will violate two prohibitions for this</b> action of using a razor, one for removing his hair and one for doing so with a razor.
§ Concerning a nazirite who shaves his hair, <b>Rav Ḥisda says:</b> With regard <b>to flogging,</b> a nazirite who shaved his hair is flogged <b>for</b> shaving even <b>one</b> hair; with regard <b>to invalidation,</b> i.e., the ritual shaving of his hair at the end of his naziriteship, the shaving is <b>invalidated</b> if he failed <b>to</b> remove <b>two</b> hairs; and as <b>for negating</b> his naziriteship, shaving during his term <b>negates</b> it <b>only if</b> he shaved <b>most of his head. And</b> this applies only if he removed his hair <b>with a razor.</b>
The Gemara asks: This indicates that according to the opinion of Rav Ḥisda, a nazirite who shaved <b>with a razor, yes,</b> he negates thirty days, whereas if he did so <b>with anything else, no,</b> he does not negate days. <b>But isn’t it taught: From where</b> is it derived <b>to include all</b> implements <b>that remove</b> hair, i.e., that a nazirite is liable if he uses any of them? The verse states: “Shall not come upon his head,” meaning in any manner. This indicates that a nazirite must add days to his term of naziriteship even if he shaved with an implement other than a razor. <b>Rather, say</b> that Rav Ḥisda meant: <b>In the manner of a razor.</b> That is, the nazirite negates his naziriteship only if he removes his hair as one does with a razor, without leaving any part of it.
<b>This</b> opinion <b>is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 4:3): With regard to <b>a nazirite who tore out, uprooted,</b> or <b>pulled out any amount</b> of hair, this <b>negates</b> days of his naziriteship <b>only if</b> he shaved <b>most of his head, and</b> only if he did so <b>in</b> the manner of <b>a razor. Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Shimon: Just as</b> leaving <b>two hairs invalidates his</b> shaving of ritual purity at the end of his term, and the act is considered incomplete, <b>so too,</b> leaving a mere <b>two hairs negates</b> days of <b>his</b> naziriteship, if he shaved during his term.
§ <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Nega’im</i> 14:4): <b>Three</b> types of people must <b>shave</b> their hair, <b>and their shaving</b> is <b>a mitzva: A nazirite; and a leper,</b> as it is stated: “And he who is to be cleansed shall wash his clothes, and shave off all his hair” (Leviticus 14:8–9). <b>And</b> the third category is <b>Levites,</b> when they were first sanctified for their service, as it is stated: “And this you shall do to them to cleanse them: Sprinkle the water of purification upon them, and let them cause a razor to pass over all their flesh” (Numbers 8:7). <b>And</b> with regard to <b>all of them, if they shaved with</b> an implement <b>other than a razor, or if they left two hairs</b> uncut, <b>they have done nothing,</b> i.e., they have not fulfilled their obligation.
The Gemara analyzes this mishna. <b>The Master said</b> there: <b>Three</b> types of people must <b>shave</b> and cut their hair, <b>and their shaving</b> is <b>a mitzva.</b> The Gemara asks: This <b>is obvious;</b> after all, doesn’t the Torah command all three to shave? The Gemara answers: The statement of the mishna is necessary, <b>lest you say</b> that the mitzva <b>is due to</b> and aimed at the <b>removal of hair, and</b> therefore <b>even one who applied a depilatory [<i>nasha</i>]</b> to remove his hair has fulfilled his obligation. The mishna therefore <b>teaches us that</b> this is <b>not</b> so, as the mitzva must be performed by shaving.
The mishna further <b>teaches: And</b> with regard to <b>all of them, if they shaved with</b> an implement <b>other than a razor,</b> they have done nothing. The Gemara asks: <b>Granted, with regard to a nazirite</b> the source of this <i>halakha</i> is clear, as <b>it is written: “A razor shall not come upon his head”</b> (Numbers 6:5), which indicates that when he does shave he must do so with a razor. <b>And</b> similarly, <b>with regard to</b> the <b>Levites it is written: “And let them cause a razor to pass over all their flesh”</b> (Numbers 8:7). <b>However, from where do we</b> derive that <b>a leper</b> must be shaved <b>with a razor</b> for his ritual purification?
<b>And if you would say</b> that this <i>halakha</i> <b>is derived from</b> the case of the <b>Levites,</b> as, <b>just as Levites require shaving and their shaving is only with a razor, so too, I will bring</b> the case of <b>a leper, who requires shaving and</b> say that <b>his shaving</b> can likewise be performed <b>only with a razor,</b> then this comparison <b>can be refuted. What</b> is unique <b>about Levites</b> is <b>that</b> they have an extra stringency, in that <b>they require waving of their bodies,</b> i.e., Aaron was required to pick up and wave the bodies of the Levites as part of the ritual of their sanctification (see Numbers 8:11). Will <b>you say</b> the same <b>with regard to a leper, who</b> does <b>not</b> require waving?
<b>Rather,</b> the <i>halakha</i> that a leper must use a razor is <b>derived from</b> the case of <b>a nazirite,</b> who does not require waving either. However, this comparison can also be refuted: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about the nazirite</b> is <b>that his offering requires bread. Will you say</b> the same <b>with regard to a leper,</b> who does <b>not</b> require bread as part of his purification process? <b>Rather,</b> clearly the <i>halakha</i> of a leper is <b>not derived</b> from either <b>one</b> of the above cases, that of the Levites or the nazirite. Therefore, <b>let it be derived from the two of them.</b>
The Gemara elaborates: As stated previously, <b>from which</b> single case <b>can it be derived?</b> If you say it <b>can be derived from</b> the <b>Levites, what</b> is unique <b>about Levites</b> is <b>that they require waving of their bodies.</b> This suggestion can be countered by saying that the case of <b>a nazirite proves</b> that this stringency is not the decisive factor leading to the requirement of a razor, as a nazirite is not waved and yet he must be shaved with a razor. And if you respond: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about the nazirite</b> is <b>that his offering requires bread,</b> one can similarly argue that the <b>Levites prove</b> that this stringency does not lead to the <i>halakha</i> of shaving with a razor, as the offering of the Levites does not require bread and nevertheless they must be shaved with a razor.
<b>And</b> in this manner <b>the derivation has reverted</b> to its starting point. However, at this stage the <i>halakha</i> is derived from a combination of the two sources: <b>The aspect of this</b> case <b>is not like the aspect of that</b> case, <b>and the aspect of that is not like the aspect of this;</b> each case has its own special features. <b>The common denominator is that they require shaving and their shaving is with a razor.</b> Therefore, <b>I will also bring</b> the case of <b>the leper, who requires shaving, and</b> say that <b>his shaving</b> must be <b>with a razor.</b>
<b>Rava of Barnish said to Rav Ashi: And let us refute</b> this derivation in the following manner: <b>What</b> is the common denominator between the Levites and the nazirite? <b>Their common denominator is that</b>

Daf 40b

the <b>offering</b> in each case does not include a level <b>of poverty.</b> In both cases the offering is fixed, i.e., a poor person does not have the option of bringing a less expensive offering due to his financial straits. Will <b>you say</b> the same <b>with regard to the leper, whose offering</b> includes a level <b>of poverty,</b> as a poor person can bring turtledoves instead of sheep (Leviticus 14:21–22)? Since the Torah was more lenient in the case of a leper than the cases of a nazirite and the Levites, it could also be that the <i>halakha</i> is also lenient with regard to his shaving, by not demanding the use of a razor. Consequently, there is no proof that a leper is obligated to shave with a razor.
In continuation of this discussion, <b>Rava bar Mesharshiyya said to Rava: This <i>tanna</i> initially said,</b> with regard to the obligation of a nazirite to shave with a razor (39b): <b>It is impossible to learn</b> this requirement <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> that <b>a leper</b> must use a razor, <b>as one does not derive</b> a <i>halakha</i> in <b>a lenient</b> case <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> in a more <b>stringent</b> one in a manner that would cause one <b>to be stringent</b> in the more lenient case. This indicates that it is obvious to the <i>tanna</i> that a leper himself must shave with a razor. <b>And he then said: Let us derive</b> by means <b>of an inference</b> that a leper must use a razor, <b>and</b> ultimately <b>he did not derive</b> it <b>from an inference</b> by analogy <b>either,</b> due to Rava of Barnish’s objection. What, then, is the source for the <i>halakha</i> that a leper must use a razor?
Rava <b>said to</b> Rava bar Mesharshiyya: <b>That</b> <i>baraita</i>, which states that one cannot derive the <i>halakha</i> from the case of a leper, which indicates that it is evident that a leper must shave with a razor, is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> They derive the <i>halakha</i> of a leper’s shaving from the prohibition against destroying one’s beard. Conversely, <b>this</b> source, which attempted to derive the shaving of a leper from that of a nazirite and the Levites, is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer,</b> who does not derive the <i>halakha</i> of a leper’s shaving from the prohibition against destroying one’s beard. Rabbi Eliezer must therefore derive this <i>halakha</i> by analogy from the cases of a nazirite and the Levites. This is <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Makkot</i> 20a): <b>And</b> one is <b>liable</b> for destroying his beard <b>only if he removes it with a razor. Rabbi Eliezer says: Even</b> if <b>he removed it with</b> small <b>tweezers or a plane [<i>rehitni</i>] he is liable.</b> Rabbi Eliezer maintains that one violates the prohibition even by destroying his beard with means other than a razor.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason of the Rabbis;</b> how do they derive from this <i>halakha</i> that a leper must shave with a razor? <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the verse states with regard to the shaving of a leper: “He shall shave all his hair off his head and his beard” (Leviticus 14:9). Since the verse states: “All his hair,” <b>what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “His beard”?</b> It is <b>because it is stated</b> with regard to priests: <b>“Neither shall they shave off the corners of their beards”</b> (Leviticus 21:5). One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>the same</b> should <b>also</b> apply to <b>a leper,</b> i.e. that a leper who was a priest should be prohibited from shaving his beard. For this reason <b>the verse states: “His beard,”</b> which emphasizes that despite the general prohibition barring a priest from shaving his beard, a priest who is a leper is obligated to do so.
<b>And from where do we</b> derive <b>that</b> this shaving of a leper must be performed <b>with a razor?</b> It is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>, with regard to the prohibition against a priest shaving his beard in the verse <b>“Neither shall they shave off the corners of their beards”</b> (Leviticus 21:5): One <b>might</b> have thought that a priest should <b>be liable even if he shaved</b> his beard <b>with scissors. The verse states,</b> in the general prohibition issued to all Jewish men: <b>“Neither shall you destroy</b> the corners of your beard” (Leviticus 19:27). This teaches that one is liable only if he shaves in a destructive manner, by uprooting the hairs entirely, which excludes the use of scissors.
One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>even</b> if <b>he removed it with tweezers or planes he</b> should <b>be liable. The verse states: “Neither shall they shave off the corners of their beards”</b> (Leviticus 21:5), which indicates that the priests are liable only for removing their beards in a manner of shaving. <b>How so? What is</b> the manner of <b>shaving that involves destruction? You must say this is</b> shaving with <b>a razor.</b> The Rabbis learn from here that the implement forbidden to a priest is the same one that must be used for the shaving of a leper, namely a razor.
The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> do we know that this is correct? <b>Perhaps</b> if a leper <b>actually removed it with tweezers or a plane he also performs the mitzva</b> and thereby fulfills his obligation, <b>and this</b> verse <b>is coming to teach us that even</b> if the leper shaved <b>with a razor he is not liable for</b> destroying his beard. In other words, one might have thought that a leper is prohibited from shaving with a razor, and the verse teaches that this is not correct. If so, there is no proof from here that the shaving of a leper must be performed with a razor.
<b>They say</b> in response: <b>If it should enter your mind that if</b> a leper <b>performs</b> his shaving <b>with tweezers or a plane too,</b> it is <b>well</b> and he has performed the mitzva, then <b>let the verse be silent</b> and refrain <b>from</b> the extra phrase, “his beard.” <b>And</b> I would <b>say</b> the following: <b>And just as with regard to a nazirite, who performs a transgression</b> by shaving his hair during his naziriteship and who, <b>even so,</b> is <b>deemed liable</b> for removing hair without the use of a razor, <b>here</b> too, in the case of a leper, <b>where</b> his shaving is <b>a mitzva,</b> does it <b>not</b> follow <b>all the more so</b> that he should be permitted to shave with any implement?

Daf 41a

<b>And furthermore, if it should enter your mind</b> that <b>when he performs</b> his shaving <b>with tweezers or a plane he performs a mitzva, from</b> the fact that the word <b>razor is not written</b> he should not be allowed to use a razor at all, <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish, who says</b> a principle in this regard.
The Gemara cites the relevant principle: <b>Any place where you find a positive</b> mitzva <b>and a prohibition</b> that clash with one another, <b>if you can</b> find some way <b>to fulfill both,</b> that is <b>preferable; and if that is not</b> possible, the <b>positive</b> mitzva <b>will come and override the prohibition.</b> In this case the leper can fulfill his duty with tweezers or a plane, and therefore it should be prohibited for him to shave with a razor. Since it was necessary for the verse to say that the positive mitzva for a leper to shave overrides the prohibition against destroying one’s beard, this proves that the mitzva can be performed only with a razor.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Eliezer, what is</b> his <b>reason?</b> From where does he derive the <i>halakha</i> that a leper must use a razor? He <b>derives</b> it <b>from</b> the fact that the verse states with regard to a leper: “He shall shave all his hair off <b>his head</b> and his beard” (Leviticus 14:9). <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Why does the verse state: “His head,”</b> if it already stated: “All his hair”? It is <b>because it is stated with regard to a nazirite: “No razor shall come upon his head”</b> (Numbers 6:5), and therefore one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>the same</b> should <b>also</b> apply to <b>a nazirite</b> who is <b>a leper,</b> i.e., it should be prohibited for him to shave his head even for the purification ritual for his leprosy. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “His head,”</b> to teach that a nazirite who is a leper must shave his head with a razor. Being that a nazirite is prohibited only to use a razor, if a leper could fulfill his obligation to remove his hair using other implements, a nazirite who is a leper would not be permitted to use a razor. Therefore, it can be inferred from the <i>halakha</i> of a nazirite who is a leper that the only way for a leper to remove his hair is with a razor.
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: And <b>from where</b> do you know that a leper must shave his head with a razor? <b>Perhaps even</b> if he <b>actually removed it with tweezers or a plane he performs a mitzva. And if you would say:</b> If so, <b>why do I</b> need the verse to teach me that he uses <b>a razor,</b> through the derivation of the superfluous term “his hair,” as above, one can answer: <b>That is to say that</b> he is permitted to shave <b>even with a razor.</b> As, <b>it might enter your mind to say</b> that <b>since with regard to a nazirite, when</b> he <b>performs</b> the act of shaving <b>with a razor</b> he is <b>liable</b> for transgressing the prohibition, <b>with regard to</b> a nazirite who is <b>a leper</b> as well, he should <b>also be liable</b> even if he shaved for his leprosy. The verse therefore <b>teaches us</b> that this is <b>not</b> the case, but it does not teach that a leper must use a razor.
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>If it should enter your mind</b> that <b>when</b> a leper <b>performs</b> his hair removal <b>with tweezers or a plane he performs a mitzva,</b> then <b>from</b> the fact that the verse <b>did not</b> explicitly <b>write: Razor,</b> it can be inferred that he is not allowed to use one, <b>in accordance with</b> the aforementioned principle of <b>Reish Lakish</b> that one may not violate a prohibition, even for the sake of a mitzva, if it is possible to perform the mitzva in a different manner. Rather, the verse must be coming to teach that the mitzva of the shaving of a leper can be fulfilled only with a razor.
The Gemara asks: <b>And the Rabbis,</b> with regard to <b>this</b> term: <b>“His head,”</b> which is referring to a leper, <b>what do they learn from it?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>They require</b> this term <b>to override</b> the <b>prohibition of rounding</b> the corners of the head. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse <b>“You shall not round the corners of your heads”</b> (Leviticus 19:27): One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>the same</b> should <b>also</b> apply to <b>a leper,</b> that it should also be prohibited for him to round the corners of his head when he shaves. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “His head,”</b> with regard to a leper.
The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need the Torah <b>to write: “His head”? And let one derive</b> this <i>halakha</i> that the mitzva of shaving overrides that prohibition <b>from</b> the term <b>“his beard”</b> (Leviticus 14:9). <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Why does the verse state: “His beard”?</b> It is <b>because</b> the verse <b>states</b> with regard to priests: <b>“Neither shall they shave off the corners of their beards”</b> (Leviticus 21:5), and therefore one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>the same</b> prohibition against shaving one’s beard should <b>also</b> apply to <b>a leper.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “His beard.”</b>
The Gemara asks: If so, <b>why do I</b> need the Torah <b>to write: “His head,” and why do I</b> need it <b>to write: “His beard”?</b> One source should suffice to teach that the shaving of a leper overrides any prohibitions that would be violated by that act. The Gemara answers: Both verses <b>are necessary. As, had the Merciful One written</b> only: <b>“His beard,” and not written: “His head,” I would say</b> that <b>the rounding of the entire head,</b> i.e., shaving all the hair off one’s head and not merely the sides, is <b>not called</b> a prohibited <b>rounding.</b> If that were so, the shaving of a leper’s head would not be prohibited by Torah law. <b>For this</b> reason <b>the Merciful One writes: “His head,”</b> to teach that the shaving that the leper performs would be considered a prohibited rounding of the head had the Torah not commanded him to shave.

Daf 41b

<b>And had</b> the Torah <b>written</b> only: <b>“His head,” and not written: “His beard,” I would say</b> that “his head” <b>teaches two</b> matters. First, <b>that a positive</b> mitzva <b>comes and overrides a prohibition. And it</b> also <b>teaches that rounding the entire head is called rounding. And still, from where do we</b> derive that a leper must shave <b>with a razor? For this</b> reason <b>the Merciful One</b> also <b>wrote: “His beard,”</b> and we derive from the prohibition that bars priests from destroying their beards that the removal of the beard involves the use of a razor.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Eliezer, from where does he</b> derive the general principle <b>that a positive</b> mitzva <b>will come and override a prohibition?</b> The Gemara answers: He <b>derives</b> it <b>from</b> the mitzva of ritual <b>fringes. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: This verse: <b>“You shall not wear diverse kinds</b> of wool and linen” (Deuteronomy 22:11),

Daf 42a

indicates that fringes in the mitzva stated in the adjacent verse: <b>“You shall make for yourself fringes”</b> (Deuteronomy 22:12), can be <b>from them,</b> wool and linen. By juxtaposing the mitzva of ritual fringes to the prohibition against diverse kinds of cloth, the Torah teaches that the positive mitzva of ritual fringes, which includes dyed blue wool, overrides the prohibition against diverse kinds of cloth, i.e., one may attach woolen ritual fringes to a linen garment. From here one derives the general principle that a positive mitzva overrides a prohibition.
§ The Gemara returns to the mishna that teaches that nazirites, lepers, and Levites must shave their hair. <b>The Master said</b> above: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>all of them, if they shaved with</b> an implement <b>other than a razor, or if they left two hairs</b> uncut, <b>they have done nothing. Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Ika, said: That is to say</b> that the principle: <b>The majority of</b> an entity is considered <b>like all of it,</b> applies <b>by Torah law.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> do we learn this? The Gemara explains: This principle is derived <b>from</b> the fact <b>that the Merciful One revealed</b> in the Torah and specified <b>with regard to a nazirite: “On the seventh day he shall shave it”</b> (Numbers 6:9), despite the fact that the same verse already stated: “And he shall shave his head on the day of his cleansing.” This teaches that <b>it is</b> only in this case <b>here</b> that he does not fulfill the mitzva of shaving <b>until there is</b> the removal <b>of all of it,</b> i.e., shaving part of his head is insufficient. This shows that <b>in general the majority of</b> an entity is <b>like all of it.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, objects to this</b> assertion. <b>This</b> verse: “On the seventh day he shall shave it,” <b>is written with regard to a ritually impure nazirite,</b> not a pure one, whereas the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna applies even to a pure nazirite. This shows that the above inference is invalid. <b>They laughed at</b> this difficulty <b>in the West,</b> i.e., Eretz Yisrael: <b>After all, from where do we</b> derive the <i>halakha</i> that <b>an impure nazirite</b> shaves <b>with a razor?</b> It is <b>derived from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a pure nazirite.</b> If so, <b>let</b> the case of <b>a pure nazirite come and derive</b> the following <i>halakha</i> <b>from</b> the case of <b>an impure nazirite: Just as</b> with regard to <b>an impure</b> nazirite, <b>if he leaves two hairs he has done nothing, here too,</b> if a pure nazirite <b>leaves two hairs he has done nothing.</b>
On the same topic, <b>Abaye raised a dilemma:</b> With regard to <b>a nazirite who shaved and left two hairs,</b> which is not considered an act of shaving, if the hairs of <b>his head grew and he again shaved,</b> this time those two hairs alone, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Do</b> these hairs <b>invalidate</b> the fulfillment of his obligation <b>or not?</b> Has he now completed his initial act of shaving, or is the shaving of two hairs from a head full of hair of no significance, and he must now shave his entire head?
Similarly, <b>Rava raised a dilemma:</b> With regard to <b>a nazirite who shaved and left two hairs, and</b> afterward <b>shaved one</b> of them, <b>and</b> the other <b>one fell out</b> of its own accord, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is this considered shaving one’s entire head or not? <b>Rav Aḥa of Difti said to Ravina: Is Rava raising a dilemma</b> as to whether one can <b>shave</b> his head one <b>hair by</b> one <b>hair?</b> How does this case differ from that of one who shaves his entire head one hair at a time, which is a fulfillment of his obligation?
<b>Rather, say</b> that the dilemma is as follows: If <b>one</b> hair <b>fell out and he shaved</b> the other <b>one, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Has he performed the obligation of shaving if there was only one hair left when he came to shave? Ravina <b>said to him:</b> In that case <b>there is no shaving here; there is no hair here.</b> The Gemara expresses surprise at this expression: <b>If there is no hair here,</b> then <b>there is shaving here,</b> as no hair remains. The Gemara explains: <b>This is what he said: Even though there is no hair here,</b> as only one hair remains, nevertheless <b>there is no</b> fulfillment of the <b>mitzva of shaving here,</b> as he failed to shave it all on the first attempt, and the second time he shaved less than the required amount.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A nazirite may shampoo [<i>ḥofef</i> ]</b> his head <b>and separate [<i>mefaspes</i>]</b> his hairs manually, without concern that hairs might fall out. <b>However, he may not comb</b> his hair.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara clarifies: <b>Who is</b> the <i>tanna</i> who maintains that a nazirite <b>may shampoo and separate</b> his hairs? <b>It is Rabbi Shimon, who says: An unintentional act is permitted.</b> Even if hairs do fall out as a result of this action, as he did not intend this to happen the action is permitted. Yet in the latter clause of the mishna, which states: <b>However, he may not comb</b> his hair, <b>we have come to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> Although this nazirite also does not intend to tear out any hair when he combs it, it is nevertheless prohibited.
This leads to a surprising conclusion, that <b>the first clause</b> represents the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon and the latter clause</b> is the opinion of <b>the Rabbis. Rabba said:</b> The <b>entire</b> mishna <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon,</b> as he maintains that <b>anyone who combs</b> his hair <b>intends to remove stray hairs,</b> and therefore this is considered an intentional act.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Yishmael says:</b> A nazirite <b>may not shampoo</b> his hair <b>with earth because</b> this <b>causes the hair to fall out.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: What is the precise wording of the mishna? Do <b>we learn: Because it removes hair,</b> i.e., earth in general removes hair, <b>or do we perhaps learn: Because of that which removes</b> hair. In other words, although some types of earth do not remove hair, it is prohibited to use these as well, due to those types that do remove hair. The Gemara inquires: <b>What</b> is the <b>difference</b> of this textual question?
The Gemara explains: There is a difference in a case <b>where there is</b> a type of <b>earth that does not remove</b> hair. <b>If you say</b> that <b>we learned</b> in the mishna: <b>Because it removes</b> hair, then in a case <b>where we know that it does not remove</b> hair <b>it is fine</b> to shampoo with that substance. <b>However, if you say</b> the text reads: <b>Because of that which removes</b> hair, this indicates that the Sages prohibited using any type of earth, due to the type that removes hair. If so, a nazirite may <b>not</b> shampoo his head with any earth <b>at all,</b> not even if it does not remove hair. No answer was found, and the Gemara says that the dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A nazirite who was drinking wine all day is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. If people <b>said to him</b> during the course of the day: <b>Do not drink, do not drink, and</b> nevertheless <b>he</b> continues to <b>drink, he is liable for each and every</b> time he was warned. If a nazirite <b>kept shaving all day, he is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. If <b>they said to him: Do not shave, do not shave, and he shaves, he is liable for each and every</b> time he was warned. If he <b>became ritually impure from a corpse</b> many times <b>all day, he is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. If <b>they said to him: Do not become impure, do not become impure, and he</b> continues to <b>become impure, he is liable for each and every</b> time he was warned.

Daf 42b

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> A dispute among <i>amora’im</i> <b>was stated. Rabba said</b> that <b>Rav Huna said: The Torah stated</b> a <i>halakha</i> involving a nazirite in <b>a categorical verse: “He shall not become impure</b> for his father, or for his mother, for his brother, or for his sister, when they die” (Numbers 6:7). This includes all manners of contracting impurity imparted by a corpse, whether ritual impurity imparted by contact, by carrying, or in a tent, i.e., a corpse under the same roof. <b>When</b> the Torah <b>states: “He shall not come</b> near to a dead body” (Numbers 6:6), it serves <b>to warn him with regard to</b> contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse in any manner, as above, and <b>to warn him with regard to entering</b> an enclosure with a corpse, which is a unique prohibition applicable to a nazirite that is added by the phrase “He shall not come near to a dead body,” and he is liable separately for each. <b>However,</b> with regard to one contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse <b>and</b> again contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse, the verse does <b>not</b> warn him, and he is liable to receive only one set of lashes.
<b>And Rav Yosef says</b> in the form of an oath: <b>By God! Rav Huna</b> actually <b>says</b> that he is separately liable <b>even</b> for contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse <b>and</b> again contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse, not only if he entered an enclosure with a corpse. <b>As Rav Huna says:</b> With regard to <b>a nazirite who was standing in a cemetery,</b> who is already ritually impure, <b>and they extended his corpse,</b> i.e., the corpse of his relative, <b>to him, and</b> similarly if they extended <b>a different corpse</b> to him <b>and he touched it,</b> he is <b>liable.</b> But <b>why</b> is he liable; he <b>has already become impure and is standing</b> in his state of impurity? <b>Rather, isn’t it</b> correct to <b>conclude from</b> this that <b>Rav Huna said</b> he is separately liable <b>even</b> for contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse <b>and</b> again contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse?
<b>Abaye raised an objection to</b> Rav Yosef from a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a priest who had a corpse placed on his shoulder, and they extended his corpse,</b> i.e., the corpse of his relative, <b>to him, and</b> similarly if they extended <b>a different corpse</b> to him <b>and he touched it,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>he should be liable</b> even for this contact. Therefore, <b>the verse states,</b> with regard to the prohibition against a High Priest becoming impure: <b>“And he shall not profane</b> the Sanctuary of his God” (Leviticus 21:12). This teaches that the prohibition of impurity applies <b>to one who is not</b> yet <b>profaned, excluding this one who is</b> already <b>profaned and standing</b> in that state of ritual impurity.
Rav Yosef <b>said to him: But</b> if, as you claim, one is not liable for contracting one impurity after another, <b>the mishna should pose a difficulty for you. As we learned</b> in the mishna: If a nazirite <b>became ritually impure from corpses</b> many times <b>all day, he is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. <b>If they said to him: Do not become impure, do not become impure,</b> and he continues to become impure, <b>he is liable for each and every</b> time he was warned. <b>But why</b> should this be so? <b>He has already become impure and is standing</b> in his state of impurity.
<b>Rather,</b> the mishna and <i>baraita</i> pose <b>a difficulty for each other.</b> The Gemara answers: This is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>here</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to a concurrent</b> contact with impurity, i.e., when he touched the second corpse he was still in contact with the first, so he is not liable for the second impurity. Conversely, <b>there</b> the mishna is referring <b>to</b> impurity that was <b>not a concurrent</b> contact. He touched the second corpse only after he had separated himself from the first, and therefore he is liable for each impurity.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> this <i>halakha</i>, that a nazirite is exempt from being flogged for a second contact with a corpse in a case of <b>concurrent impurity,</b> does it apply <b>by Torah law? Didn’t Rav Yitzḥak bar Yosef say</b> that <b>Rabbi Yannai said: They stated</b> the principle of <b>concurrent impurity only with regard to</b> partaking of <b><i>teruma</i> and consecrated</b> foods, i.e., that one who touches a person who is in contact with a corpse is ritually impure with impurity imparted by a corpse for seven days. <b>However, with regard to a nazirite,</b> i.e., the question of whether a nazirite is considered ritually impure and has to bring offerings due to this contact, <b>and</b> with regard to <b>one who performs</b> the ritual of <b>the Paschal offering,</b> this <i>halakha</i> does <b>not</b> apply. <b>And if you say</b> that this <i>halakha</i> applies <b>by Torah law, what is different</b> between the case of <i>teruma</i> and the case of a nazirite?
The Gemara explains that there are two different types of concurrent impurity. <b>Here,</b> where there is a difference between <i>teruma</i> and a nazirite, it is referring to <b>concurrent</b> contact of one <b>person with</b> another <b>person.</b> If one touched another while the other was in contact with a corpse, the impurity of the first is by rabbinic law. By contrast, <b>there</b> it is referring <b>to concurrent</b> contact of <b>a person with a corpse.</b> One who is touching a corpse is considered linked to impurity by Torah law with regard to his second contact with a corpse.
The Gemara summarizes: The fact that one who is touching a corpse is not liable for contact with a second corpse leads to Rabba’s aforementioned ruling: <b>However,</b> with regard to contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse <b>and</b> again contracting <b>impurity</b> from a corpse, i.e., if a person contracted impurity imparted by a corpse and then touched another corpse while still in contact with the first corpse, he is <b>not</b> liable for the second impurity, <b>as he has already become impure and is standing</b> in his state of ritual impurity.
The Gemara asks: If so, with regard to <b>impurity and entering</b> one should say <b>likewise,</b> that when he enters an enclosure containing a corpse when he is in contact with a corpse, <b>he has already become impure and is standing</b> in his impurity. Why should he be flogged again for entering the enclosure? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> In the case of impurity and entering one must again distinguish between two instances: <b>Here,</b> where Rav Huna said that one will be liable for both contractions of impurity, it is referring to one who was pure and who went <b>into a house</b> that contained a corpse, rendering him liable twice. The entering the house and the ritual impurity imparted by a corpse in a tent, i.e., to that which is under the same roof, occurred simultaneously, and therefore he is liable twice, once for contracting ritual impurity and once for violating the particular prohibition against a nazirite entering an enclosure with a corpse in it.
Whereas <b>there,</b> where he is liable only once, it is referring to one who was <b>in a field.</b> In other words, if a nazirite touched a corpse in a field and subsequently entered an enclosure with a corpse in it while he was still in contact with the first corpse, he is not liable separately for that entering, as he was already ritually impure.

Daf 43a

The Gemara asks: With regard to one who enters <b>into a house too,</b> why should he be liable twice? Since one typically enters a place with his hands before his body, <b>once he inserts his hand he</b> immediately <b>becomes ritually impure.</b> This means that <b>when all</b> the rest <b>of him enters, this</b> person <b>is</b> already <b>impure.</b>
<b>Rather, Rabbi Elazar said:</b> If <b>he inserted his hand</b> into the house first, <b>there is</b> liability <b>due to</b> contracting <b>ritual impurity;</b> however, <b>there is no</b> liability <b>due to entering</b> the enclosure. <b>But</b> if <b>he joined his body</b> and his hands, i.e., all of him entered at once, contraction of <b>impurity and entering</b> the enclosure <b>occur simultaneously,</b> and in that case he is liable twice. The Gemara objects: <b>It is impossible</b> that <b>his nose would not enter first, and</b> once it does, <b>impurity would descend to it</b> and thereby to this person immediately, before the rest of his body entered the house.
<b>Rather, Rava said:</b> If <b>he entered</b> with only <b>his hand there is</b> liability <b>due to</b> contracting <b>ritual impurity,</b> but <b>there is no</b> liability <b>due to entering</b> an enclosure with a corpse, as he cannot be considered inside the house. If <b>he entered</b> with <b>his body</b> by standing upright so that his head would not enter first, contraction of <b>impurity and entering</b> the enclosure <b>occur simultaneously.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> even so, <b>it is impossible</b> that <b>his toes would not enter first, and</b> once they do, <b>impurity would</b> thereby <b>descend to them,</b> causing him to become impure before his entire body enters the house.
<b>Rather, Rav Pappa said:</b> We are dealing with a case <b>where one entered</b> into a house <b>in a chest, a box, or a cabinet,</b> which are not susceptible to ritual impurity and which protect their contents from impurity when they can hold more than forty <i>se’a</i>, <b>and another came and opened the cover</b> of the vessel <b>from over him.</b> In <b>that</b> case contracting <b>impurity and entering</b> the enclosure <b>occur simultaneously. Mar bar Rav Ashi said:</b> It is referring to a case <b>where one entered</b> the house <b>when</b> someone there <b>was dying, and</b> the latter’s <b>soul departed when</b> he <b>was sitting</b> there. In <b>that</b> case too, contracting <b>impurity and entering</b> the enclosure with a corpse <b>occur simultaneously.</b> Since there was no corpse in the enclosure when he entered, he is considered to have entered an enclosure with a corpse at the moment the person died.
§ With regard to the ritual impurity of a corpse, <b>the Sages taught:</b> The Torah states concerning a priest’s exposure to ritual impurity imparted by a corpse: “He shall not become impure, a chief among his people, <b>to profane himself”</b> (Leviticus 21:4), from which it is derived that the prohibition does not apply <b>until the time that</b> the person with whom he comes into contact <b>dies.</b> A priest does not become impure or profane his priesthood at any earlier stage. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> that the verse stated with regard to a nazirite: “He shall not become impure for his father, or for his mother, for his brother, or for his sister, when they die” (Numbers 6:7), from which one can infer that <b>when they die,</b> one <b>contracts ritual impurity</b> from them, i.e., not <b>until</b> the other person actually <b>dies.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the difference <b>between</b> these two derivations? They apparently state the same <i>halakha</i> from different verses. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: The interpretation of the meaning</b> of the verse <b>is</b> the difference <b>between them.</b> There is no practical difference between them; rather, they derive the <i>halakha</i> from different verses. <b>Reish Lakish said:</b> The difference <b>between them</b> is with regard to <b>a dying person: According to the one who says</b> that one derives the <i>halakha</i> from “He shall not become impure, a chief among his people, <b>to profane himself,” even a dying person</b> is included in the prohibition of impurity. <b>According to the one who says</b> that it is derived from <b>“when they die,” once he dies, yes</b> there is impurity, whereas <b>a dying person, no,</b> he does not impart impurity.
The Gemara asks with regard to Reish Lakish’s opinion: <b>And according to the one who says</b> that it is derived from <b>“to profane himself,” isn’t it written: “When they die”?</b> What does he derive from that verse? The Gemara answers: <b>He requires</b> that verse <b>for that which</b> was taught <b>by Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> that the verse stresses “when they die,” to teach: In a case <b>when they die he may not become impure; however, he may become impure from their leprosy or from their gonorrhea-like discharge.</b> A nazirite is prohibited from contracting ritual impurity only if it is from a corpse.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the one who says</b> that the earliest time the prohibition takes effect is derived from the phrase <b>“when they die,” he</b> also <b>requires that</b> verse <b>for this reason;</b> how does he derive two <i>halakhot</i> from the same verse? The Gemara answers: <b>If so, let the verse state: When he dies; what is</b> the reason for the emphasis of <b>“when they die”? You can learn from</b> this verse <b>two</b> <i>halakhot</i>, that one does not impart impurity until he is actually dead, and that a nazirite is prohibited from contracting only the impurity of a corpse.
The Gemara asks the reverse question: <b>And according to the one who says</b> that the source for the earliest time of the impurity of a corpse is the verse <b>“when they die,” isn’t it written: “To profane himself”?</b> What does he derive from that verse? The Gemara answers: <b>“To profane himself” comes for this</b> purpose, that the prohibition against becoming impure apply only <b>to one who is not profaned, excluding one who is</b> already <b>profaned.</b> There is no prohibition against a ritually impure priest becoming impure from a corpse.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the one who says</b> that the source for the commencement of impurity imparted by a corpse is <b>“to profane himself,” he</b> also <b>requires that</b> verse <b>for this reason;</b> how does he derive two <i>halakhot</i> from the same verse? The Gemara answers: <b>If so, let the verse state: To profane; what is</b> the reason for the emphasis of <b>“to profane himself”? You can learn from</b> this verse <b>two</b> <i>halakhot</i>, that a nazirite is prohibited from becoming impure even through contact with a dying person, and that there is no prohibition against contracting impurity a second time for one who is already impure.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a mishna (<i>Oholot</i> 1:6): <b>A person renders</b> others <b>impure only when his soul departs</b> from <b>him, even if he has severe lacerations [<i>meguyyad</i>], and even if</b> he <b>is dying. But according to the one who says</b> that the commencement of the impurity of a corpse is derived <b>from “to profane himself,”</b> this <i>baraita</i> is difficult, as it <b>teaches</b> that a dying person <b>does not impart impurity.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>With regard to imparting impurity,</b> he does not impart impurity <b>until his soul departs, but with regard to profaning</b> the sanctity of the priesthood, a priest <b>is profaned</b> by a dying person.

Daf 43b

§ <b>Rav Ḥisda said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> If <b>the head of his father,</b> or any other relative for whom a priest becomes impure, <b>was severed, he may not become impure to</b> bury <b>him. What is the reason</b> for this? <b>The verse states:</b> “None shall become impure for the dead among his people, except…<b>for his father”</b> (Leviticus 21:1–2), which indicates that this applies <b>when</b> his father <b>is whole, and not when he is lacking. Rav Hamnuna said to</b> Rav Ḥisda: <b>If that is so,</b> then in a case where one <b>was walking in the valley [<i>pakta</i>] of Aravot,</b> a place frequented by bandits, <b>and robbers severed his head,</b> will you <b>also</b> say that his son the priest <b>does not become impure to</b> bury <b>him</b> because he is not whole?
Rav Ḥisda <b>said to him:</b> Do <b>you speak</b> of <b>a corpse with no one to bury it [<i>met mitzva</i>]?</b> This <i>halakha</i> certainly does not apply to a case of this kind. For <b>now</b> consider, if <b>one can say</b> that <b>to</b> bury <b>others,</b> i.e., non-relatives, <b>he is obligated</b> to become ritually impure, as even priests and nazirites must become impure to bury an unattended corpse, then <b>with regard to</b> a <i>met mitzva</i> who is <b>his father</b> is it <b>not all the more so</b> the case that he must become impure to bury him even if his head is severed?
The Gemara asks: <b>And is this a <i>met mitzva</i>? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Which is a <i>met mitzva</i></b> for whom a priest must become impure? It is <b>anyone who does not have</b> people <b>to bury him</b> apart from this priest. If the corpse is in a place where if <b>he</b> would <b>call, others would answer him, that is not</b> considered <b>a <i>met mitzva</i>. And</b> if this dead father <b>has a son</b> who was with him, it means the father is not classified as a <i>met mitzva</i>, and therefore his son the priest must ensure that others tend to his burial, without doing so himself. The Gemara answers: <b>Since</b> he was <b>walking along the way, he is considered like one who does not have</b> people <b>to bury</b> him.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> against Rav Ḥisda’s opinion. The superfluous term “for her” in the verse “And for his sister a virgin who is near to him, who has no husband, <b>for her he becomes impure”</b> (Leviticus 21:3) teaches that it is <b>to</b> bury <b>her</b> in her whole state that <b>he becomes impure, but he does not become impure to</b> bury <b>her limbs.</b> If a limb was severed from her during her lifetime he does not tend to it, <b>because he may not become impure to</b> bury <b>a limb</b> severed <b>from</b> one of his <b>living</b> relatives, including <b>that of</b> his <b>father. However, he may search for a bone that is a barley-grain-bulk.</b> If he was already impure from his father’s body, he may search for and bury a bone that was detached from the corpse, even if it is large enough to impart impurity itself.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the meaning of: <b>He may search for a bone that is a barley-grain-bulk? Isn’t this to say that if</b> the deceased father <b>is lacking a small</b> part, i.e., a single bone, his son the priest becomes impure to bury him? This would contradict Rav Ḥisda’s statement in the name of Rav that he becomes impure to bury his father only if he is whole.
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> this presents no difficulty, as <b>that</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> “For her he becomes impure” (Leviticus 21:3), this indicates that <b>to</b> bury <b>her he becomes impure but he does not become impure to</b> bury <b>her limbs, as he does not become impure to</b> bury <b>a limb</b> severed <b>from</b> one of his <b>living</b> relatives, including <b>that of</b> his <b>father. However, he does become impure to</b> bury <b>a limb from his dead father.</b> Rav Ḥisda disagrees with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda and rules in accordance with the Rabbis, who maintain that a priest may become impure only to bury his relative’s whole body.
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rav Kahana, son of Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov,</b> said: The words “for her he becomes impure” (Leviticus 21:3) indicate that <b>to</b> bury <b>her he becomes impure</b> when she is whole, <b>but he does not become impure to</b> bury her <b>limbs.</b> This serves to <b>exclude an olive-bulk</b> of solid material <b>from a corpse, and an olive-bulk of fluid</b> from a corpse, <b>and a full spade of dust</b> from a corpse.
One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>he may not become impure for a spine and a skull, or for most of the skeleton or most of the number</b> of bones from his sister’s corpse. Therefore, it is <b>written</b> at the beginning of this passage dealing with the impurity of priests: “Speak to the priests, the sons of Aaron, <b>and say to them”</b> (Leviticus 21:1). The repetition of “speak” and “say” indicates that <b>the verse added a different</b> form of <b>impurity</b> permitted to a priest.

Daf 44a

One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>he may not become impure to</b> bury <b>a spine, or for a skull, or to</b> bury <b>most of the skeleton or most of the number</b> of bones <b>of other</b> relatives for whom a priest becomes impure. <b>You say</b> in response: <b>Just as his sister is unique</b> in <b>that her body is dependent upon</b> the brother tending to her burial, <b>and he becomes impure to</b> bury <b>a spine, or to</b> bury <b>a skull, or to</b> bury <b>most of her skeleton, or to</b> bury <b>most of her number</b> of bones, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>every</b> person <b>whose body is dependent upon him,</b> i.e., his other close relatives, <b>he becomes impure to</b> bury <b>a spine, or to</b> bury <b>a skull, or to</b> bury <b>most of his skeleton, or to</b> bury <b>most of his number</b> of bones. This presents a difficulty for the opinion of Rav that a priest may not become impure to bury any relative whose head has been severed.
The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>also</b> represents the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda. And Rav stated</b> his opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>this <i>tanna</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>An incident</b> occurred in <b>which the father of Rabbi Yitzḥak</b> the priest <b>died in Ginzak, and they came and informed him after three years</b> had passed, <b>and he came and asked Rabbi Yehoshua ben Elisha and four Elders who were with him</b> whether he was permitted to become ritually impure by transferring his father’s remains to his ancestral grave, as was the custom.
<b>And they said</b> to Rabbi Yitzḥak that the verse states: <b>“For his father”</b> (Leviticus 21:2), which indicates a priest may become impure only <b>when</b> his father <b>is whole, and not when he is lacking.</b> After three years the father’s body was certainly not whole, and therefore his son, a priest, was no longer permitted to become impure to bury him.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Three types</b> of actions <b>are prohibited for a nazirite:</b> Contracting <b>ritual impurity</b> imparted by a corpse, <b>and shaving</b> his hair, <b>and</b> eating or drinking any substances <b>that emerge from the vine.</b> There is a greater <b>stricture with regard to</b> the prohibitions of <b>impurity and shaving than</b> that of substances <b>that emerge from the vine, as impurity and shaving negate</b> his naziriteship, i.e., he must add thirty days to his term of naziriteship or start it afresh. <b>But</b> if he eats or drinks <b>that</b> which <b>emerges from the vine,</b> this <b>does not negate</b> his naziriteship.
Conversely, there is a greater <b>stricture with regard to</b> substances <b>that emerge from the vine than</b> with regard to <b>impurity and shaving, as</b> in the case of products <b>that emerge from the vine nothing is exempted from its general prohibition</b> in certain circumstances, i.e., there are no exceptions. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>impurity and shaving</b> certain cases <b>are exempted from their general prohibition.</b> For example, there are the cases <b>of obligatory shaving,</b> e.g., a leper who was purified during his naziriteship, <b>and of a corpse with no one to bury it [<i>met mitzva</i>].</b> A nazirite may tend to the burial of a <i>met mitzva</i>, despite the fact that he will thereby contract ritual impurity from a corpse.
The mishna adds: <b>And</b> there is a greater <b>stricture with regard to impurity than</b> with regard to <b>shaving, as</b> a nazirite’s <b>impurity negates all</b> his days of naziriteship and begins his term afresh, <b>and he is liable to</b> bring <b>an offering for it,</b> before starting his new term of naziriteship. <b>But shaving negates only thirty</b> days at most, <b>and he is not liable to</b> bring <b>an offering for it.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> perhaps one should say that the <b>ritual impurity</b> of a nazirite <b>should not be exempted from its general prohibition</b> even for a <i>met mitzva</i>, due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>wine: And if wine,</b> whose prohibition is lighter, <b>as it does not negate</b> his naziriteship, <b>is</b> nevertheless <b>not exempted from its general prohibition,</b> then with regard to <b>impurity,</b> which is stringent, <b>as it does negate</b> his naziriteship, <b>is it not logical that it should not be exempted from its general prohibition?</b>
The Gemara answers: Therefore, <b>the verse states,</b> in addition to the general prohibition: “He shall not come near to a dead body” (Numbers 6:6), that: <b>“For his father or his mother,</b> for his brother or for his sister, <b>he shall not become impure</b> when they die” (Numbers 6:7). This verse teaches that it is <b>to</b> bury <b>his father or for his mother</b> that <b>he may not become impure; however, he becomes impure to</b> bury <b>a <i>met mitzva</i>.</b>
The Gemara suggests: If so, one can make the reverse argument. <b>And let wine be exempted from its general prohibition</b> due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>impurity: If impurity, which negates</b> naziriteship, is <b>exempted from its general prohibition;</b> with regard to <b>wine, which does not negate</b> naziriteship, <b>is it not logical that it should be exempted from its general prohibition</b> for the sake of a mitzva, e.g., for one who took an oath to drink wine? The Gemara answers: It is for this reason that <b>the verse states</b> with regard to a nazirite: <b>“He shall abstain from wine and strong drink”</b> (Numbers 6:3). The emphasis on the words “wine and strong drink” comes <b>to prohibit obligatory wine like optional wine.</b>
The Gemara further asks: <b>And let wine negate all</b> his days of naziriteship, due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>impurity: If impurity, which is exempted from its general prohibition, negates all</b> of his naziriteship, then with regard to <b>wine, which is not exempted from its general prohibition, is it not all the more so</b> logical <b>that it should negate</b> his entire naziriteship?
The Gemara answers: Therefore, <b>the verse states: “But the former days shall be void, for his naziriteship was rendered impure”</b> (Numbers 6:12). The phrase “for his naziriteship was rendered impure” is apparently redundant, as it is clear from the context that the verse is referring to an impure nazirite. Rather, this teaches that only <b>impurity negates</b> his naziriteship, <b>and wine does not negate</b> it.
The Gemara continues to ask along the same lines: <b>And let shaving negate all</b> his naziriteship, due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>impurity: And if</b> in the case of <b>impurity, in which the one who renders</b> another <b>impure is not like the one who becomes impure,</b> i.e., one who renders a nazirite ritually impure does not perform a transgression, as only the nazirite who contracts the impurity has performed a transgression, impurity nevertheless <b>negates all</b> his naziriteship; then with regard to <b>shaving, in which the one who shaves is like the one who is shaved,</b> as someone who shaves a nazirite also performs a transgression, <b>is it not logical that it should negate all</b> his naziriteship?
The Gemara answers: Therefore, <b>the verse states: “But the former days shall be void, for his naziriteship was rendered impure”</b> (Numbers 6:12). The emphasis on the phrase “for his naziriteship was rendered impure” teaches that <b>impurity negates all, and shaving does not negate all.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>But</b> in that case, one can argue the opposite: With regard to <b>impurity, let the one who renders</b> another <b>impure be like the one who becomes impure,</b> due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>shaving: If</b> in the case of <b>shaving, which negates only thirty</b> days, <b>the one who shaves is like the one who is shaved;</b> then, with regard to <b>impurity, which negates all, is it not logical that the one who renders</b> another <b>impure should be like the one who becomes impure?</b>
The Gemara responds: Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “And if any man dies very suddenly beside him, <b>and he renders impure his consecrated head”</b> (Numbers 6:9). This teaches that the prohibition of impurity applies only <b>to one who renders impure his consecrated head,</b> but not to others who render him impure.
The Gemara suggests: <b>But</b> if so, one can say the reverse: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>shaving, let the one who shaves not be like the one who is shaved,</b> due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>impurity: And if</b> in the case of <b>impurity, which</b> is stringent in that it <b>negates all</b> his naziriteship, <b>the one who renders</b> another <b>impure is</b> nevertheless <b>not like the one who becomes impure;</b> then with regard to <b>shaving, which negates only thirty days,</b> is it <b>not all the more so</b> logical <b>that one who shaves should not be like the one who is shaved?</b>
The Gemara answers: Therefore, <b>the verse states: “No razor shall come upon his head”</b> (Numbers 6:5). Since the verse is written in the passive, <b>read into</b> the verse that <b>he,</b> the nazirite himself, <b>shall not</b> cause a razor to <b>come upon</b> his head; <b>and</b> read the verse as also referring <b>to</b> any <b>other</b> person, who <b>shall not</b> cause a razor to <b>come upon</b> the nazirite’s head.
The Gemara asks: <b>And let shaving not be exempted from its general prohibition</b> in the case of a leper, due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>wine: And if wine, which does not negate</b> naziriteship, <b>is not exempted from its general prohibition;</b> then, with regard to <b>shaving, which does negate</b> naziriteship, <b>is it not logical that it should not be exempted from its general prohibition?</b> The Gemara answers: Therefore, <b>the Merciful One states</b> with regard to a leper: “That he shall shave all his hair,” and adds: “Off <b>his head”</b> (Leviticus 14:9). <b>And the Merciful One</b> further <b>states:</b> “And <b>his beard,”</b> which teaches that he shaves despite the prohibition of naziriteship.
The Gemara proposes the reverse argument: <b>And let shaving not negate</b> naziriteship <b>at all,</b> due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>wine: And if wine, which is not exempted from its general prohibition, does not negate</b> naziriteship; then with regard to <b>shaving, which is exempted from its general prohibition, is it not logical that it should not negate</b> naziriteship? The Gemara answers: <b>We require hair growth, and there is none</b> at that point. Consequently, the nazirite must necessarily wait until his hair is of sufficient length to shave.
The Gemara asks: <b>And let wine negate thirty days,</b> due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from</b> the prohibition of <b>shaving: And if shaving, which is exempted from its general prohibition,</b> nevertheless <b>negates</b> thirty days; then with regard to <b>wine, which is not exempted from its general prohibition, is it not logical that it should negate</b> thirty days? The Gemara answers: <b>As that reason</b> for the <i>halakha</i> that shaving causes a nazirite to negate thirty days is <b>only due to hair growth,</b> so that he has sufficient hair at the end of his naziriteship to shave, the <i>halakha</i> does not apply <b>with regard to wine,</b> since <b>his hair remains</b> in place. The nazirite himself has not changed, so the fact that he has drunk wine is not sufficient reason to negate any time.

Daf 44b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to the <b>shaving of ritual impurity</b> performed by a nazirite who became impure during his naziriteship, <b>how</b> is it performed? The priest <b>would sprinkle</b> the waters of purification on him <b>on the third and the seventh</b> days after he contracted his impurity, as performed for all those who contracted impurity imparted by a corpse. <b>And he shaves</b> his hair <b>on the seventh</b> day <b>and brings his offerings on the eighth</b> day. <b>And if he shaved on the eighth</b> day <b>he brings his offerings on that day,</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. Rabbi Tarfon said to him: What is</b> the difference <b>between this</b> ritual <b>and</b> that of <b>a leper?</b> A leper also shaves on the seventh day and sacrifices his offerings on the eighth. However, if a leper shaves on the eighth day he brings his offerings on the ninth day, not on the day of his shaving.
Rabbi Akiva <b>said to him: The purification of this</b> impure nazirite <b>depends on his days,</b> as he immerses on the seventh day like all those who contract impurity imparted by a corpse, which means he is already ritually pure on the eighth day. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>a leper, his purification depends on his shaving.</b> Any immersion performed earlier is of no account, and must be repeated. <b>And</b> a leper <b>brings</b> his <b>offering only if the sun has set</b> following his immersion. Since offerings are not sacrificed at night, the bringing of his offering is postponed until the following day.
