This teaches that the positive mitzva of ritual fringes overrides the prohibition of diverse kinds.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>who derives</b> the principle that a positive mitzva overrides a prohibition <b>from “his head”</b> stated with regard to a leper, <b>what is the reason that he does not derive it from</b> the verse that deals with ritual <b>fringes?</b> Why does he require another source? The Gemara answers: He could have <b>said to you</b> that the verse concerning ritual fringes does not teach that principle, as <b>it comes for that which Rava</b> said.
<b>As Rava raises a contradiction: It is written: “And that they put on the fringe of each corner</b> a thread of sky blue” (Numbers 15:38). The phrase “the fringe of each corner” indicates that ritual fringes must be of the same <b>type as the corner</b> of the robe itself, upon which they must place <b>“a thread of sky blue.” And</b> yet <b>it is written:</b> “You shall not wear diverse kinds, <b>wool and linen together”</b> (Deuteronomy 22:11). The juxtaposition of these two verses teaches that ritual fringes must be made from wool and linen.
<b>How so?</b> How can ritual fringes be made from the same material as the garment itself and also from wool and linen? The answer is that ritual fringes of <b>wool and linen exempt</b> a garment and fulfill the obligation of ritual fringes <b>whether</b> the garment is <b>of their</b> own <b>type,</b> i.e., wool and linen, or <b>whether it is not of their own type,</b> i.e., all other materials. By contrast, with regard to ritual fringes of <b>other types</b> of materials, <b>they exempt</b> a garment <b>of their</b> own <b>type;</b> but <b>they do not exempt</b> a garment that is <b>not of their type.</b> According to this, the verse juxtaposing ritual fringes to diverse kinds serves to teach that ritual fringes made of wool and linen exempt any garment, not to teach that a positive mitzva overrides a prohibition.
The Gemara continues this line of inquiry: <b>And</b> according to <b>this <i>tanna</i>, who derives from</b> the term <b>“his head”</b> that a positive mitzva overrides <b>only a prohibition, from where do we</b> derive the <i>halakha</i> <b>that</b> the <b>positive mitzva</b> of a leper’s shaving <b>comes and overrides</b> the <b>prohibition and the positive mitzva</b> of a nazirite? From where does this <b>tanna</b> learn that a nazirite who contracted leprosy is obligated to shave? The Gemara answers: <b>He derives it from</b> the verse: “He shall shave all his hair; his head and <b>his beard”</b> (Leviticus 14:9).
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Why</b> must <b>the verse state: “His beard”?</b> After all, a beard is already included in the phrase “all his hair.” The <i>baraita</i> answers: <b>Since it is stated,</b> with regard to priests: <b>“Neither shall they shave off the corners of their beard”</b> (Leviticus 21:5), one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>even a priest who is a leper is included</b> in this prohibition against shaving his beard. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “His beard”</b> in connection to a leper.
The Gemara asks: <b>And the one who derives</b> from <b>“his head” that</b> the mitzva of shaving overrides both <b>the positive mitzva and prohibition</b> of a nazirite, <b>let him derive</b> this principle <b>from “his beard.”</b> The Gemara refutes this suggestion: <b>And according to your reasoning,</b> that the term “his beard” teaches that a positive mitzva overrides the prohibition and positive mitzva of a nazirite, that <b>which we maintain generally,</b>

Daf 58b

<b>that a positive mitzva does not come and override a prohibition and a positive mitzva, let him derive from</b> the fact that a leprous <b>priest</b> must shave <b>that</b> this mitzva <b>does override</b> a prohibition and another positive mitzva.
<b>Rather,</b> he would explain that <b>we do not derive</b> other cases of the Torah <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a priest,</b> for the following reason: <b>What</b> is different about the prohibition <b>of a priest is that</b> it is <b>a prohibition that is not equally</b> applicable <b>to all,</b> i.e., the prohibitions of a priest do not apply to Israelites. For the same reason, the case of <b>a nazirite also cannot be derived from</b> that of <b>a priest, as</b> the prohibition of a priest is <b>a prohibition that is not equally</b> applicable <b>to all,</b> in contrast to that of a nazirite. Consequently, the derivation from “his head” is necessary to teach that the shaving of a leper overrides the prohibition stated with regard to a nazirite.
The Gemara turns its attention to the other opinion: <b>And</b> according to <b>the one who establishes this</b> verse: “He shall shave all his hair; <b>his head</b> and his beard” (Leviticus 14:9), as referring <b>to a nazirite,</b> i.e., he derives from this verse the principle that in certain cases, including that of a leprous priest, a positive mitzva overrides a prohibition and a positive mitzva, <b>why do I</b> need the term <b>“his beard”?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>He requires it for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the shaving of a leper, which must be performed with a razor: <b>Why</b> must <b>the verse state: “His beard,”</b> as it already stated that he shaves all his hair? <b>Since it is stated: “Neither shall they shave off the corners of their beard”</b> (Leviticus 21:5), one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>even a priest</b> who is <b>a leper is included</b> in the prohibition against shaving. <b>The verse</b> therefore <b>states: “His beard,”</b> to teach that a leprous priest must shave his beard as well.
<b>And from where do we</b> derive that a leper must shave <b>with a razor? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>“Neither shall they shave off the corners of their beard”</b> (Leviticus 21:5). One <b>might</b> have thought that priests <b>should be liable even if they shaved</b> their beards <b>with scissors. The verse states: “And you shall not destroy</b> the corners of your beard” (Leviticus 19:27), which teaches that the prohibition applies only to the destruction of the beard from its roots. <b>If</b> the sole criterion is the phrase <b>“you shall not destroy,”</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>if he extracted</b> his hair <b>with tweezers or</b> removed his hair <b>with</b> a carpenter’s <b>plane, he should</b> likewise <b>be liable</b> due to the prohibition against destroying his hair.
<b>The verse</b> therefore <b>states: “Neither shall they shave off the corners of their beard.”</b> Together the two verses lead to the following conclusion: <b>Which</b> is the type of <b>shaving [<i>giluaḥ</i>] that involves destruction [<i>hashḥata</i>]? You must say it is a razor.</b> The fact that it was necessary for the Torah to permit a leper to shave his beard with a razor, notwithstanding the prohibition against using this implement, proves that the positive mitzva overrides this prohibition, as derived from the term “his beard.”
The Gemara continues to inquire: <b>And</b> according to <b>the one who derives from</b> the term <b>“his head”</b> the principle <b>that</b> a positive mitzva overrides <b>only a prohibition, why do I</b> need the verse <b>to write: “His head,” and why do I</b> also need the same verse <b>to write: “His beard”?</b> Once is it derived from the phrase “his beard” that a positive mitzva overrides a positive mitzva and a prohibition, the same should certainly apply to a prohibition by itself.
The Gemara answers: Both the term “his head” and the term “his beard” could come to <b>teach the overriding of only a prohibition, and</b> they also could <b>teach the overriding of a prohibition and a positive mitzva.</b> Since the verse is formulated in general terms, it includes a priest or a nazirite. <b>Therefore,</b> as it cannot be determined to which case the Torah is referring, <b>it is even,</b> i.e., equally balanced, <b>and</b> consequently <b>let both</b> terms <b>come</b> and teach this principle, one with regard to a nazirite, and the other with regard to a priest.
The Gemara adds that both terms are necessary, as the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a priest cannot be derived from</b> that of <b>a nazirite, as</b> the case of a nazirite is lenient in that <b>it is</b> possible to dissolve naziriteship <b>by request</b> from a halakhic authority. Likewise, the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a nazirite cannot be derived from</b> that of <b>a priest, as</b> the case of a priest involves <b>a prohibition that is not equally</b> applicable <b>to all.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>And generally,</b> with regard to other <i>halakhot</i>, <b>we cannot derive from</b> these two cases that a positive mitzva overrides a prohibition and a positive mitzva, <b>due to</b> the fact that this argument <b>can be refuted as we said</b> here.
§ <b>Rav said: A person</b> who is not a nazirite <b>may lighten</b> his burden by removing <b>all</b> the hair of <b>his body with a razor.</b> One who feels he has too much hair may shave all of it off with a razor, apart from his beard and the corners of his head. The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> against this from a <i>baraita</i>: A man <b>who removes</b> the hair <b>of the armpit or the pubic</b> hair <b>is flogged</b> for transgressing the prohibition: “A man shall not put on a woman’s garment” (Deuteronomy 22:5), as this behavior is the manner of women.
The Gemara answers: In <b>this</b> case he is flogged because he shaved <b>with a razor,</b> whereas in <b>that</b> case Rav said it is permitted because he was referring to one who removes hair <b>with scissors,</b> an act that is not considered a prohibition. The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But Rav said</b> it is permitted <b>with a razor as well.</b> The Gemara answers: He did not mean an actual razor; rather, he said that one may use an implement that is <b>similar to a razor,</b> i.e., scissors that cut very close to the skin, in the manner of a razor.
<b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> A man <b>who removes</b> the hair <b>of the armpit or the pubic</b> hair <b>is flogged.</b> The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> against this ruling: <b>The removal of hair is not</b> prohibited <b>by Torah law but by rabbinic law.</b> Why, then, is he liable to receive lashes? The Gemara explains: <b>What</b> does it mean <b>that</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>says</b> that he <b>is flogged?</b> This means that he is flogged <b>by rabbinic law,</b> a punishment known as lashes for rebelliousness, for disobeying a rabbinic decree.

Daf 59a

<b>Some say</b> a different version of this statement: <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> A man <b>who removes</b> the hair <b>of the armpit or the pubic</b> hair <b>is flogged, due to</b> the prohibition: <b>“A man shall not put on a woman’s garment”</b> (Deuteronomy 22:5). The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The removal of hair is not</b> prohibited <b>by Torah law but by rabbinic law.</b> How then does Rabbi Yoḥanan say that he is flogged, which by definition is a punishment for individuals who have transgressed a Torah law? The Gemara answers: It was <b>he who said</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>that <i>tanna</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: A man <b>who removes</b> the hair <b>of the armpit or the pubic</b> hair <b>violates</b> the prohibition <b>of: “A man shall not put on a woman’s garment.”</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And what</b> does <b>the first <i>tanna</i>,</b> who holds that the prohibition is by rabbinic law, <b>learn from this</b> verse: <b>“A man shall not put on</b> a woman’s garment”? The Gemara answers: <b>He requires it for that which is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i>, where it states: <b>“A woman shall not wear that which pertains to a man,</b> and a man shall not put on a woman’s garment, for whoever does these things is an abomination to the Lord your God” (Deuteronomy 22:5). <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> this? <b>If</b> it teaches only <b>that a man may not put on a woman’s garment, and a woman</b> may not wear <b>a man’s garment, it is already stated</b> in explanation of this prohibition that <b>“it is an abomination</b> to the Lord your God,” <b>and there is no abomination here</b> in the mere act of wearing a garment.
<b>Rather,</b> it means <b>that a man may not wear a woman’s garment</b> and thereby go <b>and sit among the women; and a woman</b> may not wear <b>a man’s garment and sit among the men. Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: From where</b> is it derived <b>that a woman may not go out with weapons to war? The verse states: “A woman shall not wear that which pertains to a man, and a man shall not put on a woman’s garment,”</b> which indicates <b>that a man may not adorn himself with the cosmetics</b> and ornaments <b>of a woman,</b> and similarly a woman may not go out with weapons to war, as those are for the use of males. Rabbi Yoḥanan’s ruling follows this opinion.
§ <b>Rav Naḥman said: For a nazirite,</b> it is <b>permitted</b> to shave armpit hair. The Gemara comments: <b>And the <i>halakha</i> is not in accordance with his</b> opinion. The Gemara reports that <b>the Sages said to Rabbi Shimon bar Abba: We</b> have <b>observed that Rabbi Yoḥanan does not have</b> armpit hairs, despite his own ruling that it is prohibited to shave them. <b>He said to them: They fell out due to old age.</b>
The Gemara relates: There was <b>a certain</b> person who committed a transgression and <b>was</b> found <b>liable to receive lashes before Rabbi Ami.</b> When they removed his clothes to flog him, his <b>armpit was exposed,</b> and Rabbi Ami <b>saw that he had not shaved</b> his armpit hair. <b>Rabbi Ami said to</b> his attendants: <b>Leave him; this is one of those who are meticulous in observance of mitzvot.</b> We can see this is so, as he is particular about prohibitions that ordinary people do not observe.
<b>Rav raised a dilemma before Rabbi Ḥiyya: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to shaving</b> armpit hair? <b>He said to him:</b> It is <b>prohibited.</b> Rav <b>said to him: But it grows</b> and is uncomfortable. Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to him: Son of nobles [<i>bar paḥtei</i>],</b> this hair <b>has a</b> limited <b>time. Whenever</b> a hair <b>grows</b> too long <b>it falls out,</b> and therefore there is no concern that one’s armpit hair will become too long.
<b>Rav raised</b> another <b>dilemma before Rabbi Ḥiyya: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to rubbing</b> armpit hair and thereby removing it manually? Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to him:</b> It is <b>prohibited.</b> Rav continued to ask: <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to rubbing armpit hair indirectly <b>with one’s garment?</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to him:</b> It is <b>permitted. Some say</b> that this was not Rav’s question; rather, <b>he raised</b> the following <b>dilemma before him: What</b> is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to rubbing the armpit with <b>one’s garment during prayer?</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to him:</b> It is <b>prohibited.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>And the <i>halakha</i> is not in accordance with his</b> opinion in this case.

Daf 59b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The previous mishna described how two nazirites sacrifice offerings of impurity and purity, in a situation in which one of them has become impure but they do not know which one. This mishna discusses what must be done if <b>one of them dies</b> before bringing his offerings. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua said:</b> The surviving nazirite <b>asks someone in the marketplace,</b> a non-nazirite, <b>to vow to be a nazirite corresponding to him,</b> i.e., under the same conditions as his own naziriteship, so that he can bring offerings together with him.
<b>And he says</b> to him as follows: <b>If I was impure, you are hereby a nazirite immediately; and if I was pure, you are hereby a nazirite after thirty days. And they</b> both proceed to <b>count thirty days and bring an offering of impurity and an offering of purity. And</b> the nazirite who was defined as having uncertain impurity <b>says: If I am the impure</b> one, <b>the offering of impurity is mine and the offering of purity is yours; and if I am the pure one, the offering of purity is mine and the offering of impurity</b> we brought shall be <b>of uncertain</b> status.
<b>And they</b> subsequently <b>count</b> another <b>thirty days and bring an offering of purity, and</b> the first nazirite <b>says: If I</b> was <b>the impure</b> one, <b>the offering of impurity</b> that we sacrificed at the end of the first thirty days was <b>mine, and the offering of purity</b> we brought then was <b>yours; and this</b> offering I am bringing now is <b>my offering of purity. And if I</b> was <b>the pure</b> one, and the deceased nazirite was impure, <b>the offering of purity</b> we brought thirty days ago was <b>mine, and the offering of impurity</b> we brought earlier was <b>of uncertain</b> status, <b>and this is your offering of purity.</b>
<b>Ben Zoma said to</b> Rabbi Yehoshua: <b>And who will listen to him to vow to be a nazirite corresponding to him?</b> How can one design a <i>halakha</i> on the assumption that a non-nazirite will agree to be a nazirite for a lengthy term? <b>Rather,</b> a different procedure is available: After thirty days of naziriteship <b>he brings a bird sin-offering and an animal burnt-offering, and says: If I was impure, the sin-offering is for my obligation</b> as an impure nazirite, <b>and the burnt-offering</b> is a regular <b>gift</b> offering. <b>And if I was pure, the burnt-offering is for my obligation</b> as a pure nazirite, <b>and the sin-offering</b> is <b>of uncertain</b> status.
<b>And he counts</b> another <b>thirty days, and brings an offering of purity, and says: If I was impure, the first burnt-offering</b> I brought should be considered <b>a gift</b> offering, <b>and this</b> one I am bringing now is for my <b>obligation. And if I was pure, the first burnt-offering</b> I brought is for my <b>obligation</b> as a pure nazirite, <b>and this</b> one I am bringing now is <b>a gift</b> offering. <b>And these,</b> i.e., the sin-offering and peace-offering I am sacrificing now, comprises <b>the rest of my offerings</b> that I was obligated to bring earlier.
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said:</b> According to your opinion, <b>it turns out</b> that <b>this</b> nazirite <b>brings his offerings in halves,</b> i.e., in stages. If he was pure, he brings his burnt-offering thirty days before the rest of his offerings. <b>However, the Rabbis agreed with ben Zoma,</b> and disregarded the concern about splitting up the offerings.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that Rabbi Yehoshua countered ben Zoma’s opinion by pointing out that his solution would cause the nazirite to bring his offerings in stages. The Gemara asks: What is wrong with that? <b>And let him bring</b> the offerings in stages. <b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: Rabbi Yehoshua said</b> this comment <b>only to sharpen</b> the minds of <b>the students.</b> He did not really maintain that it is prohibited to act in this manner; rather, he wanted to test his disciples to see if they were aware of the <i>halakha</i>.
<b>Rav Naḥman said,</b> in a light-hearted vein: <b>What will Rabbi Yehoshua do with the intestines</b> of <b>his</b> animals so <b>that they will not spoil?</b> If he insists that the offerings of purity must all be brought at the same time, the only way to do so is to wait thirty days after slaughtering the burnt-offering before burning its intestines, which is certainly impractical.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <b>a nazirite who,</b> on the first day of his naziriteship, <b>was impure</b> from a corpse as a matter of <b>uncertainty and</b> was also <b>a confirmed leper</b> as a matter of <b>uncertainty,</b> i.e., it was uncertain whether or not he had leprosy, how can he fulfill the shaving obligations of a pure nazirite and an impure leper? The problem facing this nazirite is that a leper must shave both when he begins his purification process and at the close of it, one week later. However, a nazirite is prohibited from shaving. Additionally, a leper may not partake of sacrificial food, but a nazirite may. Therefore, <b>he may partake of sacrificial food sixty days after</b> he may have become impure, when the uncertainty with regard to leprosy will have been clarified. He shaves for the first time for his leprosy after thirty days, and for the second time thirty days later, the shaving of the end of the purification process; at which point he brings the offerings of a purified leper and may partake of sacrificial food.
<b>But</b> he <b>may drink wine and become impure from the dead,</b> effectively ending his naziriteship, only <b>after 120 days.</b> This is because he might have been a full-fledged leper, which means that his shavings count toward his leprosy, not his naziriteship. Consequently, after the first sixty days he must observe another thirty days of naziriteship and shave again. Even then he has yet to fulfill all his obligations, as he might have been impure from a corpse, which means his shaving after ninety days was for his impurity. He must therefore remain a nazirite for another thirty days, before shaving one final time at the end of 120 days to fulfill his naziriteship obligation.
The mishna notes: The reason that he cannot shave for his leprosy after seven days and perform the second shaving of a leper seven days later is <b>because the shaving of leprosy overrides</b> the prohibition of <b>the shaving of a nazirite</b> only <b>when his</b> status as <b>a leper</b> is <b>definite. However, when his</b> status as <b>a leper</b> is <b>uncertain,</b> the shaving <b>does not override</b> his naziriteship, and therefore he must wait thirty days before each of his shavings for leprosy.

Daf 60a

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Sage <b>taught: In what</b> case <b>is this statement</b> of the mishna <b>said? With regard to a short naziriteship</b> of thirty days. <b>However, with regard to a naziriteship of a year, he partakes of sacrificial food</b> only <b>after two years.</b> He cannot shave until a year has passed, in case he is not a leper; he may shave the second time only a year later, in case he was impure during the first year and this second year was his naziriteship observed in purity. At this stage he may partake of sacrificial meat, for if he was a full-fledged leper he has shaved twice.
Yet if the first two shavings were for his leprosy, he has not shaved for his naziriteship at all, and therefore he must observe a third year of naziriteship and shave. He then must observe another year of naziriteship and shave again, as perhaps his third shaving was for impurity and the fourth will be for his naziriteship in purity. <b>And</b> consequently <b>he may drink wine and become impure from the dead</b> only <b>after four years.</b>
<b>And it is taught with regard to</b> the <i>halakha</i> of the mishna (<i>Tosefta</i> 6:1): <b>And he shaves</b> with <b>four</b> separate acts of <b>shaving,</b> each time for its own reason. How so? For his <b>first shaving,</b> after the thirty days of an unspecified naziriteship or at the end of the period he vowed to observe naziriteship, he <b>brings</b> two <b>birds</b> of a leper: One is slaughtered over spring water and the other is sent away (Leviticus 14:4–7).
<b>And</b> he also brings <b>a bird sin-offering</b> for his shaving of impure naziriteship, as he may have contracted impurity from a corpse, <b>and an animal burnt-offering</b> for his shaving of purity, as he may not have contracted impurity from a corpse. For his <b>second</b> shaving he <b>brings a bird sin-offering and an animal burnt-offering.</b> For his <b>third</b> shaving he again <b>brings a bird sin-offering and an animal burnt-offering.</b> And for the <b>fourth</b> shaving he <b>brings an offering of purity.</b>
The Gemara analyzes the details and reasons for this <i>halakha</i>: <b>You said</b> in this <i>baraita</i> that for the <b>first shaving</b> he <b>brings</b> the two birds of a leper, as well as a bird sin-offering and an animal burnt-offering. The Gemara explains: In this manner <b>he brings</b> the <b>proper</b> offerings <b>whichever way you</b> look at it. <b>As, if his</b> status as <b>a leper</b> is <b>definite and he is not impure,</b> the <b>birds</b> he brings are for <b>his obligation</b> to purify himself from leprosy. <b>And</b> as <b>the bird sin-offering</b> in case he was impure was brought due to <b>uncertainty,</b> it <b>goes for burial,</b> like all bird sin-offerings sacrificed in a case of uncertainty, which cannot be eaten. <b>And</b> the bird <b>burnt-offering</b> that he brought in case he was an impure nazirite <b>is a gift</b> offering.
The Gemara further explains: <b>But</b> it <b>is not possible to shave him</b> right <b>after seven days,</b> as one usually does when purified from leprosy, <b>as perhaps he is not a confirmed</b> leper and has no obligation to shave but is bound by the naziriteship vow, <b>and the Merciful One says: “No razor shall come upon his head; until</b> the days are <b>fulfilled</b> in which he consecrated himself to the Lord” (Numbers 6:5). This verse teaches that he cannot shave until the end of his naziriteship. Therefore, he shaves only after thirty days.
The Gemara continues to clarify the various uncertainties concerning the first shaving. <b>And if he is not a definite leper but he is impure</b> from a corpse, <b>the bird sin-offering</b> is for <b>his obligation</b> as an impure nazirite, <b>and</b> there is no problem with the unnecessary rituals of a leper’s <b>birds,</b> as <b>they are performed outside</b> the Temple, <b>and are not</b> subject to the prohibition against bringing <b>non-sacred</b> animals <b>into the</b> Temple <b>courtyard.</b> Therefore, the slaughter of one of the birds outside the Temple is not a transgression. <b>And</b> the <b>animal burnt-offering is</b> considered <b>a gift</b> offering.
<b>And if he is neither a leper nor impure</b> he has also acted correctly, as <b>the birds are performed outside</b> the Temple, which means no transgression is committed, while the <b>bird sin-offering</b> of uncertain necessity goes <b>to burial,</b> and the <b>animal burnt-offering is</b> for <b>his obligation,</b> like any nazirite who completes his naziriteship. The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> isn’t a leper <b>required</b> to bring <b>a guilt-offering</b> to complete his atonement and permit him to partake of sacrificial food? The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> ruling is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, who said</b> that one <b>may bring</b> a guilt-offering <b>and stipulate</b> as follows: If I am obligated to bring this offering then it is for my obligation; and if not, it shall be a voluntary peace-offering.
For the <b>second and third shavings</b> he does <b>not need</b> to bring <b>birds</b> again, <b>as he has</b> already <b>performed</b> this ritual. <b>What is</b> the potential concern? That <b>perhaps he</b> was previously <b>a confirmed</b> leper of <b>definite</b> status. For this reason, <b>one</b> of the bird sin-offerings he brings at the second shaving is <b>for</b> the days of <b>his counting</b> when his status was <b>uncertain.</b> After the first purification of a confirmed leper he counts seven days, or thirty days in this case due to the uncertainty, and brings a sin-offering.
<b>And</b> the <b>one</b> sin-offering he brings at his third shaving is <b>for his uncertain impurity.</b> If he was a leper, the offerings he brought on the previous occasion were for purification of his leprosy, not his naziriteship. This means that, if he was impure, he is still required to bring a sin-offering for his impurity as a nazirite. Furthermore, the animal burnt-offerings he brings for his second and third shavings are for his naziriteship of purity. For his <b>fourth shaving he brings an offering of purity and stipulates</b> as follows:

Daf 60b

<b>And if he was a definite nazirite</b> from the start and was neither impure from a corpse nor a leper, <b>the first burnt-offering</b> he brought after his initial shaving was for his <b>obligation</b> of pure naziriteship, <b>and this</b> one he is bringing now is <b>a gift</b> offering. <b>And if he was impure</b> from a corpse <b>and a confirmed</b> leper, <b>the first burnt-offering</b> he brought was <b>a gift</b> offering <b>and this</b> one he is bringing now is for his <b>obligation. And these,</b> the guilt-offering and peace-offering he is currently bringing, are for <b>the rest of his</b> obligatory <b>offerings.</b> This concludes the Gemara’s analysis of the case of the <i>baraita</i>.
§ The Gemara addresses similar situations: A nazirite about whom it was <b>uncertain</b> if he was <b>impure</b> from a corpse, <b>and</b> he was <b>a confirmed</b> leper whose status was <b>definite, partakes of sacrificial food after eight days.</b> He counts seven days, after which he is purified from his leprosy. On the following day he brings his offerings and may partake of sacrificial food. <b>And he may drink wine and become impure from the dead after sixty-seven days.</b> He is required to count another thirty days for his naziriteship and shave, at which point he must observe another thirty days, in case his first shaving was for his impurity, and his second shaving is for his purity.
The Gemara discusses another case: A nazirite whose status as <b>a confirmed</b> leper was <b>uncertain and</b> who was <b>definitely impure</b> from a corpse <b>partakes of sacrificial food after thirty-seven days.</b> He shaves after seven days for his definite impurity and starts counting thirty days of naziriteship afresh. At the conclusion of this period he shaves again, which renders him pure from any uncertain leprosy, and may partake of sacrificial food. However, <b>he may drink wine and become impure from the dead</b> only <b>after seventy-four days.</b> Since he might have been a confirmed leper, his first two shavings, after seven and thirty-seven days, count only toward the two stages of his purification from leprosy, for his first shaving and after the days of his counting. He must therefore observe a further thirty days for his naziriteship of purity.
The Gemara mentions yet another case: A nazirite who was <b>definitely impure</b> from a corpse <b>and</b> he was <b>a confirmed</b> leper whose status was <b>definite partakes of sacrificial food after eight days.</b> He immediately shaves his first shaving for his leprosy, then counts seven days and shaves again for his leprosy and partakes of sacrificial food on the following day. <b>And he may drink wine and become impure from the dead after forty-four days.</b> He counts a further seven days of hair growth for his shaving of impurity, shaves, and finally observes thirty more days for his naziriteship of purity.
§ <b>The students of</b> Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai <b>asked Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai:</b> With regard to one who was <b>a pure nazirite and a leper, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> concerning the possibility <b>that he may shave one shaving and it will count for him</b> both <b>for this and for that?</b> In other words, can it serve for his shaving of leprosy as well as for his naziriteship? <b>He said to them: He may not shave</b> once for both requirements.
<b>They said to him: Why</b> not? <b>He said to them: If</b> the aim of both shavings were the same, <b>this one to grow</b> hair <b>and that one to grow</b> hair, <b>or this one to remove</b> hair <b>and that one to remove</b> hair, <b>you</b> would have <b>spoken well. Now</b> in actual fact the two shavings have different functions: <b>A nazirite</b> shaves <b>to remove</b> his hair, <b>and a leper</b> shaves <b>to grow</b> hair, so that he can shave again after the days of his counting.
His students posed another question: <b>But</b> even if his shaving of naziriteship does <b>not count for him as</b> the shaving of the completion of <b>his days of confirmed</b> leprosy, <b>let it</b> at least <b>count for him</b> as the shaving at the end of the <b>days of his counting,</b> which is not followed by another act of shaving, and therefore is performed only for the purpose of removing his hair. <b>And</b> Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai <b>said to them: If this one</b> were performed <b>before the sprinkling of blood and that one before the sprinkling of blood, you</b> would have <b>spoken well. However, a leper</b> shaves <b>before the sprinkling of</b> his offering’s <b>blood, and a nazirite</b> does so <b>after the sprinkling of</b> the <b>blood.</b> Therefore, the two shavings are not equivalent.
Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai’s students asked him yet another question: <b>And</b> granted that his shaving does <b>not count for his days of leprosy and his naziriteship, let it</b> at least <b>count for his days of leprosy and his</b> shaving of naziriteship of <b>impurity,</b> both of which are performed before the sprinkling of the blood. <b>He said to them: If this one</b> shaved <b>before</b> his <b>immersion in water, and that one before immersion in water, you</b> would have <b>spoken well. However, an impure</b> nazirite shaves <b>after immersion in water,</b> whereas <b>a leper</b> shaves <b>before immersion in water.</b>
<b>They said to</b> Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai: <b>You</b> have <b>spoken well,</b> and convinced us <b>that</b> one shaving <b>should not count for</b> the <b>days of his counting and for his naziriteship. But</b> why not say that it should at least <b>count for his days of confirmed</b> leprosy <b>and his</b> naziriteship of <b>impurity, as</b> the purpose of <b>this</b> shaving is <b>to grow</b> hair <b>and</b> the purpose of <b>that one</b> is <b>to grow</b> hair.
<b>He said to them:</b> Here too the acts of shaving for naziriteship and leprosy are not exactly the same: If he is <b>a pure nazirite and he is</b> also <b>a leper,</b> the difference is that the aim of <b>this</b> one, the shaving of a leper, is <b>to grow</b> hair, <b>and</b> the aim of <b>that</b> one, the shaving of the pure nazirite, is <b>to remove</b> his hair. <b>And if</b> he is <b>an impure nazirite and he is</b> also <b>a leper,</b> the difference is that <b>this</b> shaving, of a leper, occurs <b>before immersion in water, and that</b> one, the shaving of an impure nazirite, is performed <b>after immersion in water.</b>

Daf 61a

<b>Rabbi Ḥiyya teaches</b> the same <i>halakha</i>: The shaving of a leper does not count for the shaving of naziriteship, as <b>this</b> one, a leper, shaves <b>before immersion in water, and that</b> one, an impure nazirite, shaves <b>after immersion water. This</b> one, the leper, shaves <b>before</b> the <b>blood is sprinkled, and that</b> one, the pure nazirite, shaves <b>after</b> the <b>blood is sprinkled.</b>
§ The mishna taught <b>that the shaving of leprosy</b> overrides the shaving of a nazirite only when he is a confirmed leper. <b>Rami bar Ḥama raises a dilemma: Those four shavings about which</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>spoke,</b> the four that a nazirite performs for his leprosy and impurity, are they all <b>due to</b> the <b>mitzva</b> of shaving, <b>or</b> are some of them not performed for the sake of a mitzva, but <b>for the sake of removing hair</b> that grew in <b>impurity,</b> so that other hair can grow?
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the <b>difference</b> whether he shaves for one reason or another? The Gemara explains: It is relevant with regard <b>to removing</b> hair <b>with a depilatory. If you say</b> that the reason is <b>due to a mitzva, removing with a depilatory</b> is <b>not</b> an option, as the mitzva is specifically to shave. <b>But if you say</b> the reason is <b>due to the removal of hair of impurity, even if he rubs it with a depilatory</b> that is <b>also</b> effective.
<b>What</b> then is the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Rava said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a resolution from a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 6:1): <b>And he shaves</b> with <b>four</b> acts of <b>shaving.</b> Now <b>if it should enter your mind</b> that the reason is <b>for the sake of removing hair of impurity, even three</b> shavings should <b>be enough for him,</b> two for his leprosy, and the last one for his naziriteship of purity. Since the third shaving is performed only to remove his hair of impurity so that he can start his pure naziriteship afresh, why is it included? <b>Conclude from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that all four shavings are <b>due to</b> the <b>mitzva</b> of shaving. The Gemara says: <b>Conclude from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that this is the case.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Gentiles do not have naziriteship,</b> i.e., the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship do not apply to gentiles. They are not subject to the prohibitions of a nazirite, nor does one accept their offerings at the end of naziriteship. However, <b>women and</b> Canaanite <b>slaves do have naziriteship.</b> The mishna adds: There is a greater <b>stringency in</b> the case of <b>women than in</b> the case of <b>slaves, as</b> a master <b>may force his slave</b> to drink wine, shave his hair, or become ritually impure from a corpse, despite the slave’s vow of naziriteship, <b>but</b> a husband <b>cannot force his wife</b> to transgress her naziriteship.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna <b>teaches</b> that <b>gentiles do not have naziriteship,</b> whereas women and slaves can be nazirites. The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? It is <b>as the Sages taught,</b> that the beginning of the passage about naziriteship, which states: <b>“Speak to the children of Israel”</b> (Numbers 6:2), serves to emphasize that these <i>halakhot</i> apply to Jews, <b>but not to the gentiles.</b> Furthermore, the continuation of the verse: <b>“And say to them,</b> when a man or woman shall clearly utter a vow,” serves <b>to include slaves.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need <b>a verse</b> to include slaves? <b>Haven’t you said</b> the following principle: <b>Any mitzva that a woman is obligated in</b> its performance, <b>a slave is</b> also <b>obligated in</b> its performance? Since the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship apply to a woman, they should likewise apply to slaves.
<b>Rava said: Here</b> it <b>is different, as</b> with regard to vows <b>the verse states: “To bind his soul with a bond”</b> (Numbers 30:3), and the Sages expounded that this is referring only <b>to one whose soul is in his possession,</b> i.e., who is under his own jurisdiction. This <b>excludes a slave, whose soul is not in his possession,</b> but who is under his master’s control. The verse from Leviticus is necessary, lest you say that <b>since</b> a slave’s <b>soul is not in his possession,</b> one should <b>say</b> that <b>with regard to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>a nazirite as well,</b> he <b>cannot</b> undertake this vow notwithstanding the principle that slaves have similar <i>halakhot</i> to women with regard to obligations, who can become nazirites. The aforementioned verse <b>teaches us</b> that a slave can in fact take a vow of naziriteship.
The Gemara returns to the earlier exposition of the verse. <b>The Master said</b> in the <i>baraita</i> that the verse specifies: <b>“Speak to the children of Israel,” but not to the gentiles.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And anywhere that “Israel” is written,</b> are <b>gentiles not</b> included in that verse? <b>But</b> there is a counterexample in the verse written <b>with regard to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>valuations, as it is written: “Speak to the children of Israel”</b> (Leviticus 27:2), <b>and it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The children of Israel can take a valuation vow but gentiles cannot take a valuation vow.</b> If a gentile declares: I undertake to donate the value of so-and-so, his vow does not take effect.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: One <b>might</b> have thought that this means that gentiles <b>cannot be</b> the subject <b>of a valuation</b> either, i.e., if a Jew says: I am obligated to give the value of so-and-so the gentile, his vow would not take effect. Therefore, <b>the verse states</b> the inclusive expression: “When <b>a man</b> shall clearly utter a vow of persons to the Lord, according to your valuation” (Leviticus 27:2), to teach that in this regard, every “man,” even a gentile, is included in <i>halakhot</i> of valuations. Inasmuch as the Torah also states with regard to a nazirite: “When a man or woman shall clearly utter a vow, the vow of a nazirite” (Numbers 6:2), perhaps one should include gentiles in the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship as well.
The Gemara answers: <b>Here,</b> in the case of naziriteship, it <b>is different, as the verse states: “For his father, or for his mother,</b> for his brother, or for his sister, <b>he shall not defile himself”</b> (Numbers 6:7). From here it is derived that this mitzva applies only <b>to one who has a father.</b> This <b>excludes a gentile, who does not have a father.</b> The Gemara asks: With regard <b>to what</b> <i>halakha</i> does a gentile lack a father? <b>If we say</b> it is <b>with regard to inheritance, but didn’t Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Avin say</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: A gentile inherits</b> the estate of <b>his father by Torah law, as it is stated: “Because I have given Mount Seir to Esau as an inheritance”</b> (Deuteronomy 2:5)?
<b>Rather,</b> this mitzva of naziriteship applies <b>to one who is warned concerning the honor of his father,</b> and as the mitzva to honor one’s father does not apply to a gentile, it is as though he has no father. The Gemara asks: <b>Is it written: Honor your father, in the context of a nazirite?</b> What is the connection between these two issues? <b>Rather, the verse states</b> with regard to a nazirite: <b>“For his father, or for his mother,</b> for his brother, or for his sister, <b>he shall not defile himself”</b> (Numbers 6:7), and it should be understood as referring <b>to one who has</b> the potential to become <b>ritually impure,</b>

Daf 61b

which <b>excludes a gentile, who has no</b> potential to become <b>ritually impure.</b> A gentile is not rendered impure even if he touches a corpse. An individual of this kind cannot become a nazirite. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive <b>that</b> gentiles <b>do not have</b> the ability to become <b>ritually impure?</b> It is <b>as the verse states</b> with regard to one who enters the Temple in a state of impurity: <b>“But the man who shall be impure and shall not purify himself, that soul shall be cut off from the midst of the assembly”</b> (Numbers 19:20). This indicates that the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual impurity apply only <b>to one who has</b> membership in the <b>assembly</b> of the Jewish people and <b>excludes this</b> gentile, <b>who has no</b> membership in the <b>assembly,</b> i.e., he is not part of the Jewish people.
The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> is it derived that this verse teaches that ritual impurity does not apply to a gentile at all? <b>Perhaps</b> the verse <b>is</b> referring merely to <b>excision from the World-to-Come [<i>karet</i>],</b> i.e., it is teaching <b>that he is not liable</b> to receive <i>karet</i> for entering the Temple when impure, <b>but</b> that perhaps a gentile <b>does become impure.</b> The Gemara answers that <b>the</b> previous <b>verse states: “And the pure</b> person <b>shall sprinkle upon the impure”;</b> this indicates that <b>anyone who has</b> the possibility of attaining <b>ritual purity</b> by means of the waters of purification also <b>has</b> the potential for <b>ritual impurity, and anyone who does not have</b> the possibility of attaining <b>purity</b> by means of the waters of purification <b>does not have</b> the potential for <b>impurity</b> either.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> one can <b>say</b> that <b>it is</b> only <b>purity</b> by means of the waters of purification <b>that he does not have,</b> but <b>he does have</b> the potential for <b>impurity.</b> The Gemara answers: It is for this reason that <b>the verse states: “But the man who shall be impure and shall not purify himself”</b> (Numbers 19:20), to teach that these two statuses are interdependent. One who cannot be purified by the waters of purification cannot become impure in the first place.
<b>Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov said</b> a different reason why a gentile cannot become a nazirite, despite the fact that the term “man” does include gentiles in the case of valuations. <b>Here,</b> with regard to naziriteship, it <b>is different, as</b> the verse prohibits a nazirite from becoming impure for his father and mother. This prohibition is not relevant for gentiles because <b>the verse states: “And you shall make them an inheritance for your children after you”</b> (Leviticus 25:46). This verse establishes a child as one to whom a person can bequeath his slaves, the subject of this verse.
Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov explains: This verse teaches that <b>anyone who has inheritance,</b> i.e., he has the ability to bequeath his slaves to his children, also <b>has</b> the status of a father with regard to <b>impurity,</b> and the verse prohibiting a nazirite from becoming impure to bury his father can be addressing him. <b>But anyone who does not have inheritance does not have</b> the status of a father with regard to <b>impurity.</b> Since a gentile cannot bequeath slaves to his child (see <i>Gittin</i> 38a), the status of a father with regard to impurity also does not apply to him. Consequently, the verse prohibiting a nazirite from becoming impure to bury his father cannot be addressing him. The Gemara asks: <b>If so, slaves should also not</b> be included in the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual impurity or naziriteship, as they too cannot bequeath slaves to their children.
<b>Rather, Rava said</b> a different reason why a gentile cannot become a nazirite, even though he is included in the <i>halakhot</i> of valuations, and despite the fact that the term “man” appears in both passages. <b>Granted, with regard to valuations, as it is stated:</b> “Speak to <b>the children of Israel”</b> (Leviticus 27:2), one can derive that <b>the children of Israel can take a valuation vow but gentiles cannot take a valuation vow.</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that gentiles <b>cannot be valuated</b> either. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Man”</b> (Leviticus 27:2), so as to include a gentile in only one aspect of the <i>halakhot</i> of valuations, i.e., that a gentile can be the subject of a valuation. This does not negate the derivation from “the children of Israel” that gentiles are generally excluded.
Rava continues his explanation: However, <b>here,</b> in the case of naziriteship, there is no possibility of interpreting the word “man” to include a gentile in only one aspect of the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship. Rava explains: If one would suggest the following derivation: From the phrase “the children of Israel” (Numbers 6:2) derive that <b>the children of Israel can</b> both <b>take a vow</b> of <b>naziriteship and bring</b> the nazirite <b>offering, but gentiles cannot</b> both <b>take a vow</b> of <b>naziriteship and bring</b> the nazirite <b>offering,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that gentiles <b>cannot be nazirites at all;</b> therefore, <b>the verse states: “Man,”</b> which includes gentiles in one aspect of the <i>halakhot</i> of naziriteship, i.e., that they can become nazirites.
Rava continues his explanation: However, the Sages would <b>say</b> in response to this suggestion: <b>If</b> the phrase “the children of Israel” is written <b>due to</b> the need to exclude a gentile from bringing a nazirite <b>offering, one does not</b> need to <b>derive</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>from here, and</b> this is because it is already derived <b>from there,</b> as a <i>baraita</i> teaches that the verse: “Any man from the house of Israel, or of the strangers in Israel, who sacrifices his offering, whether it be any of their vows, or any of their gift offerings, which are sacrificed to the Lord <b>as a burnt-offering”</b> (Leviticus 22:18), <b>excludes</b> a gentile from the offering of <b>naziriteship;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yosei HaGelili.</b> Therefore, the phrase “the children of Israel” must exclude gentiles from the entire passage of naziriteship, and not just from bringing the offerings.
The Gemara asks: But it can still be argued that “the children of Israel” is excluding a gentile from only some aspects of naziriteship, as one can <b>say</b> that this phrase teaches that <b>the children of Israel can take a vow</b> of <b>permanent naziriteship, but gentiles cannot take a vow</b> of <b>permanent naziriteship,</b> and one <b>might</b> have thought that gentiles <b>cannot be nazirites</b> at all. <b>The verse</b> therefore <b>states: “Man,”</b> to teach that they can become nazirites. The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Is it written: A permanent nazirite?</b> Since the verse does not specify a particular type of naziriteship, it excludes gentiles from all types.
The Gemara offers another suggestion: But it can still be argued that “the children of Israel” is excluding a gentile from only some aspects of naziriteship, as one can <b>say</b> that this phrase teaches that <b>“the children of Israel” can vow</b> that <b>their</b> minor <b>sons be nazirites, but gentiles cannot vow</b> that <b>their</b> minor <b>sons be nazirites;</b> and one <b>might</b> have thought that gentiles <b>cannot be nazirites</b> at all. <b>The verse</b> therefore <b>states: “Man,”</b> to teach that they can become nazirites. The Gemara answers: <b>Didn’t Rabbi Yoḥanan say</b> that the fact that a father can take a vow that his minor son will be a nazirite <b>is a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses at Sinai <b>with regard to a nazirite?</b> Since this <i>halakha</i> is not stated in the Torah, it cannot be excluded by a verse.
The Gemara offers another suggestion: But it can still be argued that “the children of Israel” is excluding a gentile from only some aspects of naziriteship, as one can <b>say</b> that this phrase teaches that <b>“the children of Israel” can shave</b> and cut their hair <b>by</b> means of the offerings of <b>their fathers’ naziriteships, but gentiles cannot shave</b> and cut their hair <b>by</b> means of <b>their fathers’ naziriteships.</b> In other words, if the father of a nazirite, who was himself a nazirite, died after having separated his nazirite offerings, the son is able to bring those offerings at the close of his own naziriteship.

Daf 62a

One <b>might</b> have thought that gentiles <b>cannot be nazirites</b> at all. <b>The verse</b> therefore <b>states: “Man,”</b> to teach that they can become nazirites. The Gemara answers: <b>Wasn’t it stated</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said</b> that the <i>halakha</i> that a son may use his father’s nazirite offerings <b>is a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses at Sinai <b>with regard to a nazirite?</b> Since this <i>halakha</i> does not appear in the Torah, the verse cannot be coming to exclude it.
§ The Gemara asks: <b>If so, why do I</b> need the verse: <b>“When a man shall clearly utter a vow</b> of persons to the Lord, <b>according to your valuation”</b> (Leviticus 27:2), stated <b>with regard to valuations,</b> from which it is derived that gentiles are included in the <i>halakhot</i> of valuations? <b>After all, aren’t valuations juxtaposed to vows, as the verse states: “When a man shall clearly utter a vow</b> of persons to the Lord, <b>according to your valuation.” And isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>with regard to</b> a verse dealing with <b>vows:</b> “Any <b>man [<i>ish</i>]</b> from the house of Israel, or of the strangers in Israel, who sacrifices his offering, whether it be any of their vows, or any of their gift offerings, which are sacrificed to the Lord as a burnt-offering” (Leviticus 22:18), <b>what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> the extra emphasis: <b>“Any man [<i>ish ish</i>]”?</b> The <i>baraita</i> explains: This serves <b>to include the gentiles, that they can take a vow</b> for <b>vow</b> offerings <b>and gift</b> offerings <b>like a Jew.</b> It can be derived from this juxtaposition that gentiles are included in the <i>halakhot</i> of valuations.
The Gemara now restates its question: In that case, <b>why do I</b> need the phrase <b>“when a man shall clearly utter</b> a vow” stated <b>with regard to valuations? Rather, this</b> term, <b>“man,”</b> does not include gentiles, but is <b>necessary to include a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority.</b> If a minor takes a vow one year before coming of age, and shows a clear understanding of his statement, the vow takes effect. This individual is included in the <i>halakhot</i> of valuations as well.
The Gemara comments: <b>This works out well according to the one who says</b> that <b>a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority</b> must fulfill his or her vows <b>by Torah law,</b> because the verse serves as the source for this <i>halakha</i>. <b>However, according to the one who says</b> that this <i>halakha</i> applies <b>by rabbinic law, why do I</b> need the verse <b>“when a man shall clearly utter</b> a vow”? The Gemara answers: The verse serves <b>to include a minor one year before</b> he or she <b>reaches majority who is a gentile,</b> that this <i>halakha</i> applies to gentiles by Torah law.
The Gemara comments: <b>This works out well according to the one who says</b> that it is inferred from the phrase: “The children of Israel” (Leviticus 27:2), that <b>Jews can be valuated but gentiles cannot be valuated;</b> and one <b>might</b> have thought that gentiles <b>cannot take a valuation vow.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Man.”</b> According to this opinion it is <b>fine,</b> as the phrase “when a man shall clearly utter a vow” can teach that if a gentile close to adulthood grasps the meaning of vows, he can take a valuation vow.
<b>However, according to the one who says</b> that <b>the children of Israel can take a valuation vow but gentiles cannot take a valuation vow,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that gentiles <b>cannot be valuated;</b> therefore, <b>the verse states</b> the additional term: <b>“Man,”</b> to teach that anyone, <b>even a month-old baby, can be valuated</b> according to the assigned value stated in the Torah. According to this opinion, it is of no importance how old the subject of the vow is, and therefore the verse clearly cannot be including a gentile on the verge of adulthood. Consequently, the question remains: <b>Why do I</b> need the phrase <b>“when</b> a man <b>shall clearly utter</b> a vow”?
<b>Rav Adda bar Ahava said:</b> The verse is referring to all vows, and serves <b>to include,</b> i.e., to teach, the <i>halakha</i> of a type of <b>adult gentile, who although he is an adult, he does not know</b> how <b>to clearly utter</b> a vow. The verse teaches that his vows are invalid, as derived from the phrase “when a man shall clearly vow.” Vows of a gentile are valid only if he can express them clearly.
Having clarified the verse concerning valuations, the Gemara asks: <b>However,</b> concerning the verse: <b>“When</b> a man or woman <b>shall clearly utter</b> a vow” (Numbers 6:2), <b>which the Merciful One writes with regard to naziriteship, why do I</b> need it? <b>After all, isn’t</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>naziriteship juxtaposed to vows; why do I</b> need the verse <b>“when</b> a man or woman <b>shall clearly utter”?</b>
The Gemara answers: This verse serves <b>to include</b> the case of one who vowed by means of <b>ambiguous intimations,</b> i.e., he expressed only part of the formula of the vow, so that his meaning is unclear. <b>As it was stated</b> that <i>amora’im</i> had a dispute with regard to this issue: With regard to <b>ambiguous intimations, Abaye said</b> that <b>they are</b> considered <b>intimations</b> to vows, and the vows take effect, and <b>Rava said</b> that <b>they are not</b> considered <b>intimations</b> to vows, and the vows do not take effect. <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye,</b> this answer <b>works out well,</b> as the phrase “when a man shall clearly utter,” serves to include all pronouncements indicative of vows, even ambiguous intimations. <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rava, what is there to say?</b>
<b>Rather,</b> the Gemara explains that the phrase <b>“when</b> a man or woman <b>shall clearly utter”</b> is <b>necessary for that</b> which was stated <b>by Rabbi Tarfon, as it is taught</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i> (3:19) that <b>Rabbi Yehuda said in the name of Rabbi Tarfon:</b> If several people took a vow of naziriteship as part of a wager with regard to an uncertain occurrence, <b>not one of</b> them is <b>a nazirite, as naziriteship is imposed</b> upon someone <b>only if</b> his vow is <b>explicitly</b> enunciated. That is, one is a nazirite only if he vows in a definitive manner, not if there was any uncertainty involved. The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Tarfon. However,</b> according <b>to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with his ruling, <b>what is there to say?</b>
<b>Rather,</b> the phrase “when a man or woman shall clearly utter” is <b>necessary for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The <i>halakhot</i> of <b>dissolution of vows,</b> namely that one may request from a halakhic authority to dissolve them, <b>fly in the air and have nothing to lean upon,</b> i.e., these <i>halakhot</i> are not mentioned explicitly in the Torah.
<b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> The <i>halakhot</i> of dissolution of vows <b>have</b> a basis <b>upon which to lean, as it is stated: “When a man shall clearly utter</b> a vow” (Leviticus 27:2) and: <b>“When</b> a man or woman <b>shall clearly utter</b> a vow” (Numbers 6:2), <b>twice. One explicit utterance is for prohibition,</b> i.e., when one takes a vow, he is bound to keep it, <b>and one explicit utterance is for dissolution,</b> i.e., in the event that he provides a halakhic authority with a reason why the vow should no longer apply, the vow can be dissolved and he will no longer be bound by it. This is an allusion in the Torah for the dissolution of vows.

Daf 62b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The previous mishna taught that the naziriteship of women includes a stringency that does not apply to slaves. This mishna adds: There is a greater <b>stringency in</b> the case of <b>slaves than in</b> the case of <b>women, as</b> a man <b>can nullify the vows of his wife but he cannot nullify the vows of his slave,</b> despite the fact that he can prevent him from fulfilling them in practice. Similarly, if <b>he nullified</b> the naziriteship <b>of his wife it is permanently nullified,</b> and it remains nullified even if she is later divorced or widowed. Conversely, if <b>he nullified</b> the naziriteship <b>of his slave</b> by forcing him to violate the terms of his vow of naziriteship, when the slave <b>is emancipated he completes his naziriteship.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>The Sages taught</b> (<i>Tosefta</i> 6:4): With regard <b>to what</b> matter can <b>his master force</b> a slave? <b>With regard to naziriteship. However,</b> he <b>cannot</b> force his slave in the case <b>of</b> other <b>vows and valuations.</b> This <i>baraita</i> is apparently saying that a master cannot prevent his slave from fulfilling the terms of a vow.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is different with regard to a nazirite</b> that a master can force his slave to transgress his naziriteship, <b>as the Merciful One states: “To bind his soul with a bond”</b> (Numbers 30:3), from which it is inferred that the Torah is referring only <b>to one whose soul is in his possession,</b> i.e., who is not owned by another; this <b>excludes a slave, whose soul is not in his possession</b> because he is under his master’s control. <b>If so,</b> then <b>even with regard to vows</b> that same <i>halakha</i> should apply <b>as well,</b> as that verse is written in the context of vows in general, not specifically vows of naziriteship. Why is a master unable to compel his slave to break a vow?
<b>Rav Sheshet said: With what are we dealing here?</b> With a case <b>where a cluster of grapes was placed before</b> the slave and he vowed not to derive benefit from it. <b>With regard to vows, when</b> he <b>is prohibited from</b> eating <b>this</b> cluster he <b>is not rendered prohibited from</b> eating <b>others;</b> therefore, the master <b>cannot force him</b> to eat it, as he has no reason to insist that the slave consume this particular cluster of grapes.
By contrast, <b>with regard to naziriteship, when</b> he <b>is prohibited from</b> eating <b>this</b> cluster he <b>is rendered prohibited from</b> consuming <b>all others;</b> therefore, the master <b>can force him</b> to eat. This is because the lack of food weakens the slave, who is the property of his master. The Gemara asks: <b>And with regard to vows, are we not dealing</b> even with a situation <b>where he has only this cluster</b> before him and he vows not to eat it, in <b>which</b> case <b>if he does not eat it he is weakened?</b> Why can’t the master compel him to eat the grapes in this case?
<b>Rather, Rava said</b> that the difference between vows of naziriteship and other vows concerns a case <b>where there was a grape seed,</b> which provides only negligible nourishment, that was <b>placed before</b> the slave, and he vowed not to eat it. <b>With regard to vows,</b> concerning <b>which he is prohibited to</b> eat only <b>this</b> one seed, the master <b>cannot force him</b> to eat it, as refraining from eating a grape seed would not weaken him. <b>With regard to a nazirite, who is prohibited from</b> consuming <b>other</b> grape products <b>as well,</b> the master <b>can force him.</b>
The Gemara again asks: <b>And with regard to vows, are we not dealing</b> even with a situation <b>where he has only this grape seed</b> in front of him, in <b>which</b> case <b>if he does not eat it he would be weakened?</b> Why, then, can the master compel him to violate only his naziriteship vow but not a regular vow?
<b>Rather, Abaye said</b> that the <i>baraita</i> does not in fact list cases in which a master can compel his slave to violate his vow, but rather means the following: <b>With regard to what</b> situation <b>is a master</b> who wants to negate his slave’s vow <b>required to force</b> his slave to violate his vow by stating explicitly that he does not want him to fulfill it? It is the case <b>of</b> a vow of <b>naziriteship. But he is not required to force him in</b> the case of other <b>vows and he is not required to force him in</b> the case of <b>an oath,</b> as these do not take effect at all.
<b>What is the reason</b> for this? It is <b>as the verse states</b> with regard to oaths: “Or if anyone swear clearly with his lips <b>to do evil, or to do good”</b> (Leviticus 5:4). <b>Just as</b> the <b>“good”</b> mentioned in this verse is referring to <b>a voluntary</b> action, <b>so too</b> the <b>“evil”</b> is <b>voluntary,</b> e.g., if he takes an oath not to derive benefit from an item. This <b>excludes</b> a slave, whose oath or vow <b>would cause evil to others, as it is not in his power</b> to affect his master adversely. Therefore, his statement is invalid. Here too, as the owner will suffer if his slave’s diet is restricted, a slave may not accept a vow or an oath upon himself.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In a case where a slave took a vow of naziriteship but was prevented by his master from fulfilling the terms of his vow, the Sages engaged in a dispute what the <i>halakha</i> would be if he permanently <b>left his</b> master’s <b>presence,</b> i.e., he ran away without being emancipated. <b>Rabbi Meir says: He may not drink</b> wine. Since the slave is free in practice, his vow goes into effect. <b>And Rabbi Yosei says: He may drink</b> wine, as he is not emancipated.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara suggests: <b>Shall we say</b> that Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yosei <b>disagree with regard to</b> the following statement <b>of Shmuel? As Shmuel said:</b> With regard to one <b>who renounces ownership of his slave,</b> the slave <b>is emancipated, and he does not require a bill of manumission.</b> This <i>halakha</i> indicates that if a slave is free in practice, he no longer requires a bill of manumission but is automatically considered a freeman.
The Gemara asks: If so, should one say that <b>Rabbi Meir is of</b> the opinion that the ruling is in accordance with the opinion <b>of Shmuel,</b> and therefore the slave is obligated in naziriteship as soon as he runs away, like any other freed slave, and <b>Rabbi Yosei is of</b> the opinion that the ruling is <b>not</b> in accordance with the opinion <b>of Shmuel?</b>
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>No,</b> it is possible <b>that everyone is of</b> the opinion that the ruling is in accordance with the opinion <b>of Shmuel. Rather,</b> the mishna is referring to a case where the master refused to declare the runaway ownerless, and was intent on recovering him. Consequently, the slave is not in fact a freeman at all, and the <i>tanna’im</i> disagree with regard to the following: Rabbi Yosei, <b>the one who says</b> that <b>he may drink</b> wine, <b>maintains</b> that the slave <b>will ultimately return and come back to</b> his master, and therefore it is preferable for him <b>to drink wine so that he should not be weakened</b> by the time he returns. <b>And according to</b> Rabbi Meir, <b>the one who says</b> that <b>he may not drink</b> wine, he <b>maintains</b> that it is better that the slave <b>should suffer</b> by being deprived of wine, <b>so that he will return to</b> his master, as the desire to drink wine will spur him to return.

Daf 63a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>a nazirite who shaved</b> for the conclusion of his naziriteship, <b>and it</b> later <b>became known to him that</b> during his naziriteship <b>he was ritually impure</b> from a corpse, <b>if</b> it was <b>a known impurity,</b> i.e., people were aware of the impurity when he became impure, <b>he negates</b> his entire naziriteship. <b>And if it was ritual impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths,</b> one that was unknown at the time, <b>he does not negate</b> his naziriteship. <b>If</b> he discovered he was impure <b>before he shaved, he negates</b> his naziriteship <b>in either case.</b>
The mishna asks: <b>How</b> does one differentiate between a known and an unknown impurity? If a nazirite <b>descended to immerse in a cave, and a corpse was found floating at the mouth of the cave,</b> he is <b>impure,</b> as an openly visible corpse is a known impurity. What, then, is an impurity of the depths? This is referring to a case where the corpse <b>was found sunk into the ground of the cave</b> in such a manner that it was unknown.
However, even here the circumstances of the case must be taken into account. If one <b>descended</b> not to immerse himself in the water, as he was ritually pure, but <b>to cool</b> himself, he remains <b>pure.</b> If he was impure and entered the water <b>to purify himself from the impurity from a corpse,</b> he is <b>impure.</b> The reason is that something <b>that</b> has <b>the presumptive status of impurity</b> remains <b>impure, and</b> something that has <b>the presumptive status of purity</b> is <b>pure, as there is a basis for the matter.</b> It is reasonable that items or people retain their presumptive status.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived, that there is a difference between known and unknown impurity? <b>Rabbi Eliezer said that the verse states</b> with regard to a nazirite: <b>“And if any man shall die very suddenly beside him”</b> (Numbers 6:9). The emphasis provided by the term <b>“beside him”</b> indicates <b>that</b> it is <b>clear to him</b> that he has become impure. However, one is not impure if the presence of the corpse is unknown.
<b>Reish Lakish said</b> a different source: <b>The verse states</b> with regard to the Paschal offering: “Any man of you <b>who shall be ritually impure due to a corpse or on a road far away”</b> (Numbers 9:10). The word “road” is juxtaposed in the verse to the term “ritually impure,” indicating that the impurity is <b>like a road. Just as a road is in the open, so too, every</b> impurity is <b>in the open.</b> It must be a known impurity.
The Gemara objects: <b>But</b> consider <b>that which we learned</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i> (<i>Zavim</i> 2:9): <b>Which is the ritual impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths?</b> It is impurity imparted by <b>any</b> corpse <b>of which no one is aware,</b> even <b>at the end of the earth.</b> This type of impurity is permitted for both a nazirite and one who sacrifices the Paschal offering. <b>However,</b> if even <b>one</b> person <b>is aware of it,</b> even <b>at the end of the earth, this is not impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths.</b>
The Gemara states its question: <b>Granted, according to the one who says</b> that this <i>halakha</i> is derived from the verse that states that ritual impurity is <b>like a road,</b> it is <b>fine,</b> as an item known to someone in the world can be compared to a road. <b>However, according to the one who says</b> that this <i>halakha</i> is derived from the term “beside him,” i.e., it is referring <b>to</b> an impurity that is <b>clear to him, if one</b> person <b>at the</b> other <b>end of the earth is aware of</b> this impurity, <b>what of it?</b> It was unknown to the nazirite himself.
<b>And furthermore,</b> consider <b>that which is taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Zavim</i> 2:8): In the case of <b>one who finds a corpse lying</b> across <b>the width of a road,</b> i.e., it had been buried there in such a way that it was impossible for the passerby to avoid becoming impure by passing over the corpse, then <b>with regard to <i>teruma</i>,</b> the passerby is <b>impure.</b> Therefore, if he is a priest, he may not eat <i>teruma</i>. However, <b>with regard to</b> both being <b>a nazirite and</b> being <b>one performing</b> the ritual of <b>the Paschal offering,</b> the passerby is <b>pure. What is different</b> about these situations? <b>Rather,</b> it must be that the <i>halakha</i> of <b>impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths is learned as a tradition</b> and not from the verses, which are cited merely in support.
§ The mishna taught that <b>if</b> a nazirite discovered he was ritually impure <b>before he shaved,</b> he negates his naziriteship even if he was rendered impure by impurity of the depths. The Gemara asks: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught</b> this opinion? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: It is Rabbi Eliezer, who says</b> that <b>shaving is indispensable</b> to the completion of one’s naziriteship. Consequently, if he discovered he was impure before he shaved, he became impure during his naziriteship, and he negates the period he has observed.
<b>Rami bar Ḥama raised a dilemma: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to one who <b>became impure during the full term</b> of the regular days of his naziriteship <b>but</b> his impurity <b>became known to him</b> only <b>after the full term</b> but before he shaved? Perhaps <b>we go</b> according to his <b>knowledge, and it is</b> a case of <b>knowledge after the full term, or</b> perhaps the <i>halakha</i> is <b>not</b> determined by the time of his awareness but by the actual time of the impurity, which occurred during his naziriteship. The Gemara adds: <b>And with regard to what</b> issue was this dilemma raised? It was <b>with regard to</b> his possible <b>negation</b> of the period he observed as a nazirite: Does he negate his naziriteship, or is he considered to have contracted impurity after the completion of his term, in which case he need not start his naziriteship afresh?

Daf 63b

<b>Rava said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a resolution to this dilemma from the mishna: <b>If</b> he discovered he was impure <b>before he shaved, he negates</b> the days of his naziriteship <b>in either case.</b> The Gemara seeks to clarify this: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of this case? When exactly did he find out about the impurity? <b>If</b> it <b>became known to him during the full term</b> of his naziriteship, <b>need this be said,</b> that he negates the previous days? After all, he has yet to complete his naziriteship vow. <b>Rather, is it not</b> referring to a case when the impurity was discovered <b>after the full term</b> of his naziriteship? <b>Conclude from</b> the mishna that he negates the days of his naziriteship even if he discovered the impurity after the completion of his term.
The Gemara continues to analyze the aforementioned case. <b>And you</b> can <b>still raise the dilemma:</b> Does <b>he negate</b> the <b>entire</b> period of his naziriteship <b>or</b> does <b>he negate</b> just <b>seven</b> days? The Gemara asks: <b>According to whom</b> is this dilemma raised? <b>If we say</b> this dilemma is referring <b>to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis</b> (see 16b), <b>it is obvious that</b> he <b>negates it all,</b> as they maintain that even a nazirite who becomes impure after the completion of his naziriteship must observe another thirty days. <b>And if</b> it is referring <b>to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer,</b> his ruling with regard to <b>any</b> impurity <b>after the full term</b> of his term is that one <b>negates</b> only <b>seven</b> days.
The Gemara responds: The one who raised this dilemma could have <b>said to you: This statement</b> of Rabbi Eliezer that a nazirite negates a mere seven days applies only <b>if he became impure after the full term</b> of his naziriteship, <b>but this one</b> became impure <b>before</b> the end of <b>the full term,</b> and therefore he negates the entire period. <b>Or perhaps it is different here, as it is</b> a case of <b>knowledge</b> that came to light <b>after the full term</b> of his naziriteship.
The Gemara answers: <b>And</b> one can resolve the dilemma <b>from this</b> mishna itself. The mishna <b>teaches</b> that if the nazirite discovered he was impure before he shaved <b>he negates</b> his naziriteship <b>in either case. And it does not distinguish</b> between cases where this happened before the end of the full term or after it. This indicates that in any case he negates only seven days.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Zavim</i> 2:8): In the case of <b>one who finds a corpse lying</b> across <b>the width of a road, with regard to <i>teruma</i></b> the passerby is <b>impure. But with regard to</b> both <b>a nazirite and one performing</b> the ritual of <b>the Paschal offering,</b> the passerby is <b>pure,</b> as it is considered impurity imparted by a grave in the depths. <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement said,</b> that one is impure with regard to partaking of <i>teruma</i>? It is said in a case <b>where he does not have space to pass by</b> on the road without passing over the corpse.
<b>But</b> if <b>he has space to pass by,</b> then <b>even with regard to <i>teruma</i></b> he is <b>pure.</b> This is because it is possible that the passerby did not become ritually impure, and there is a principle that if an uncertainty arises concerning the ritual purity of a person or item in the public domain, the person or item is considered pure.
Similarly, <b>in what</b> case <b>is this statement said?</b> It is said in a case <b>where one finds</b> the corpse <b>whole. However,</b> if it is <b>broken or dismembered</b> he is pure, <b>even</b> if <b>there is no space to pass by.</b> The reason is that <b>we suspect</b> that <b>perhaps he passed between the parts</b> of the corpse and did not touch or pass over any of them. This applies when he finds the corpse out in the open. <b>But</b> if he finds it <b>in a grave, even</b> if it is <b>broken</b> or <b>dismembered,</b> he is <b>impure.</b> This is <b>because the grave joins</b> the parts into one unit and renders him impure if he passed over any part of the grave, even if he did not pass over part of the corpse.
The <i>baraita</i> adds: <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement said,</b> i.e., that if the corpse was dismembered the passerby is pure? It is said <b>with regard to</b> a passerby who <b>travels by foot. However,</b> if he was <b>loaded</b> with a heavy burden <b>or</b> was <b>riding</b> an animal, he is <b>impure.</b> This is <b>because</b> in the case of <b>one who travels by foot, it is possible that he will not touch</b> the corpse <b>and will not move</b> it <b>and will not pass over</b> it, whereas in the case of one who is <b>loaded</b> with a heavy burden and therefore does not walk in a straight line, <b>or</b> one <b>riding</b> an animal, <b>it is impossible that he will not touch</b> the corpse <b>and will not move</b> it <b>and will not pass over</b> it.
<b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement said,</b> that a nazirite and one bringing a Paschal offering are considered pure? It is said <b>with regard to impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths. However,</b> if the source of <b>impurity</b> was <b>known</b> to others but not to the individual who became impure, all <b>three of them,</b> i.e., a nazirite, one performing the ritual of the Paschal offering, and the one who wishes to partake of <i>teruma</i>, are <b>impure.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>And which</b> corpse <b>is</b> considered to impart <b>impurity of the depths? Any</b> corpse <b>of which no one is aware,</b> even <b>at the end of the earth.</b> But if even <b>one</b> individual <b>is aware of it,</b> even if that person is <b>at the end of the earth, this is not</b> considered <b>impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: To ascertain whether anyone ever knew about the corpse, its condition is taken into account. If the body <b>was concealed in hay or in pebbles,</b> so the person might have died in an avalanche, it is likely that the corpse had never been found; <b>this is impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths.</b> However, if it was found <b>in water, or in a dark</b> place, <b>or in the clefts of the rocks, this is not impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths.</b> Although these are places where people do not often go, with the passage of time the corpse is likely to be discovered, and it is quite possible that someone already passed by and saw it.
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: <b>And</b> the Sages <b>said</b> that the leniency of <b>impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths</b> applies <b>only with regard to a corpse,</b> but not with regard to other sources of impurity.
§ The mishna taught: <b>How</b> does one differentiate between a known and an unknown impurity? If a nazirite <b>descended</b> to immerse in a cave, and a corpse was found floating at the mouth of the cave, he is impure. The Gemara comments: <b>A floating</b> impurity <b>does not render</b> a person or item <b>impure in the case of</b> a carcass of <b>a creeping animal. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Teharot</i> 5:6): With regard to the case of <b>uncertain impurity,</b> where an item might have touched something impure that was <b>floating, either</b> in water <b>in a vessel or</b> in water <b>in the ground,</b> e.g., a well, the item is <b>pure.
