Rabbi Shimon says:</b> If the impurity was floating in water that was <b>in a vessel,</b> the item is <b>impure;</b> if the impurity was in water <b>in the ground,</b> it is <b>pure.</b>

Daf 64a

<b>What is the reason of the first <i>tanna</i></b> for declaring that in all uncertain cases of floating impurity the person or item remains pure? <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Avudimi says:</b> In the passage dealing with the impurity of creeping animals and the prohibition of eating them <b>it is written: “With any swarming thing that swarms”</b> (Leviticus 11:43), indicating that a carcass of a creeping animal renders items impure in <b>any place where it swarms. And it is written:</b> “All swarming things that swarm <b>upon the earth”</b> (Leviticus 11:42), indicating that it transmits impurity only if it is on the earth. <b>How so?</b> How can one reconcile these two verses? <b>Definite contact</b> with <b>it</b> renders one <b>impure;</b> one who has <b>uncertain contact</b> with <b>it,</b> e.g., the impurity is floating, remains <b>pure.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And Rabbi Shimon, what is the reason</b> for his opinion? <b>Ulla said</b> that as <b>it is written: “Nevertheless a fountain</b> or a cistern in which there is a gathering of water shall be pure” (Leviticus 11:36), this indicates that a creeping animal found in one of these places does not impart impurity. <b>And it is written</b> in the same verse: “He who touches their carcass <b>shall be impure</b> until evening,” which indicates that it does render one impure. <b>How so?</b> If the impurity was <b>floating</b> in water contained <b>in vessels,</b> the item it touched <b>is</b> rendered <b>impure,</b> but if the water was <b>in the ground</b> itself, e.g., in a spring or pit, the item it touched is <b>pure.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Teharot</i> 3:13): <b>All</b> items that impart impurity <b>that are carried</b> by human hand <b>or that are dragged</b> along are not considered floating impurities, despite the fact that they are in motion. Rather, in a case where there is <b>uncertainty</b> whether a carried or dragged item affected a person, the individual is rendered <b>impure,</b> because the items are considered <b>as though</b> they were <b>at rest. And</b> in the case of items <b>that are thrown</b> by people, in <b>uncertain</b> cases that pertain to them, the individual remains <b>pure.</b>
This is the <i>halakha</i>, <b>except for an olive-bulk</b> from <b>a corpse,</b> which transmits impurity through uncertain contact even if it was thrown; <b>and that which overlies impurity</b> when thrown, i.e., an item that might have been positioned over a corpse when it was thrown; <b>and anything that renders</b> items <b>above</b> it <b>impure like</b> it renders those <b>below</b> it impure. To what does this last clause refer? It comes <b>to include a <i>zav</i> and a <i>zava</i>,</b> who render items placed above and below them impure even without contact. If something thrown or floating might have come into contact with a <i>zav</i> or <i>zava</i>, it is impure.
With regard to this <i>halakha</i> of a floating impurity, <b>Rami bar Ḥama raises a dilemma:</b> If there is <b>a corpse in a vessel and the vessel is floating on water,</b> and one did not overlie it but might have touched it, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Do <b>we go according to</b> the floating <b>vessel or</b> do <b>we go according to</b> the <b>corpse,</b> which is resting on a solid surface?
<b>If you say</b> that <b>we go according to the vessel</b> and one is rendered ritually impure, the following question arises: If <b>a corpse</b> is placed <b>on top of</b> the carcass of <b>a creeping animal,</b> which is floating on water, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Do we say that <b>since this</b> impurity of a creeping animal is <b>an impurity for an evening,</b> i.e., it lasts one day, <b>and this</b> impurity imparted by a corpse is <b>an impurity for seven</b> days, <b>it is considered as though the impurity</b> imparted by a corpse <b>were placed in a vessel,</b> and he is impure? <b>Or, perhaps</b> the fact that both a corpse and a creeping animal impart impurity means that <b>it is</b> one <b>solid impurity,</b> and because the creeping animal is floating he is pure.
<b>And if you say</b> that due to their different levels of impurity <b>it is considered as though the impurity were placed in a vessel and</b> therefore one is <b>definitely impure,</b> one can raise an additional dilemma. If <b>a creeping animal</b> is <b>atop</b> an unslaughtered <b>animal carcass and the animal carcass</b> is <b>floating,</b> then <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Do we say that <b>since they are both</b> examples of <b>an impurity for an evening, it is</b> considered a single <b>solid impurity, or perhaps</b> here too they are different, as <b>this</b> one, the carcass, renders items impure when it is the amount of <b>an olive-bulk, and this</b> one, the creeping animal, does so when it is the amount <b>of a lentil-bulk.</b> In that case, the animal carcass and the creeping animal should be considered separate items, and it is as though the impurity is placed in a vessel, and he is impure.
If in that case they are considered separate, then one can also ask: If <b>a creeping animal</b> is positioned <b>atop</b> another <b>creeping animal, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Do we say that <b>these certainly</b> have the <b>same measurement</b> with regard to transmitting impurity, and that they consequently should be viewed as a single floating items, which means he is pure? <b>Or, perhaps, since</b> they <b>are separate from each other</b> and are not in fact a single item, they are <b>not</b> considered as one unit.
<b>And if you say</b> that in the case of <b>a creeping animal atop a creeping animal, since</b> they <b>are separate from each other, it is considered as though</b> the impurity <b>were placed in a vessel</b> and is not floating, one can raise an additional dilemma. If <b>a creeping animal</b> was placed <b>atop an animal carcass that has dissolved, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Do we say that <b>since it has dissolved it has become</b> like <b>liquid,</b> and therefore it is as though the creeping animal were floating on liquid? <b>Or, perhaps this</b> carcass <b>is</b> still considered <b>food,</b> rather than a liquid, and the creeping animal is on a solid surface.
<b>And if you say that</b> a dissolved animal carcasses <b>is</b> considered <b>food,</b> one can raise an additional dilemma. If <b>a creeping animal</b> was placed <b>on top of semen,</b> which is certainly liquid, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is this creeping animal considered a floating impurity? <b>And if you say</b> that <b>once</b> semen <b>is ejaculated</b> from the body <b>it is considered like food</b> rather than drink, as it is viscous, one can raise an additional dilemma. If <b>a creeping animal</b> was placed <b>atop waters of purification,</b> i.e., the water into which the ashes of the red heifer are mixed, which becomes highly viscous, <b>and</b> the <b>waters of purification are floating on water, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara responds: <b>We do not know</b> the answer to any of these questions, and therefore the dilemmas <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.

Daf 64b

<b>Rav Hamnuna says:</b> In the case of <b>a nazirite and one performing</b> the ritual of <b>the Paschal</b> offering <b>who walked by a grave in the depths,</b> i.e., an unknown grave, <b>on their seventh</b> day of <b>their</b> purification, i.e., seven days after they were sprinkled with the purification waters after having contracted ritual impurity imparted by a corpse, <b>they are pure.</b> If the nazirite shaved for his impurity and completed his naziriteship in purity or if the one performing the ritual of the Paschal offering sacrificed his offering, then when he eventually discovers the impurity, it is considered as though he were pure all along. <b>What is the reason</b> for this lenient ruling? It is <b>because the ritual impurity</b> imparted by a grave <b>in the depths is not strong</b> enough <b>to negate</b> their actions, i.e., the nazirite offerings or the Paschal offering.
<b>Rava raised an objection</b> to this ruling from the statement of the mishna that if there is one whose impurity imparted by a grave in the depths is uncertain and he <b>descended to purify himself from the ritual impurity</b> imparted by <b>a corpse,</b> he is <b>impure,</b> as a person or item <b>that has the presumptive status of impurity</b> remains <b>impure, and</b> one that has <b>the presumptive status of purity</b> remains <b>pure.</b> Here too, the nazirite and the one performing the ritual of the Paschal offering had not completed their purification at the time, as the seventh day following their being sprinkled had not ended. As their presumptive status is impure, they are retroactively rendered impure, even by an impurity of the depths.
Rav Hamnuna <b>said to</b> Rava: <b>I agree with you with regard to a nazirite</b> who descended to purify himself and <b>who is lacking</b> the act of <b>shaving.</b> He has yet to shave his head for his impurity and is therefore not completely pure. Consequently, he follows his prior presumptive status of impurity.
<b>Rava said to him: I, too, agree with you with regard to one performing</b> the ritual of <b>the Paschal offering</b> that he is pure, <b>as he is not lacking anything.</b> Since he does not have to perform any action on his body before he can bring an offering, one can say that he already has a presumptive status of purity on the seventh day. <b>Abaye said to him: But</b> he <b>is</b> still <b>lacking sunset,</b> i.e., he is not fully pure until the sun sets on the seventh day. Rava <b>said to</b> Abaye: <b>The sun sets by itself,</b> and therefore this cannot be seen as a deficiency involving an action.
The Gemara comments: <b>And even Abaye</b> accepted Rava’s answer and <b>retracted, as</b> can be seen from that which <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>. By Torah law, a woman who gives birth to a boy is ritually impure for seven days, and a woman who gives birth to a girl is impure for fourteen days. At that point, the woman immerses in a ritual bath and is purified. Any blood that emerges from the woman during her days of purity, i.e., for forty days following the birth of a male and eighty days following the birth of a female, does not render her impure. She cannot bring the offering brought by a woman who has given birth or miscarried until she has immersed at the end of these days (see Leviticus, chapter 12). The <i>baraita</i> discusses a case where a woman who had given birth became pregnant and miscarried before she had brought her offering for the first birth.
The <i>baraita</i> teaches: If a woman miscarried on <b>the day of the fulfillment</b> of her purity, on the eighty-first day after a female, <b>she must bring</b> a separate offering for the miscarried fetus, as she was obligated to bring one offering before her miscarriage. If she miscarried <b>during the fulfillment,</b> i.e., before the conclusion of the eighty days for the birth of a daughter, <b>she does not bring</b> two offerings but only one, just as is the <i>halakha</i> in the case of one who gives birth to twins.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>she should not bring</b> an offering <b>for her childbirth,</b> i.e., miscarriage, <b>that</b> occurred <b>before the fulfillment</b> of the days of her purity <b>but she should bring for her childbirth that</b> occurred <b>after</b> its <b>completion.</b> In other words, if she had yet another miscarriage, after the days of her purity for her initial birth but within the eighty days of purity following her first miscarriage, she should bring an extra offering <b>and</b> thereby <b>discharge both</b> obligations, of her birth and her final miscarriage.
Therefore, to counteract this possibility, <b>the verse states: “And when the days of her purity are fulfilled…</b>she shall bring” (Leviticus 12:6). This teaches that it is only if she miscarried <b>on the day of the fulfillment</b> itself that <b>she must bring</b> an offering for a miscarriage, but if she miscarried <b>before the fulfillment</b> of the days of her purity of the earlier miscarriage, even if this occurred more than eighty days after the first birth, <b>she does not bring</b> another offering.
<b>Rav Kahana said</b> in explanation: <b>Here it is different.</b> The reason why she does not bring an offering for a miscarriage during her days of purity is <b>that she lacks</b> the possibility of bringing her <b>offering.</b> Since she cannot bring her offering until the end of her term of purity, she cannot incur another obligation during this period, no matter how many births occur within eighty days of the previous one. However, this leads Rav Kahana to ask: <b>There too,</b> if she had a miscarriage on the day of the completion of her term, she cannot bring her offering either, as she <b>lacks sunset.</b> Why, then, must she bring an additional offering?
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rav Kahana: <b>The sun sets by itself</b> and is not considered a deficiency with regard to her purity. This discussion shows that Abaye accepted Rava’s reasoning, as he submitted the same argument himself in a different context.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who finds a corpse for the first</b> time, i.e., he discovers a single corpse in a place that was not previously established as a cemetery, if the corpse is <b>lying in</b> the <b>usual manner</b> of Jewish burial, <b>he removes it</b> from there <b>and</b> also <b>its surrounding</b> earth. It is assumed that this corpse was buried there alone. There is no concern that this area is a cemetery and therefore the corpse may not be moved, nor does one take into account the possibility that another corpse may be buried in the vicinity.
Similarly, if he found <b>two</b> corpses, <b>he removes them and their surrounding</b> earth. In a case where he <b>found three</b> corpses, <b>if there is</b> a space <b>between this</b> corpse <b>and that</b> corpse <b>of four to eight cubits,</b> in a standard design, <b>this is a graveyard.</b> There is a concern that this might be an ancient cemetery.

Daf 65a

One must therefore <b>examine from that</b> spot <b>outward</b> for <b>twenty cubits.</b> If one <b>finds</b> another corpse <b>at the end of twenty cubits,</b> he <b>examines</b> from that spot <b>outward twenty cubits, as there is a basis for</b> anticipating <b>the matter.</b> It is likely that he has stumbled upon an ancient gravesite. He is not permitted to relocate the corpses, despite the fact <b>that if he had found</b> the single corpse by itself <b>at first</b> he could have <b>removed it and its surrounding</b> earth.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Yehuda said</b> the following inferences from the mishna: The phrase: He <b>found, excludes</b> a corpse <b>that</b> already had <b>been found.</b> If it was known that there was one corpse buried in a certain place, the discovery of two previously unknown corpses does not raise the concern that perhaps it is a forgotten graveyard. Similarly, the term <b>corpse [<i>met</i>] excludes a killed [<i>harug</i>]</b> person. Even if there were three corpses found, if there are signs that these people were killed, the area is not assumed to be a graveyard, as they may have been buried where they were found killed. Likewise, the term <b>lying excludes a sitting</b> person, as Jews were not generally buried in a seated position. The phrase: <b>In</b> the usual <b>manner, excludes</b> one <b>whose head was placed between his thighs,</b> as that is not the way Jews are buried.
<b>Ulla bar Ḥanina taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Oholot</i> 16:2): <b>A corpse that is lacking</b> a part of his body indispensable to life <b>has no</b> <i>halakha</i> of <b>surrounding</b> earth, i.e., there is no need to remove the nearby earth along with the corpse. <b>Nor</b> does it have the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a graveyard,</b> i.e., it does not join with two other corpses to establish this site as a cemetery. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>all these</b> listed above, i.e., a corpse that was buried in a sitting position or with its head between its thighs, <b>what is the reason</b> that they are <b>not</b> considered part of a graveyard? The Gemara answers: <b>We say</b> that <b>perhaps</b> the deceased <b>was a gentile,</b> as Jews are not usually buried in these ways.
The <i>baraita</i> further states: If one <b>found two</b> corpses, with <b>the head of this</b> one <b>by the feet of that</b> one <b>and the head of that</b> one <b>by the feet of this</b> one, <b>they do not have</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>surrounding earth, nor</b> do they have the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a graveyard.</b> This is not the way Jews are buried, as corpses in a Jewish cemetery always face the same direction. If one <b>found three</b> corpses, <b>one</b> of which was previously <b>known, while</b> the other <b>two</b> were found <b>for the first</b> time now, <b>or</b> if one found <b>two for the first</b> time <b>and two</b> that were <b>known, they do not have</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>surrounding</b> earth, <b>and they</b> also <b>do not have</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a graveyard.</b> One does not view these corpses as connected.
The <i>baraita</i> relates: <b>An incident</b> occurred <b>involving Rabbi Yeshevav, who examined and found two known</b> corpses <b>and one</b> corpse discovered <b>for the first</b> time, <b>and he wished to deem</b> the three corpses <b>a graveyard. Rabbi Akiva said to him: All your toil is in vain. They said</b> it is <b>a graveyard only</b> in a case <b>of three known</b> corpses buried in one spot <b>or three</b> corpses found <b>for the first</b> time. However, if some were known and others were discovered for the first time, one does not combine them.
§ The mishna taught that <b>he removes them and their surrounding</b> earth. The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of <b>surrounding</b> earth? <b>Rav Yehuda said: The verse states</b> with regard to Jacob’s instruction to Joseph to transfer his remains to Eretz Yisrael: <b>“You shall carry me out from Egypt”</b> (Genesis 47:30), which indicates: <b>Take</b> some earth out from Egypt <b>with me,</b> i.e., take the earth that is near the corpse.
The Gemara further asks: <b>And what is the measure of surrounding</b> earth? <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Tzadok, explained: One takes loose dirt</b> from near the corpse, as it is assumed it has been loosened by the blood and moisture from the corpse, <b>and digs virgin,</b> uncultivated, ground to a depth of <b>three fingerbreadths,</b> in case this earth has absorbed the blood.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to this ruling from a different <i>baraita</i>: <b>And what is the measure of surrounding</b> earth? <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Tzadok, explained: One takes wood chips</b> found nearby, which might have been part of the coffin, <b>and lumps</b> of earth that might have absorbed the blood and moisture from a corpse.
<b>And he discards</b> that which is <b>certainly</b> not from the corpse, e.g., stones. And <b>he sets aside the</b> items with regard to which it is <b>uncertain</b> if they have a connection with the corpse. <b>And the rest,</b> i.e., anything that is apparently from the corpse, <b>combine to</b> reach the amount <b>of the majority of the structure of a corpse, or of a quarter-</b><i>kav</i> <b>of bones,</b> or <b>of a full ladle of dust</b> from a corpse. The remains of a corpse impart ritual impurity in a tent only if they meet one of those three qualifications. Anything which is apparently the remains of the corpse is considered dust of a corpse with regard to this <i>halakha</i>. In any event, it is clear that this <i>baraita</i> presents a different definition of surrounding earth.
The Gemara answers: With regard to the first statement of Rabbi Elazar, it was <b>he who said in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>that <i>tanna</i>,</b> ben Azzai, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>And how much is the measure of surrounding</b> earth? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says in the name of ben Azzai: One takes loose earth and digs virgin</b> ground to a depth of <b>three fingerbreadths.</b>
§ The mishna taught that one must <b>examine</b> the ground for up to twenty cubits <b>from that</b> spot where a corpse was found.

Daf 65b

<b>Rava said:</b> With regard to one who <b>examined,</b> found a corpse, <b>and removed</b> it from its place, and again <b>examined</b> nearby, found another corpse, <b>and removed</b> it from its place as well, if he <b>examined</b> yet again <b>and discovered</b> a third corpse, <b>he does not move this</b> third one <b>alongside these two</b> he has already moved, as he now knows that this was a graveyard and the corpses were buried there intentionally. <b>And he does not</b> return <b>these two alongside this one</b> either, as he is not obligated to restore the corpses to their prior locations after they have been reburied.
<b>There are</b> those <b>who say</b> that <b>Rava said: Since permission was granted to move</b> the first and second corpses, one may therefore <b>move them</b> all, including the third one. The Gemara asks: <b>And let us deem them</b> part of <b>a graveyard.</b> Since three corpses were found buried together, there might be others in the vicinity. <b>Reish Lakish said: They found a pretext and deemed Eretz Yisrael ritually pure.</b> Since at that point there was just one corpse, the Sages were lenient and were not concerned about any further impurity. The Sages wished to avoid uncertain impurity because people were careful to observe the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual purity in Eretz Yisrael, and additional cases of uncertain impurity would be complicated to observe.
The Gemara asks another question: If one <b>examined twenty cubits</b> from the location of those corpses <b>and did not find</b> anything, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Rav Menashya bar Yirmeya says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> This is <b>a graveyard.</b> Those three corpses compose the graveyard and there is no concern for others. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for this ruling? Why is there no concern that there might be many more corpses in the surrounding area? Once again <b>Reish Lakish said: They found a pretext and deemed Eretz Yisrael ritually pure.</b> Eretz Yisrael is deemed pure in a case of uncertainty.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Any</b> case of <b>uncertainty</b> with regard to <b>leprous sores</b> is <b>initially</b> deemed <b>pure until it is established that</b> it is a case <b>of ritual impurity. Once</b> it <b>has been determined</b> to be a case <b>of impurity, uncertainty</b> concerning <b>it is</b> deemed <b>impure.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? <b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: The verse states:</b> “This is the law of the plague of leprosy…<b>to pronounce it pure or to pronounce it impure”</b> (Leviticus 13:59). <b>Since the verse opened with purity first,</b> this teaches that any case of uncertainty concerning leprous sores is deemed pure. The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> if the <i>halakha</i> is based on this verse, then <b>even once</b> it <b>has been determined</b> to be a case <b>of impurity, uncertainty</b> concerning <b>it</b> should be deemed <b>pure as well,</b> as this interpretation of the verse should apply to all cases of uncertainty with regard to leprosy.
<b>Rather, when</b> this statement, <b>that Rav Yehuda</b> said that <b>Rav said, was stated, it was stated with regard to the following</b> dispute in a mishna concerning an uncertain leprous sore (<i>Nega’im</i> 4:11): <b>If</b> the <b>snow-white leprous sore [<i>baheret</i>],</b> which is one sign of leprosy, <b>preceded</b> the <b>white hair,</b> which is another sign, <b>he is impure.</b> This <i>halakha</i> is stated in the Torah (see Leviticus 13:3). <b>And if</b> the <b>white hair preceded</b> the <b><i>baheret</i></b> he is <b>pure,</b> as this is not considered a sign of impurity. If there is <b>uncertainty</b> as to which came first, he is <b>impure. And Rabbi Yehoshua said: <i>Keiha</i>.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the meaning of <b><i>keiha</i>? Rav Yehuda said:</b> Rabbi Yehoshua <b>pronounced</b> the matter <b>unsolvable and</b> ruled it is <b>pure.</b> The Gemara further asks: <b>And perhaps</b> this means that he <b>pronounced</b> the matter <b>unsolvable and</b> ruled it is <b>impure? Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: The verse states: “To pronounce it pure or to pronounce it impure”</b> (Leviticus 13:59); <b>since the verse opened with purity first,</b> any case of uncertainty concerning leprous sores is deemed pure.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> This mishna discusses another case that includes the statement: There is a basis to anticipate the matter. <b>One examines a man who experienced a gonorrhea-like discharge [<i>zav</i>] in seven ways, as long as he has not been confirmed as</b> having <b>a gonorrhea-like discharge [<i>ziva</i>].</b> With regard to an individual’s second such discharge, before he has been established as a greater <i>zav</i>, one examines to see whether there may have been a particular trigger of his discharge. One examines him <b>with regard to food and with regard to drink,</b> in case the discharge might have been the result of overeating or excess drinking; <b>with regard to a burden,</b> as it might have been caused by the weight of a heavy burden; <b>and with regard to jumping,</b> in case he jumped and this led to the discharge; <b>and with regard to sickness; and with regard to</b> an arousing <b>sight; and with regard to</b> the <b>thought</b> of a woman.
<b>Once he has been confirmed as</b> having <b>a <i>ziva</i>,</b> after two definite discharges of <i>ziva</i>, <b>one no</b> longer <b>examines him</b> in this way, as any discharge is deemed impure. If one experiences three discharges of <i>ziva</i>, he is obligated to bring an offering following his purification. Accordingly, <b>his</b> discharge that was due to <b>circumstances beyond his control,</b> i.e., for one of the seven reasons listed above, <b>and his</b> discharge about which it is <b>uncertain</b> if it is <i>ziva</i>, <b>and</b> even <b>his semen,</b> which is not usually considered the discharge of a <i>zav</i>, <b>are</b> all <b>impure.</b> Why is this so? It is <b>because there is a basis for</b> anticipating <b>the matter.</b> Once he has the status of a <i>zav</i>, it can be assumed that subsequent discharges are of <i>ziva</i> as well.
The Sages similarly taught: With regard to <b>one who strikes another</b> with heavy blows, <b>and</b> doctors <b>assessed</b> that <b>he would die</b> as a result of the beating, <b>but his</b> health <b>improved from what it was,</b> so that they then determined that he would not die from his injuries, and <b>afterward</b> his condition <b>worsened and he died,</b> the one who struck him is <b>liable</b> to receive court-imposed capital punishment, as it is assumed that the victim’s death was caused by the assault. <b>Rabbi Neḥemya says:</b> He is <b>exempt, because there is a basis for</b> anticipating <b>the matter.</b> Since the victim began to recover during his illness, it is reasonable to assume that his death was caused by a factor other than the assault.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived, that one examines a <i>zav</i> before he has been confirmed as having a <i>ziva</i>, but not after? <b>Rabbi Natan says</b> that <b>the verse states: “And the <i>zav</i> who has an issue [<i>zav et zovo</i>],</b> whether it is a man or a woman” (Leviticus 15:33). This teaches that after two discharges, corresponding to the words <i>zav</i> and <i>zovo</i>, with regard <b>to the third sighting,</b> when one is already a <i>zav</i>, the verse <b>juxtaposes</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a male to that <b>of a female:</b> Just as it makes no difference in the case of a female whether her discharge of menstrual blood was triggered by an external factor, as she is ritually impure regardless, once a male <i>zav</i> has the status of impurity, one no longer examines him to see if his discharge was triggered by an external factor.
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Zavim</i> 2:2) that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: For the third</b> discharge <b>one</b> still <b>examines him; for the fourth</b> discharge <b>one does not examine him.</b> Where does the verse allude to the fourth discharge? The Gemara answers: <b>Rather, they disagree with regard to</b> instances of the word <b>“<i>et</i>,”</b> i.e., whether or not the word “<i>et</i>” teaches an additional <i>halakha</i>, or whether it is written purely for syntactical reasons. <b>Rabbi Eliezer expounds</b> instances of the word <b>“<i>et</i>,”</b> and therefore he counts the phrase “<i>zav et zovo</i>” as three words referring to three discharges. <b>And the Rabbis do not expound</b> instances of the word <b>“<i>et</i>,”</b> which means that in their opinion this verse alludes to only two discharges.
§ The mishna taught that <b>his</b> discharge that was due to <b>circumstances beyond his control, and his</b> discharge about which it is <b>uncertain</b> if it is <i>ziva</i>, are considered impure.

Daf 66a

<b>Rava said: Do not say</b> that it is <b>uncertain</b> whether he <b>saw</b> a discharge and <b>uncertain</b> whether he <b>did not see</b> one. The uncertainty is not with regard to whether he experienced a discharge at all. <b>Rather, he certainly saw a</b> discharge, but it is <b>uncertain</b> whether the discharge was <b>due to,</b> i.e., defined as, an emission of <b>semen,</b> and it is <b>uncertain</b> whether the discharge was <b>due to</b> the <b>sighting</b> of <i>ziva</i>. In this case, <b>once he has been confirmed as being ritually impure</b> as a <i>zav</i>, <b>his uncertain</b> case is also <b>impure.</b>
§ The mishna taught that <b>his semen is impure.</b> The Gemara asks: With regard <b>to what</b> <i>halakha</i> is the mishna stating that the semen of a <i>zav</i> is impure? <b>If we say</b> that this is referring <b>to</b> the impurity of <b>contact,</b> i.e., this semen renders one who touches it impure, there is no need to teach this <i>halakha</i>. <b>Is it</b> any <b>worse than the semen of a pure</b> man, which also imparts impurity through contact? <b>Rather,</b> the mishna is teaching that <b>the semen of a <i>zav</i> renders</b> people <b>impure</b> even <b>by carrying,</b> like the impurity of <i>ziva</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>Who did you hear that said that</b> the <b>semen of a <i>zav</i> renders</b> one <b>impure by carrying? If we say</b> it is <b>this <i>tanna</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: The semen of a <i>zav</i> does not render</b> one <b>impure by carrying, and Rabbi Yehoshua says: It</b> does <b>render</b> one <b>impure by carrying, because it is impossible for</b> his semen <b>to be without droplets of <i>ziva</i>;</b> yet this source is no proof, as <b>even Rabbi Yehoshua said</b> that this semen imparts impurity by carrying <b>only due to the droplets of <i>ziva</i></b> it contains. <b>However, he did not say</b> that semen <b>in its unadulterated</b> form renders people impure.
<b>Rather, Rav Adda bar Ahava said:</b> The mishna does not mean that the semen of a <i>zav</i> itself renders one impure by carrying. Rather, it comes <b>to say that one does not attribute</b> the <i>ziva</i> <b>to it.</b> Even if the man experienced a discharge within twenty-four hours of his ejaculation of semen, one does not say his discharge was triggered by a general weakening as a result of his seminal emission. Once the man has experienced two discharges of a <i>zav</i> he is an impure <i>zav</i>, and this third discharge is also considered <i>ziva</i>.
When he was <b>before Rava, Rav Pappa thought to say</b> the following explanation for this <i>halakha</i>: <b>Since he sees</b> his discharges <b>out of</b> the <b>weakness</b> brought about from his previous <i>ziva</i>, it can be assumed that any later discharge, even one that follows a seminal emission, is <i>ziva</i>. <b>Rava said to him: But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Zavim</i> 2:3): <b>A convert who</b> has just <b>converted can immediately become impure as a <i>zav</i>.</b> Even if he had a seminal emission as a gentile, subsequently converted, and then experienced the discharge of a <i>zav</i>, one does not associate the discharge of a <i>zav</i> with the earlier emission of semen. Rather, it is treated as the first discharge of a <i>zav</i>. In this case there is no prior weakness of a <i>zav</i> to speak of, and nevertheless the convert is impure as a <i>zav</i>. Rav Pappa <b>said to</b> Rava: <b>There is no greater illness than this.</b> The very acceptance of mitzvot can be considered an illness, as the burden of mitzvot is a weighty matter. For this reason, one does not ascribe a discharge of a <i>zav</i> to the earlier emission of semen.
The Gemara comments: <b>Rather,</b> the possibility rejected earlier, that the mishna means that semen of a <i>zav</i> imparts impurity by carrying, <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The semen of a <i>zav</i> renders</b> one <b>impure by carrying</b> like the regular discharge of a <i>zav</i> <b>for</b> a twenty-four hour <b>period.</b> In other words, if a <i>zav</i> experienced a seminal emission within twenty-four hours of a <i>ziva</i>, the seminal emission does not impart impurity only through contact in the manner of the semen of a pure individual, but by carrying as well. <b>And Rabbi Yosei says</b> that it imparts impurity by carrying only on <b>its day,</b> i.e., if the seminal emission was before the evening of the day of the <i>ziva</i>. As opposed to Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua cited earlier, these <i>tanna’im</i> both maintain that the semen of a <i>zav</i> imparts impurity by carrying like <i>ziva</i> does.
The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what do</b> the first <i>tanna</i> and Rabbi Yosei <b>disagree?</b> What is the basis of their dispute? The Gemara answers: They disagree <b>with regard to</b> the following issue discussed <b>by Shmuel, as Shmuel raises a contradiction: It is written: “If there be among you any man who is not pure, by reason of that which chances him by night,</b> then shall he go abroad out of the camp, he shall not come within the camp” (Deuteronomy 23:11). This verse is applied to a <i>zav</i> who experienced a seminal emission. <b>And it is written: “When evening comes on, he shall bathe himself in water,</b> and when the sun is down, he may come within the camp” (Deuteronomy 23:12).
The Gemara explains the dispute. <b>The one who says</b> that the semen of a <i>zav</i> imparts impurity by carrying <b>for</b> a twenty-four hour <b>period</b> after experiencing a <i>ziva</i> <b>infers from</b> the phrase <b>“when evening comes on”</b> that the period can involve two evenings, as “comes on” indicates another evening. If so, the semen imparts impurity by carrying for a full twenty-four hour period. <b>And the other</b> Sage, Rabbi Yosei, <b>infers</b> from the expression <b>“by reason of that which chances him by night”</b> that the seminal emission imparts impurity by carrying only until that night, i.e., until the end of the day on which the man experienced the <i>ziva</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> according to <b>the one who says</b> that this <i>halakha</i> is derived from <b>“when evening comes on,” isn’t it written: “By reason of that which chances him by night”?</b> The Gemara answers: He could have <b>said to you</b> that this verse affords no proof, as it is referring to the usual case and <b>it is the manner of a seminal emission to occur at night.</b> Consequently, one cannot derive a <i>halakha</i> from this expression.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The tractate concludes with an aggadic statement about nazirites. <b>Samuel</b> the prophet <b>was a nazirite, in accordance with the statement of Rabbi Nehorai, as it was stated</b> that when Hannah, his mother, prayed for a son, she vowed: <b>“And no <i>mora</i> shall come upon his head”</b> (I Samuel 1:11). How is it derived that <i>mora</i> is an expression of naziriteship? <b>It is stated with regard to Samson: “And</b> no <b>razor [<i>mora</i>]</b> shall come upon his head, for the child shall be a nazirite to God” (Judges 13:5), <b>and it is stated: “And</b> no <b><i>mora</i>,” with regard to Samuel. Just as</b> the term <b>“<i>mora</i>” that is stated with regard to Samson</b> means that he was <b>a nazirite, so too</b> the term <b>“<i>mora</i>” that is stated with regard to Samuel</b> indicates that he was <b>a nazirite.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei said: But doesn’t</b> the word <b>“<i>mora</i>”</b> mean <b>nothing other</b> than the fear <b>of flesh and blood?</b> The word should be read as though it were written with an <i>alef</i>, and not a <i>heh</i>, so that it means fear. <b>Rabbi Nehorai said to him: But isn’t it already stated: “And Samuel said: How can I go; if Saul hears it he will kill me”</b> (I Samuel 16:2). This verse indicates <b>that there was fear of flesh and blood upon</b> Samuel. Consequently, the term <i>mora</i> must be understood in accordance with its plain meaning of a razor. If so, Samuel was indeed a nazirite.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav said to his son Ḥiyya:</b>

Daf 66b

<b>Seize</b> the opportunity <b>and</b> quickly <b>recite a blessing</b> over the cup of blessing for the Grace after Meals. <b>And similarly, Rav Huna said to his son, Rabba: Seize</b> the opportunity <b>and recite a blessing.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Is this to say that one who recites a blessing is preferable</b> to one who answers amen? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei says: The one who answers amen is greater than the one who recites the blessing? And Rabbi Nehorai said to him: By Heavens, it is so. Know</b> that this is true, <b>as the military assistants [<i>gulyarim</i>]</b> descend to the battlefield and <b>initiate the war and the mighty</b> follow them and <b>prevail.</b> The amen that follows a blessing is compared to the mighty who join the war after the assistants, illustrating that answering amen is more significant than reciting the initial blessing.
The Gemara responds: This <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Both the one who recites a blessing and the one who answers amen are included</b> among those who “stand up and bless” (Nehemiah 9:5), <b>but one hurries to first</b> reward <b>the one who recites the blessing.</b> This <i>baraita</i> apparently holds that reciting the blessing is greater than answering amen.
<b>Rabbi Elazar said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ḥanina said: Torah scholars increase peace in the world, as it is stated: “And all your children [<i>banayikh</i>] shall be taught of the Lord, and great shall be the peace of your children”</b> (Isaiah 54:13). The Sages interpreted this verse homiletically: Do not read it as: “Your children [<i>banayikh</i>],” but as: Your builders [<i>bonayikh</i>]. Torah scholars are those who build peace for their generation.
